Temurah



Daf 1a



Daf 1b



Daf 2a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>Everyone substitutes</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, <b>both men and women.</b> That is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for a person to effect substitution; rather,</b> it means <b>that if one substituted</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, <b>the substitution takes effect,</b> and the non-sacred animal becomes consecrated, and the consecrated animal remains sacred. <b>And</b> the one who substituted the non-sacred animal <b>incurs the forty [<i>sofeg et ha’arba’im</i>]</b> lashes.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara <b>notes</b> an apparent contradiction in the mishna: <b>This</b> mishna <b>itself</b> is <b>difficult: You say</b> that <b>everyone substitutes</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated one, which indicates that substitution may be performed <b><i>ab initio</i>. And then you teach:</b> That is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for a person to effect substitution; rather,</b> it means only <b>that if one</b> unlawfully <b>substituted</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, <b>the substitution takes effect,</b> indicating that it is effective only <b>after the fact.</b>
The Gemara responds: <b>And can you understand</b> the phrase: <b>Everyone substitutes,</b> as indicating that substitution may be performed <b><i>ab initio</i>?</b> If so, <b>before the mishna poses a difficulty for you, the verse should pose a difficulty for you, as it is written: “He shall not alter it, nor substitute it”</b> (Leviticus 27:10). The verse clearly states that it is prohibited to perform substitution.
<b>Rather, Rav Yehuda says: This</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching: Everyone can apply</b> the status of a consecrated animal to a non-sacred animal <b>by</b> the act of <b>substitution, both men and women.</b> But that is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for a person to effect substitution, as, if</b> one <b>did effect substitution,</b> the <b>substitution takes effect</b> only after the fact, <b>and he incurs the forty</b> lashes.
§ The mishna teaches: Everyone substitutes a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> does the comprehensive term: <b>Everyone,</b> serve <b>to include?</b> The Gemara answers: It serves <b>to include an inheritor, and</b> accordingly, this mishna is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
<b>As we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>An inheritor</b> who inherited a consecrated animal <b>places</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering when he sacrifices it as would the original owner, and likewise <b>an inheritor is able to substitute</b> a non-sacred animal for the consecrated one that he inherited. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. Rabbi Yehuda says: An inheritor does not place</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering when he sacrifices it, <b>and an inheritor does not substitute</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated one that he inherited. These acts may be performed only by the one who initially consecrated the animal. The mishna indicates that an inheritor is able to effect substitution, contrary to the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda.
The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yehuda?</b> The Gemara explains: <b>He derives</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the</b> animal’s <b>initial consecration from the end of the consecration,</b> i.e., the sacrifice of the consecrated animal, as follows: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>the end of a consecrated</b> animal, <b>an inheritor does not place</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering before slaughter, <b>so too,</b> with regard to <b>the initial consecration, an inheritor does not effect substitution</b> to consecrate the animal.
The Gemara asks: <b>And from where do we</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> of <b>placing hands itself?</b> The Gemara answers: The term <b>“his offering” is written three</b> times in the passage discussing a peace offering, in connection with the placing of hands (Leviticus 3:2, 8, 12). <b>One</b> instance teaches that the requirement of placing hands applies to <b>his offering but not the offering of a gentile. And one</b> instance teaches that it applies to <b>his offering but not the offering of another,</b> as only the owner places his hands on the offering. <b>And one</b> instance teaches that it applies to <b>his offering but not the offering of his father,</b> as an inheritor does not place his hands on an offering he inherited.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to Rabbi Meir, who said</b> that <b>an inheritor places</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering when he sacrifices it, <b>isn’t</b> the term <b>“his offering” written</b> in the verse? How does he interpret the third instance of that term? The Gemara explains: <b>He requires that</b> term <b>to include all joint owners [<i>ba’alei ḥoverin</i>] in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>placing hands,</b> to teach that they all must place their hands on the offering’s head.
The Gemara notes: <b>And</b> as for <b>Rabbi Yehuda, he does not accept</b> that <b>joint owners</b> are included in the requirement of <b>placing hands. What is the reason</b> for this? The reason is <b>that their offering is not specific</b> to one person and is therefore not included in the term “his offering.” <b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that <b>actually, he accepts</b> that one instance of “his offering” serves to include joint owners, <b>and he derives</b> both the <i>halakha</i> of <b>the offering of a gentile and</b> that of <b>the offering of another from one verse,</b> so <b>that one</b> instance of the term “his offering” <b>remains for him to</b> include <b>joint owners in</b> the requirement of <b>placing hands</b> on the head of an offering, and the third instance serves to teach that an inheritor does not place hands on the offering of his father.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is the reasoning</b> of <b>Rabbi Meir, who said</b> that <b>an inheritor</b> can <b>effect substitution?</b> The Gemara answers: Rabbi Meir could <b>say to you</b> that the repetition of the verb in the verse: “And <b>if he shall at all substitute [<i>hamer yamir</i>]</b> animal for animal” (Leviticus 27:10), serves <b>to include the inheritor.</b>

Daf 2b

<b>And he derives</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>the end of a consecrated</b> animal <b>from the initial consecration</b> of the animal, as follows: <b>Just as</b> with regard to <b>the initial</b> state of <b>consecration</b> of the animal, <b>an inheritor</b> can <b>effect substitution, so too,</b> with regard to <b>the end of the consecrated</b> animal, <b>an inheritor places</b> his <b>hands</b> on its head.
The Gemara asks: According to <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who holds that an inheritor is not able to effect substitution, <b>what does he do with this</b> repetitive language in the verse: <b>“And if he shall at all substitute”?</b> How does he interpret it? The Gemara answers: It serves <b>to include a woman</b> as able to effect substitution and to incur the penalty of forty lashes for doing so. <b>And</b> this is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Since</b> the verses concerning <b>the entire matter</b> of substitution <b>speak only in the masculine, as it is stated: “He shall not exchange it, nor substitute it”</b> (Leviticus 27:10), <b>from where</b> is it derived that <b>a woman</b> is included? <b>The verse states: “And if he shall at all substitute,” to include a woman.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to Rabbi Meir,</b> who derives a different <i>halakha</i> from that verse, <b>from where</b> does <b>he</b> derive that <b>a woman</b> is included? The Gemara answers: <b>He derives it from</b> the addition of the conjunction “and” in the verse: <b>“And if</b> he shall at all substitute.” <b>And Rabbi Yehuda does not interpret</b> the term <b>“and if”</b> as having any special significance.
The Gemara analyzes this dispute. <b>And</b> according to <b>both</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir and</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, the reason</b> why a woman is included is <b>that the verse</b> explicitly <b>includes a woman,</b> either by adding a conjunction or by repetition of the verb. The Gemara asks: Should one infer that if <b>the verse did not include</b> a woman, <b>I would say</b> that <b>when</b> a woman <b>performs</b> an act of <b>substitution she is not flogged? But didn’t Rav Yehuda say</b> that <b>Rav says, and so too the school of Rabbi Yishmael taught,</b> that when the verse states: <b>“When a man or woman shall commit any sin that people commit”</b> (Numbers 5:6), <b>the verse equates a woman with a man with regard to all punishments of the Torah?</b> Why, then, is there a need for the verse to include women in the prohibition against substitution?
The Gemara answers: It <b>was necessary</b> for the verse to include women specifically, <b>lest you say</b> that <b>this statement</b> that equates women with men in general refers only to <b>a punishment that</b> applies <b>equally to an individual and to the public. But here, since it is a punishment that does not</b> apply <b>equally to all, as we learned</b> in a mishna (13a): <b>The public and partners do not render</b> an animal <b>a substitute,</b> one could claim that <b>a woman as well, when she performs</b> an act of substitution, <b>is not flogged.</b> The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that she is in fact liable.
§ <b>Rami bar Ḥama raises a dilemma:</b> With regard to <b>a minor, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Is he able <b>to effect substitution</b> or not? The Gemara clarifies: <b>What are the circumstances</b> where this question arises? <b>If we say</b> that it is referring <b>to a minor who has yet to reach the age of</b> responsibility for his <b>vows,</b> i.e., twelve years and one day, <b>you should not raise the dilemma, as since he cannot consecrate</b> an animal by means of a vow, <b>can he effect substitution? Rather, when he raises this dilemma,</b> it is <b>with regard to a minor who has reached the age of</b> responsibility for his <b>vows.</b>
The Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Do we say</b> that a minor can effect substitution, <b>since the Master said</b> with regard to consecration: The verse states: “When <b>a man</b> shall clearly utter a vow” (Numbers 30:3). <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> the unusual formulation: <b>Shall clearly utter [<i>yafli</i>] a vow,</b> instead of the more conventional term: Shall take a vow [<i>yiddor</i>]? This serves <b>to include a discriminating</b> minor <b>[<i>mufla</i>] on the brink of adulthood,</b> teaching <b>that his consecration takes effect.</b> Perhaps, from the fact <b>that he can consecrate</b> an animal, <b>he can also effect substitution. Or perhaps, since no</b> minor <b>is subject to punishments, he cannot apply</b> sanctity to an animal <b>by</b> an act of <b>substitution,</b> which would incur a punishment.
And even <b>if you say</b> that <b>a minor can perform substitution, as he will come to</b> an age when he is <b>subject to punishments, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as to whether <b>a gentile</b> can <b>effect substitution? Do you say</b> that he can do so from the fact <b>that his consecration takes effect, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“Any man [<i>ish ish</i>]…</b>that brings his offering.” <b>What</b> is the meaning when <b>the verse states</b> repetitively <b>“<i>ish ish</i>”?</b> This serves <b>to include gentiles,</b> teaching <b>that they</b> can <b>take vows</b> to bring <b>vow offerings and gift offerings as a Jew</b> can. Since the consecration of a gentile takes effect, perhaps <b>he can effect substitution as well. Or perhaps, since he will not come to</b> a time when he is <b>subject to punishments,</b> therefore <b>when he performs</b> an act of <b>substitution,</b> the animal is <b>not consecrated.</b>
<b>Rava says: Come</b> and <b>hear, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Zevaḥim</i> 5:6): With regard to animals <b>consecrated by gentiles,</b> one <b>may not derive benefit</b> from them <i>ab initio</i>, <b>but</b> if one derived benefit from them, he is <b>not</b> liable for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property after the fact. <b>And</b> if one consumes <b>them one is not liable for</b> committing a transgression <b>with regard to</b> the prohibitions of <b><i>piggul</i></b> if they were sacrificed with the intent to consume them beyond their designated time, of <b><i>notar</i></b> if he consumed them beyond their designated time, <b>and</b> of consuming <b>ritually impure</b> offerings if he was impure. Gentiles <b>cannot render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute</b> for one they consecrate. <b>And one does not bring libations for</b> the offerings of a gentile as independent offerings, <b>but his offering requires libations.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: With regard to all of them I see</b> fit <b>to be stringent. In what</b> case <b>is this statement,</b> that Rabbi Shimon exempts animals consecrated by gentiles from liability for misuse, <b>said?</b> It is said <b>with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar,</b> i.e., offerings; <b>but with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> one who derives benefit from <b>them</b> is liable for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property. Rava notes: <b>In any event,</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that gentiles <b>cannot render</b> an animal <b>a substitute</b> for his offering.
The Gemara responds: <b>And Rami bar Ḥama</b> can say: <b>I do not raise a dilemma</b> in a case <b>where a gentile consecrated</b> an animal as an offering <b>for a gentile</b> such as himself <b>to achieve atonement.</b> In this case the <i>baraita</i> rules explicitly that he cannot effect substitution. <b>When I raise the dilemma,</b> it is in a case <b>where a gentile consecrated</b> an animal as an offering <b>and a Jew achieves atonement</b> with it. In this situation, <b>do we follow</b> the <b>one who consecrated</b> it, in which case the gentile cannot effect substitution, <b>or do we follow the one achieving atonement,</b> in which case he can?
The Gemara suggests: <b>Solve this</b> dilemma <b>by</b> invoking a statement <b>of Rabbi Abbahu, as Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> If <b>one consecrates</b> an animal as an offering to be brought by another, and the animal develops a blemish disqualifying it for sacrifice, if the one who consecrated it desires to redeem it, he <b>adds one-fifth</b> to its value, just as he would were it his own offering. By contrast, if the one achieving atonement with the offering desires to redeem it, he does not need to add one-fifth. <b>But the one achieving atonement</b> with the offering <b>can render</b> another animal <b>a substitute</b> for it as if he had consecrated it. <b>And</b> if <b>one separates <i>teruma</i>,</b> the portion of produce designated for a priest, <b>from his own</b> produce

Daf 3a

<b>for</b> the purpose of exempting the produce <b>of another, the benefit of discretion</b> as to which priest or Levite will receive it <b>is his.</b> Apparently, the <i>halakhot</i> of substitution follow the one achieving atonement.
Rami bar Ḥama could <b>say to</b> this: <b>There,</b> in Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement, the offering <b>comes on behalf of</b> the <b>Jew</b> who consecrated it. <b>Due to that</b> reason, <b>we follow</b> the <b>one atoning, and</b> during both the <b>initial</b> consecration of the offering <b>and its ultimate</b> sacrifice the animal <b>is in the possession of a Jew,</b> who is able to effect substitution. <b>But here,</b> where a gentile consecrated the animal, <b>this is how he raises the dilemma: Do we require that from the initial</b> consecration of the offering <b>until the ultimate</b> act of sacrifice <b>it</b> must <b>be in the possession of one who</b> is able to <b>effect substitution</b> or not? The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand [<i>teiku</i>]</b> unresolved.
§ <b>The Master said</b> in the <i>baraita</i> cited on the previous <i>amud</i>: With regard to animals <b>consecrated by gentiles,</b> one <b>may not derive benefit</b> <i>ab initio</i>, <b>but</b> if one benefited from them after the fact, he is <b>not</b> liable for <b>misusing</b> consecrated property. The Gemara explains: The <i>halakha</i> that one <b>may not benefit</b> from them <i>ab initio</i> is <b>by rabbinic law. And</b> that <i>halakha</i> that if one benefited from them after the fact <b>he is not</b> liable for <b>misusing</b> consecrated property is <b>by Torah law.</b>
<b>What is the reason</b> that one is not liable for misuse? <b>As it is written</b> with regard to misuse of consecrated property: <b>“If anyone commit a trespass, and sin through error,</b> in the holy items of the Lord” (Leviticus 5:15). <b>And we derive</b> a verbal analogy <b>from</b> the use of the word <b>“sin”</b> in this verse and the word <b>“sin”</b> used in the context of <b><i>teruma</i>:</b> “They shall therefore keep My charge, lest they bear sin for it” (Leviticus 22:9). <b>And with regard to <i>teruma</i> it is written</b> explicitly: “Even of all the hallowed items of <b>the children of Israel”</b> (Numbers 18:8), from which one can infer: <b>But not</b> of <b>gentiles.</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of misuse as well, therefore, do not apply to animals consecrated by gentiles.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And</b> if one consumes <b>them,</b> i.e., animals consecrated by gentiles, <b>one is not liable for</b> committing a transgression, <b>with regard to</b> the prohibitions of <b><i>piggul</i>, <i>notar</i>, or</b> consuming offerings while <b>ritually impure.</b> The source for this is a verse, <b>as it is written with regard to ritual impurity: “Speak to Aaron and to his sons, that they separate themselves from the holy items of the children of Israel,</b> which they hallow unto Me, <b>and that they profane not</b> My holy <b>name”</b> (Leviticus 22:2). This verse explicitly mentions items consecrated by the children of Israel, to emphasize that the <i>halakha</i> of distancing oneself from consecrated items while in a state of ritual impurity does not include items consecrated by gentiles.
<b>And one derives</b> the exemption from <b><i>notar</i></b> by drawing a verbal analogy <b>between</b> the word <b>“profane”</b> used in the context of <i>notar</i> and the word <b>“profane”</b> used in the context of <b>ritual impurity. As it is written</b> in the verse cited above <b>with regard to ritual impurity:</b> “That they separate themselves from the holy items of <b>the children of Israel,</b> which they hallow unto Me, <b>and that they profane not</b> My holy name.” <b>And it is written with regard to <i>notar</i>: “But every one that eats it shall bear his iniquity, because he has profaned the holy item of the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 19:8). Since the prohibition against consuming consecrated items while impure does not apply to the offerings of gentiles, neither does the prohibition of <i>notar</i>.
<b>And</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>derives</b> the gentiles’ exemption from <b><i>piggul</i></b> by verbal analogy <b>between</b> the word <b>“iniquity”</b> used in the context of <i>piggul</i> and the word <b>“iniquity”</b> used in the context of <b><i>notar</i>, as it is written with regard to <i>piggul</i>: “And the soul that eats of it shall bear his iniquity”</b> (Leviticus 7:18), <b>and it is written with regard to <i>notar</i>: “But everyone that eats it shall bear his iniquity, because he has profaned the holy item of the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 19:8). <b>And</b> based on these two verbal analogies, one concludes that with regard to <b>all of them,</b> i.e., <i>piggul</i>, <i>notar</i>, and ritually impure offerings, the prohibition against consumption applies only to offerings of <b>the children of Israel, but not</b> to those of <b>gentiles.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And</b> a gentile <b>cannot render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute</b> for one he has consecrated. The Gemara cites the source for this <i>halakha</i>: <b>As it is written</b> with regard to substitutions: <b>“He shall not exchange it, nor substitute</b> it” (Leviticus 27:10), <b>and it is written at the beginning of the matter: “Speak to the children of Israel, and say to them: When a man shall clearly utter a vow</b> of persons to the Lord, <b>according to your valuation”</b> (Leviticus 27:2). Since the passage specifically addresses the children of Israel, the <i>halakhot</i> of substitution apply only to them, not to gentiles.
The Gemara presents <b>another version</b> of the source for this <i>halakha</i>: The <i>baraita</i> states: <b>And</b> a gentile <b>cannot render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute</b> for one he has consecrated. <b>What is the reason?</b> The reason is <b>that</b> the <b>substitution of</b> consecrated <b>animals is juxtaposed with the animal tithe</b> (see Leviticus 27:32), <b>and the animal tithe</b> is juxtaposed <b>with the grain tithe. And with regard to the grain tithe it is written: “For the tithe of the children of Israel, which they set apart as a gift to the Lord,</b> I have given to the Levites for an inheritance” (Numbers 18:24), indicating that the <i>halakhot</i> of the grain tithe apply only to the <b>children of Israel, but not</b> to <b>gentiles.</b> From the juxtapositions, one derives that the same holds for substitution.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>And one does not bring libations</b> for a gentile as an independent offering, <b>but his offering requires libations.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? They are derived from a verse, <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to libations: “All that are <b>homeborn</b> shall do these after this manner, in presenting an offering made by fire, of a sweet savor to the Lord” (Numbers 15:13). One who is <b>homeborn,</b> i.e., a Jew, <b>brings libations, but a gentile does not bring libations.</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that a gentile’s <b>burnt offering should not require libations;</b> therefore, <b>the verse states: “So</b> shall it be done for each bull, or for each ram, or for each of the lambs, or of the goats” (Numbers 15:10–11). This teaches that every burnt offering, even that of a gentile, requires libations.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Yosei said: I see</b> the logic of the opinion that <b>in all of</b> these cases it is correct <b>to be stringent</b> about the offerings of gentiles. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason? With regard to</b> the offering of a gentile, <b>it is written:</b> “Whosoever he be of the house of Israel, or of the strangers in Israel, that brings his offering, whether it be any of their vows, or any of their gift offerings, which are brought <b>to the Lord</b> for a burnt offering” (Leviticus 22:18). The verse equates the offerings of gentiles, “the strangers in Israel,” with those of Jews, and indicates that the consecrated status of the former is identical to that of the latter.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>In what</b> case <b>is this statement said?</b> It is said <b>with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar, but with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> one who benefits <b>from them</b> is liable for <b>misusing</b> consecrated property. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason?</b> The reason is <b>that when we learn</b> that items consecrated by gentiles are not subject to liability for <b>misuse</b> by the verbal analogy between the word <b>sin</b> and another instance of the word <b>sin from</b> the context of <b><i>teruma</i>,</b> this applies only to consecrated property that is <b>similar to <i>teruma</i>, which is consecrated</b> with <b>inherent sanctity. But with regard to</b> an item that has the <b>sanctity of the Temple maintenance, whose sanctity</b> inheres in its <b>value,</b> as it will ultimately be sold and the proceeds used for Temple maintenance, the analogy does <b>not</b> apply.
§ The mishna teaches that one who effects substitution receives lashes. <b>Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> With regard to <b>any prohibition in the Torah,</b> if one <b>performed an action</b> to transgress <b>it,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes. But if he <b>did not perform an action</b> to transgress <b>it,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from receiving lashes.
The Gemara asks: <b>But is</b> it an established <b>principle that</b> one who transgresses <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action</b> is <b>exempt? But there is</b> the prohibition of <b>substitution, which is a prohibition that does not involve an action, and</b> one is <b>flogged</b> for transgressing it, <b>as we learned</b> in the mishna: That is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for</b> a person <b>to effect substitution; rather,</b> it means <b>that if one substituted</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, <b>the substitution takes effect, and</b> the one who substituted the non-sacred animal <b>incurs the forty</b> lashes.
The Gemara answers: <b>Rav</b> could have <b>said to you:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna written? <b>It is</b> in accordance with that opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who said:</b> With regard to <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action, one is flogged for</b> transgressing <b>it.</b> The Rabbis, however, disagree.
The Gemara counters: <b>But can you establish the mishna</b> as being <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda? But we established the first clause</b> of the mishna <b>as not</b> being <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, as it teaches: Everyone substitutes</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, and the Gemara added (2a): <b>What</b> does the expansive term <b>everyone</b> serve <b>to include?</b> It serves <b>to include an inheritor, and</b> accordingly, this mishna is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who holds that an inheritor cannot effect substitution.
The Gemara answers: The <b><i>tanna</i></b> of <b>this</b> mishna <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda <b>with regard to one</b> matter, <b>that one is flogged for</b> transgressing <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action, and disagrees with him with regard to one</b> other matter, <b>as Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that <b>an inheritor does not place</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering when he sacrifices it, and <b>an inheritor</b> is <b>not</b> able to <b>substitute</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated one that he inherited; <b>and the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna <b>holds</b> that <b>an inheritor places</b> his <b>hands</b> on the head of the offering when he sacrifices it, and <b>an inheritor</b> is able to <b>substitute</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated one that he inherited.
<b>Rav Idi bar Avin says</b> that <b>Rav Amram says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili:</b> With regard to <b>every prohibition in the Torah,</b> if one <b>performed an action</b> to transgress <b>it,</b> he is <b>flogged.</b> But if he <b>did not perform an action</b> to transgress <b>it,</b> he is <b>exempt</b> from lashes, <b>except for one who takes an oath, and one who effects substitution, and one who curses another using the name</b> of God. For these three transgressions, <b>even though he did not perform an action,</b> he is <b>liable</b> to receive lashes. <b>They said in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina: Also one who</b> separates <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> designating the <b>first fruits</b> is flogged, even though <i>teruma</i> can be separated by intention alone, without an action.
The Gemara discusses the sources for these exceptions. <b>From where do we</b> derive that <b>one who takes an oath</b> using the name of God is flogged? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says in the name of Rabbi Meir</b> that <b>the verse states: “For the Lord will not hold him guiltless that takes His name in vain”</b> (Exodus 20:7). One can infer: <b>The higher,</b> i.e., heavenly, <b>court</b>

Daf 3b

<b>does not hold him guiltless,</b> as repentance alone will not atone for the sin, <b>but the lower,</b> i.e., earthly, <b>court flogs him,</b> thereby allowing him to atone, <b>and absolves him</b> of guilt.
<b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye: But</b> why not <b>say</b> that the verse means <b>he cannot be absolved</b> of guilt <b>at all?</b> Abaye <b>said to him: If so, let the verse write</b> only: <b>“Will not hold him guiltless,” and be silent. Why do I</b> need the verse to specify: “For <b>the Lord</b> will not hold him guiltless”? This serves to teach that <b>it is the higher court that will not hold him guiltless, but the lower court flogs</b> him <b>and absolves him</b> of guilt.
§ The Gemara continues: <b>We found</b> a source for the <i>halakha</i> that one who takes <b>an oath in vain</b> using the name of God is flogged. <b>From where do we</b> derive that the same holds for <b>a false oath?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan himself says</b> the derivation: The verse states with regard to an oath taken in vain: “You shall not take the name of the Lord your God in vain; for the Lord will not hold him guiltless that takes His name in vain” (Exodus 20:7). It says: <b>“In vain…in vain,” twice</b> in this verse. <b>If</b> the second instance of the term <b>is not</b> necessary for the <b>matter of an oath</b> taken <b>in vain,</b> which is derived from the first instance, <b>apply it</b> to the <b>matter of a false oath,</b> to teach <b>that</b> one who takes such an oath <b>is flogged.</b>
<b>Rabbi Abbahu objects to this</b> explanation of Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of the <b>false oath</b> under discussion? <b>If we say that</b> the one taking the oath <b>said:</b> I take an oath <b>that I will not eat</b> a certain item, <b>and he</b> then <b>ate</b> that item; <b>there he performed an action</b> to violate his oath by eating the item, and the prohibition would then involve an action. <b>And</b> if it was <b>rather</b> a case <b>where he said:</b> I take an oath <b>that I will eat</b> a certain item, <b>and he did not eat</b> that item; <b>is that</b> individual <b>flogged? But wasn’t it stated:</b> If one says: I take <b>an oath that I will eat this loaf today, and the day passed and he had not eaten</b> it, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan and Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish</b> both <b>say: He is not flogged.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> that <b>he is not flogged, as it is a prohibition that does not involve an action, and one is not flogged for any prohibition that does not involve an action. And Reish Lakish says</b> that <b>he is not flogged, as</b> there was only <b>an uncertain forewarning for</b> the transgressor. One must be warned immediately before the transgression to be held liable, but here there was no single moment of transgression, <b>and one is not flogged for any</b> transgression that follows <b>an uncertain forewarning.</b> If Rabbi Yoḥanan holds he is not flogged, to what case is he referring when he states that one is flogged for a false oath even though no action is involved?
<b>Rather, Rabbi Abbahu says: Let</b> the case <b>be where</b> one says: I take an oath that <b>I ate</b> a certain item, and in fact he did not eat it; <b>or</b> he said: I take an oath that <b>I did not eat</b> a certain item, and in fact he did eat it. In these cases, he transgresses the prohibition by speech alone, without an action.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is different</b> about an oath concerning the past, <b>that</b> the verse <b>includes</b> one who takes an oath of: <b>I ate, or: I did not eat,</b> as liable to be flogged, more <b>than</b> one who takes an oath of: <b>I will eat, and</b> then <b>did not eat?</b> In both cases, there is no action performed. <b>Rava says: The Torah explicitly includes</b> a case of <b>a false</b> oath that is <b>similar to</b> a case of an oath taken <b>in vain: Just as</b> an oath taken <b>in vain</b> refers <b>to the past, so too,</b> the <b>false</b> oath for which one is flogged must pertain <b>to the past.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised an objection to Rabbi Abbahu</b> from a mishna (<i>Shevuot</i> 27b): If one says: I take <b>an oath that I will not eat this loaf;</b> I take <b>an oath that I will not eat it;</b> I take <b>an oath that I will not eat it, and he</b> then <b>ate it, he is liable</b> for <b>only one</b> violation. <b>And this is</b> the <b>oath of an utterance for which one is liable</b> to receive <b>lashes for its intentional</b> violation, <b>and</b> to bring <b>a sliding-scale offering for its unwitting</b> violation. Rabbi Yirmeya asks: When the mishna uses the limiting term: <b>This is</b> the oath, <b>what</b> does it serve <b>to exclude?</b> Is it <b>not to exclude</b> a case where one said: I take an oath that <b>I ate</b> a certain item, when in fact he did not eat it, <b>or:</b> I take an oath that <b>I did not eat</b> a certain item, when he did eat it, to teach <b>that</b> he is <b>not flogged</b> in those cases? This would contradict the explanation of Rabbi Abbahu.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> in both cases one is flogged for violating the prohibition intentionally. Rather, the phrase means: <b>This is</b> the oath <b>for which</b> one <b>brings an offering for its unwitting</b> violation, <b>but</b> in a case where one unwittingly said: <b>I ate</b> a certain item, when he in fact did not eat it, <b>or: I did not</b> eat a certain item, when he did eat it, <b>he does not bring an offering. And whose</b> opinion does this mishna represent? <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael, who said:</b> One is <b>liable</b> to bring an offering <b>only for</b> an oath that refers to <b>the future, but</b> one is <b>flogged</b> even for an oath that pertains to the past.
The Gemara counters: But <b>say the latter clause</b> of the mishna (<i>Shevuot</i> 29a): If one takes an oath that a stone is gold or some similar absurdity, <b>this is</b> the <b>oath</b> taken <b>in vain, for which one is liable</b> to receive <b>lashes</b> if he utters <b>it intentionally and for</b> which <b>one is exempt</b> from bringing an offering if he utters <b>it unwittingly. What</b> does the limiting term: <b>This is</b> the oath, used by the mishna serve <b>to exclude?</b> Does it <b>not</b> serve <b>to exclude</b> a case where one said: <b>I ate</b> a certain item, when he in fact did not eat it, <b>or: I did not eat</b> a certain item, when he did eat it, to teach <b>that he is not flogged</b> in those cases?
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> in both cases one is flogged for violating the prohibition intentionally. Rather, the phrase means: <b>This is</b> the oath <b>for which one is exempt from</b> bringing <b>an offering</b> if he utters <b>it unwittingly, but</b> in a case where one said: <b>I ate</b> a certain item, when he in fact did not eat it, <b>or: I did not eat</b> a certain item, when he did eat it, <b>he brings an offering. And whose</b> opinion does this represent? <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, who said: One brings an offering</b> even <b>for</b> the violation of an oath that refers to <b>the past.</b>
The Gemara protests: <b>But didn’t you say</b> earlier that <b>the first clause</b> represents the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> that one does not bring an offering for a false oath pertaining to the past? <b>Rather,</b> from the fact <b>that the latter clause</b> represents the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva,</b> it follows that <b>the first clause</b> must represent the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva as well. And</b> one must therefore suggest an alternative explanation of <b>the first clause.</b> The phrase: This is the oath, employed there does <b>not,</b> as previously claimed, serve <b>to exclude</b> a case where one said: <b>I ate</b> a certain item, <b>or: I did not eat</b> a certain item. <b>Rather,</b> it serves <b>to exclude</b> a case where one said: <b>I will eat</b> a certain item, <b>and he did not</b> subsequently <b>eat</b> it.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is different</b> about an oath pertaining to the future, that the mishna should exclude it specifically? The Gemara responds: <b>Since</b> this clause of the mishna <b>deals with</b> an oath that pertains to <b>the future,</b> it stands to reason that it would <b>exclude</b> an oath pertaining to <b>the future.</b> If it <b>deals with</b> an oath that pertains to <b>the future,</b> why would it <b>exclude</b> an oath pertaining to <b>the past?</b> In summary, the entire mishna is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Akiva. The first clause excludes from the punishment of lashes the passive violation of an oath pertaining to the future, and the latter clause includes in the obligation to bring an offering a false oath pertaining to the past if he uttered it unwittingly. Neither clause contradicts the explanation of Rabbi Abbahu.
§ In listing those who incur the punishment of lashes even though they did not perform an action in their transgression, Rabbi Yosei HaGelili was cited above as including: <b>And one who effects substitution.</b> The Gemara adds: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to the <i>tanna</i></b> who would recite the <i>baraitot</i> in his academy: <b>Do not teach: And one who effects substitution,</b> in this regard, <b>because</b> one who effects substitution <b>performs an action</b> of consecration <b>by his speech.</b>
§ Rabbi Yosei HaGelili also lists <b>one who curses another using the name</b> of God as liable to be flogged. The Gemara asks: <b>From where do we</b> derive this? <b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that <b>Rabbi Oshaya says: The verse states: “If you will not observe</b> to perform all the words of this law that are written in this book, that you may fear this glorious and awesome name, the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 28:58). This refers to one who curses using the name of God. <b>And it is written</b> immediately thereafter: <b>“Then the Lord will make your plagues wondrous [<i>vehifla</i>]”</b> (Deuteronomy 28:59). With regard to <b>this</b> term <b><i>hafla’a</i>, I do not know what its</b> meaning <b>is. When it says: “And the judge shall cause him to lie down [<i>vehippilo</i>] and to be beaten before him”</b> (Deuteronomy 25:2), <b>you must say</b> that the term <b><i>hafla’a</i> is</b> referring to <b>lashes.</b>
The Gemara asks: Why not <b>say</b> that <b>even</b> one who utters <b>a true oath</b> is flogged, as this too could be considered exhibiting insufficient fear of God’s name? The Gemara answers: <b>It is written explicitly</b> that a bailee may take an oath in court, as the verse states: <b>“The oath of the Lord shall be between them both,</b> to see whether he has not damaged his neighbor’s goods” (Exodus 22:10).
The Gemara counters: Why not <b>say</b> that <b>this statement</b> in the verse means only that a bailee is able <b>to appease the other,</b> i.e., the owner, and exempt himself from payment for the damaged goods, <b>but</b> he <b>should</b> nevertheless <b>be flogged?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>You cannot say</b> this, <b>as it is written:</b> “You shall fear the Lord your God, and Him you shall serve, <b>and by His name you shall swear”</b> (Deuteronomy 6:13). This verse clearly indicates that it is permitted to take a truthful oath using the name of God.
The Gemara asks: Isn’t <b>that</b> verse <b>required</b> as a source <b>for</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>of Rav? As Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said: From where</b> is it derived <b>that</b> it is permitted for one to <b>take an oath</b> obligating himself <b>to fulfill the mitzvot?</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as it is stated: “I have sworn, and have confirmed it, to observe Your righteous ordinances”</b> (Psalms 119:106). The Gemara counters that there is another source: <b>Isn’t another verse written:</b> “You shall fear the Lord your God; Him you shall serve; <b>and to Him you shall cleave, and by His name you shall swear”</b> (Deuteronomy 10:20). This second verse teaches that it is permitted for one to take a truthful oath.
The Gemara resumes the discussion of one who curses another: <b>Rather,</b> the term <i>vehifla</i> in Deuteronomy 28:59 <b>comes to</b> teach <b>what?</b> It comes <b>to</b> teach that <b>one who curses another using the name</b> of God is flogged. The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> why not <b>say</b> that that verse refers <b>to one who pronounces the name of Heaven in vain?</b> The Gemara answers: Even if it does, <b>is cursing another using the name</b> of God any <b>less</b> of a sin <b>than pronouncing the name of Heaven in vain?</b> If one is flogged for the latter, certainly one is flogged for the former.
The Gemara clarifies its question: <b>This is what is difficult for us:</b> One can <b>say</b> that <b>for one who pronounces the name of Heaven in vain, it is sufficient for him</b> to receive <b>lashes</b> to atone for his act, <b>but</b> in the case of <b>one who curses another using the name of God, since he has performed two</b> improper acts, first <b>that he pronounces the name of Heaven in vain, and</b> second that <b>he inflicts pain on another, it should not be sufficient for him to</b> receive <b>lashes</b> to atone for his act.

Daf 4a

The Gemara responds: <b>You cannot say</b> that Deuteronomy 28:59 is referring to one who pronounces the name of Heaven in vain, <b>as it is written: “You shall not curse the deaf”</b> (Leviticus 19:14), which prohibits all curses, even those using God’s name. <b>Granted, if you say</b> that Deuteronomy 28:59 is referring <b>to cursing another,</b> one can say that the separate <b>warning</b> of punishment <b>for this</b> prohibition is <b>from here, as it is written: “You shall not curse the deaf.”</b> One verse articulates the prohibition, and the other indicates liability for punishment. <b>But if you say</b> that Deuteronomy 28:59 is referring to <b>pronouncing the name of Heaven in vain, from where is the warning of</b> this prohibition? A prohibition requires two verses to include liability for punishment.
The Gemara counters: <b>Why not,</b> i.e., what is the difficulty? <b>But isn’t it written: “You shall fear the Lord your God, and Him you shall serve”</b> (Deuteronomy 6:13)? Fearing God certainly includes not pronouncing His name in vain, and this verse can therefore serve as the warning. The Gemara answers: <b>That</b> verse <b>is a warning</b> stated as <b>a positive mitzva.</b> In order to qualify as a warning, the verse must prohibit, not command.
§ It was stated (3a): <b>They said in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina: Also one who</b> separates <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> the separation of <b>the first fruits</b> is liable to be flogged. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason for</b> the prohibition mentioned by <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina? The verse states: “You shall not delay to offer of the fullness of your harvest and the outflow of your presses”</b> (Exodus 22:28). When the verse states: <b>The fullness of the harvest, these are</b> the <b>first fruits;</b> and when the verse states: <b>“And the outflow of your presses,” this is <i>teruma</i>. And</b> the verse <b>says: “You shall not delay,”</b> i.e., do not delay the separation of the first fruits by separating <i>teruma</i> beforehand.
<b>It was stated:</b> If one separated <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> the separation of <b>the first fruits, Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> disagreed. <b>One said</b> that <b>he is flogged, and one said</b> that <b>he is not flogged.</b> The Gemara suggests: <b>It may be concluded that it is Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, who said</b> that <b>he is flogged,</b> from the fact <b>that Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said</b> earlier: <b>Also one who</b> separates <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> the separation of <b>the first fruits is flogged.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>On the contrary, it may be concluded that it is Rabbi Elazar who said</b> that <b>he is flogged, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Demai</i> 7:6): <b>If two baskets of untithed produce were before</b> someone, <b>and he said:</b> The <b>tithe of this</b> basket <b>is in that</b> basket, then the produce of <b>the first</b> basket is thereby <b>tithed.</b> And when he separates sufficient tithe from the second basket to exempt both baskets, the produce of the second basket will be considered tithed as well.
If he said: The tithe <b>of this</b> basket is <b>in that</b> basket, <b>and</b> the tithe <b>of that</b> basket is <b>in this</b> basket, the produce of <b>the first</b> basket is <b>tithed,</b> as stated, <b>but</b> the produce of <b>the second</b> basket is <b>not tithed.</b> Since the produce of the first basket had just been tithed, it could not be used to tithe the second, since the tithe may be separated only from untithed produce. If he said: <b>Their tithes</b> should be separated as <b>tithe,</b> each <b>basket in the other,</b> he has <b>declared the assignation</b> of tithe concurrently, and the produce of both baskets is thereby tithed.
<b>And it was stated</b> with regard to the first clause of that mishna that <b>Rabbi Elazar said: He is flogged, because he</b> separated the <b>second tithe of</b> the produce of the first basket <b>prior to</b> the separation of the <b>first tithe of the other</b> basket. One must always separate tithes in order, the first tithe before the second tithe. If Rabbi Elazar holds that one is flogged for separating tithes in the wrong order, he presumably also holds that one is flogged for separating <i>teruma</i> before first fruits. The Gemara affirms: <b>It may be concluded</b> that Rabbi Elazar is the one who said he is flogged.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if that is so, it follows that it is <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, who said</b> that <b>he is not flogged. Shall we say</b> then that the statement of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> here poses <b>a difficulty to</b> the earlier statement of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> that one who separates <i>teruma</i> before separating first fruits is flogged?
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> when <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> stated simply: Even one who separates <i>teruma</i> prior to the separation of the first fruits,

Daf 4b

he was <b>referring</b> not to liability for lashes, but <b>to the exemption</b> from lashes stated in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan (3a). <b>And this</b> is what he <b>is saying: One is not flogged for</b> transgressing <b>a prohibition that does not involve an action</b>…<b>They said in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina,</b> that <b>also one who</b> separates <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> the separation of <b>the first fruits</b> is not flogged, since he performs no action.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what is different</b> about <b>one who effects substitution, that he is flogged</b> despite not having performed an action? Is it <b>because he has performed an action with his speech,</b> by consecrating the animal? If so, one who separates <b><i>teruma</i> prior to</b> the separation of <b>the first fruits should be flogged as well, because he has performed an action with his speech,</b> by consecrating the produce.
<b>Rabbi Avin said: It is different there,</b> in the case of one who tithes produce in the wrong order, <b>as it is a prohibition</b> whose violation <b>can be rectified by</b> fulfilling <b>a positive mitzva.</b> As a rule, if the Torah specifies a positive mitzva to be performed after transgressing a prohibition to rectify it, that prohibition does not carry a punishment of lashes. This is the case here, <b>as it is written: “Out of all of your tithes you shall set apart</b> all of that which is due to the Lord” (Numbers 18:29), which teaches that one who separated tithes in the incorrect order or who separated <i>teruma</i> before separating the first fruits, must still separate the earlier tithes even after the later tithes, or the first fruits even after the <i>teruma</i>.
§ <b>Rav Dimi was sitting and saying this <i>halakha</i>,</b> that one who separates <i>teruma</i> prior to separating the first fruits is not flogged because it is a prohibition whose violation can be rectified. <b>Abaye said to him: And</b> is it correct that <b>one is not flogged</b> for transgressing <b>any prohibition</b> whose violation <b>can be rectified by</b> fulfilling <b>a positive mitzva?</b>
<b>But</b> there is the case of <b>one who effects substitution, which is a prohibition</b> whose violation <b>can be rectified by</b> fulfilling <b>a positive mitzva,</b> as the verse states: “He shall not exchange it, nor substitute it, a good for a bad, or a bad for a good; and if he shall at all change animal for animal, then both it and that for which it is changed shall be holy” (Leviticus 27:10). <b>And</b> even so, one who effects substitution <b>is flogged, as we learned</b> in the mishna (2a): That is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for a person to effect substitution; rather,</b> it means <b>that if one substituted</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, the <b>substitution takes effect and</b> the one who substituted the non-sacred animal <b>incurs</b> the punishment of <b>the forty</b> lashes.
Rav Dimi answered: <b>There are two prohibitions</b> specified in the verse as transgressed by one who effects substitution: “He shall not exchange it,” and: “Nor substitute it.” <b>But</b> there is only <b>one positive mitzva:</b> “Both it and that for which it is changed shall be holy.” <b>And one positive mitzva does not come and uproot two prohibitions.</b> Therefore, although generally, one who transgresses a prohibition that can be rectified is not flogged, one who effects substitution is flogged.
The Gemara counters: <b>But there is</b> the case of the <b>rapist</b> who forces himself upon a virgin, who is obligated to marry the victim if she wishes and is then prohibited from divorcing her. <b>As</b> here the verse states <b>one prohibition:</b> “He may not send her away all his days,” <b>and one positive mitzva:</b> “And she shall be his wife” (Deuteronomy 22:29). This teaches that he can rectify the transgression of divorcing her by remarrying her. <b>And</b> yet, the <b>one positive mitzva does not come and uproot</b> the <b>prohibition, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a rapist who</b> married and then <b>divorced</b> his victim, <b>if he is an Israelite,</b> who is permitted to marry a divorcée, <b>he remarries</b> her <b>and he is not flogged. But if he is a priest,</b> who is prohibited from marrying a divorcée, <b>he is flogged and he does not remarry</b> her.
The Gemara answers: <b>You say</b> that a case that concerns <b>priests</b> challenges the principle that a rectifiable transgression does not make one liable for flogging. But in the case of <b>priests</b> there <b>is another reason</b> why they are flogged, <b>as the Merciful One increased</b> the severity of their transgressions, for they have <b>greater holiness.</b> By contrast, one who transgresses a prohibition unrelated to the priesthood will not be flogged if its violation can be rectified by fulfilling a positive mitzva.
§ Which prohibitions carry the punishment of lashes is <b>subject to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>,</b> as it is taught in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states concerning the Paschal offering: “And <b>you shall let nothing of it remain until the morning,</b> but that which remains of it until the morning you shall burn with fire” (Exodus 12:10). <b>The verse comes to place a positive mitzva after</b> the <b>prohibition</b> in order <b>to say that one is not flogged for</b> transgressing <b>it.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
<b>Rabbi Ya’akov says:</b> He <b>is not</b> exempt from lashes <b>for</b> that <b>reason,</b> but <b>rather because it is a prohibition that does not involve an action, and one is not flogged for</b> transgressing <b>any prohibition that does not involve an action.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>By inference,</b> it may be concluded <b>that Rabbi Yehuda holds</b> that one <b>is flogged for</b> transgressing a prohibition that does not involve an action.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Rabbi Ya’akov,</b> who holds that the exemption from lashes stems from the prohibition’s not involving an action, <b>for what</b> purpose does the clause <b>“but that which remains of it until the morning you shall burn with fire” come?</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>He requires it for that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Pesaḥim</i> 83a): <b>The bones</b> of the Paschal offering that contain edible marrow but cannot be eaten because it is prohibited to break the bones of the Paschal offering, <b>and the sinews, and the leftover</b> meat must all <b>be burned on the sixteenth</b> of Nisan, immediately after the first day of the Festival. If the <b>sixteenth occurs on Shabbat, they</b> must <b>be burned on the seventeenth, because</b> the mitzva to burn <b>them does not override Shabbat or</b> the <b>Festival.</b> Therefore, they are burned on the first weekday.
<b>And Ḥizkiyya says, and so it is taught in the school of Ḥizkiyya: What is the reason</b> for this? <b>The verse states:</b> “And you shall let nothing of it remain until the morning, <b>but that which remains of it until the morning you shall burn with fire.”</b> By using the word “morning” twice, <b>the verse comes to provide a second morning for</b> the offering’s <b>burning</b> if the first morning falls on Shabbat or a Festival.
§ <b>Abaye said:</b> With regard to <b>any matter that the Merciful One states</b> in the Torah <b>not to perform, if one performed</b> it, his action <b>is effective,</b> but the violator is flogged. <b>As, if it enters your mind</b> that <b>it is not effective, why</b> would he be <b>flogged</b> for accomplishing nothing? <b>Rava said:</b> If one performed it, <b>it is not effective at all. And this</b> is the reason <b>that he is flogged: Because he transgressed the statement of the Merciful One.</b>

Daf 5a

The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a rapist who</b> married and then <b>divorced</b> his victim, <b>if he is an Israelite,</b> who is permitted to marry a divorcée, <b>he remarries</b> her <b>and he is not flogged. And if you say,</b> like Rava, that <b>since one violates the statement of the Merciful One, he is flogged, this one should be flogged as well</b> for divorcing his victim. But according to the opinion of Abaye, it stands to reason that he should not be flogged, since his remarriage nullifies the effects of the divorce. This should be <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara answers that Rava could <b>say to you:</b> It <b>is different there, as the verse states:</b> “He may not send her away <b>all his days”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:29). This teaches that for <b>all his days,</b> he remains <b>under</b> the obligation to <b>arise and remarry</b> her. Once he remarries her, it turns out that he did not divorce her for all of his days and therefore did not violate the prohibition. This is why he is not flogged.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> what is derived from the phrase “all his days”? The Gemara answers: <b>If the Merciful One did not state “all his days,” I would say</b> that <b>he has violated a prohibition</b> by divorcing her, and that <b>if he desires</b> he may choose <b>to remarry</b> her, <b>and if he</b> so <b>desires</b> he may choose <b>not to remarry</b> her. The phrase “all his days” <b>teaches us</b> that he is obligated to remarry her.
The Gemara records <b>another version</b> of the discussion, in which it <b>raises an objection</b> from the <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>a rapist who</b> married and then <b>divorced</b> his victim, <b>if he is an Israelite, he remarries</b> her <b>and he is not flogged. But if he is a priest, he is flogged and he does not remarry</b> her. The <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that <b>if he is an Israelite he remarries</b> her and he is not flogged, indicating that he must take her back because his divorce was not effective. This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye,</b> who holds that transgressions are legally effective.
The Gemara answers that it <b>is different there, as the Merciful One states:</b> “He may not send her away <b>all his days”</b> (Deuteronomy 22:29), which teaches that for <b>all his days,</b> he remains <b>under</b> the obligation to <b>arise and remarry</b> her. Therefore, it is only in this specific case that the divorce is not effective.
The Gemara comments: <b>And</b> as for <b>Rava,</b> he could <b>say to you</b> that <b>if the Merciful One had not stated “all his days,” I would say</b> that the Israelite <b>should be flogged and should</b> still <b>remarry</b> her, <b>for it is solely a prohibition</b> that he has violated, <b>as it is written: He may not send her away. Therefore, the verse writes “all his days,” to render</b> the case of <b>a rapist a prohibition</b> whose violation can be <b>rectified by</b> fulfilling <b>a positive mitzva, for which one is not flogged.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of one who <b>separates <i>teruma</i> from poor-quality</b> produce <b>for superior-quality</b> produce, i.e., he separated <i>teruma</i> from the inferior produce in order to fulfill the obligation of separating <i>teruma</i> from other produce that is high-quality. This is prohibited, <b>as the Merciful One states:</b> “Out of all that is given you, you shall set apart all of that which is due to the Lord, <b>of all the best thereof”</b> (Numbers 18:29).
And the Sages interpret this verse as follows: “Of all <b>the best thereof,” yes,</b> but one should <b>not</b> separate <b>poor-quality</b> produce. <b>And</b> yet <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 2:4): <b>One may not separate <i>teruma</i> from poor-quality</b> produce <b>for superior-quality</b> produce, <b>and if one did separate <i>teruma</i></b> in that manner, <b>his <i>teruma</i> is</b> valid <b><i>teruma</i>. Apparently,</b> his action <b>is effective,</b> which is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, in accordance with</b> the statement of <b>Rabbi Ile’a. As Rabbi Ile’a said: From where</b> is it derived <b>with regard to one who separates <i>teruma</i> from poor-quality</b> produce <b>for superior-quality</b> produce <b>that his <i>teruma</i> is</b> valid <b><i>teruma</i>? As it is stated</b> with regard to <i>teruma</i>: <b>“And you shall bear no sin by reason of it, seeing that you have set apart from it the best thereof”</b> (Numbers 18:32). The verse defines separation from inferior produce as a transgression but teaches that it is nevertheless effective, because <b>if it is not consecrated</b> as <i>teruma</i>, <b>why</b> would one <b>bear a sin</b> for accomplishing nothing? <b>From here</b> it is derived <b>with regard to one who separates <i>teruma</i> from poor-quality</b> produce <b>for superior-quality</b> produce <b>that his <i>teruma</i> is</b> valid <b><i>teruma</i>.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> that all transgressions are legally effective, what does the phrase “And you shall bear no sin by reason of it” teach? The Gemara answers: <b>If the Merciful One had not stated: “And you shall bear no sin by reason of it,” I would say</b> that <b>this is what the Merciful One said: Perform the mitzva in the optimal</b> manner by separating <i>teruma</i> from superior-quality produce, <b>but if one did not perform</b> the mitzva in that manner, <b>he is not called a sinner.</b> The verse <b>teaches us</b> that one who fails to perform this mitzva in the optimal manner sins.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of one who separates <i>teruma</i> <b>from</b> one <b>type</b> of produce <b>to</b> exempt <b>another type</b> of produce, <b>as the Merciful One states: “All the best of the oil,</b> and all the best of the wine, and of the grain, the first part of them which they give to the Lord” (Numbers 18:12)? This teaches that one is obligated <b>to give the best of one</b> type of produce <b>and the best of another</b> type of produce, each individually. <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 2:4): <b>One may not separate <i>teruma</i> from</b> one <b>type</b> of produce <b>for another type, and if one did separate <i>teruma</i></b> in that manner, <b>his <i>teruma</i> is not</b> valid <b><i>teruma</i>. Apparently,</b> the transgression <b>is not effective.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
The Gemara answers that <b>Abaye</b> could <b>say to you:</b> It <b>is different there, as the verse</b> repeats this prohibition and <b>states: “The first part of them,”</b> indicating that one must give <b>a first part for this</b> type of produce <b>and a first part for that</b> type of produce. If the verse had not taught so explicitly in this case, one would have assumed that the transgression is effective. <b>And Rabbi Ile’a likewise says</b> that the phrase in the verse <b>“the first part</b> of them” is the exception that proves the rule.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava,</b> that transgressions are not effective, what does the term “the first part of them” teach? The Gemara answers: <b>If the Merciful One had not stated: “The first part</b> of them,” <b>I would say</b> that the prohibition applies only to <b>wine and</b> olive <b>oil, with regard to which it is written: “Best…best,”</b> teaching <b>that one may not separate <i>teruma</i> from this</b> type <b>for that</b> type.
<b>But</b> as for <b>wine and grain,</b> or one type of <b>grain and</b> another type of <b>grain, with regard to which</b> the term <b>“best” is written</b> only <b>once, when one separates <i>teruma</i> from this</b> grain or wine <b>for that</b> grain or wine, he is not held liable for transgressing Torah law, and <b>he is not flogged.</b> Therefore, <b>the Merciful One writes: “The first part</b> of them,” to teach that one must <b>give the best of this and the best of that.</b>
The Gemara records <b>another version</b> of the last point: But as for <b>wine and grain, with regard to which</b> the term <b>“best” is written</b> only <b>once, one may separate <i>teruma</i> from this for that</b> <i>ab initio</i>. Therefore, <b>the Merciful One writes: “The first part</b> of them,” to teach that even in the case of wine and grain, one may not separate <i>teruma</i> from one for the other.
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>dedications</b> of property to the priests, with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states: “No</b> devoted item, that a man may devote unto the Lord of all that he has, whether of man or animal, or of the field of his possession, <b>shall be sold or redeemed;</b> every devoted item is most holy unto the Lord” (Leviticus 27:28). <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Arakhin</i> 28b): <b>Items dedicated to priests are not redeemed; rather, one gives them to the priest. Apparently,</b> if one transgresses the prohibition and redeems a dedicated item, his action <b>is not effective.</b> This seems to be <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
The Gemara explains that Abaye could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the Merciful One states: “Is most holy,”</b> to teach that <b>it shall be as it is.</b> Once it is dedicated, its status cannot be changed by means of redemption. But in other matters the transgression is effective.

Daf 5b

<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava, this</b> term: <b>“Is</b> most holy,” serves <b>to exclude</b> the case of <b>a firstborn</b> offering from the prohibition of sale. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>It is stated with regard to a firstborn</b> offering: “But the firstling of an ox, or the firstling of a sheep, or the firstling of a goat, <b>you shall not redeem;</b> they are holy” (Numbers 18:17). <b>But</b> if it develops a blemish <b>it</b> may still be <b>sold.</b> By contrast, <b>it is stated with regard to</b> the animal <b>tithe</b> offering: <b>“It shall not be redeemed”</b> (Leviticus 27:33), <b>and</b> the animal tithe may <b>not be sold, not</b> when <b>alive and not</b> when <b>slaughtered, not</b> when <b>unblemished and not</b> when <b>blemished.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of <b>substitution,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states: “He shall not exchange it, nor substitute it”</b> (Leviticus 27:10), <b>and it is taught</b> in the mishna (2a): That is <b>not</b> to say <b>that it is permitted for a person to effect substitution; rather,</b> it means <b>that if one substituted</b> a non-sacred animal for a consecrated animal, <b>the substitution takes effect, and</b> the one who substituted the non-sacred animal <b>incurs the forty</b> lashes. <b>Apparently,</b> his action <b>is effective,</b> and this seems to be <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara explains that Rava could <b>say to you: It is different there, as</b> that same <b>verse states:</b> “And if he shall at all substitute animal for animal, then <b>both it and that for which it is substituted shall be holy,”</b> which teaches that his action is effective in this context specifically.
<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> that transgressions are effective in general, that clause is still necessary, because <b>if the Merciful One had not stated: “Both it and that for which it is substituted</b> shall be holy,” <b>I would say</b> that <b>this</b> initially consecrated animal <b>will leave</b> its consecrated state, <b>and that</b> non-sacred animal <b>will enter</b> into sanctity instead. Therefore, the verse <b>teaches us</b> that the first animal retains its sanctity as well.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of <b>a firstborn</b> offering, with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states:</b> “But the firstling of an ox, or the firstling of a sheep, or the firstling of a goat, <b>you shall not redeem;</b> they are holy” (Numbers 18:17)? <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (21a): All sacrificial animals that became blemished <b>are subject to redemption</b> through sale, <b>and their substitutes</b> are also subject <b>to redemption</b> through sale, <b>except for the firstborn and the</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offerings. <b>Apparently,</b> if one attempts to redeem a firstborn offering, his action <b>is not effective,</b> and this seems to be <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
The Gemara explains that Abaye could <b>say to you: It is different there, as</b> that same <b>verse states: “They are</b> holy,” thereby teaching that <b>they shall</b> always <b>be as they are,</b> even if one attempts to redeem them.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to Rava,</b> who maintains that transgressions are not effective, <b>why does he</b> need the term <b>“They are</b> holy”? The Gemara answers: This term teaches that if one substituted another animal for a firstborn offering or for an animal tithe offering, <b>they,</b> the originally consecrated animals, <b>are sacrificed, but their substitutes,</b> although they have sanctity, <b>are not sacrificed.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to Abaye, from where does he</b> derive <b>this conclusion</b> that the substitutes are not sacrificed? The Gemara answers: The verse states concerning firstborn offerings: <b>“Whether it be ox or sheep, it is the Lord’s”</b> (Leviticus 27:26). One can infer from this wording that it <b>is sacrificed to the Lord but its substitute is not sacrificed.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> what does <b>Rava</b> derive from that verse? The Gemara responds: <b>Yes,</b> it <b>is indeed so that</b> he, like Abaye, derives <b>from that verse,</b> not from Numbers 18:17 as originally suggested, the <i>halakha</i> <b>that</b> the substitute of a firstborn is not sacrificed. <b>Rather, why do I</b> need the term <b>“They are</b> holy” which appears in that verse? It <b>teaches with regard to a firstborn</b> offering <b>or</b> an animal <b>tithe</b> offering <b>whose blood was mixed with</b> the blood of <b>any</b> other offering <b>brought upon</b> the altar <b>that</b> the blood <b>is</b> nevertheless <b>sacrificed on the altar</b> as it would have been individually.
The Gemara asks: <b>And Abaye, from where does he</b> derive <b>this conclusion,</b> that such blood is sacrificed? The Gemara answers: He derives it <b>from</b> the verse concerning the High Priest’s service on Yom Kippur: <b>“And he shall take of the blood of the bull, and of the blood of the goat,</b> and put it upon the corners of the altar” (Leviticus 16:18). One might ask: <b>But isn’t there more blood of the bull than of the goat?</b> Why is the blood of the goat not nullified? <b>From here</b> it is derived <b>that</b> offerings <b>brought upon</b> the altar <b>do not nullify one another, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that the phrase in the verse <b>“And he shall take of the blood of the bull, and of the blood of the goat”</b> serves to teach <b>that they must be mixed.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yoshiya.</b> Accordingly, Abaye derives that mixtures of blood may be sacrificed.
<b>And</b> as for <b>Rava,</b> he holds that <b>there,</b> the High Priest would take <b>from this</b> blood of the bull <b>by itself and from that</b> blood of the goat <b>by itself,</b> rather than mixing them together. <b>And</b> in this matter, <b>he holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yonatan,</b> who disagrees with Rabbi Yoshiya.
The Gemara continues its analysis of the dispute between Abaye and Rava. <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of <b>an animal tithe</b> offering, with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states: “It shall not be redeemed”</b> (Leviticus 27:33)? <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (21a): All sacrificial animals that became blemished <b>are subject to redemption</b> through sale, <b>and their substitutes</b> are also subject to redemption through sale, <b>except for the firstborn and the</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offering. <b>Apparently,</b> if one attempts to redeem an animal tithe offering, his act <b>is not effective,</b> and this seems to be <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
The Gemara explains that Abaye could <b>say to you: It is different there, as one derives</b> that <i>halakha</i> <b>from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a firstborn offering, by verbal analogy between the term <b>“<i>avara</i>”</b> that is stated with regard to an animal tithe offering: “Whatsoever passes [<i>ya’avor</i>] under the rod” (Leviticus 27:32), and the term <b>“<i>avara</i>”</b> that is stated with regard to <b>a firstborn</b> offering: “You shall set apart [<i>veha’avarta</i>] to the Lord all that opens the womb” (Exodus 13:12). It was already derived above that a firstborn offering cannot be redeemed. But in general, transgressions are effective.
The Gemara objects: But <b>isn’t there</b> the case of one who separated <b><i>teruma</i> prior to the</b> separation of the <b>first fruits,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states: “You shall not delay to offer of the fullness of your harvest and the outflow of your presses”</b> (Exodus 22:28)? The verse was expounded earlier (4b) as teaching that one must separate first fruits before separating <i>teruma</i>. <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 3:6): If <b>one</b> separates <i>teruma</i> <b>prior</b> to the separation of the first fruits, <b>although he has</b> transgressed <b>a prohibition, what he did is done,</b> and produce has the status of <i>teruma</i>. This appears to refute the opinion of Rava.
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the verse states: “From all that is given you, you shall set apart</b> that which is the Lord’s <b><i>teruma</i>”</b> (Numbers 18:29), thereby teaching that the separation of <i>teruma</i> is effective in any case. But in general, transgressions are not effective.
<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye</b> that transgressions are generally effective, that verse <b>is required</b> to teach another <i>halakha</i>, <b>as Rav Pappa said to Abaye</b> (<i>Beitza</i> 13b): Rabbi Shimon ben Lakish stated that if the first tithe was separated while the grain was still on the stalks, that amount is exempt from <i>teruma</i>, even though the amount of <i>teruma</i> the priest receives is thereby reduced. <b>If that is so,</b> then <b>even</b> if the Levite <b>preceded</b> the priest by taking the first tithe after the grain had been threshed and arranged <b>in a pile, we should exempt</b> that grain from the obligation of <i>teruma</i> <b>as well.</b>
Abaye <b>said to</b> Rav Pappa: <b>With regard to your</b> claim, <b>the verse states: “From all that is given you, you shall set apart.”</b> This verse teaches that the Levites must designate a portion of all the gifts they receive and give it to the priests, even if they received them before <i>teruma</i> had been separated.
Rav Pappa asked: <b>What did you see</b> that leads you <b>to include</b> the first tithe taken from <b>the pile</b> in the category: “All that is given,” <b>and to exclude</b> that which is taken from <b>the stalks?</b> Abaye answered: <b>I include</b> a tithe taken from <b>the pile, as</b> it has been processed to the point where <b>it is included in the category of grain,</b> since it is written: “The first fruits of your grain…you shall give him” (Deuteronomy 18:4); <b>and I exclude</b> a tithe taken from <b>the stalks, as</b> it <b>is not included in the category of grain</b> and is not yet obligated in <i>teruma</i>.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of <b>a widow</b> betrothed <b>to a High Priest,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One stated: “A widow, or one divorced,</b> or a profaned woman, or a harlot, these <b>shall he not take;</b> but a virgin of his own people shall he take to wife. And he shall not profane his seed among his people” (Leviticus 21:14–15)? <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Kiddushin</i> 66b): <b>Any case where there is</b> a valid <b>betrothal and</b> yet <b>there is a transgression, the offspring follows the flawed</b> lineage. For example, if a widow, who may not marry a High Priest, nevertheless did so, the offspring may not marry a priest. Still, the marriage is in force, contrary to the opinion of Rava.
The Gemara explains that Rava could say: <b>It is different there, as the verse states:</b> “And <b>he shall not profane [<i>lo yeḥallel</i>] his seed.”</b> The verse states only that the offspring is profaned, not that he has the status of a <i>mamzer</i>, which would hold for one born of a union between two people with regard to whom marriage cannot take effect. Rava infers from the verse that the betrothal of a High Priest and a widow specifically does take effect. But in general, transgressions are not effective.
<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> that transgressions are generally effective, what is derived from that phrase? Abaye can say: If it merely means to teach that the betrothal takes effect, <b>let the verse state</b> simply: <b><i>Lo yaḥel</i>,</b> which would have the same meaning. <b>What</b> is indicated by the use of the longer form: <b><i>Lo yeḥallel</i>?</b> This teaches that there are two profanations: <b>One for him,</b> i.e., that the offspring is profaned, <b>and one for her,</b> i.e., that the mother is disqualified from marrying even a common priest.
The Gemara objects: <b>But isn’t there</b> the case of one who <b>consecrated blemished</b> animals <b>for</b> sacrifice on <b>the altar,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states:</b> “But <b>whatsoever has a blemish, that you shall not bring;</b> for it shall not be acceptable for you” (Leviticus 22:20)? <b>And we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one consecrates blemished</b> animals <b>for</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar, even though he has</b> transgressed <b>a prohibition, what he did is done,</b> and the consecration takes effect. This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the verse states</b> with regard to blemished animals: <b>“But for a vow it shall not be accepted”</b> (Leviticus 22:23). Since the verse specifies only <b>that it is</b> its sacrifice <b>which does not effect acceptance,</b> one may <b>consequently</b> infer that if one <b>consecrates</b> them, <b>they are</b> still <b>consecrated.</b> But in general, transgressions are not effective.
<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> that transgressions are generally effective, what does this phrase teach? Abaye can say that <b>if the Merciful One had not stated: “But for a vow it shall not be accepted,” I would say</b> that one who consecrated it is considered <b>like</b> one who <b>transgressed a mitzva but</b> the offering is still <b>fit</b> to be sacrificed. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that it may not be sacrificed as an offering.
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of one who <b>consecrates unblemished</b> animals <b>for Temple maintenance,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states:</b>

Daf 6a

“Either a bull or a lamb that has anything too long or too short, <b>that may you offer for a gift;</b> but for a vow it shall not be accepted” (Leviticus 22:23). <b>This</b> verse teaches that only blemished animals may be <b>consecrated</b> for <b>Temple maintenance. And we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one who consecrates unblemished</b> animals for <b>Temple maintenance, even though he has</b> transgressed <b>a prohibition, what he did is done.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: From the</b> same <b>place</b> that it was derived earlier <b>that blemished</b> animals <b>are included,</b> i.e., that their consecration <b>for</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar</b> is effective after the fact, it is likewise derived that <b>unblemished</b> animals <b>are included,</b> that their consecration <b>for Temple maintenance</b> is effective. But in general, transgressions are not effective.
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>robbery,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states:</b> “You shall not oppress you neighbor, and <b>you shall not rob</b> him” (Leviticus 19:13), <b>and we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Kamma</i> 93b): <b>One who robs</b> another of <b>wood and fashions it</b> into <b>vessels,</b> or one who robs another of <b>wool and fashions it</b> into <b>garments, pays</b> the victim <b>according to</b> the value of the goods at <b>the time of the robbery,</b> but he need not return the vessels or garments, since by changing the stolen items he acquired them. If he can acquire the stolen item, this is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
