The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the verse states:</b> “He shall restore the stolen item <b>that he took by robbery”</b> (Leviticus 5:23). This teaches that he must return the original item only if it is still <b>like</b> that <b>which he took by robbery</b> and has not been altered. But in general, transgressions are not effective.
<b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye, this</b> phrase: <b>“That he took by robbery,” is necessary</b> to teach that one who takes a false oath denying he robbed another, who must then pay an additional fifth of the value of the stolen item when he returns it, need <b>add a fifth</b> only <b>for</b> denying <b>his</b> own act of <b>robbery,</b> but <b>for</b> denying <b>his</b> deceased <b>father’s</b> act of <b>robbery</b> when sued by the victim as the robber’s heir, he need <b>not add</b> an additional <b>fifth.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>collateral,</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One states:</b> “When you lend your neighbor any manner of loan, <b>you shall not go into his house to fetch his pledge”</b> (Deuteronomy 24:10), teaching that a creditor may not seize collateral if the debtor delays payment. <b>And we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bava Metzia</i> 113a): If the creditor nevertheless seized two items as collateral, <b>he returns a mattress by night,</b> as the debtor requires it for sleeping, <b>and a plow,</b> which is needed for his daytime work, <b>by day.</b> The creditor may keep possession of seized collateral when it is not being used, which is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rava.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Rava</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the verse states:</b> “And if he be a poor man, you shall not sleep with his pledge; <b>you shall restore [<i>hashev tashiv</i>]</b> to him the pledge” (Deuteronomy 24:12–13). The repetition of the verb teaches that the creditor must return the collateral many times, e.g., he must return a plow each morning and take it back each night, and return a mattress each night and take it back each morning, but he does not have to return the collateral permanently. But in general, transgressions are not effective.
The Gemara notes: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye,</b> that transgressions are generally effective, the repetition of the verb may be explained as follows: <b>If the Merciful One had not stated: “<i>Hashev tashiv</i>,” I would say</b> that a creditor who seizes collateral from the home of the debtor has <b>performed a prohibition,</b> but having done so, <b>if he desires, he may return</b> the collateral, <b>and if he desires, he may not return</b> it. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that he must return the items the debtor needs.
The Gemara objects: <b>But there is</b> the case of <b>produce in the corner of the field, which is given to the poor [<i>pe’a</i>],</b> with regard to <b>which the Merciful One stated:</b> And when you reap the harvest of your land, <b>you shall not wholly reap the corner of your field”</b> (Leviticus 23:22), but one must leave a corner of the field for the poor. <b>And we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Pe’a</i> 1:5): The optimal way to fulfill <b>the mitzva of <i>pe’a</i></b> is for the owner <b>to separate</b> it <b>from the standing</b> grain, i.e., grain that has not been harvested. If <b>he did not separate</b> it <b>from the standing</b> grain, <b>he separates</b> it <b>from the sheaves</b> of grain that have already been harvested. If <b>he did not separate</b> it <b>from the sheaves, he separates</b> it <b>from the pile</b> of grain, <b>as long as he has not</b> yet <b>smoothed</b> the pile.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: Once <b>he smooths</b> the pile of grain, it becomes obligated in tithes. Therefore, <b>he</b> must first <b>tithe</b> the grain <b>and</b> then <b>give</b> a portion of the produce <b>to</b> the poor as <i>pe’a</i>, so that the poor need not tithe what they receive. Additionally, the Sages <b>said in the name of Rabbi Yishmael:</b> If he did not separate <i>pe’a</i> during any of these stages, and he milled the grain and kneaded it into dough, <b>he separates</b> <i>pe’a</i> <b>even from the dough</b> and gives it to the poor. Even if the owner harvested the grain, the <i>pe’a</i> is still not considered his, which is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Abaye</b> could <b>say to you: It is different there, as the verse states: “You shall leave</b> them for the poor and for the stranger” (Leviticus 19:10), and subsequently, it again states: <b>“You shall leave</b> them for the poor and for the stranger” (Leviticus 23:22). The <b>superfluous</b> phrase teaches that the owner must give <i>pe’a</i> in any event, even if he harvests the grain and kneads it into dough. But in general, transgressions are effective.
<b>And Rava,</b> who maintains that in general transgressions are not effective, could <b>say to you</b> that the extra verse teaches that <b>there is another</b> type of <b>leaving</b> over one’s grain <b>that is like this. And what is that? It is</b> the case of <b>one who renounces ownership of his vineyard.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one renounced ownership of his vineyard,</b> thereby exempting it from tithes and gifts to the poor, <b>and he rose early in the morning,</b> retook possession of it, <b>and harvested it, he is</b> still <b>obligated in</b> the mitzva of <b>individual fallen</b> grapes left for the poor <b>[<i>peret</i>], and</b> in the mitzva of <b>incompletely formed clusters</b> of grapes left for the poor <b>[<i>olelot</i>],</b> and <b>in</b> the mitzva of <b>forgotten</b> clusters of grapes left for the poor, <b>and in</b> the mitzva of <b><i>pe’a</i>, but he</b> remains <b>exempt from the</b> mitzva to <b>tithe</b> his produce. His obligation to give the gifts to the poor even though the vineyard was at some point ownerless is derived from the repeated phrase: “You shall leave them for the poor.”
§ <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rava, said to Rav Ashi: And now that we have given all of these answers,</b> explaining that Abaye and Rava concede to one another in all the above cases, <b>with regard to what</b> cases do <b>Abaye and Rava disagree?</b>
Rav Ashi answered: <b>They disagree with regard to fixed [<i>ketzutza</i>] interest, and</b> their dispute is <b>like</b> that <b>of Rabbi Elazar</b> and Rabbi Yoḥanan. <b>As Rabbi Elazar said:</b> If a debtor paid <b>fixed interest</b> and petitions the court to have it returned to him, since the Torah prohibits the charging of interest, it <b>is repossessed</b> from the creditor <b>by</b> the <b>judges</b> of the court.

Daf 6b

If a debtor paid <b>a hint of interest</b> and petitions the court to have it returned to him, it <b>is not repossessed</b> from the creditor <b>by</b> the <b>judges</b> of the court. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan says: Even fixed interest is not repossessed by</b> the <b>judges.</b> Abaye holds that transgressions are effective, and the interest now belongs to the creditor and cannot be repossessed, in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan. Therefore, the judges do not appropriate it. Rava holds that transgressions are not effective, and the creditor is in unlawful possession of the interest. Therefore, the judges repossess it, in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Elazar.
Rav Aḥa <b>said to</b> Rav Ashi: <b>There,</b> in the dispute concerning fixed interest, do Rabbi Elazar and Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>disagree with regard to logical reasoning,</b> i.e., the principle of whether transgressions are effective? Not so; rather, <b>they disagree there with regard to</b> the interpretation of the <b>verses, as Rabbi Yitzḥak said: What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yoḥanan? The verse states: “[He] has given forth upon interest, and has taken increase; shall he then live? He shall not live;</b> he has done all these abominations; he shall be put to death; his blood shall be upon him” (Ezekiel 18:13). Apparently, a usurer <b>is subject</b> to divine punishment <b>for</b> his <b>life, but</b> the interest is <b>not</b> subject <b>to returning.</b> This is the source for the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan.
<b>Rav Aḥa bar Adda said</b> that the source for the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan is <b>from here:</b> “Take no interest of him or increase, <b>but fear your God,</b> that your brother may live with you. You shall not give him your money upon interest, nor give him your provisions for increase; <b>I am the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 25:36–38). In effect, God is saying: <b>I have made</b> a usurer <b>subject to</b> punishment for insufficient <b>fear</b> of Me, <b>but</b> the interest is <b>not</b> subject <b>to returning.</b>
<b>Rava said</b> that the source for the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan is <b>from here:</b> “Has lent at advance interest, or exacted accrued interest; shall he live? He shall not live! <b>He has done all these abominations; he shall be put to death; his blood shall be upon him”</b> (Ezekiel 18:13). And an earlier verse states: <b>“If he fathers a son that is a robber, a shedder of blood”</b> (Ezekiel 18:10). <b>Usurers are</b> thereby <b>juxtaposed to shedders of blood,</b> i.e., murderers: <b>Just as</b> the sins of <b>shedders of blood cannot be undone, so too,</b> the sins of <b>usurers cannot be undone.</b>
<b>And Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak said: What is the reasoning of Rabbi Elazar? As the verse states:</b> “Take no interest of him or increase; but fear your God, <b>that your brother may live with you”</b> (Leviticus 25:36). The verse teaches that one must <b>return</b> the interest <b>to</b> the debtor <b>so that he will</b> be able to <b>live</b> together <b>with you.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if this matter is not a consequence of the dispute of Abaye and Rava, <b>with regard to what</b> case <b>do Abaye and Rava disagree?</b> The Gemara answers: There is perhaps no practical dispute between them, and they disagree only <b>with regard to</b> the theoretical question of whether <b>deviation</b> from what is stated in the Torah is <b>effective.</b> According to the opinion of Abaye, if there is no verse teaching otherwise, a transgression is presumed to be effective. According to the opinion of Rava, it is presumed to be not effective. They, therefore, disagree as to which <i>halakhot</i> reflect the rule and which are exceptions.
§ The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the answer to this question: In accordance <b>with the answers we answered</b> above, Abaye and Rava disagree <b>with regard to fixed interest. According to</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye</b> that transgressions are effective, the creditor <b>does not</b> need to <b>return</b> the <b>interest,</b> as he has acquired it. But <b>according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava</b> that transgressions are not effective, the creditor must <b>return</b> the <b>interest,</b> as it still belongs to the debtor.
The Gemara objects: <b>But Abaye also holds</b> that <b>we repossess fixed interest by</b> the authority of the <b>judges, as Abaye said:</b> In the event that <b>this</b> one, <b>who was collecting</b> a debt <b>from another,</b> demanded that he pay <b>four dinars as interest, and</b> the debtor <b>gave to the creditor, in his store, a cloak that was worth five</b> dinars <b>in</b> place of the <b>four</b> dinars of interest, the <i>halakha</i> is that <b>when we repossess</b> the interest <b>from</b> the creditor, <b>we repossess</b> only <b>four</b> dinars <b>from him. And</b> as for <b>that</b> additional one <b>dinar,</b> one presumes the debtor <b>gave</b> it <b>to him as a gift. And Rava said: We repossess</b> all <b>five</b> dinars <b>from him. What is the reason? All of it came into his possession by virtue of</b> a payment of <b>interest.</b> In any event, it is clear that Abaye holds that fixed interest is repossessed by the court.
The Gemara concludes as earlier: <b>Rather,</b> there may be no practical dispute between them, as <b>when Abaye and Rava disagree,</b> it is only <b>with regard to</b> the theoretical question of whether <b>deviation</b> from what is stated in the Torah is <b>effective.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to the topic of blemished animals. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state: “But whatever has a blemish, you shall not offer;</b> for it shall not be acceptable for you” (Leviticus 22:20)? <b>If</b> it serves to teach that <b>you may not slaughter</b> a blemished animal as a sacrifice even if it has been consecrated, <b>that is</b> already <b>stated below,</b> later on in the passage, as the Gemara will soon explain. <b>Rather, why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> that <b>you may not offer</b> a blemished animal? This serves to teach that <b>you may not consecrate</b> it. <b>From here,</b> the Sages <b>stated</b> (<i>Tosefta</i> 1:10): <b>One who consecrates blemished</b> animals <b>for</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar violates five</b> separate <b>categories</b> of prohibition.
He is liable <b>for</b> violating the prohibitions: <b>You may not offer</b> a blemished animal as an offering, i.e., <b>you may not consecrate</b> it; <b>you may not slaughter</b> it; <b>and for</b> the prohibition: <b>You may not sprinkle</b> its blood; <b>and for</b> the prohibition: <b>You may not burn all of it</b> on the altar; <b>and for</b> the prohibition: <b>You may not burn part of it.</b> The Sages <b>said in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda:</b> One <b>also</b> violates a prohibition against <b>the collection of the blood.</b>
The Gemara interjects: <b>The Master said</b> above: <b>If</b> one suggests that the prohibition in Leviticus 22:20 teaches that <b>you may not slaughter</b> a blemished animal as an offering, <b>that is</b> already <b>stated below,</b> later on in the passage. The Gemara asks: <b>Where is</b> it <b>stated?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state: “Blind, or broken, or maimed, or having a cyst,</b> or scabbed, or scurvy, <b>you shall not offer”</b> (Leviticus 22:22)? <b>If</b> this serves to teach that <b>you may not consecrate</b> them, <b>that is already stated above,</b> in verse 20. <b>Rather, why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> that <b>you may not offer</b> these animals? This serves to teach that <b>you may not slaughter</b> them if they have been consecrated.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: The verse states: <b>“Nor make an offering by fire of them</b> upon the altar unto the Lord” (Leviticus 22:22). <b>These</b> words teach that one may not burn a blemished animal in the <b>fires</b> of the altar. <b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that it is prohibited to burn <b>all of</b> the animal. <b>From where</b> is it derived that it is prohibited to burn <b>some</b> portions <b>of</b> the animal? <b>The verse states: “Of them,”</b> which prohibits the burning of even part of them.
<b>From where</b> is it derived that <b>sprinkling the blood</b> of a blemished animal is prohibited? <b>The verse states: “Upon the altar,”</b> thereby indicating that one may not sacrifice any part of it, even its blood, on the altar. The verse continues: <b>“Unto the Lord,”</b> which serves <b>to include the scapegoat</b> of Yom Kippur in this prohibition, that it may not be blemished even though it is not sacrificed on the altar.
The Gemara interjects: <b>And</b> does the term: <b>“Unto the Lord,”</b> serve <b>to include</b> the scapegoat? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The Torah condemns one who slaughters an offering outside the Temple “and has not brought it unto the door of the Tent of Meeting, to present it as an offering unto the Lord” (Leviticus 17:4)? <b>If</b> the verse had stated only <b>“offering,” I</b> would <b>derive</b> that one is liable <b>even</b> for slaughtering animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> outside the Temple, <b>for they are</b> also <b>called an offering, as it is stated: “And we have brought the Lord’s offering,</b> what every man has found: Articles of gold” (Numbers 31:50).
Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And</b> has not brought it <b>to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting”</b> (Leviticus 17:4). This teaches that <b>one is liable</b> only <b>for</b> sacrificing an animal <b>fit to</b> be sacrificed within the <b>entrance of the Tent of Meeting</b> or the Temple <b>due to</b> the prohibition of <b>slaughtering</b> offerings <b>outside</b> the Temple. But <b>one is not liable for</b> sacrificing an animal <b>not fit to</b> be sacrificed within the <b>entrance of the Tent of Meeting due to</b> the prohibition of <b>slaughtering</b> offerings <b>outside</b> the Temple.
<b>I</b> might <b>exclude these</b> animals consecrated for Temple maintenance, <b>but not exclude the</b> red <b>heifer of purification,</b> whose ashes are used to purge the impurity imparted by a corpse, <b>and</b> the <b>scapegoat,</b> despite the fact that neither are sacrificed on the altar, <b>as they are</b> unblemished and therefore <b>fit</b> to come <b>to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Unto the Lord,”</b> to teach that one is liable only for <b>those</b> animals <b>that are exclusively the Lord’s</b> as offerings. <b>These,</b> the red heifer of purification and the scapegoat, <b>are excluded, as they are not exclusively the Lord’s</b> as offerings, but rather they serve unique ritual functions. In any event, the term “unto the Lord” is interpreted as excluding, rather than including, the scapegoat.
<b>Rava said: There,</b> in the <i>baraita</i> discussing offerings slaughtered outside the Temple, the <i>halakha</i> is derived <b>from the context of the verse.</b> The verse states: <b>“And</b> has not brought it <b>to the entrance</b> of the Tent of Meeting” (Leviticus 17:4), which serves <b>to include</b> any unblemished animal. Therefore, the following term: <b>“Unto the Lord,”</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> certain exceptions to the rule. <b>Here, where</b> the verse states <b>“fire”</b> (Leviticus 22:22), which serves <b>to exclude</b> animals not burned on the altar from the prohibition, the term: <b>“Unto the Lord,”</b> serves <b>to include</b> exceptions to that principle.
The Gemara objects: The <i>baraita</i> indicates that <b>the reason</b> why a blemished animal <b>may not be brought</b> as the <b>scapegoat</b> is <b>that the verse writes: “Unto the Lord.”</b> One may infer that <b>had the verse not included</b> the scapegoat by employing the term: <b>“Unto the Lord,” I would say</b> that it is <b>permitted</b> to sacrifice <b>a blemished scapegoat. Now,</b> of the two goats of the Yom Kippur service, one is selected as the scapegoat and one as an offering by lottery. As a rule, <b>the lottery can establish</b> the animals in their respective roles <b>only</b> if each <b>is fit</b> for either role. Given that a blemished animal cannot be sacrificed as an offering, it cannot be designated as the scapegoat either. The term “unto the Lord” therefore seems unnecessary.
<b>Rav Yosef said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Ḥanan the Egyptian, who said</b> that if the scapegoat dies before being sent to the wilderness, <b>even</b> if the other goat has already been slaughtered and its <b>blood</b> collected <b>in the cup,</b> the slaughtered goat is nevertheless a fit offering, and the priest <b>brings another</b> goat <b>and pairs it with</b> the slaughtered goat to serve as the scapegoat. This new scapegoat is thereby designated without a lottery, and one might therefore suppose it can be blemished. The term “unto the Lord” is therefore needed to teach that it must be unblemished.
The Gemara challenges: <b>Granted that you learn from</b> the statement of <b>Ḥanan the Egyptian</b> that he holds <b>that there is no deferral</b> of the second goat once it has already been slaughtered. But <b>do you learn from him</b> that the new scapegoat <b>need not be</b> designated by <b>a lottery? Perhaps</b> the priest <b>brings</b> two potential scapegoats <b>and performs a lottery</b> to decide between them. Therefore, the question again arises: What need is there for the term “unto the Lord”?
<b>Rather, Rav Yosef said:</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> <i>baraita</i>? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one of</b> the two goats <b>dies,</b> the priest <b>brings another</b> goat <b>without</b> another <b>lottery;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon.</b> It is this scapegoat that, absent the term “unto the Lord,” could have been blemished.
<b>Rava said: No,</b> the term in the verse <b>is necessary</b> for a case <b>where</b> the scapegoat <b>became blemished on that day</b> of Yom Kippur, after it had been designated, <b>and</b> then the priest <b>desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another</b> blemished goat.

Daf 7a

Since it might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>granted,</b> if one does this <b>initially,</b> before the lottery, <b>we do not know if this</b> blemished animal will <b>be designated</b> as the one sacrificed <b>to the Lord</b> or sent to the wilderness. Therefore, the one who consecrated the blemished animal is flogged. <b>But here, since it is</b> already <b>clear</b> that the other animal is to be sacrificed to the <b>Lord,</b> and the one he consecrates will be sent to the wilderness, perhaps he is <b>not flogged</b> for consecrating it. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that this is also a violation of the prohibition and he is flogged.
§ <b>The Master said</b> above: The Sages said <b>in the name of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda:</b> One who sacrifices a blemished animal violates a prohibition against <b>the collection of the blood as well.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>The verse states: “That which has its testicles bruised, or crushed, or torn, or cut,</b> you shall not offer unto the Lord” (Leviticus 22:24). The phrase “You shall not offer unto the Lord” is apparently superfluous, as the Torah already stated earlier: “But whatever has a blemish, you shall not offer” (Leviticus 22:20). Rather, <b>this</b> extra phrase is referring to <b>the collection of the blood,</b> for <b>which Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, said</b> that one is liable.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> who holds that one is not liable for collection of the blood per se, <b>why do I</b> need <b>this</b> phrase: <b>“You shall not offer,”</b> stated with regard to damaged testicles? The Gemara answers: <b>He requires</b> it <b>to</b> teach that one is liable for <b>sprinkling the blood.</b> The Gemara challenges: <b>But he derives this from</b> the phrase: <b>“Upon the altar”</b> (Leviticus 22:22), which indicates that one may not sacrifice any part of such an animal on the altar, even its blood. The Gemara answers: With regard to that phrase, the first <i>tanna</i> holds that it is simply <b>the</b> normal <b>manner of the verse that it speaks like this.</b> It does not teach any additional <i>halakha</i>.
The Gemara challenges: <b>But also</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> one may claim that <b>this is the</b> normal <b>manner of the verse.</b> Why does he derive a <i>halakha</i> from this phrase? The Gemara answers: <b>Yes, it is indeed so.</b> He does not derive liability for sprinkling of the blood from the phrase “upon the altar.” <b>Rather, from where does he</b> derive the prohibition against <b>collection of the blood? He derives it from this</b> verse: <b>“Neither from the hand of a foreigner shall you offer</b> the bread of your God of any of these, because their corruption is in them; there is a blemish in them; they shall not be accepted for you” (Leviticus 22:25). <b>This</b> verse <b>is</b> referring to <b>the collection of the blood</b> when sacrificing a blemished animal, for <b>which Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, said</b> that one is liable.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the first <i>tanna</i>,</b> who holds that one is not liable for collection of the blood, <b>why do I</b> need <b>this</b> phrase: <b>Neither</b> from the hand of a foreigner <b>shall you offer?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>He requires</b> it <b>to</b> teach <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i>: It may <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since the descendants of Noah are commanded only with regard to</b> the sacrifice of an animal <b>lacking limbs</b> but they are permitted to sacrifice animals with minor blemishes, perhaps <b>there is no difference</b> if this is performed <b>on their altar</b> outside the Temple <b>or</b> if it is done <b>on our altar</b> in the Temple. Accordingly, one might claim that a gentile may sacrifice a blemished animal in the Temple in Jerusalem. The verse therefore <b>teaches us</b> that they may not sacrifice a blemished animal in such a manner.
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the discussion. <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, says:</b> One violates a prohibition <b>for the collection of the blood as well.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reasoning</b> of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda? The Gemara answers: <b>As it is written: “That which has its testicles bruised, or crushed,</b> or torn, or cut, <b>you shall not offer</b> unto the Lord” (Leviticus 22:24). <b>This</b> verse is referring to <b>the collection of the blood. And he derives</b> the prohibition against <b>sprinkling</b> the blood <b>from</b> the phrase: <b>“Upon the altar”</b> (Leviticus 22:22).
The Gemara objects: <b>And according to the Rabbis as well,</b> who hold that one is not liable for collection of the blood, <b>let them derive</b> liability for <b>sprinkling</b> the blood <b>from</b> the phrase <b>“upon the altar.”</b> The Gemara explains that it is <b>indeed</b> so, that they derive it from that phrase. <b>But for what</b> purpose <b>comes</b> the phrase <b>“You shall not offer” with regard to bruised</b> testicles? <b>We learn</b> from this phrase the prohibition <b>of</b> the sacrifice of a blemished animal even on <b>a private altar,</b> where offerings were sacrificed before the construction of the Temple.
The Gemara asks: <b>But according to Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> how can he then derive the prohibition against collection of the blood of a blemished animal from the phrase “You shall not offer”? After all, <b>he requires this</b> phrase <b>to</b> render prohibited sacrifice on <b>a private altar.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Yes,</b> it <b>is indeed so. Rather, where does he</b> find another instance of the term <b>“shall offer” for</b> prohibiting <b>collection</b> of the blood? From the following verse: <b>“Neither from the hand of a foreigner shall you offer</b> the bread of your God of any of these, because their corruption is in them; there is a blemish in them; they shall not be accepted for you” (Leviticus 22:25). <b>This</b> verse is referring to <b>the collection of the blood.</b>
<b>And the Rabbis</b> maintain that this verse is <b>necessary</b> for a different <i>halakha</i>, as it <b>may enter your mind to say</b> that <b>since the descendants of Noah are commanded only with regard to</b> sacrificing an animal <b>lacking a limb on their</b> private <b>altar</b> but are permitted to sacrifice animals with minor blemishes, <b>perhaps we</b> should <b>accept</b> blemished offerings <b>from them</b> in the Temple <b>as well.</b> Therefore, the verse <b>teaches us</b> by the use of the phrase <b>“of any of these” that we do not accept</b> them.
§ The <i>baraita</i> cited earlier stated that one who consecrates a blemished animal violates several prohibitions, but it did not differentiate between animals born with blemishes and those that acquired them during their lives. <b>Reish Lakish objects to this: Perhaps we learned</b> the prohibition <b>only with regard to an unblemished</b> animal <b>that became blemished, and</b> only one who consecrates such an animal <b>transgresses</b> the prohibition. <b>As, if</b> he consecrated an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset, it is</b> akin to consecrating <b>a mere date palm,</b> and his presumed intention is to consecrate its value, with the proceeds from its sale being used to purchase an offering. This would be permitted.
<b>Rav Ḥiyya bar Yosef said to</b> Reish Lakish: The blemishes mentioned in the phrase in the verse that states: “Either a bull or a lamb <b>that has anything too long or too short”</b> (Leviticus 22:23), <b>are written in the passage</b> that prohibits the sacrifice of blemished animals, <b>and</b> animals such as <b>these are blemished from the outset.</b>
Reish Lakish <b>said to</b> Rav Ḥiyya bar Yosef: <b>Perhaps</b> in the case of animals born with blemishes, <b>we learned</b> the prohibition <b>only with regard to substitution, as we learned</b> in a mishna (16b): There is <b>greater stringency with regard to a substitute than</b> there is <b>with regard to</b> the initial consecration of <b>an offering, as,</b> if one substituted a non-sacred animal <b>with a permanent blemish</b> for a consecrated unblemished animal, the blemished animal <b>is imbued with sanctity.</b> But initial consecration of an animal with such a blemish is effective only with regard to its value, so perhaps one who does so is exempt from punishment.
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said to</b> Reish Lakish: <b>Didn’t you hear that which Rabbi Yannai says,</b> that when the Sages sat <b>in a group,</b> their opinions <b>were counted and they concluded: One who consecrates blemished</b> animals <b>for</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar violates five</b> separate <b>categories</b> of prohibition. <b>But if</b> the <i>baraita</i> is <b>also</b> referring to a case of <b>substitution, there are six</b> categories, <b>as there is</b> also <b>the prohibition of</b> performing <b>substitution.</b> Reish Lakish responded: <b>Rather, what</b> can you say? Is the <i>baraita</i> speaking <b>of</b> one who consecrated an animal that is <b>blemished from the outset? Why</b> then <b>is he flogged? It is</b> akin to one who consecrates <b>a mere date palm.</b>
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Reish Lakish: There is a difference between the cases. When one consecrates <b>a date palm, the matter is not disgraceful,</b> as <b>it is a type of wood</b> that one may burn on the altar. Therefore, he is not flogged. By contrast, when one consecrates an animal that is <b>blemished from the outset, the matter is disgraceful. Since he forsook unblemished</b> animals <b>and consecrated blemished</b> animals, <b>he is liable</b> to be flogged.
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the previous point. Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Reish Lakish: <b>Nevertheless, the matter</b> of consecrating an animal blemished from the outset <b>is disgraceful. For</b> in the case of <b>a date palm,</b> as <b>there is no</b> item <b>of its type</b> that can be sacrificed as an offering, <b>he is not flogged</b> for consecrating it. This is <b>to the exclusion of a blemished</b> animal; <b>since there are</b> items <b>of its type</b> that can be sacrificed as offerings, i.e., unblemished animals, <b>he is flogged</b> for consecrating it.
<b>Rava said: Now that it has been said</b> that <b>the reason that</b> one who consecrates <b>a blemished</b> animal <b>is flogged</b> is <b>due to</b> the fact that <b>the matter is disgraceful,</b> then <b>even one who consecrates it to</b> be sold and its <b>money</b> used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>libations</b> should be <b>flogged as well,</b> as consecrating a blemished animal is in and of itself a disgraceful act.
The Gemara notes: <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rava:</b>

Daf 7b

A vow offering differs from a gift offering in that if it dies prematurely, one is liable to replace the former but not the latter. The verse states: “Either a bull or a lamb that has anything too long or too short, you may offer it for a gift, and for a vow it shall not be accepted” (Leviticus 22:23). The phrase <b>“You may offer it for a gift” is</b> referring to a blemished animal <b>consecrated</b> for <b>Temple maintenance. And I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that one may consecrate it in the manner of <b>a gift; from where</b> is it derived that one may consecrate it in the manner of <b>a vow</b> as well? <b>The verse states:</b> “You may offer it for a gift <b>and for a vow.”</b>
One <b>might</b> have thought that one can consecrate blemished animals <b>even</b> as <b>offerings</b> to be sacrificed on <b>the altar.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And for a vow it shall not be accepted.” This</b> is referring to <b>offerings</b> sacrificed on <b>the altar,</b> for which acceptance is a relevant term. <b>And I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that one may not consecrate such an animal as <b>a vow</b> offering. <b>From where</b> is it derived that one may not do so as <b>a gift</b> offering? <b>The verse states: “A gift</b>…and for a vow it shall not be accepted.” <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> In employing the phrase <b>“It shall not be accepted,” the verse is speaking of</b> consecration that depends upon <b>the acceptance of</b> the animal’s <b>body,</b> i.e., consecration as an offering. Nevertheless, they may be consecrated for Temple maintenance.
The Gemara asks: The opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>is</b> identical to the opinion of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>.</b> What is their dispute? The Gemara suggests: <b>What, is it not</b> that <b>they disagree with regard to this, that the first <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that <b>even</b> if one <b>consecrates it to</b> be sold and its <b>money</b> is used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>libations, he is flogged as well; and Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that <b>in</b> a case where there is <b>acceptance of</b> the animal’s <b>body,</b> the one who consecrated it <b>is indeed flogged, but</b> if one consecrates it to be sold and its <b>money</b> is used <b>for</b> purchasing <b>libations, he is not flogged. Learn from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that the first <i>tanna</i> holds that consecration toward libations is equivalent to consecration as an offering, as Rava suggested above.
§ The Gemara analyzes the wording of Leviticus 22:23: <b>But why do I</b> need the word <b>“it,”</b> in the phrase: “You may offer it”? What does this serve to exclude? It is required <b>for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: When the verse states: <b>“You may offer it for a gift,”</b> this teaches that <b>you may offer this</b> blemished animal <b>as a gift</b> for Temple maintenance, <b>but you may not offer an unblemished</b> animal <b>as a gift for Temple maintenance. From here</b> the Sages <b>said: One who consecrates unblemished</b> animals <b>for Temple maintenance transgresses a positive mitzva.</b>
<b>And from where</b> is it derived that he has transgressed <b>a prohibition as well? As it is stated</b> at the beginning of that passage: <b>“And the Lord spoke to Moses, saying [<i>lemor</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 22:1). This <b>teaches that</b> every mitzva stated in <b>the passage is</b> considered <b>a prohibition.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
The Gemara relates that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said to bar Kappara: From where may</b> it <b>be inferred</b> that every mitzva stated in the passage is considered a prohibition? Bar Kappara <b>said to him</b> that this is <b>as it is written:</b> As the verse states: <b>“<i>Lemor</i>,”</b> the term can be read as though it states: <b>No [</b><i>lo</i><b>],</b> an expression of prohibition, <b>is stated [<i>ne’emar</i>] with regard to</b> the subsequent <b>matters. In the school of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>they say:</b> The term should be understood to mean that God said to Moses: <b>No [<i>lo</i>] shall you say [<i>emor</i>]</b> to them these matters.
§ A <i>baraita</i> above (6b) stated that one who sacrifices a blemished animal on the altar transgresses the prohibition “You may not burn all of it,” and if he sacrificed part of it, he transgresses the prohibition “You may not burn part of it.” With regard to this it <b>was stated:</b> In the case of <b>one who brings up the limbs of blemished</b> animals <b>onto the altar, Rava says</b> that <b>he violates</b> both the prohibition <b>of “You may not burn all of it;” and</b> the prohibition <b>of “You may not burn part of it,”</b> and he receives two sets of lashes. <b>Abaye says: One is not flogged</b> twice <b>for</b> violating <b>a general prohibition.</b> Since one verse serves as the source for both prohibitions, one is not flogged twice for its violation.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from the <i>Tosefta</i> (1:10): <b>One who consecrates blemished</b> animals <b>for</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar violates five</b> separate <b>categories</b> of prohibition. The aforementioned <i>baraita</i> enumerated these and included both the prohibition “You may not burn all of it” and the prohibition “You may not burn part of it,” indicating that one is flogged for transgressing each of these prohibitions. This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
<b>Abaye said:</b> The <i>baraita</i> <b>is teaching</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to</b> different <b>men,</b> one of whom burned an entire animal on the altar, and one of whom burned only part of it. One person cannot be liable for both prohibitions. The Gemara challenges: <b>If</b> it is teaching the <i>halakha</i> <b>of</b> different <b>men,</b> why does it say: <b>Violates,</b> in the third person singular? It <b>should have</b> stated: <b>Violate,</b> in the plural. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to one man, and</b> this is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Abaye.</b>
<b>Abaye said:</b> From the list of five prohibitions, <b>remove</b> that of <b>burning part of</b> the offering, <b>and insert</b> the <b>collection of the blood,</b> which means that there is only one prohibition for burning the offering, and the number of prohibitions still remains at five. The Gemara raises a difficulty: With regard to the <b>collection of the blood, Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, is of</b> the opinion that one transgresses that prohibition, but <b>the first <i>tanna</i> is not of</b> that opinion. How, then, can Abaye contradict the first <i>tanna</i>? The Gemara concludes: It is <b>difficult.</b>
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the previous point: <b>But</b> from the fact <b>that the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> it may be inferred that <b>the first clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> The Gemara concludes: <b>The refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Abaye</b> is indeed <b>a conclusive refutation.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The priests substitute for their</b> own offerings <b>and Israelites substitute for their</b> own offerings. <b>The priests substitute neither for a sin offering, nor for a guilt offering, nor for a firstborn</b> offering that they received from an Israelite, as those animals are not their property, and one does not substitute an animal that is not his. <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri said: For what</b> reason can priests <b>not substitute for a firstborn</b> offering that they received from an Israelite? Does it not belong to them? <b>Rabbi Akiva said to him: A sin offering and a guilt offering</b> are <b>a gift to the priest, and the firstborn</b> offering is likewise <b>a gift to the priest. Just as</b> in the cases of <b>a sin offering and a guilt offering,</b> priests that receive one of them from an Israelite <b>cannot substitute for it, so too</b> with regard to <b>a firstborn</b> offering, priests that receive it from an Israelite <b>cannot substitute for it.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri said: What</b> is this comparison <b>for him? If</b> a priest <b>does not substitute for a sin offering and a guilt offering, which</b> priests <b>do not acquire during</b> the animals’ <b>lifetimes, will you say</b> the same <b>with regard to a firstborn, which</b> priests <b>do acquire during</b> the animal’s <b>lifetime? Rabbi Akiva said to him: But isn’t it already stated: “Then both it and its substitute shall be sacred”</b> (Leviticus 27:10), which juxtaposes the consecration of the consecrated animal with that of its non-sacred substitute? <b>Where is</b> the consecrated animal <b>imbued with sanctity?</b> It is <b>in the house of the owner. So too,</b> the <b>substitute</b> animal is consecrated <b>in the house of the owner.</b> Therefore, the priest cannot substitute for the firstborn that he received because he is not the owner that initially consecrated it.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>elsewhere</b> (<i>Ma’aser Sheni</i> 1:2): With regard to <b>a firstborn</b> offering, <b>one may sell it</b> if it is <b>unblemished</b> only while it is still <b>alive,</b> as after it is slaughtered outside the Temple one may not derive benefit from it, and if it was offered in the Temple, one may not sell sacrificial meat. <b>And</b> if it is <b>blemished,</b> one may sell it while it is <b>alive or</b> after it has been <b>slaughtered. And</b> a priest <b>can betroth a woman with it,</b> as it is his property. <b>Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh says: They taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>only with regard to the present,</b> when there is no Temple, <b>since the priest has</b> the ability to <b>acquire</b> the firstborn offering. <b>But when the Temple is standing, since</b> the <b>unblemished</b> firstborn animal <b>stands for sacrifice,</b> one may <b>not sell it unblemished</b> while it is <b>alive.</b>
<b>Rava raised an objection to Rav Naḥman:</b> The mishna teaches that <b>one may sell an unblemished</b> firstborn offering while it is <b>alive.</b> One can infer that while it is <b>alive, yes,</b> one may sell it, but once it has been <b>slaughtered,</b> one may <b>not</b> sell it. <b>When</b> is this the <i>halakha</i>? <b>If we say</b> that this mishna is speaking <b>in the present, is there an unblemished, slaughtered,</b> firstborn offering? Offerings are not slaughtered in the present day. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the mishna is speaking of the time <b>when the Temple is standing. And</b> yet, the mishna <b>still teaches</b> that <b>one may sell an unblemished</b> firstborn offering while it is <b>alive,</b> which contradicts the statement of Rav Naḥman.
Rav Naḥman responds: <b>No; actually</b> the mishna is referring to the <i>halakha</i> <b>in the present.</b> I disagree with the above inference. <b>Does</b> the mishna explicitly <b>teach: One may sell an unblemished</b> firstborn offering while it is <b>alive, yes,</b> but once it has been <b>slaughtered, no?</b> The mishna does not mean to rule out selling a slaughtered animal; rather, <b>it is coming to teach us the</b> matter itself, <b>that even in the present, one may sell it unblemished</b> while it is <b>alive.</b>

Daf 8a

§ Rava <b>raised an objection to</b> the statement of Rav Naḥman from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>With regard to a firstborn</b> offering, it <b>is stated:</b> “But the firstling of an ox, or the firstling of a sheep, or the firstling of a goat, <b>you shall not redeem;</b> they are holy: And you shall sprinkle their blood against the altar” (Numbers 18:17). <b>But it may be sold</b> to another priest for consumption after its sacrifice. <b>What</b> era <b>are we dealing with</b> in this <i>baraita</i>? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>in the present, say the latter clause</b> of the verse: <b>“And you shall sprinkle their blood against the altar.” Is there an altar in the present? Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that it is referring to the time <b>when the Temple is standing.</b>
<b>And what</b> sort of animal are we dealing <b>with</b> here? <b>If we say</b> that it is referring <b>to a blemished</b> animal, <b>say the latter clause</b> of the verse: <b>“And you shall sprinkle their blood against the altar, and burn their fat.” Is</b> a blemished animal <b>fit for sacrifice? Rather, is it not</b> referring <b>to an unblemished</b> animal? <b>And</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that <b>it may be sold. Evidently, the priest has</b> the right of <b>acquisition to</b> an unblemished firstborn offering even when the Temple is standing and the animal must be sacrificed.
Rav Naḥman responds: <b>Are</b> the two clauses of the verse <b>comparable? The first clause</b> is stated <b>with regard to a blemished</b> animal, and it is teaching that it may be sold, and <b>the latter clause</b> is stated <b>with regard to an unblemished</b> animal, and it teaches that it is sacrificed as an offering. Unblemished animals may not be sold when the Temple is standing.
§ <b>Rav Mesharshiyya raises an objection</b> from a mishna (<i>Yevamot</i> 99a): In the case of <b>a priestess’s offspring who was comingled with her maidservant’s offspring,</b> when <b>the comingled</b> children <b>have grown up, they free each other,</b> and therefore whichever one was a slave will be emancipated. <b>And</b> even beforehand, <b>they both</b> may <b>partake of <i>teruma</i>,</b> as both a priest and the slave of a priest may partake of <i>teruma</i>. <b>And they receive one share</b> of <i>teruma</i> <b>in the granary,</b> because the slave of a priest receives no share. And <b>their firstborn</b> animals must <b>graze until they become unfit</b> for sacrifice by virtue of a blemish, <b>and</b> then <b>they may be eaten with their blemish.</b>
<b>What</b> era <b>are we dealing with</b> in this mishna? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>in the present, what is different</b> about <b>our</b> firstborn offerings, i.e., those of Israelites, <b>and what is different</b> about <b>their</b> firstborn offerings, those of priests? <b>Ours also require</b> that they have <b>blemishes</b> before they may be eaten. Why is the <i>halakha</i> stated specifically with regard to priests? <b>Rather, is it not</b> speaking of the time <b>when the Temple is standing? Granted, if you say</b> that <b>the priest has</b> the right of <b>acquisition to</b> an unblemished firstborn offering when the Temple is standing, it is <b>well. But if you say</b> that a priest <b>does not have</b> the right of acquisition, <b>let</b> the Temple <b>treasurer [<i>gizbar</i>] come and take</b> the firstborn offerings for sacrifice. Why are they left in the offspring’s possession until they develop blemishes?
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the mishna is speaking <b>of the present. And</b> as for <b>that which</b> is <b>difficult for you: What is different</b> about <b>our</b> firstborn offerings <b>and what is different</b> about <b>their</b> firstborn offerings, there is in fact a difference, as <b>we</b> must <b>give</b> ours <b>to a priest once they are blemished.</b> Although it is permitted for Israelites to eat blemished firstborn offerings, they are still required to give them to priests. By contrast, <b>they,</b> the offspring mentioned in the mishna, are different, <b>since there is part of a priest in</b> each of <b>them</b> due to the uncertainty of their parentage. And this is sufficient to <b>dislodge the</b> claims of all other <b>priests</b> to the animal.
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the previous discussion: If the mishna is referring <b>to the present, why specifically</b> discuss the <b>firstborn</b> offerings <b>of these</b> cases of <b>uncertain</b> parentage? <b>Even our</b> firstborn offerings <b>should</b> be left to <b>graze</b> until they are blemished. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the mishna is referring <b>to</b> the time <b>when the Temple is standing. And if</b> the mishna is <b>speaking of a blemished</b> animal, does it make sense to say: <b>They</b> should <b>graze until they become unfit</b> for sacrifice? <b>They are already unfit. Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the mishna is referring <b>to unblemished</b> animals, <b>and it is</b> only in a case <b>such as this,</b> when the owners might not be priests, <b>that they definitely may not sell</b> the animal. One can infer that a priest may sell an unblemished firstborn offering when the Temple is standing.
The Gemara explains: <b>Actually,</b> the mishna is referring <b>to the present. What</b> is <b>difficult for you,</b> the fact that <b>even our</b> firstborn offerings should <b>graze</b> until they are blemished? <b>We</b> Israelites <b>are unable to deny the priest</b> who claims the firstborn offering, <b>as there is no</b> member of the <b>priesthood,</b> however <b>uncertain, here</b> among the owners of the animal. By contrast, in <b>these</b> cases of <b>uncertain</b> parentage described in the mishna, they can <b>deny the priest,</b> as <b>both of them</b> can <b>say to the priest: I am a priest, and I am a priest.</b> Since the burden of proof rests upon the claimant, both may keep their animals until they are blemished.
§ The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Sanhedrin</i> 4:5): A verse discussing an idolatrous city states: “You shall smite the inhabitants of that city with the edge of the sword, destroying it utterly, and all that is therein and its animals, with the edge of the sword. And you shall gather all its spoil into the midst of the broad place thereof, and shall burn with fire the city, and all the spoil thereof” (Deuteronomy 13:16–17). <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> The term <b>“its animals”</b> serves <b>to exclude firstborn</b> kosher <b>animals and</b> the animal <b>tithe that are in</b> the city, as they are sacred. The term <b>“its spoil”</b> serves <b>to exclude money</b> used to redeem the <b>second tithe that is</b> found <b>in</b> the city, as it too is sacred.
<b>What</b> era <b>are we dealing with</b> in this <i>baraita</i>? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>in the present, can there be an idolatrous city</b> in the present day? <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Sanhedrin</i> 2a): A city <b>may be designated</b> as <b>an idolatrous city only in</b> accordance with the ruling of <b>a court of seventy-one</b> judges, i.e., the Great Sanhedrin? <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the mishna is referring <b>to</b> the period <b>when the Temple is standing.</b>
<b>And to what</b> type of animal is the <i>baraita</i> referring? <b>If you say</b> that it is referring <b>to a blemished</b> firstborn animal, which belongs to its owners, <b>this is</b> included in the term <b>“its animals,”</b> which one is commanded to destroy. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to an unblemished</b> firstborn offering. <b>And granted, if you say</b> that the priest <b>has</b> the right of <b>acquisition to</b> an unblemished firstborn offering when the Temple is standing, it is <b>well,</b> as although it belongs to an individual, the Torah nevertheless exempts it from destruction. <b>But if you say</b> that a priest <b>does not have</b> the right of acquisition, <b>why do I</b> need to derive its exemption <b>from</b> the term “its <b>animals”? One should derive its</b> exemption <b>from</b> the term <b>“its spoil,”</b> from which one can infer: <b>But not the spoils of Heaven.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is speaking <b>of a blemished</b> animal. <b>And</b> as for <b>that which</b> is <b>difficult for you,</b> that <b>this is</b> considered <b>“its animals,”</b> one can answer that this term includes only <b>that which is eaten in the manner of its animals,</b> without additional restrictions. This <b>excludes</b> even a blemished <b>firstborn</b> offering <b>and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offering, <b>which is not</b> included <b>in the category of “its animals.”</b>
And which restrictions apply to the consumption of blemished firstborn animals? The restrictions are <b>as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 31a): <b>All disqualified consecrated</b> animals are redeemed and then <b>sold in the butchers’ market [<i>ba’itliz</i>],</b> where they fetch a higher price, in order to benefit the Temple treasury. <b>And they are slaughtered in the butchers’ market, and</b> their meat <b>is weighed</b> and sold <b>by the <i>litra</i>,</b> as is done with non-sacred meat, <b>with the exception of a firstborn</b> offering <b>and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offering, <b>as their benefit</b> is accrued <b>to their owners.</b> The money will benefit their owners rather than the Temple treasury, and private individuals may not disgrace disqualified offerings by treating them as one would non-sacred meat.
§ The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: If one takes an oath falsely denying possession of another’s property, he must pay the value of that property and an additional one-fifth to the owner. The passage stating this <i>halakha</i> begins: “If anyone sin, and commit a trespass against the Lord, and deal falsely with his neighbor in a matter of deposit, or of pledge, or of robbery” (Leviticus 5:21). By stating: <b>“And commit a trespass against the Lord,”</b> the verse serves <b>to include</b> one who denies possession of <b>offerings of lesser sanctity</b> belonging to another, <b>as they are the property of</b> their <b>owner,</b> who has the right to consume their meat after the sacrifice. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili.</b>
<b>Ben Azzai says:</b> The verse serves <b>to include</b> only one who denies possession of <b>a peace offering</b> of another, not other offerings of lesser sanctity. <b>Abba Yosei ben Dosai says: Ben Azzai stated</b> his <i>halakha</i> <b>only with regard to a firstborn</b> offering, that it is considered the property of the owner, i.e., the priest, but not with regard to other offerings of lesser sanctity.
<b>What</b> era <b>are we dealing with</b> in this <i>baraita</i>? <b>If we say</b> it is <b>in the present, doesn’t</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a firstborn offering as <b>similar to</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a peace offering?</b> Peace offerings are not a relevant category in the present day. <b>Rather,</b> it is <b>obvious</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to</b> the period <b>when the Temple is standing.</b>
<b>And what</b> type of animal <b>are we dealing with? If we say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring specifically <b>to a blemished</b> animal, <b>doesn’t it teach</b> the <i>halakha</i> of a firstborn offering as <b>similar to</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a peace offering?</b> Peace offerings are the property of their owners even when they are unblemished. <b>Rather, is it not</b> referring even <b>to an unblemished</b> firstborn animal? <b>And</b> the <i>baraita</i> teaches that this animal is the property of its owner. <b>Learn from it</b> that <b>the priest has</b> the right of <b>acquisition to</b> an unblemished firstborn offering when the Temple is standing.

Daf 8b

<b>Abaye says: No, actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to an unblemished</b> animal when the Temple is standing, but it is speaking <b>of a firstborn</b> offering <b>outside of Eretz</b> Yisrael, <b>and it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said: If unblemished</b> firstborn offerings <b>came</b> from outside of Eretz Yisrael, <b>they may be sacrificed</b> on the altar. This language indicates that there is no obligation to sacrifice such an animal, as people did not generally bring their firstborn offerings from outside Eretz Yisrael. Therefore, a priest is able to acquire it even when it is unblemished. Firstborn offerings from Eretz Yisrael, by contrast, cannot be acquired by the priests.
§ The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> from the mishna: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri said to him: What</b> is this comparison <b>for me? If</b> a priest <b>does not substitute for a sin offering and a guilt offering, which</b> priests <b>do not acquire during</b> the animals’ <b>lifetimes, will you say</b> the same <b>with regard to a firstborn, which</b> priests <b>acquire during</b> the animal’s <b>lifetime? What are we dealing with</b> in the mishna? <b>If we say</b> that it is referring <b>to a blemished</b> animal, <b>isn’t</b> the mishna <b>saying</b> that the firstborn offering is <b>similar to a sin offering and a guilt offering,</b> which the priests acquire only unblemished? <b>Rather, is it not</b> referring <b>to unblemished</b> animals? <b>And it</b> nevertheless <b>teaches:</b> Which priests <b>acquire during</b> the animal’s <b>lifetime.</b>
<b>Ravina said: This</b> mishna <b>too</b> is referring <b>to a firstborn</b> offering <b>outside of Eretz</b> Yisrael, <b>and it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said: If unblemished</b> firstborn offerings <b>came</b> from outside of Eretz Yisrael, <b>they may be sacrificed</b> on the altar. The priests do not acquire firstborn offerings from Eretz Yisrael.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Let us say</b> that acquisition of the firstborn offerings by the priests is <b>the subject of</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>.</b> The <i>baraita</i> states: In the case of <b>a firstborn</b> offering, when it is <b>in the house of</b> its <b>owner,</b> who did not yet give it to a priest, its owner <b>can render</b> another animal <b>a substitute</b> for it. Once it is <b>in the house of the priest,</b> its owner <b>cannot render a substitute</b> for it. <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar says: Once it has entered the possession of the priest,</b> its owner <b>cannot render a substitute</b> for it.
Apparently, the opinion of Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar <b>is</b> identical to that of <b>the first <i>tanna</i>.</b> What is their dispute? <b>What, is it not</b> correct that <b>this</b> is what the first <i>tanna</i> <b>is saying:</b> Once it is <b>in the house,</b> i.e., possession, <b>of the priest,</b> the priest <b>can render a substitute</b> for it, <b>but</b> the <b>owner cannot render a substitute</b> for it, as the priest has acquired it; while Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar holds that once it enters the possession of the priest, neither of them can render a substitute with it. <b>Apparently,</b> the first <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that <b>the priest has</b> the right of <b>acquisition to</b> an unblemished firstborn offering when the Temple is standing.
The Gemara explains: The opinion of the first <i>tanna</i> is <b>not difficult;</b> one can say that the dispute in the <i>baraita</i> concerns a firstborn offering outside of Eretz Yisrael, while those from Eretz Yisrael are not acquired by the priests. And <b>this</b> opinion of the first <i>tanna</i> accords with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan ben Nuri</b> in the mishna above, and <b>that</b> opinion of Rabbi Shimon ben Elazar accords with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva</b> in that mishna.
§ The Gemara earlier (7b) cited a mishna (<i>Ma’aser Sheni</i> 1:2) which states that a firstborn offering may be sold unblemished only while it is alive. <b>Rav Ḥisda says:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>only</b> with regard to <b>a priest</b> selling <b>to a priest, but</b> with regard to <b>a priest</b> selling it <b>to an Israelite,</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> even when the animal is alive. <b>What is the reason?</b> Since an Israelite can consume a firstborn animal only if it is blemished, there is a concern that <b>perhaps the Israelite will go and inflict a blemish on</b> the firstborn offering <b>and bring</b> it <b>to a halakhic authority</b> to attain permission to eat it, <b>and</b> he will <b>say to him: A priest gave me this firstborn</b> offering <b>when it</b> was already <b>blemished.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And may a halakhic authority permit</b> the animal in a case <b>such as this? But didn’t Rav say:</b> A halakhic authority <b>may not look at a firstborn</b> offering brought <b>by an Israelite</b> to determine if it has the type of permanent blemish allowing it to be slaughtered <b>unless a priest</b> is <b>with him,</b> out of concern that the Israelite will keep the blemished animal for himself without performing the mitzva of giving it to a priest? If so, one can demand that the priest who sold him the animal must be present, and this should prevent the Israelite from lying about the blemish.
<b>Rav Huna, son of Rabbi Yehoshua, said: This is the reason why</b> it is <b>prohibited</b> for a priest to sell an unblemished firstborn offering to <b>an Israelite: Because</b> this <b>looks like a priest who assists at the threshing floor</b> in order to receive <i>teruma</i> from an Israelite. Unblemished firstborn offerings have a low purchase price, as the owner must wait for it to become blemished before eating it. It might appear that the priest sold the animal to an Israelite at a discount in order to receive future firstborns from him.
The Gemara relates: <b>Mar Zutra arrived at the home of Rav Ashi.</b> Those present <b>said to him: Let</b> the <b>Master taste some</b> food. <b>They brought meat before him.</b> The one who placed the meat before him <b>said: Let</b> the <b>Master eat, as</b> this meat <b>is</b> particularly <b>healthful, because it is a firstborn.</b> Mar Zutra asked: <b>From where did you</b> receive <b>this</b> firstborn animal? <b>They said to him:</b> It is an animal <b>that so-and-so</b> the <b>priest sold to us.</b>
Mar Zutra <b>said to them: Do you not hold</b> like <b>that which Rav Huna, son of Rabbi Yehoshua, said,</b> that Israelites may not receive an unblemished firstborn offering from a priest <b>because it looks like a priest who assists at the threshing floor? They said to</b> Mar Zutra: <b>We hold</b> that this is <b>not</b> a concern, <b>as we purchased</b> it for its fair market value, and did not receive it at a discount. Therefore, there is no appearance of impropriety.
Mar Zutra <b>said to them: But do you not hold</b> like <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 26b): <b>Until when</b> is <b>an Israelite obligated to tend to a firstborn</b> animal before giving it to the priest? In the case <b>of a small</b> animal, a sheep or goat, <b>thirty</b> days, <b>and</b> in the case <b>of a large</b> animal, one of a herd of cattle, <b>fifty days. And if</b> the priest <b>said to</b> the owner: <b>Give it to me within its period,</b> the owner <b>may not give it to him. And Rav Sheshet says: What is</b> the <b>reason</b> for this? <b>Because it looks like a priest who assists at the threshing floor,</b> as the priest is assisting the Israelite by assuming the responsibility of tending to the firstborn.
<b>They said to</b> Mar Zutra: <b>There</b> the circumstances of <b>the matter prove</b> that the priest accepted responsibility to care for the animal in return for some benefit, and it has the appearance of impropriety. <b>Here, we purchased it</b> for its fair market value, and there is no appearance of impropriety.
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of this final response. <b>They said to</b> Mar Zutra: <b>There</b> the Israelite <b>does not give payment</b> to the priest for accepting responsibility to care for the animal. <b>Here,</b> the Israelite <b>gives payment</b> to the priest, as we purchased the animal. And <b>what</b> would <b>you say</b> to this claim? Would you say that perhaps the <b>priest lowered</b> the price of the animal <b>for</b> us, <b>as the priest thinks that when</b> we <b>have another firstborn</b> offering we <b>will give it to him,</b> and therefore it has the appearance of impropriety? This is <b>not</b> a concern, <b>as he thinks:</b>

Daf 9a

<b>A zucchini</b> now <b>is better than</b> the possibility of <b>a gourd</b> later. There is no reason to give up an animal now for the hope of receiving another later.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> It is written: “He shall neither exchange it, nor substitute it, good for bad, or bad for good; and if he substitutes an animal for an animal, then both it and its substitute shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:10). The mishna enumerates the consecrated and non-sacred animals this verse applies to. <b>One substitutes</b> for consecrated animals <b>from the flock</b> of sheep or goats, and the sanctity takes effect <b>upon</b> animals from <b>the herd</b> of cattle, <b>and</b> one substitutes <b>from the herd</b> and the sanctity takes effect <b>upon</b> animals from <b>the flock. And</b> one substitutes <b>from the sheep</b> and the sanctity takes effect <b>upon the goats, and from the goats upon the sheep;</b> and <b>from the males upon the females, and from the females upon the males;</b> and <b>from the unblemished</b> animals <b>upon the blemished</b> animals, and <b>from the blemished</b> animals <b>upon the unblemished</b> animals.
The source for this is <b>as it is stated: “He shall neither exchange it, nor substitute it, good for bad, or bad for good”</b> (Leviticus 27:10). <b>And which is</b> the case of <b>good for bad</b> where the substitution takes effect? It is a case where one substitutes for <b>blemished</b> animals <b>whose consecration preceded their blemish.</b> But if an animal was consecrated after it was blemished, substitution for it does not take effect.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? They are derived from a verse, <b>as the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “And if he substitutes an animal for an animal, then both it and its substitute shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:10). When the verse states: <b>“An animal for an animal,” from here</b> it is derived <b>that one substitutes</b> for consecrated animals <b>from the flock</b> and the sanctity takes effect <b>upon</b> animals from <b>the herd, and</b> one likewise substitutes <b>from the herd upon</b> animals from <b>the flock;</b> and one substitutes <b>from the sheep</b> and the sanctity takes effect <b>upon the goats, and from the goats upon the sheep; and from the males upon the females and from the females upon the males; and from the blemished</b> animals <b>upon the unblemished</b> animals <b>and from the unblemished</b> animals <b>upon the blemished</b> animals.
One <b>might</b> have thought that this is the <i>halakha</i> <b>even</b> for animals whose <b>blemish preceded their consecration.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “He shall neither exchange it, nor substitute it, good for bad, or bad for good”</b> (Leviticus 27:10), <b>and which is</b> the case of <b>good for bad</b> where the substitution takes effect? It is a case of <b>blemished</b> animals <b>whose consecration preceded their blemish.</b> If an animal was consecrated after it was blemished, substitution for it does not take effect.
The Gemara discusses the second half of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>What is the</b> biblical <b>derivation</b> that leads to this conclusion? <b>Abaye said</b> that <b>the verse should have stated: He shall neither exchange it, nor substitute it, good for bad, or bad for it. Why do I</b> need <b>another</b> instance of the term <b>“for good”? Learn from</b> this repetition that if the animal is <b>good,</b> i.e., unblemished, <b>from its beginning,</b> before it was consecrated, one can <b>render a substitute</b> for it, but if it is <b>bad from its beginning,</b> one <b>cannot render a substitute</b> for it.
<b>Rava said: Both</b> instances <b>of</b> the word <b>“good”</b> in the verse <b>are superfluous. If so, let the verse write: He shall neither exchange it, nor substitute it for bad, or bad for it. Why do I</b> need the verse <b>to write both</b> instances of the word <b>“good”? One</b> instance of the word <b>“good”</b> teaches that <b>even</b> if one substitutes a good animal <b>for a good</b> animal, <b>when he effects substitution</b> he is <b>flogged. And the other</b> instance teaches that if the animal is <b>good from its beginning</b> one can <b>render a substitute</b> for it, but if it is <b>bad from its beginning</b> one <b>cannot render a substitute</b> for it.
<b>And Abaye said:</b> The first derivation of Rava is unnecessary, as that <i>halakha</i> <b>is</b> already derived by <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference, as follows: <b>Just as</b> one who substitutes <b>a good</b> animal <b>for a bad</b> blemished one, <b>where</b> he seeks to <b>improve</b> the standing of the consecrated animal by making it fit for sacrifice, is nevertheless <b>flogged,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> the case <b>that</b> one who substitutes <b>a good</b> animal <b>for a good</b> animal, <b>which are equivalent to each other,</b> should be <b>flogged?</b>
<b>And Rava</b> would respond that an <i>a fortiori</i> inference is not sufficient, as <b>one does not administer punishment</b> based <b>on</b> an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference.</b> Punishment with lashes can be based only on the explicit wording of a verse. <b>And Abaye</b> could <b>say to you</b> that <b>this is not</b> a mere <b>logical derivation,</b> but it is included in the language of the verse, as, <b>is</b> substituting <b>a good,</b> unblemished, animal <b>less</b> of an act of substitution <b>than</b> substituting <b>a bad</b> one? The prohibition stated in the verse clearly applies in either case.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that when the verse states: <b>“He shall neither exchange it,”</b> this is referring to substituting one’s animal <b>for that of others.</b> The phrase <b>“nor substitute it”</b> is referring to substituting one’s non-sacred animal <b>for his own</b> sacred one. The Gemara objects: <b>But let</b> the verse <b>write</b> simply: <b>“He shall not exchange it,” and</b> there will be <b>no need</b> to write: <b>“Nor substitute it,”</b> as the prohibition against substituting for one’s own animal can be inferred <i>a fortiori</i> from the prohibition against substituting for another’s animal.
The Gemara explains: <b>If</b> the verse had <b>written</b> the prohibition in <b>that manner, I would say that</b> one is <b>flogged</b> only if he stated: <b>This</b> consecrated animal <b>should leave</b> its consecrated status <b>and this</b> non-sacred animal <b>should enter</b> in its stead. <b>But</b> if one <b>effects substitution</b> by simply stating: This is a substitute for that, <b>as he has consecrated both of</b> the animals, <b>I would say</b> that he is <b>not flogged.</b> The additional phrase in the verse <b>teaches us</b> that he is flogged in this case as well.
The Gemara explains: <b>What are the circumstances</b> of substituting his animal <b>for that of others? If we say</b> that the <b>consecrated animal</b> is <b>his and</b> the <b>non-sacred</b> animal belongs <b>to others, is he able to consecrate</b> an animal in this manner? <b>The Merciful One states</b> in the Torah: <b>“When a man shall consecrate his house to be sacred unto the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 27:14), which teaches that <b>just as his house is in his possession, so too, any</b> item one desires to consecrate must be <b>in his possession.</b> One cannot consecrate another’s animal. <b>But rather,</b> if we say that the <b>consecrated animal</b> belongs <b>to others, and</b> the <b>non-sacred</b> animal is <b>his, can one effect</b> substitution <b>for an item that is not his?</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>Actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to a consecrated</b> animal <b>of others and his non-sacred</b> animal, and it is a case <b>where the owner of the consecrated animal said</b> that <b>whoever wants to effect substitution for his animal can come and effect substitution.</b> In this case, one can effect substitution even for a consecrated item that is not his.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One substitutes one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for two</b> consecrated animals <b>and two</b> non-sacred animals <b>for one</b> consecrated animal, and one substitutes <b>one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for one hundred</b> consecrated animals <b>and one hundred</b> non-sacred animals <b>for one</b> consecrated animal. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: One substitutes only one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for one</b> consecrated animal, <b>as it is stated: “Then both it and its substitute shall be sacred”</b> (Leviticus 27:10). <b>Just as “it”</b> indicates one <b>specific</b> animal, <b>so too,</b> its substitute can be only one <b>specific</b> animal.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the dispute between the first <i>tanna</i> and Rabbi Shimon, the Gemara explains: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? <b>As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“An animal for an animal”</b> (Leviticus 27:10). <b>From here</b> it is derived that <b>one substitutes one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for two</b> consecrated animals <b>and two</b> non-sacred animals <b>for one</b> consecrated animal, and <b>one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for one hundred</b> consecrated animals <b>and one hundred</b> non-sacred animals <b>for one</b> consecrated animal. <b>Rabbi Shimon says: One substitutes only one</b> non-sacred animal <b>for one</b> consecrated animal, <b>as it is stated: “An animal [<i>behema</i>] for an animal,” and</b> it is <b>not</b> stated: <b>An animal for animals [<i>bivehemot</i>], nor: Animals for an animal.</b>
The first <i>tanna</i> <b>said to</b> Rabbi Shimon: <b>We have found that</b> a group of <b>animals is called</b> by the singular term <b><i>behema</i>, as it is stated: “And many animals [<i>uvhema rabba</i>]”</b> (Jonah 4:11). The Gemara notes: <b>And Rabbi Shimon</b> could respond that many animals <b>are</b> indeed <b>called “<i>behema rabba</i>,”</b> but they <b>are not called “<i>behema</i>” without</b> further <b>specification.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And is the reason of Rabbi Shimon</b> really <b>due to</b> the verse’s phrase <b>“an animal</b> for an animal”? <b>But isn’t the reason of Rabbi Shimon,</b> as explained in the mishna, <b>due to</b> the word <b>“it”</b> in the verse? <b>Just as “it”</b> indicates one <b>specific</b> animal, <b>so too,</b> its <b>substitute</b> can be only one <b>specific</b> animal.
The Gemara answers: <b>Initially, Rabbi Shimon stated</b> his opinion <b>to</b> the Sages by drawing support <b>from</b> the phrase: <b>“Then both it and its substitute.” But when he saw that the Rabbis taught</b> their opinion drawing support <b>from</b> the phrase <b>“an animal for an animal,” he said to them: You can learn the reason for my</b> opinion <b>from there too.</b>
<b>Reish Lakish says:</b> Although he holds that one cannot substitute two animals for one, <b>Rabbi Shimon concedes that one can effect substitution</b> once <b>and then effect substitution again</b> for the same consecrated animal. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? The reason is that one can ask: <b>Where has the initial consecration of the consecrated</b> animal <b>gone?</b> Even after one effects substitution, it remains consecrated as it was before. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> Rabbi Shimon maintains that <b>just as one cannot substitute two</b> animals <b>for one, so</b> too, one <b>cannot effect substitution and then effect substitution again</b> using the same consecrated animal.
The Gemara comments: It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> and it <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Reish Lakish.</b> It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan: Just as</b> one <b>cannot substitute one</b> animal <b>for two, so too, one cannot effect substitution and then effect substitution again.</b> It <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Reish Lakish:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>just as Rabbi Shimon said</b> that one <b>cannot substitute two</b> animals <b>for one, so too, one cannot effect substitution and then effect substitution again.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Then both it and its substitute”</b> (Leviticus 27:10), which teaches that one can effect substitution <b>with even one hundred</b> non-sacred animals.
<b>Rabbi Avin raises a dilemma: According to the statement of the one who says</b> that <b>one cannot effect substitution</b> once <b>and then effect substitution again,</b> if one <b>designated</b> an animal as <b>a guilt offering with which to atone and effected substitution for it,</b>

Daf 9b

<b>and</b> the original guilt offering <b>became blemished, and he desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another</b> animal, which was then lost, <b>and</b> the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another,</b> third, animal as <b>a guilt offering,</b> and then the second animal was found <b>and was consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering,</b> as is the case for any guilt offering whose owner has achieved atonement by means of another offering, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether <b>he</b> can <b>effect substitution for</b> the second animal? Since this animal’s sanctity stems from that of the original blemished animal, and he had already effected a substitute for that original animal, perhaps he cannot now substitute for the second animal, as this would constitute repeat substitution.
<b>Abaye said: What is the dilemma he is raising? If</b> his essential dilemma concerns the fact that there are <b>two bodies,</b> i.e., two different animals, then the dilemma would stand even if the original animal <b>and</b> the replacement were of <b>one</b> type of <b>sanctity.</b> If so, <b>let the dilemma be raised</b> even <b>in</b> a case where he <b>did not achieve atonement</b> by another guilt offering, and the second animal remained a guilt offering, rather than being consigned to be sacrificed as a burnt offering.
And <b>if</b> the dilemma is due to the fact that there are <b>two</b> types of <b>sanctity,</b> it would stand even if it was only <b>one body.</b> If so, <b>let the dilemma be raised</b> even where the animal <b>did not become blemished</b> but was simply lost and found after the owner atoned by means of another animal, and it was thereby consigned to be sacrificed as a burnt offering. Why did Rabbi Avin present such a complex case?
The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi Avin raises one</b> dilemma <b>as a result of</b> the anticipated solution to <b>another</b> dilemma: <b>If you say</b> that in the case of <b>two bodies and one</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> it is <b>not</b> possible to effect substitution on the second animal, perhaps that is <b>because this sanctity</b> already <b>had been substituted one time.</b> If so, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> in the case of <b>two bodies and two</b> types of <b>sanctity?</b> Is the offering changed enough to enable another substitution for it? The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
