The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the previous dilemma. <b>Rabbi Avin raises a dilemma: According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said</b> that Rabbi Shimon holds that <b>one cannot effect substitution and then effect substitution again</b> for the same animal, if one <b>designated</b> an animal as <b>a guilt offering with which to achieve atonement and substituted for it, and</b> the initial guilt offering <b>became blemished and he desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another</b> animal, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether <b>he can then effect substitution</b> for this last animal? On the one hand, it is a different animal; on the other hand, it possesses the same sanctity.
Rabbi Avin raises another dilemma: If the first animal was lost and the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another</b> as a <b>guilt offering, and</b> the first animal was found and <b>was consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether <b>he can then effect substitution</b> for it? On the one hand, it is the same animal; on the other hand, it possesses a different type of sanctity.
<b>Abaye said: Which</b> of these two is the essential <b>dilemma he is raising?</b> If his dilemma concerns a case of <b>another</b> type of <b>sanctity in the same body, he should not raise the dilemma</b> in the case where the initial guilt offering <b>became blemished and he desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another</b> animal. And <b>if</b> his dilemma concerns a case of <b>another body with the same sanctity, he should not raise the dilemma</b> in the case where the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another</b> animal as <b>a guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi Avin raises one</b> dilemma <b>as a result of</b> the anticipated solution to <b>another</b> dilemma, as follows: If the initial guilt offering <b>became blemished and he desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another</b> animal, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as to <b>whether he</b> can <b>then effect substitution</b> for the second animal? <b>Do we say</b> that it <b>is with the first guilt offering that he cannot effect substitution again, but with another body, even though it is sanctified with the same</b> type of <b>sanctity, he</b> can <b>effect substitution again? Or perhaps</b> we say that with regard to <b>any</b> animal <b>with the same</b> type of <b>sanctity, he cannot effect substitution again?</b>
<b>And if you say</b> that with regard to <b>this other body, since it maintains the same</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> as the original animal one <b>cannot effect substitution again, but</b> what then of a case where the first animal was lost and the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another</b> animal as <b>a guilt offering, and this first</b> animal was then found and <b>was consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering? What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether <b>he can effect substitution again</b> for the same animal, since now its sanctity has changed?
The Gemara elaborates: <b>Do we say</b> that <b>when</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan states that the owner <b>cannot effect substitution and then effect substitution again, this statement</b> applies to <b>the same body with the same</b> type of <b>sanctity, but</b> as for <b>the same body with another</b> type of <b>sanctity, he can effect substitution again? Or perhaps</b> we say that <b>even concerning another</b> type of <b>sanctity, since it is the same body, he cannot effect substitution again</b> with it. The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ The Gemara cites a similar discussion. <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi says:</b> One who redeems an animal that was <b>originally consecrated</b> as an offering and developed a blemish must pay an amount equivalent to its value and <b>add</b> an additional <b>one-fifth</b> of its value. <b>But</b> one who redeems an animal that was <b>consecrated second,</b> i.e., in place of a desacralized blemished offering, and then developed a blemish itself, must pay an amount equivalent to its value but <b>does not add</b> an additional <b>one-fifth</b> of its value.
<b>Rav Pappa said: What is the reasoning of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi? The verse states: “And if he that consecrated it will redeem his house, then he shall add the fifth part</b> of the money of your valuation unto it, and it shall be his” (Leviticus 27:15), from which Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi infers that the requirement to pay an additional one-fifth applies to one <b>who consecrates</b> an item directly, <b>but not</b> to <b>the one who applies</b> the sanctity of another item to it.
<b>Rabbi Avin raises a dilemma</b> concerning a similar matter: If one <b>designated</b> an animal as <b>a guilt offering by which to achieve atonement and it became blemished, and he added one-fifth to its</b> value <b>and desacralized it,</b> and he then used the consecrated money to purchase <b>another</b> guilt offering, which was then lost, <b>and</b> the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another,</b> third, animal as <b>a guilt offering, and</b> the second animal was found and <b>consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering,</b> and then it developed a blemish, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether the owner must <b>add one-fifth to its</b> value when he redeems it?
<b>Abaye said: What is the dilemma he is raising? If he is raising a dilemma</b> with regard to <b>two bodies,</b> i.e., two different animals, the problem should stand even if both are of <b>one</b> type of <b>sanctity.</b> If so, <b>let the dilemma be raised</b> even <b>in</b> a case where he <b>did not achieve atonement</b> through another guilt offering, and the second animal was never consigned to be sacrificed as a burnt offering.
And <b>if he is raising a dilemma</b> with regard to <b>two</b> types of <b>sanctity,</b> it should apply even in the case of <b>one body.</b> If so, <b>let the dilemma be raised</b> even <b>in</b> a case where the original animal <b>did not become blemished</b> but was simply lost and found after the owner atoned by means of another offering and thereby consigned the original animal to be sacrificed as a burnt offering.
The Gemara explains that <b>Rabbi Avin raises one</b> dilemma <b>as a result of</b> the anticipated solution to <b>another</b> dilemma, as follows: <b>If you say</b> that in a case of <b>two bodies and one</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> the owner does <b>not</b> add one-fifth, perhaps that is <b>because one-fifth was</b> already <b>added once to</b> redeem an animal <b>with that</b> type of <b>sanctity.</b> If so, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i> in the case of <b>two bodies and two</b> types of <b>sanctity?</b> Perhaps, since the second animal possesses a different sanctity, it is considered to possess its own original sanctity, rather than derivative sanctity, and therefore one who redeems it adds one-fifth. The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
The Gemara presents <b>an alternative version</b> of the previous dilemma. <b>Rabbi Avin raises a dilemma:</b> If one <b>designated</b> an animal as <b>a guilt offering with which to achieve atonement, and it became blemished and he desacralized</b> it by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another animal, and he added one-fifth</b> of its value, and the second animal was lost and the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another,</b> third, animal as <b>a guilt offering, and</b> the second animal was found and <b>was consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering,</b> and then it developed a blemish, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether the owner must <b>add one-fifth to its</b> value when he redeems it?
<b>Abaye said: Which</b> of these two <b>is</b> the essential <b>dilemma he is raising? If he raises a dilemma</b> with regard to the case of <b>another</b> type of <b>sanctity in the same body, he should not raise the dilemma</b> about a case where the initial guilt offering <b>became blemished</b> and he transferred its sanctity to another animal. And <b>if</b> his essential dilemma concerns the case of <b>another body with the same</b> type of <b>sanctity,</b> he could pose his dilemma with regard to a case where the second animal was not lost and found and consigned to be a burnt offering.
The Gemara explains that Rabbi Avin raises one dilemma as a result of the anticipated solution to another dilemma. If the initial guilt offering became blemished <b>and he desacralized it</b> by transferring its sanctity <b>onto another,</b> second, animal, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether the owner must <b>add one-fifth to its</b> value when he redeems it? <b>Do we say</b> that <b>with regard to</b> a second redemption of the <b>first guilt offering he</b> does not <b>add</b> one-fifth, since it is the same body and the same sanctity, <b>but</b> here, when it is <b>another body, even though it maintains the same</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> as the initial animal, he <b>adds one-fifth,</b> as it is considered to be directly consecrated?

Daf 10a

<b>Or perhaps</b> we say that if one redeems <b>any</b> animal that possesses the <b>same sanctity</b> as the original <b>he does not add</b> an additional <b>one-fifth.</b>
<b>And if you say</b> that with regard to <b>this other body, since it maintains the same</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> as the initial animal, one <b>does not add one-fifth</b> when redeeming it, <b>but</b> what of a case where the first animal was lost and the owner <b>achieved atonement by</b> bringing <b>another, and this</b> first animal was found and <b>was consigned to</b> be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering? What is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Do we say that <b>when</b> one <b>does not add</b> an additional <b>one-fifth,</b> that is only <b>with regard to the same body with the same</b> type of <b>sanctity, but</b> in the case <b>of another</b> type of <b>sanctity</b> the exemption does <b>not</b> apply? <b>Or perhaps</b> we say that <b>since it is the same body,</b> one <b>does not add one-fifth.</b> The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<b>Rami bar Ḥama raises a dilemma:</b> If one person consecrates his animal to be used as an offering by another, does the <b>one who consecrates</b> it <b>add one-fifth</b> when he redeems it, <b>or</b> does the <b>one atoning</b> by means of the offering <b>add one-fifth?</b> Who is considered the owner of the offering? <b>Rava said</b> that <b>the verse states: “And if he that consecrated it will redeem his house,</b> then he shall add the fifth part of the money” (Leviticus 27:15). It may be inferred from here that only the <b>one who consecrates</b> the item adds one-fifth, <b>but not</b> the <b>one for whom</b> the offering <b>atones.</b>
<b>Rami bar Ḥama raises a dilemma:</b> If one person consecrates an animal to be used as an offering by another, can the <b>one who consecrates</b> it <b>render</b> another animal <b>a substitute</b> for it, <b>or</b> can the <b>one achieving atonement</b> through it <b>render a substitute</b> for it? <b>Rava said: If</b> it were <b>so,</b> that the one who consecrates it can render a substitute for it, <b>we</b> would <b>find</b> a case where the <b>community or partners can render a substitute</b> for their consecrated animal, <b>for example where they appointed an agent to consecrate</b> an animal for them. The mishna (13a) states that one cannot substitute for consecrated animals belonging to the community or to partners. By contrast, in this case, since only one person consecrated it, he would be able to substitute for it, contrary to the mishna.
<b>And furthermore, Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Rav Huna says:</b> The Sages <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: “This is the law of the nazirite who vows, <b>and of his offering unto the Lord for his naziriteship, besides that for which his means suffice;</b> according to his vow which he vows” (Numbers 6:21). <b>But is</b> the offering of <b>a nazirite judged according to his means?</b> The offerings of a nazirite are fixed by the Torah. <b>How</b> is it possible to understand <b>this</b> clause: “Besides that for which his means suffice”? Rather, the clause <b>“his offering unto the Lord for his naziriteship”</b> is referring to a case <b>where he separated</b> an offering <b>from his own</b> animals, whereas the clause <b>“besides that for which his means suffice”</b> is referring to a case <b>where others separated</b> the offering <b>for him.</b> This teaches that designation by others is effective.
<b>With regard to what <i>halakha</i></b> is this <i>baraita</i> stated? <b>If we say</b> that it is <b>with regard to the matter of atonement,</b> and it is teaching that a nazirite can atone even by means of an offering designated for him by others, isn’t it <b>obvious that</b> such an offering can <b>atone for him?</b> Once it is his offering, he can certainly use it. <b>Rather,</b> the ruling of this <i>baraita</i> must be stated <b>with regard to substitution, and this</b> is what it <b>is saying: Even</b> in a case <b>where others separated</b> the offering <b>for him,</b> he alone can <b>render a substitute</b> for it. <b>Conclude from</b> this verse that <b>we follow</b> the <b>one for whom</b> the offering <b>atones,</b> and only he can effect substitution for the offering.
The Gemara responds: The <i>baraita</i> is <b>not</b> referring <b>to substitution,</b> but to the matter of atonement. <b>And that which is difficult for you,</b> that it is obvious that the nazirite can atone by bringing an offering consecrated by another, as <b>they have given it to him as a gift,</b> this is in fact not obvious. <b>Had the Merciful One not included</b> an offering received from another by writing the clause <b>“besides that for which his means suffice,” I would say</b> that <b>it is a Torah edict</b> expressed in the term <b>“his offering,”</b> that a nazirite can <b>atone</b> only with an offering consecrated <b>from his own</b> animals, <b>but not</b> through one received <b>from</b> the animals <b>of others.</b> Therefore, the verse <b>teaches us</b> that he can atone even with an offering received from others.
Having failed to resolve Rami bar Ḥama’s dilemma, the Gemara asks: <b>What</b> halakhic conclusion <b>was</b> reached <b>about this</b> matter? The Gemara answers: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a proof from that <b>which Rabbi Abbahu said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> If <b>one consecrates</b> his animal as an offering for someone else’s atonement, and he subsequently redeems the animal, <b>he adds one-fifth</b> to its value as the owner. <b>But</b> only the <b>one for whom</b> the offering <b>atones</b> can <b>render a substitute</b> for it. This resolves the dilemma of Rami bar Ḥama.
Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement continues: And if one <b>separates <i>teruma</i> from his</b> produce <b>to</b> exempt the produce <b>of others,</b> so that the other’s produce will be permitted in consumption, the <b>benefit of discretion is his.</b> He is entitled to determine which priest receives the <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for this? <b>The verse states:</b> “When you have made an end of tithing <b>all the tithe of your produce</b> in the third year, which is the year of tithing, <b>and have given</b> it to the Levite, to the stranger, to the fatherless, and to the widow” (Deuteronomy 26:12). This indicates that the one actually giving the produce chooses to whom to give it.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One does not substitute</b> non-sacred <b>limbs for</b> consecrated <b>fetuses,</b> i.e., if one says that a certain limb of a non-sacred animal is substituted for a fetus in the womb of a consecrated animal, it is not consecrated. <b>And</b> likewise, one does <b>not</b> substitute non-sacred <b>fetuses for</b> consecrated <b>limbs. And</b> one substitutes <b>neither</b> non-sacred <b>limbs nor fetuses for whole</b> consecrated animals <b>nor</b> non-sacred <b>whole</b> animals <b>for</b> consecrated limbs or fetuses.
<b>Rabbi Yosei says: One substitutes</b> non-sacred <b>limbs for whole</b> consecrated animals, <b>but not whole</b> animals <b>for</b> consecrated limbs. <b>Rabbi Yosei said: But isn’t</b> it so <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> animals, that if <b>one says: The</b> hind <b>leg of this</b> animal is <b>a burnt offering, the entire</b> animal is <b>a burnt offering? So too, when he says: The</b> non-sacred hind <b>leg of this</b> animal is <b>in exchange for that</b> animal, <b>the entire</b> animal is <b>a substitute in exchange for it.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>It was stated</b> that <b>Bar Padda says: Fetuses are not imbued with sanctity</b> if one attempts to consecrate them for sacrifice, and <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: Fetuses are imbued with sanctity.</b> The Gemara notes: <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan follows his</b> line of <b>reasoning</b> in this regard, <b>as Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> In the case of <b>one who consecrated a pregnant animal</b> as <b>a sin offering, and it</b> later <b>gave birth,</b> if <b>he wishes he may achieve atonement by</b> sacrificing the mother, and the offspring will be left to graze until it develops a blemish that renders it unfit, whereupon it will be sold and the money is used to purchase a gift burnt offering; and if <b>he wishes he may achieve atonement through</b> the animal’s <b>offspring,</b> and the mother will be left to graze until it develops a blemish.
The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> both statements of Rabbi Yoḥanan are <b>necessary,</b> despite the fact that both affirm that fetuses may be consecrated. <b>As, if</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan had <b>taught us</b> only <b>this first</b> case, of one who consecrates a fetus by itself, I might say that only <b>there</b> is the fetus imbued with sanctity, <b>as he consecrated</b>

Daf 10b

<b>the fetus itself. But here, where he consecrated its</b> pregnant <b>mother,</b> not the fetus itself, one might think that <b>this</b> fetus <b>is not consecrated along with its mother.</b>
<b>And if</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan had <b>taught us</b> only <b>this last</b> case, of one who consecrates a pregnant animal, I might have said that <b>it is</b> only <b>there</b> that its fetus is sacred, <b>as he consecrated</b> the animal <b>and all that is inside it,</b> including the fetus. <b>But here, where he consecrated</b> the fetus <b>itself, since</b> the object of the consecration <b>is not outside</b> its mother, as it has yet to be born, one might say that it is <b>not consecrated.</b> Therefore, it was <b>necessary</b> for Rabbi Yoḥanan to state both <i>halakhot</i>.
§ The Gemara cites <b>another version</b> of the discussion: <b>What is</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>teaching us</b> when he says above that one may atone with either the mother or the offspring? He is evidently saying that <b>if he</b> explicitly <b>excluded</b> the fetus from his consecration, it is <b>excluded,</b> and only the mother is consecrated. <b>And</b> the reason is that <b>a fetus is not</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother</b> but a separate entity that can possess a different status. <b>But</b> if so, <b>why do I</b> need <b>two</b> <i>halakhot</i> to teach me this, both with regard to one who consecrates a pregnant animal and with regard to one who consecrates the fetus alone?
The Gemara answers: Both <i>halakhot</i> are <b>necessary, as, if it was stated</b> only <b>with regard to that</b> case of one who consecrates a pregnant animal, I would say that the fetus is consecrated <b>because its mother is fit to</b> be consecrated <b>itself,</b> and <b>since sanctity applies to her, it likewise applies to the offspring. But</b> in <b>the other</b> case of one who consecrates a fetus alone, perhaps it is <b>not</b> consecrated. Therefore, Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>teaches us this</b> case as well.
<b>And if it was stated</b> only <b>with regard to this</b> case of one who consecrates a fetus, one might say that the fetus is sanctified <b>because he expressly</b> states that <b>sanctity</b> should apply <b>to the offspring. But</b> with regard to <b>that</b> case of one who consecrates a pregnant animal, where he does not explicitly mention the fetus, perhaps is it <b>not</b> consecrated. Therefore, both statements are <b>necessary.</b>
§ <b>Rabbi Zeira sat and stated this <i>halakha</i></b> of the dispute between bar Padda and Rabbi Yoḥanan with regard to the consecration of fetuses. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya raised an objection to Rabbi Zeira</b> from a mishna (24b): <b>How</b> may <b>one employ artifice to</b> circumvent the obligation to give <b>the firstborn</b> to the priest and use the animal instead to fulfill a different obligation? He may approach <b>an animal</b> that is going to <b>give birth to its firstborn</b> but <b>which is</b> still <b>pregnant,</b> and <b>say: That which is in the womb of this</b> animal, if it is male, is designated as <b>a burnt offering.</b> If he did so, and the animal <b>gave birth to a male, it</b> will be sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering,</b> and it is not consecrated as a firstborn. <b>Evidently, a fetus</b> can be <b>consecrated,</b> contrary to the opinion of bar Padda.
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: Bar Padda can answer that <b>when that</b> mishna <b>is taught,</b> it is referring <b>to</b> one who consecrates the fetus for <b>sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value,</b> i.e., that the offspring should be sold and a burnt offering brought with the proceeds. This form of sanctity does apply to a fetus, but inherent sanctity rendering it obligated to be sacrificed itself does not. Rabbi Yirmeya again asked Rabbi Zeira: <b>Is sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>value</b> so <b>strong that it removes the firstborn</b> status <b>from</b> an animal?
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to him: Yes, and</b> so <b>we learned</b> in a mishna in tractate <i>Bekhorot</i> (14a): <b>All sacrificial</b> animals in <b>which a permanent blemish preceded their consecration</b> assume not inherent sanctity but sanctity of value, <b>and</b> once <b>they are redeemed</b> they <b>are obligated,</b> i.e., subject <b>to,</b> accounting their offspring <b>a firstborn, and</b> one is obligated <b>to</b> give the <b>gifts</b> of the foreleg, the jaw, and the maw to a priest as for any non-sacred slaughtered animal. One can infer from the mishna that the <b>reason that</b> these animals <b>are obligated</b> is <b>that they have already been redeemed. But before they are redeemed,</b> they are <b>exempt</b> from the obligation of the firstborn and the gifts, despite the fact that only their value is sacred. <b>Evidently, sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>value is strong</b> enough <b>to remove firstborn status from</b> an animal.
Rabbi Yirmeya <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> bar Padda’s opinion from a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one says: That which is in the womb of this</b> animal is <b>a burnt offering,</b> it is <b>permitted</b> for the mother <b>to be shorn but</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> to use it <b>for labor, due to</b> the inevitable <b>weakening</b> and consequent reduction in value <b>of</b> the <b>fetus that is inside it.</b> This indicates that fetuses are imbued with sanctity, contrary to the opinion of bar Padda. Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: <b>This</b> <i>baraita</i> is <b>also</b> referring to one who consecrates the fetus for <b>sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value,</b> and bar Padda concedes that this sanctity applies to the fetus.
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: <b>But is sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>value</b> so <b>strong that it renders</b> the mother <b>prohibited for labor?</b> Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> Rabbi Yirmeya: <b>Yes, and</b> so <b>we learned</b> in the mishna cited previously, that blemished animals that were consecrated for their value and redeemed <b>emerge</b> from their sacred status and assume complete <b>non-sacred</b> status and may <b>be shorn and used for labor.</b> Apparently, the <b>reason</b> they may be used for labor is <b>that they were redeemed.</b> One may infer that <b>before they are redeemed, they are prohibited for labor. Evidently, sanctity</b> that inheres in the animal’s <b>value renders</b> the mother of the fetus <b>prohibited for labor.</b>
Rabbi Yirmeya <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> bar Padda’s opinion from the mishna: <b>One does not substitute</b> non-sacred <b>limbs for</b> consecrated <b>fetuses, and</b> likewise one does <b>not</b> substitute non-sacred <b>fetuses for</b> consecrated <b>limbs,</b> or non-sacred limbs or fetuses for whole consecrated animals, or non-sacred whole animals for consecrated limbs or fetuses. One may infer from the mishna that <b>it is a substitution</b> of fetuses <b>that one cannot effect,</b> as the Torah states with regard to substitution: “And if he substitutes an animal for an animal” (Leviticus 27:10), and a fetus is not classified as an animal. But if one <b>consecrates</b> fetuses directly, not in the manner of substitution, they are <b>consecrated,</b> contrary to the opinion of bar Padda.
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> him: The mishna <b>is</b> referring <b>to the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals that became pregnant after they were consecrated. That case is different, <b>as they are already consecrated</b> through their mothers. It does not indicate that one can consecrate a fetus directly.
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: <b>If</b> the mishna is referring to <b>the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, and it is teaching that one does not substitute them because fetuses are not considered animals, one can infer that <b>it is</b> only when they are <b>in their mother’s womb that one cannot effect</b> substitution with them. But once they are <b>outside</b> their mother’s womb, i.e., once they are born, one can <b>effect</b> substitution with them. <b>But</b> this is not the case, as <b>isn’t it taught</b> in the next mishna (12a) that <b>the offspring</b> born of a consecrated animal that was not itself consecrated <b>does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute?</b>
Rabbi Zeira answered: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> first mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who</b> disagreed with that ruling in the next mishna and <b>said</b> that <b>the offspring renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b>
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: <b>If</b> this mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> one encounters another difficulty. The mishna indicates that <b>it is substitution that one cannot perform</b> with consecrated limbs, as the sanctity of substitution does not extend to the entire animal. Consequently, if one <b>consecrates</b> limbs directly, they are <b>consecrated</b> and the sanctity extends to the whole animal. But <b>doesn’t Rabbi Yehuda say</b> that <b>limbs are not consecrated</b> in this manner, that the sanctity extends to the whole animal? Rabbi Zeira <b>said</b> him: <b>What are we dealing with here? With a limb upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life depends.</b> Rabbi Yehuda’s ruling that limbs cannot be consecrated applies only to non-vital organs, e.g., legs. He concedes that vital organs are consecrated and the consecration extends to the entire animal.
Rabbi Yirmeya <b>raised</b> another <b>objection to</b> bar Padda’s opinion: A later mishna (16b) lists stringencies that apply to sacrificial animals but not to substitutes: <b>One consecrates</b> an animal’s <b>limbs and fetuses</b> in utero, <b>but one does not substitute</b> non-sacred animals for them. This contradicts bar Padda’s opinion that one cannot consecrate fetuses. Rabbi Zeira answered: <b>Here too,</b> the mishna is referring <b>to the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals that became pregnant after they were consecrated.
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: <b>If</b> the mishna is referring <b>to the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, <b>what</b> is the meaning of the term: <b>One consecrates?</b> After all, these offspring <b>are already consecrated</b> through their mothers.

Daf 11a

Rabbi Zeira answered that <b>this</b> is what the mishna <b>is saying: One consecrates</b> certain animal’s <b>limbs,</b> and the sanctity extends to the entire animal. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>one substitutes</b> these animals despite the fact that he initially consecrated only the limbs. <b>But one does not substitute</b> the limbs alone of non-sacred animals <b>for</b> consecrated animals or their limbs, since sanctity cannot be transferred to a limb through substitution. <b>And</b> as for <b>fetuses that were consecrated in their mother’s womb</b> through consecration of the mother, <b>one does not substitute for them</b> as long as they are in their mother’s womb. In other words, the clause of this mishna that refers to consecration is not referring to fetuses at all.
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: If the mishna is referring <b>to the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, and it is teaching that <b>it is</b> only when they are <b>in their mother’s womb that one may not render a substitute</b> for them, one can infer that once they are <b>outside</b> their mother’s womb, <b>one can render</b> a substitute for them. <b>But didn’t we learn</b> in the next mishna that <b>the offspring</b> born of a consecrated animal <b>do not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for them <b>a substitute?</b> Rabbi Zeira answered: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> who disagrees and maintains that one can substitute for the offspring.
Rabbi Yirmeya objected: <b>If</b> the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda,</b> one encounters a difficulty. According to Rabbi Yehuda, <b>are limbs consecrated</b> in this manner, in that the sanctity extends to the whole animal? <b>But Rabbi Yehuda does not accept</b> that if <b>one says: The</b> hind <b>leg of this</b> animal is <b>a burnt offering, the entire</b> animal is <b>a burnt offering.</b>
Rabbi Zeira <b>said to</b> him: <b>What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing <b>with an item whose</b> removal <b>renders it a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> i.e., will cause it to die within twelve months. Rabbi Yehuda’s ruling that the consecration of a limb does not extend to the entire animal applies only to limbs whose removal would not render the animal a <i>tereifa</i>. He concedes that the consecration of limbs which would render an animal <i>tereifa</i> were they removed does extend to the whole animal. In sum, bar Padda’s opinion that fetuses cannot be consecrated has not been refuted.
§ The Gemara suggests. <b>Let us say</b> that the dispute between bar Padda and Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>is parallel to</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>,</b> as we learned: If <b>one slaughtered</b> a pregnant animal that was consecrated as <b>a sin offering, and he found inside it</b> a female fetus <b>four</b> months <b>old,</b> which is <b>alive,</b> despite the fact that the mother usually carries its young for five months, <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches:</b> This animal is treated as a sin offering, and therefore <b>it is eaten only by the males of the priesthood, and</b> it <b>is eaten only within the hangings,</b> i.e., within the Temple courtyard, <b>and</b> it <b>is eaten only for one day</b> and night.
<b>And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that it is treated as a non-sacred animal, which means <b>it is eaten by any person,</b> not only priests, <b>and</b> such animals <b>may be eaten anywhere</b> outside the Temple courtyard, <b>but they may not be eaten in the</b> Temple <b>courtyard. What, is it not</b> correct that the dispute between bar Padda and Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>is</b> a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the first <i>baraita</i>, <b>holds</b> in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan, that <b>fetuses are imbued with sanctity, and</b> one <b>Sage,</b> the <i>tanna</i> of the second <i>baraita</i>, <b>holds</b> that <b>fetuses are not imbued with sanctity,</b> as maintained by bar Padda?
The Gemara responds: <b>No,</b> the <i>tanna’im</i> of both <i>baraitot</i> agree that sanctity can apply to fetuses if the animal was consecrated while pregnant. Rather, the <i>baraitot</i> are dealing with an animal that was consecrated and then became pregnant, and <b>these <i>tanna’im</i> disagree about this, as</b> one <b>Sage,</b> in the second <i>baraita</i>, <b>holds</b> that with regard to the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, only <b>when they come into being,</b> at the time of their birth, <b>are they sanctified.</b> Since this fetus was never born, it is not sacred. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage,</b> in the first <i>baraita</i>, <b>holds</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are sanctified</b> already <b>in their mother’s womb,</b> and therefore all the <i>halakhot</i> of a sin offering apply to the fetus immediately.
<b>And if you wish, say</b> instead that there is no contradiction between these two <i>baraitot</i> at all, and <b>it is one <i>tanna</i></b> who taught both of them, in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan that fetuses are imbued with sanctity. And the second <b>one of these <i>baraitot</i></b> is referring <b>to</b> a case where <b>one consecrates an animal</b> as a sin offering <b>and it became pregnant</b> only <b>afterward.</b> Since the fetus did not exist at the moment of consecration, it is not sacred. <b>And</b> the first <b>one of</b> the <i>baraitot</i> is referring to a case <b>where he consecrated</b> the animal when it was already <b>pregnant,</b> and therefore its fetus is imbued with its sanctity.
§ <b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> An animal crossbred from <b>diverse kinds, and a <i>tereifa</i>, and</b> an animal <b>born by caesarean section,</b> and an animal <b>whose sexual organs are indeterminate [<i>tumtum</i>], and</b> an animal that is <b>a hermaphrodite [<i>ve’androginos</i>] are neither sacred nor do they sanctify</b> another animal. <b>And Shmuel says,</b> in explanation of the <i>baraita</i>: These animals <b>are not sacred as a substitute,</b> i.e., if one of these was non-sacred it cannot be consecrated as a substitute for a sacred animal. <b>And</b> they <b>do not sanctify</b> another animal <b>to render</b> it <b>a substitute;</b> that is, if one of these animals was sacred, no non-sacred animal can be consecrated as a substitute for them.
<b>And it is taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir said:</b> Why is it necessary for Rabbi Eliezer to state that one cannot substitute for these animals? After all, <b>since</b> he has already said that <b>they are not sanctified, how could they sanctify</b> another animal exchanged for them? Rabbi Meir himself responded: <b>You find</b> that these animals can be sacred, but <b>only</b> in specific situations, for example, <b>where one consecrated an animal and subsequently it became a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> or <b>where one consecrated</b> an animal’s <b>offspring and it was</b> then <b>born by caesarean section.</b> One can infer from Rabbi Meir’s statement that <b>evidently an offspring can be sanctified</b> while in the womb, contrary to the opinion of bar Padda.
The Sages <b>say</b> in response: In the case <b>of an unblemished</b> animal <b>in the womb of an unblemished</b> mother, <b>even bar Padda concedes that it can be consecrated.</b> Bar Padda and Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>disagree only with regard to an unblemished</b> animal <b>in the womb of a blemished</b> mother. <b>Bar Padda maintains</b> that <b>since its mother cannot be consecrated with inherent sanctity,</b> its offspring <b>is not consecrated either. And Rabbi Yoḥanan maintains: These are</b> considered <b>as two</b> separate <b>animals; it is its mother</b> alone <b>that is not consecrated, but</b> the fetus itself is <b>consecrated.</b>
The Gemara cites <b>another version</b> of the above discussion, which cites the continuation of Rabbi Meir’s statement: <b>But</b> with regard to an animal born of <b>diverse kinds, and a <i>tumtum</i>, and a hermaphrodite, you find</b> that they can be consecrated <b>only when</b> they are <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals, i.e., a consecrated animal impregnated by an animal of a different species, or whose fetus was a <i>tumtum</i> or a hermaphrodite.
Rabbi Meir adds: <b>And</b> Rabbi Eliezer holds <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who would say</b> that <b>the offspring</b> of a consecrated animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> These offspring are exceptions, as they are disqualified for the altar. The Gemara infers: <b>It is</b> only for <b>these</b> disqualified offspring that one cannot substitute, <b>as they cannot have inherent sanctity, but</b> other offspring that are fit for the altar, and likewise <b>other fetuses, can be consecrated,</b> contrary to the opinion of bar Padda.
<b>Abaye said:</b> In the case of <b>an unblemished</b> animal <b>in the womb of an unblemished</b> mother, <b>everyone agrees that</b> the fetus <b>can have inherent sanctity. Rather, when</b> bar Padda and Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>disagree,</b> it is with regard to an unblemished animal <b>in the womb of a blemished</b> mother. <b>As bar Padda maintains: Since even its mother cannot have inherent sanctity,</b> the offspring <b>too can be consecrated only for</b> its <b>value.</b> And <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan maintains: A fetus is</b> considered <b>not the thigh of its mother</b> but a separate entity, <b>and</b> therefore <b>even though its mother’s sanctity is not inherent, the sanctity of</b> its <b>offspring can still be inherent.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that according to the Rabbis, one cannot substitute non-sacred limbs for whole consecrated animals, but <b>Rabbi Yosei said</b> that one can substitute in this manner. He claims: <b>But isn’t</b> it so <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> animals, that if <b>one says: The</b> hind <b>leg of this</b> animal is a burnt offering, the entire animal is a burnt offering? The same should apply to substitution.

Daf 11b

<b>The Sages taught:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that if <b>one says: The leg of this</b> animal <b>is a burnt offering,</b> the sanctity should extend to the whole animal and <b>all of</b> the animal <b>will be a burnt offering.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And if it is an animal of those that they bring as an offering to the Lord, <b>anything of it that one gives to the Lord, it shall be sacred”</b> (Leviticus 27:9). The verse indicates that the part <b>of it</b> that one gives will be sacred <b>unto the Lord, and not all of</b> the animal will be sacred <b>unto the Lord.</b>
If so, one <b>might</b> have thought that the consecrated limb may be redeemed and thereby <b>transferred to non-sacred</b> status. Therefore <b>the verse states: “Shall be sacred,”</b> teaching that the limb remains consecrated. <b>How</b> is <b>this</b> possible, i.e., what should one do with the animal? The animal <b>should be sold for the needs of burnt offerings</b> to an individual who will sacrifice the entire animal as a burnt offering, <b>and the payment</b> received <b>for</b> the animal will be <b>non-sacred, except for the payment</b> received in exchange <b>for</b> the <b>limb of</b> the animal that was consecrated. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
<b>But Rabbi Yosei and Rabbi Shimon say: From where</b> is it derived in the case of <b>one who says: The leg of this</b> animal <b>is a burnt offering, that all of it becomes a burnt offering? Since it is stated: “All that any man give of such to the Lord</b> shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:9), one might have thought that individual limbs can be consecrated. <b>When it says: “Shall be sacred,”</b> this phrase serves <b>to include all of</b> the animal, indicating that it all becomes sacred.
The Gemara objects: <b>The Master said:</b> The animal <b>should be sold for the needs of burnt offerings. But</b> it is as though the purchaser is <b>bringing an animal</b> that <b>lacks</b> that <b>limb</b> that was consecrated by the previous owner, as his share in the offering consists only of the balance of the animal. <b>Rava says:</b> The <i>baraita</i> does not mean that the animal should be sold to anyone obligated to bring a burnt offering, but specifically <b>to one who says: It</b> is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a burnt offering with</b> all the limbs upon which <b>its life</b> depends. Since the consecrated limb is not one of these, it does not detract from the purchaser’s vow.
The Gemara cites a dispute between <i>amora’im</i>: <b>Rav Ḥisda says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yehuda</b> and Rabbi Meir <b>concede with regard to</b> a limb <b>whose</b> removal <b>renders</b> the animal <b>a <i>tereifa</i>.</b> If one consecrated such a limb, the sanctity extends throughout the entire animal. <b>Rava says:</b> Even in such a case, Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda maintain that the sanctity does not extend throughout the entire animal. Yet, they concede <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>whose</b> removal <b>renders it an animal carcass,</b> i.e., without which the animal would die so soon that it assumes the status of a carcass. <b>And Rav Sheshet says:</b> They conceded only <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>whose</b> removal would cause <b>it</b> to <b>die</b> immediately.
The Gemara explains: <b>What</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Ḥisda and</b> that of <b>Rava?</b> The difference <b>between them</b> is with regard to the issue of whether <b>a <i>tereifa</i> can survive. Rav Ḥisda holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the one who said</b> that <b>a <i>tereifa</i> cannot survive</b> but will shortly die. Therefore, Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda concede that if one consecrates a limb whose removal would render an animal a <i>tereifa</i>, the sanctity applies to the whole animal, since it is a vital organ. <b>And Rava holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the one who says</b> that <b>a <i>tereifa</i> can survive,</b> and consequently the consecration of such a limb does not extend to the entire animal.
The Gemara further explains: <b>And what</b> difference <b>is there between</b> the opinion of <b>Rava and</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Sheshet?</b> The difference <b>between them</b> is with regard to the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar, as Rabbi Elazar said:</b> If <b>the thigh,</b> i.e., the hind leg of the animal, <b>and its recess were removed,</b> the animal is considered <b>a carcass</b> and imparts ritual impurity even while still alive. <b>Rava holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar,</b> and therefore he maintains that according to Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yehuda, if one consecrates its thigh and its recess the sanctity extends throughout the animal, as it is a vital limb. But <b>Rav Sheshet does not hold in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar,</b> and therefore the sanctity in such a case does not extend to the entire animal.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection: Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said:</b> With regard to the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda and Rabbi Yosei, <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda,</b> that the sanctity does not extend to the whole animal, <b>appears</b> correct <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life does not depend, and the statement of Rabbi Yosei</b> appears correct <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life depends.</b> May one <b>not</b> conclude <b>by inference</b> from the statement of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>that</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>disagrees with Rabbi Yehuda</b> even with regard to a limb upon which the animal’s life depends, contrary to the statements of the <i>amora’im</i> above?
<b>Granted,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s comment: <b>The statement of Rabbi Yehuda appears</b> correct <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life does not depend,</b> is not difficult, as one learns <b>by inference</b> only <b>that</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>disagrees with Rabbi Yosei</b> with regard to such a case. <b>But</b> when he says that <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei appears</b> correct <b>with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life depends,</b> must one <b>not</b> conclude <b>by inference that</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>disagrees with Rabbi Yehuda</b> with regard to this case as well? <b>And</b> this is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation of all</b> the opinions of the <i>amora’im</i> cited above, who maintain that Rabbi Yehuda concedes with regard to vital organs.
The Gemara answers: <b>No,</b> the <i>baraita</i> citing Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi’s statement <b>is incomplete, and this</b> is what <b>it is teaching: The statement of Rabbi Yosei appears</b> correct <b>to Rabbi Yehuda</b> in the case <b>of</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life depends, as even Rabbi Yehuda disagreed with</b> Rabbi Yosei <b>only with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life does not depend. But with regard to</b> a limb <b>upon which</b> the animal’s <b>life depends,</b> Rabbi Yehuda <b>concedes to him</b> that the sanctity extends to the entire animal.
§ According to Rabbi Yosei, if one consecrates one limb of an animal the sanctity extends to the entire animal. <b>Rava raises a dilemma: With regard to a bird</b> that is fit for sacrifice, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> according to this opinion? Since this <i>halakha</i> is derived, as stated above, from the verse: “And if it is an animal of those that they bring as an offering to the Lord, anything of it that one gives to the Lord, it shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:9), one can contend that <b>the Merciful One states “animal,” and this</b> bird is <b>not</b> considered <b>an animal. Or perhaps</b> one should stress that <b>the Merciful One states “offering,” and this</b> bird is <b>also</b> considered <b>an offering.</b> The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<b>Rava raises</b> another <b>dilemma:</b> According to Rabbi Yosei, if one <b>consecrated a limb</b> with the intention of selling it and purchasing an offering with <b>its payment, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether the entire animal <b>is imbued with inherent sanctity? Does</b> one <b>say</b> that <b>since it is imbued with sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value, it is likewise imbued with inherent sanctity, and</b> then furthermore, from the fact <b>that he consecrated one limb he has consecrated the entire</b> animal? <b>Or perhaps</b> one <b>says</b> a claim using the logic that begins with: <b>Since,</b> only <b>once,</b> but one does <b>not say two</b> claims together using the logic that begins with: <b>Since,</b> i.e., one cannot make both of the suggested expansions simultaneously.
The Gemara suggests: <b>Resolve</b> the dilemma <b>by</b> citing Rava’s <b>own</b> statement. <b>As, didn’t Rava say:</b> If <b>one consecrated a male</b> ram <b>for its value,</b> since the ram is fit for sacrifice, it is automatically <b>consecrated</b> with <b>inherent sanctity?</b> The same reasoning should apply here: Although the limb was consecrated for its value, since the animal is fit for sacrifice, once the entire animal is consecrated for its value it should automatically assume inherent sanctity.
The Gemara responds that this is not a proof: <b>There, where he</b> initially <b>consecrated all of it,</b> the whole animal assumes inherent sanctity. <b>Here,</b> Rava is referring to a case <b>where he consecrated</b> only <b>one limb</b> at the outset, and one must invoke two separate expansions to apply inherent sanctity to the whole animal. The question stands: <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ According to Rabbi Yehuda, if one consecrates a limb, the sanctity does not extend to the entire animal. A Sage <b>posed a dilemma to Rava:</b> If one <b>consecrated one limb, what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>with regard to shearing</b> the wool of that limb? Rava replied: <b>You</b> can <b>resolve</b> the dilemma <b>from that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states: <b>“Nor shear the firstborn of your flock”</b> (Deuteronomy 15:19). This verse indicates that the prohibition against shearing applies only to an animal belonging entirely to you, <b>but you may shear</b> the wool <b>of</b> a firstborn that <b>belongs</b> both <b>to you and to others,</b> i.e., gentiles. Here too, since the animal is jointly owned by the Temple and by the one who consecrated it, the prohibition against shearing should not apply at all.
The Sage responded that this is not a proof: <b>There,</b> a firstborn animal owned partly by a gentile <b>was not imbued with sanctity at all,</b> whereas <b>here it was</b> at least partly <b>imbued with sanctity,</b> and therefore it is possible that the limb is prohibited with respect to shearing. The Gemara cites <b>another version</b> of this answer: <b>There,</b> in the case of a firstborn animal owned partly by a gentile, <b>it is not in</b> the Jew’s <b>power to consecrate</b> his share. Therefore, the prohibition against shearing does not apply. But <b>here it is in his power to consecrate</b> one limb, and sanctity applies to it. Therefore, one can claim that the limb should be prohibited with respect to shearing.
<b>Abaye posed a dilemma to Rava:</b> If one <b>consecrated</b> only <b>the skin of</b> an animal, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> as <b>to</b> whether it may be used <b>for labor?</b> Rava responded: <b>Come</b> and <b>hear</b> a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who says: That which is in the womb of this</b> animal <b>is a burnt offering,</b> it is <b>permitted</b> for the mother <b>to be shorn but</b> it <b>is prohibited</b> to use the animal <b>for labor, due to</b> the inevitable <b>weakening of the fetus inside it.</b> The same should apply to an animal whose skin alone was consecrated, as performing labor with the animal would reduce the value of its skin.
Abaye <b>said to</b> Rava: <b>When</b> that <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches</b> that it <b>is prohibited</b> to use the mother <b>for labor,</b> this applies <b>by rabbinic law.</b> I was asking whether it is prohibited by Torah law. Rava responded: <b>If so,</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring to rabbinic law, then <b>even shearing it should be prohibited.</b> Abaye <b>said to him: With regard to labor, which weakens</b> the animal, <b>the Sages issued a decree</b> rendering it prohibited; <b>with regard to shearing,</b> which does not weaken the animal, <b>the Sages did not issue a decree</b> rendering it prohibited.
<b>Abaye posed a dilemma to Rav Yosef:</b> If one consecrates a pregnant animal as a peace offering but excludes its fetus, and as a result <b>it is</b> sanctified as <b>a peace offering and its offspring is non-sacred, and he slaughtered</b> the pregnant animal <b>inside</b> the Temple courtyard as appropriate for a peace offering, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Or, if one consecrates an animal and it subsequently becomes pregnant, then <b>according to the one who said</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are sanctified</b> only <b>when they come into being,</b> i.e., at the time of their birth, <b>are</b> offspring such as this subject to the prohibition against slaughtering <b>non-sacred</b> animals <b>in the</b> Temple <b>courtyard,</b> since they are not yet born? <b>Or</b> are they <b>not</b> subject to the prohibition, since they themselves were not slaughtered?

Daf 12a

Rav Yosef <b>said to</b> Abaye: Slaughtering a pregnant peace offering containing a non-sacred fetus is not subject to the prohibition against slaughtering non-sacred animals in the Temple courtyard. <b>Do we read</b> the verse: <b>“If the place</b> which the Lord your God shall choose to put His name there <b>be too far from you, then you shall slaughter</b> of your herd and of your flock” (Deuteronomy 12:21), as applying <b>to</b> such a case? This verse prohibits the slaughter of non-sacred animals in the Temple, but only those that could be slaughtered elsewhere. But here, he had no choice but to slaughter the pregnant mother in the Temple, since it was a peace offering.
<b>Abaye posed</b> the opposite <b>dilemma to Rav Yosef:</b> According to the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan that fetuses can be consecrated, if one consecrated the fetus, but not its mother, as a peace offering, such that <b>it,</b> the mother, is <b>non-sacred, and its offspring is a peace offering, and he slaughtered it outside</b> the Temple courtyard as a non-sacred animal, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? <b>Is one liable for it due to</b> the prohibition against <b>slaughtering</b> offerings <b>outside</b> the Temple courtyard, <b>or not?</b>
Rav Yosef <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>Do we read</b> the verse: <b>“That they may bring them to the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 17:5), as applying <b>to</b> this case? The full passage reads: “Any man of the house of Israel who shall slaughter a bull, or a lamb, or a goat…and has not brought it to the entrance to the Tent of Meeting…that man shall be cut off from among his people. To the end that the children of Israel may bring their sacrifices, which they sacrifice in the open field, even that they may bring them to the Lord, to the door of the Tent of Meeting” (Leviticus 17:3–5). The passage indicates that the prohibition against slaughtering offerings outside the Temple applies only to those offerings that are fit to be slaughtered inside. But this fetus is not fit to be slaughtered at all until it is born.
The Gemara cites <b>another version</b> of this answer. Rav Yosef <b>said to</b> Abaye: The verse states: “And has not brought it to the entrance to the Tent of Meeting.” This teaches that <b>one is liable for</b> slaughtering an offering <b>outside</b> the Temple only if it is <b>fit to</b> come through <b>the entrance to the Tent of Meeting,</b> i.e., to be sacrificed in the Temple. This fetus is not yet fit.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If <i>teruma</i>, the portion of the produce designated for the priest, was intermingled with non-sacred produce, and it is impossible to distinguish between them, if the ratio of non-sacred produce to <i>teruma</i> was less than one hundred to one, the <i>teruma</i> is not nullified and all the produce is forbidden to those for whom <i>teruma</i> is forbidden. If the mixture was then intermingled with other non-sacred produce, that <b>mixture renders</b> it <b>a mixture</b> of <i>teruma</i> <b>only according to</b> the <b>calculation</b> of the percentage of the original <i>teruma</i> produce in the entire mixture.
<b>And</b> dough <b>that was leavened</b> with a <i>teruma</i> leavening agent is forbidden to those for whom <i>teruma</i> is forbidden even if the ratio between the non-sacred and the <i>teruma</i> is greater than one hundred to one. If a portion of that dough was intermingled with non-sacred dough, it <b>leavens only according to</b> the <b>calculation</b> of the percentage of the original leavening agent in the entire dough, and the second dough is forbidden only if the quantity of the original <i>teruma</i> leavening agent inside it is sufficient to leaven it. <b>And</b> if three <i>log</i> of drawn water were poured into a ritual bath with less than forty <i>se’a</i> to complete the requisite forty <i>se’a</i>, the ritual bath is invalidated. But <b>drawn water invalidates the ritual bath only according to calculation,</b> as explained in the Gemara.
<b>And the water of purification</b> of the red heifer <b>becomes water of purification only with</b> the <b>placement of</b> the <b>ashes</b> into the water, but not by placement of water onto the ashes. <b>And</b> one <b><i>beit haperas</i> does not create</b> another <b><i>beit haperas</i>.</b> The Sages decreed ritual impurity on a field in which a grave was plowed, scattering the bones throughout the field. This field is called a <i>beit haperas</i>. That impurity extends to the area of one hundred cubits surrounding the grave. Nevertheless, they did not decree impurity on the second field if one plowed from that field into another field. <b>And there is no <i>teruma</i> after <i>teruma</i>.</b> Once one designates produce from his crop as <i>teruma</i>, if he then designates additional produce from that crop as <i>teruma</i>, it is not <i>teruma</i>.
<b>And a substitute</b> animal that was consecrated when it was substituted for a consecrated animal <b>does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute;</b> rather, it remains non-sacred. <b>And the offspring</b> born of a consecrated animal that was not consecrated itself <b>does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute. Rabbi Yehuda says: The offspring renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> The Sages <b>said to him: A consecrated</b> animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute, but the offspring does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that if a mixture of <i>teruma</i> produce and non-sacred produce was intermingled with other non-sacred produce, that mixture is prohibited only according to the calculation of the percentage of the original <i>teruma</i> produce in the entire mixture. The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> whose opinion is reflected in this ruling? <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> This ruling is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 5:6): If <b>a <i>se’a</i> of <i>teruma</i> fell into less than one hundred</b> <i>se’a</i> of <b>non-sacred</b> produce and thereby caused it to <b>become</b> a prohibited <b>mixture,</b> since the amount of <i>teruma</i> is too great to be nullified by the non-sacred produce, <b>and</b> a <i>se’a</i> <b>from the mixture</b> subsequently <b>fell into a different place</b> with non-sacred produce, <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> The <i>se’a</i> from the original mixture <b>renders it</b> a prohibited <b>mixture as definite <i>teruma</i></b> would, in the same ratio, since I say: It is possible that the same <i>se’a</i> of <i>teruma</i> that fell into the first mixture was not mixed evenly throughout, and it all came out of it intact and fell into the second mixture. Therefore, it requires nullification as if it were unadulterated <i>teruma</i>.
<b>And the Rabbis say:</b> The <i>se’a</i> from the original prohibited mixture <b>renders</b> the derivative <b>mixture</b> prohibited <b>only according to</b> the <b>calculation</b> of actual <i>teruma</i> <b>in</b> the entire mixture, calculated as if the <i>teruma</i> were evenly distributed throughout the first mixture. The mishna evidently follows the opinion of the Rabbis.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And</b> if a portion of dough <b>that was leavened</b> with a <i>teruma</i> leavening agent was intermingled with non-sacred dough, it <b>leavens only</b> according to the calculation. <b>Rabbi Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer.</b>
<b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Orla</i> 2:11): In a case of <b>non-sacred leaven and</b> leaven <b>of <i>teruma</i> that fell</b> together <b>into</b> a non-sacred batch of <b>dough,</b> and <b>this one</b> alone was <b>not</b> potent enough <b>to cause</b> the dough <b>to become leavened, and that one</b> alone was <b>not</b> potent enough <b>to cause</b> the dough <b>to become leavened, and they combined and caused</b> the dough <b>to become leavened, Rabbi Eliezer says: I follow the final</b> element to fall into the dough. If the <i>teruma</i> fell in last, the dough is prohibited to non-priests. <b>And the Rabbis say: Whether the forbidden item,</b> i.e., the <i>teruma</i>, <b>fell</b> in <b>first or the forbidden item fell</b> in <b>last, it is prohibited only if there is enough of</b> the prohibited leaven itself <b>to cause</b> the dough <b>to leaven.</b>
§ The mishna further teaches: <b>And drawn water invalidates the ritual bath only</b> according to calculation. The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> whose opinion is reflected in this ruling? <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: It is Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov, as we learned</b> in a <i>baraita</i> (<i>Tosefta</i>, <i>Mikvaot</i> 4:3) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov says:</b> If <b>a ritual bath contains twenty-one <i>se’a</i> of rainwater,</b> the majority of a valid ritual bath of forty <i>se’a</i>, what can one do to render it valid? <b>One fills</b> drawn water <b>in</b> a bucket carried on <b>the shoulder</b> in the amount of <b>nineteen <i>se’a</i>,</b> and with it fills a pit adjacent to the ritual bath, <b>and</b> one <b>lets</b> the water <b>flow [<i>ufotekan</i>]</b> through a passage from the pit <b>into the ritual bath,</b>

Daf 12b

<b>and</b> in this manner all forty <i>se’a</i> <b>are ritually pure.</b> The reason is <b>that drawn</b> water <b>is purified by a majority</b> of fit water that was already present in the ritual bath <b>and by</b> the drawn water <b>flowing</b> into the ritual bath. This is the meaning of the mishna’s statement that drawn water invalidates the ritual bath only according to calculation: One calculates the amount of drawn water that flowed into the ritual bath, as the ritual bath is invalid only if most of the forty <i>se’a</i> is of that drawn water.
The Gemara asks: If this is the opinion of Rabbi Eliezer ben Ya’akov alone, one must conclude <b>by inference that the Rabbis hold that</b> the ritual bath is <b>not</b> fit even <b>by</b> a <b>majority</b> of fit water <b>and by</b> the <b>flowing</b> of the drawn water. <b>But</b> if so, consider <b>that</b> which <b>Ravin</b> said <b>when</b> he <b>came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, namely, that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> A ritual bath that consists <b>in its entirety</b> of <b>drawn</b> water <b>that one made flow</b> into it <b>is pure.</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is this? It is <b>not</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that drawn water invalidates the ritual bath even if it flowed into it, <b>and</b> it is <b>not</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> ben Ya’akov, who holds that drawn water invalidates the ritual bath if it constitutes a majority of the forty <i>se’a</i>.
<b>Rather, Rabba says:</b> The mishna’s ruling, that drawn water invalidates the ritual bath only according to calculation, is not referring to drawn water that flowed. Instead, it means <b>according to</b> the <b>calculation of</b> the number of <b>vessels</b> from which the drawn water was directly poured into the ritual bath, <b>and</b> this <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Yosef ben Ḥoni.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>three <i>log</i> of drawn water fell into the water</b> of a ritual bath that lacked the requisite measure, <b>from two or three vessels,</b> with each containing at least one <i>log</i> of drawn water, <b>or even from four or five vessels,</b> where no whole <i>log</i> fell in at once, it <b>invalidates the ritual bath. Yosef ben Ḥoni says:</b> It is only if the drawn water was <b>in two or three vessels</b> that the water <b>invalidates the ritual bath.</b> But if the drawn water was <b>in four or five</b> vessels, the water <b>does not invalidate the ritual bath.</b> This is what the mishna means.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the water of purification</b> of the red heifer <b>becomes water of purification only</b> with the placement of the ashes into the water, but not by the placement of water onto the ashes. The Gemara asks: <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> whose opinion is reflected in this ruling? <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said that</b> it is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: A <i>sota</i>, a woman suspected of unfaithfulness by her husband, must drink bitter waters prepared in the Temple. The verse states: “And the priest shall take holy water in an earthen vessel; and of the dust that is on the floor of the Tabernacle the priest shall take and put it into the water” (Numbers 5:17). This verse teaches that the water must be placed in the vessel first, and the dust is placed on top of it. If one places the <b>dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water,</b> the mixture is <b>unfit, but Rabbi Shimon deems</b> it <b>fit.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reasoning of Rabbi Shimon? As it is written</b> with regard to the red heifer: <b>“And for the impure they shall take of the dust of the burning of the purification from sin, and place on it flowing water</b> in a vessel” (Numbers 19:17). <b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon said: But is it dust [<i>afar</i>]</b> that is taken? <b>Isn’t it</b> really <b>ashes [<i>efer</i>]?</b>
Evidently <b>the verse altered its</b> standard <b>usage</b> and referred to ashes as dust in order <b>to derive a verbal analogy from it: “Dust” is stated</b> in the verse <b>here, and “dust” is stated there,</b> with regard to the <i>sota</i>. <b>Just as there,</b> in the case of the <i>sota</i>, the verse teaches that the <b>dust</b> must be placed <b>on top of the water, so too here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, the <b>dust,</b> i.e., ashes, must be placed <b>on top of the water. And</b> likewise, <b>just as here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, if one places the <b>dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water,</b> it is <b>fit</b> after the fact, <b>so too there,</b> in the case of the <i>sota</i>, if one places the <b>dust</b> in the vessel <b>before the water,</b> it is <b>fit</b> after the fact.
The Gemara asks: <b>And here,</b> with regard to the red heifer, <b>from where do we</b> derive that the mixture is fit even if the dust is placed first? <b>Two phrases are written.</b> First it is written: “They shall take of the dust of the burning of the purification from sin <b>and place on it.” Apparently,</b> the <b>ashes</b> should be placed in the vessel <b>first</b> and the water afterward. <b>And then it is written: “Running water in a vessel,”</b> indicating that the water should be placed in the vessel first, while it is still empty. <b>How</b> can <b>these</b> texts be reconciled? They can be reconciled by concluding that if <b>he desires</b> to place the <b>dust,</b> i.e., the ashes of the red heifer, <b>below,</b> and put water on top, he may do so; and if <b>he desires</b> he may place the water first and then place the <b>dust above</b> the water.
<b>And the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna, who deems the mixture unfit if the ashes are placed first, <b>what is</b> his <b>reasoning?</b> He could <b>say to you</b> that <b>the last clause of the verse:</b> “Running water in a vessel,” is meant <b>specifically,</b> i.e., the water must be placed first. And when the verse states beforehand: “They shall take of the dust of the burning of the purification from sin <b>and place on it,”</b> this teaches only that after placing the ashes upon the water one is required <b>to mix</b> the ashes with the water, so that the water covers the ashes.
The Gemara objects: But one could just as easily say the opposite. <b>What did you see that</b> led <b>you</b> to <b>say</b> that <b>the last clause of the verse</b> is meant <b>specifically? Perhaps the first clause</b> of the verse is meant <b>specifically.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>You cannot say</b> so, as <b>just as we find in every instance</b> that the <b>facilitating item</b> goes <b>above,</b> e.g., in the case of a <i>sota</i> the dust, which enables the water to be used, goes on top of the water, <b>so too here,</b> in the case of the red heifer, the <b>facilitating item</b> must go <b>above.</b> The water must be placed first, and only then the ashes.
§ The mishna further teaches: <b>And</b> one <b><i>beit haperas</i> does not create</b> another <b><i>beit haperas</i>.</b> The Gemara comments: <b>The mishna is not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Eliezer, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Oholot</i> 17:2) that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> One <b><i>beit haperas</i> creates</b> another <b><i>beit haperas</i>.</b> If one plowed from a <i>beit haperas</i> into another field, that field is also ritually impure.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>the Rabbis,</b> who disagree with Rabbi Eliezer, <b>how far</b> does a <i>beit haperas</i> extend? <b>When Rav Dimi came</b> from Eretz Yisrael to Babylonia, he said that <b>Reish Lakish said</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon bar Abba said:</b>

Daf 13a

A <i>beit haperas</i> extends over <b>three fields,</b> the field that was plowed and the two adjacent fields in the direction it was plowed. For example, if one plowed the field from north to south, each of the fields adjacent to it on the north and south is also considered a <i>beit haperas</i>. <b>But</b> whereas the plowed field is a <i>beit haperas</i> in its entirety, the two adjacent fields are a <i>beit haperas</i> only to the extent of <b>two furrows,</b> one furrow on each side. <b>And how much</b> is the <b>full</b> length of <b>a furrow [<i>ma’ana</i>]?</b> It is <b>one hundred cubits, as it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Oholot</i> 17:1): <b>One who plows</b> a field containing <b>a grave,</b> and who may have strewn the bones throughout the field, <b>renders</b> the field <b>a <i>beit haperas</i></b> up to <b>the full</b> length of <b>a furrow,</b> which is <b>one hundred cubits.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And there is no <i>teruma</i> after <i>teruma</i>.</b> Once one designates produce from his crop as <i>teruma</i>, if he then designates additional produce from that crop as <i>teruma</i>, it is not <i>teruma</i>. The Gemara explains: <b>Whose</b> opinion is expressed in <b>the mishna? It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva, as we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Terumot</i> 3:3): In the case of <b>joint</b> owners of produce <b>who separated <i>teruma</i> one after the other, Rabbi Eliezer says: The <i>teruma</i> of both of them is <i>teruma</i>,</b> as each is considered to have separated from his share of the produce.
<b>Rabbi Akiva says: The <i>teruma</i> of neither of them is <i>teruma</i>.</b> Since each separated <i>teruma</i> independently, it is clear that neither accepts the separation of the other, and therefore neither is valid. <b>And the Rabbis say: If the first one separated <i>teruma</i> of</b> the correct <b>measure,</b> i.e., one-fiftieth of the produce, the produce is thereby tithed, and therefore <b>the <i>teruma</i> of the second is not <i>teruma</i>; but if</b> the first <b>did not separate <i>teruma</i> of</b> the correct <b>measure,</b> and he separated too little, <b>the <i>teruma</i> of the second is <i>teruma</i>.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And a substitute</b> animal that was consecrated when it was substituted for a consecrated animal <b>does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute;</b> rather, it remains non-sacred. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason,</b> i.e., the source for this <i>halakha</i> in the Torah? <b>The verse states:</b> “And if he substitutes an animal for an animal, then both it <b>and that for which it is substituted</b> shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:10). The verse teaches that the <i>halakha</i> of substitution applies only to a consecrated animal and its substitute, <b>but not the substitute of its substitute.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: <b>And the offspring</b> born of a consecrated animal that was not consecrated itself <b>does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> The Gemara explains: The reason for this is <b>that the verse states:</b> “Then both <b>it</b> and that for which it is substituted shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:10), which teaches that only <b>it,</b> a consecrated animal, <b>but not</b> its <b>offspring,</b> renders a non-sacred animal exchanged for it a substitute.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Yehuda says: The offspring renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> The Gemara explains that Rabbi Yehuda reasons <b>that</b> when <b>the verse states: “Shall be</b> sacred,” this serves <b>to include the offspring.</b> The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> as for <b>the Rabbis,</b> who hold that one cannot substitute for the offspring of a consecrated animal, they maintain that this phrase serves <b>to include</b> one who substitutes <b>unwittingly,</b> so that the substitution is valid <b>as</b> if he had done so <b>intentionally.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The birds</b> sacrificed as offerings, i.e., doves and pigeons, <b>and the meal offerings do not render</b> non-sacred items exchanged for them <b>substitutes, as only</b> the term <b>“an animal” is stated</b> with regard to substitution, in the verse: “And if he substitutes an animal for an animal” (Leviticus 27:10). A consecrated animal belonging to <b>the community or</b> to <b>partners does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute, as it is stated</b> in the same verse: <b>“He shall neither exchange it nor substitute</b> it.” One derives from the singular pronoun in the verse that <b>an individual renders</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute, but the community and partners do not render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute.</b> Items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance do not render</b> non-sacred items exchanged for them <b>substitutes.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon said:</b> The fact that animals belonging to the community or partners do not render animals exchanged for them substitutes is derived as follows: The <b>animal tithe was</b> included <b>in</b> the <b>category</b> of all offerings, <b>and why was it</b> singled <b>out</b> in the verse: “And all the tithe of the herd or the flock, whatever passes under the rod, the tenth shall be sacred unto the Lord. He shall not inquire whether it is good or bad, neither shall he substitute for it; and if he substitutes it, then both it and that for which it is substituted shall be sacred” (Leviticus 27:32–33)?
Rabbi Shimon explains: It was singled out to juxtapose substitution to the animal tithe, <b>to tell you: Just as</b> the animal <b>tithe</b> is brought exclusively as <b>an individual offering,</b> so too, all offerings that render their substitutes sacred are individual offerings, <b>excluding communal offerings</b> and the offerings of partners from the <i>halakha</i> of substitution. <b>And just as</b> the animal <b>tithe</b> is <b>an offering</b> sacrificed on the <b>altar,</b> so too, all offerings that render their substitutes sacred are offerings sacrificed on the altar, <b>excluding</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of substitution.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> According to the mishna, items consecrated for Temple maintenance do not render non-sacred items exchanged for them substitutes. <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: One <b>might</b> have thought that items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance render</b> non-sacred items exchanged for them <b>substitutes.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And if it is an animal of which men bring <b>an offering</b> to the Lord…he shall not alter it, nor substitute it, good for bad” (Leviticus 27:9–10). This teaches that the <i>halakha</i> of substitution applies to <b>that which is called an offering, excluding</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, which are not called an offering.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> are items consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>not</b> called offerings? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states with regard to sacrificial animals: “Or who slaughters it outside the camp, and has not brought it to the entrance to the Tent of Meeting to present it as an offering to the Lord before the Tabernacle of the Lord” (Leviticus 17:3–4). <b>If</b> the verse had mentioned just the word <b>“offering,” I</b> would <b>derive</b> that the prohibition against slaughtering outside the Temple applies <b>even</b> to items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, as</b> they too <b>are called offerings, as is stated in the matter</b> of the spoils of the war against Midian: <b>“And we have brought the Lord’s offering,</b> what every man has gotten, of jewels of gold, armlets, and bracelets, signet rings, earrings, and girdles, to make atonement for our souls before the Lord” (Numbers 31:50). These were certainly not items consecrated for the altar.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: Therefore <b>the verse states: “And to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting he did not bring it”</b> (Leviticus 17:4), to teach that <b>for any</b> item <b>that</b> is fit to <b>come to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting,</b> i.e., that is fit to be sacrificed, <b>one is liable for it due to</b> the prohibition against <b>slaughtering offerings outside</b> of the Temple courtyard. <b>And</b> by contrast, <b>for any</b> animal <b>that</b> is <b>not</b> fit to <b>come to the entrance of the Tent of Meeting, one is not liable for it due to</b> the prohibition against <b>slaughtering offerings outside</b> of the Temple courtyard. <b>Evidently,</b> as a verse is necessary to exclude items consecrated for Temple maintenance, such items <b>are</b> generally <b>called an offering,</b> contrary to the first <i>baraita</i> cited.
<b>Rabbi Ḥanina said:</b> This is <b>not difficult. This</b> second <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> whereas <b>that</b> first <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis. According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon,</b> items consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>are</b> in fact <b>called an offering,</b> and therefore in the mishna he derives that items consecrated for Temple maintenance are excluded from the <i>halakhot</i> of substitution from the juxtaposition with the animal tithe, not from word “offering.” <b>According to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> items consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>are not called an offering,</b> and they therefore derive the <i>halakha</i> from the word “offering.”
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to the opinion of the Rabbis, are items consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>not</b> called an offering? <b>But isn’t it written: “And we have brought the Lord’s offering”</b> (Numbers 31:50)? The Gemara answers: These items <b>are called: “The Lord’s offering,”</b> but <b>they are not called: “An offering to the Lord,”</b> which is used only with regard to offerings sacrificed upon the altar.
