The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> in which one who acts <b>unwittingly</b> is considered <b>like</b> one who acts <b>intentionally</b> with regard to substitution? <b>Ḥizkiyya said:</b> The circumstances are <b>of</b> one <b>who thinks that it is permitted to substitute</b> one animal for another. <b>With regard to a substitute,</b> the animal becomes consecrated, and the individual is liable to receive <b>lashes</b> for transgressing the Torah’s prohibition, due to the inclusion of “shall be,” despite the fact that he acted unwittingly. But <b>in</b> the equivalent case, where one unwittingly <b>sanctified</b> an animal that was unfit to be an offering, he is <b>not</b> liable to receive <b>lashes,</b> as in this case one is liable to receive lashes only if he acted intentionally and with prior warning.
The Gemara cites <b>an alternative version</b> of Ḥizkiyya’s statement: <b>With regard to</b> a non-sacred animal that was declared <b>a substitute,</b> the animal becomes <b>consecrated,</b> despite the fact that one acted unwittingly. But <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> animals, if it was consecrated unwittingly it is <b>not consecrated.</b>
<b>Reish Lakish and Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> both <b>state</b> a different example of the ruling discussed by Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda. They say that the case is of one <b>who thinks to say:</b> This animal shall be <b>a substitute for a burnt offering</b> that I own, <b>but</b> he unwittingly <b>said:</b> This animal shall be <b>a substitute for a peace offering</b> that I own. In such a case involving substitution he is liable to receive lashes, but if he erred in this manner with regard to consecration, he is exempt from lashes. The Gemara cites <b>an alternative version: With regard to a substitute of a peace offering,</b> if one unwittingly declared it a substitute, it <b>is consecrated. With regard</b> to <b>sacrificial</b> animals, if the animal was unwittingly consecrated, it is <b>not consecrated.</b>
The Gemara cites another <b>alternative version</b> of the difference between substitution and consecration of an animal: The case is <b>of</b> one <b>who thinks to say:</b> The first <b>black</b> animal that comes out of my house will be a substitute, <b>but he</b> unwittingly <b>said:</b> The first <b>white</b> animal that comes out of my house will be a substitute. <b>With regard to a substitute,</b> the white animal is consecrated, and the individual therefore is liable to receive <b>lashes</b> for transgressing the Torah’s prohibition, despite the fact that he acted unwittingly. Conversely, <b>in</b> a similar case of <b>sacrificial</b> animals, e.g., one meant to say: The first blemished black animal that comes out of my house shall be consecrated, but he unwittingly said: The first blemished white animal shall be consecrated, if a white animal came out of his house, the animal is not consecrated, and therefore he is <b>not</b> liable to receive <b>lashes.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says</b> another case of unwitting substitution of an animal. This is referring to a case <b>of</b> one who <b>says:</b> Let <b>this</b> animal <b>emerge</b> from its sanctity and become non-sacred, <b>and</b> let <b>that</b> animal <b>enter</b> in its place and become consecrated. This person acted unwittingly, because he mistakenly thought that in this manner the consecrated animal would no longer be sacred. <b>With regard to</b> a similar case involving <b>sacrificial</b> animals, <b>where he</b> has a sacrificial animal that developed a blemish and he incorrectly <b>says:</b> Any animals <b>that have developed a blemish may be eaten without redemption,</b> and he proceeded to eat the animal without first redeeming it, he is <b>not</b> liable to <b>receive lashes.</b> But <b>with regard to a substitute,</b> if he did this he would be liable to <b>receive lashes,</b> as stated above.
<b>Rav Sheshet says</b> yet another example of unwittingly performing substitution: It is referring to a case <b>of</b> one who <b>says: I will enter this house and consecrate</b> an animal intentionally, or: I will enter this house <b>and substitute</b> an animal <b>intentionally, and he entered and substituted or consecrated unintentionally,</b> i.e., he acted absentmindedly. For his transgression <b>with regard to substitution</b> he is liable to <b>receive lashes,</b> and for his transgression <b>with regard to sacrificial</b> animals he is <b>not</b> liable to <b>receive lashes.</b>
§ The mishna taught that <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> An animal crossbred from <b>diverse kinds, and a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> and an animal born by caesarean section, and a <i>tumtum</i> animal, and a hermaphrodite animal, are neither sacred through consecration nor do they sanctify non-sacred animals by means of substitution. <b>Shmuel says</b> in explanation: They <b>are not sanctified by substitution,</b> i.e., if one said that an animal in any of these categories should be a substitute for a sacrificial animal, it does not become sanctified. <b>And</b> the clause that they <b>do not sanctify</b> non-sacred animals means that if one of these types of animal was sanctified and one attempted <b>to render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute</b> for it, that non-sacred animal does not become sanctified.
<b>It is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir said: But since</b> these animals <b>do not become sanctified, how can</b> it even be suggested that <b>they</b> could <b>sanctify</b> another non-sacred animal? <b>Rather, you find</b> that the ruling of Rabbi Elazar applies <b>only when</b> one <b>sanctifies an animal and afterward it became a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> or <b>when</b> one <b>sanctifies a fetus and it emerged</b> from the womb <b>by caesarean section. But</b> with regard to an animal crossbred from <b>diverse kinds, and a <i>tumtum</i>, and a hermaphrodite, you can find</b> this ruling <b>only in</b> a case of <b>the offspring of consecrated</b> female animals, which have sanctity without an act of consecration. <b>And</b> this is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehuda, who said</b> that <b>the offspring</b> of a consecrated animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it consecrated as <b>a substitute.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said: What is the reason</b> for the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar?</b> He holds that the <i>halakhot</i> of these categories of animal is <b>like</b> that of <b>a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal: Just as a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal is not sacrificed</b> as an offering <b>and inherent sanctity does not rest upon it, so too, these</b> animals in the categories of a <i>tereifa</i>, an animal born by caesarean section, a <i>tumtum</i>, or a hermaphrodite <b>may not be sacrificed</b> as offerings <b>and inherent sanctity does not rest upon them.</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said to Rava: But</b> why aren’t animals in these categories compared to <b>a blemished</b> animal, <b>which may not be sacrificed</b> as an offering <b>but</b> nevertheless <b>inherent sanctity</b> does <b>rest upon it?</b> Rava <b>said to</b> Rav Pappa in response: Although <b>a blemished</b> animal is not sacrificed on the altar, another animal of <b>its</b> same <b>type,</b> i.e., an unblemished animal, <b>is sacrificed</b> as an offering. By contrast, an animal born by caesarean section, a <i>tumtum</i>, and a hermaphrodite may never be sacrificed. Rav Pappa said to Rava: <b>If so,</b> what will you say about <b>a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> as other animals <b>of its type are sacrificed.</b> According to your explanation, why doesn’t inherent sanctity rest upon a <i>tereifa</i>?
<b>Rather, Rava</b> retracts his previous answer and instead <b>says:</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of animals in these categories are <b>like</b> those of <b>a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal,</b> in the following manner: <b>Just as</b> the category of <b>a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal is an inherent disqualification,</b> and such an animal cannot be sanctified or render another animal sanctified, <b>so too</b> the same applies to <b>any</b> other category of animal that <b>is an inherent disqualification,</b> such as a <i>tereifa</i>, an animal born by caesarean section, a <i>tumtum</i> animal, and a hermaphrodite. This serves <b>to exclude blemished</b> animals, <b>which are disqualified</b> only because they <b>lack</b> certain limbs.
<b>Rav Adda</b> raised a difficulty with regard to this answer and <b>said to Rava:</b> Is the disqualification of a blemished animal always because it is lacking a limb? <b>But isn’t</b> the phrase: <b>Too long or closed</b> hooves, <b>written in the passage</b> of the Torah addressing blemishes that disqualify an animal; <b>and these are inherent disqualifications.</b> The verse states: “Either a bull or a lamb that has any limb too long or closed hooves, you may offer for a voluntary offering; but for a vow it shall not be accepted” (Leviticus 22:23).
<b>Rather, Rava</b> again retracted his previous answer and <b>said:</b> The explanation of Rabbi Elazar’s opinion is as I explained initially. The <i>halakhot</i> of animals in these categories are <b>like</b> those of <b>a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal: Just as a non-kosher</b> domesticated <b>animal is not</b> sacrificed on the altar and neither are any animals <b>of its type, so too</b> these <i>halakhot</i> apply to <b>any</b> animal that <b>is not</b> sacrificed on the altar and neither are any animals <b>of its type.</b> This serves <b>to exclude a blemished</b> animal, <b>because</b> other animals of <b>its type are</b> sacrificed on the altar.
<b>What</b> did <b>you say</b> to challenge this explanation? You said that in the case of <b>a <i>tereifa</i>, there are</b> other animals <b>of its type</b> that are sacrificed, and if so, why doesn’t inherent sanctity rest upon a <i>tereifa</i>? The answer is that a <i>tereifa</i> <b>is not similar to a blemished animal,</b> as <b>a non-kosher animal is forbidden for consumption, and a <i>tereifa</i> is forbidden for consumption.</b> This serves <b>to exclude a blemished</b> animal, <b>which is permitted for consumption.</b>
§ With regard to a <i>tereifa</i>, <b>Shmuel says:</b> One <b>who consecrates</b> an animal that is <b>a <i>tereifa</i> is required</b> to wait until it develops <b>a permanent blemish,</b> on account <b>of which he may redeem it.</b> The Gemara raises a difficulty with regard to this statement of Shmuel. Since the meat of a <i>tereifa</i> is unfit for consumption and may only be fed to the dogs, why does it require redemption? Should one <b>learn from</b> this that one <b>redeems sacrificial</b> animals in order <b>to feed them to the dogs?</b> But this is contrary to the accepted <i>halakha</i> (see 31a).
<b>Rather, say</b> that the statement of Shmuel was as follows: If one consecrates an animal that is a <i>tereifa</i>, it <b>becomes sanctified</b> until it is time for the animal <b>to die,</b> so that even after its death it must be buried and may not be redeemed. <b>And Rabbi Oshaya says:</b> If one consecrates an animal that is a <i>tereifa</i>, this is <b>like nothing other than</b> one who <b>consecrates mere wood and stones,</b> which cannot become sanctified with inherent sanctity but only for their value, and which therefore can be redeemed. Consequently, a consecrated <i>tereifa</i> animal may be redeemed even if it is going to be fed to the dogs.
The Gemara raises a difficulty against the opinion of Shmuel. <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (31a): With regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals <b>that became <i>tereifa</i>, one may not redeem them</b> and render them non-sacred, <b>because</b> their consumption is forbidden; and <b>one does not redeem sacrificial</b> animals <b>to feed them to</b> the <b>dogs.</b> The Gemara analyzes this ruling: <b>The reason</b> that they may not be redeemed is because <b>they became</b> <i>tereifa</i> after they had already been consecrated. <b>Consequently,</b> if <b>they were</b> already <i>tereifa</i> <b>from the outset,</b> when they were consecrated, one <b>may redeem them.</b> The Gemara answers: <b>Perhaps this <i>tanna</i> holds</b> that <b>anywhere that</b> an animal <b>itself</b> is <b>not fit</b> for sacrifice, <b>inherent sanctity does not rest upon it</b> at all.
The Gemara raises another challenge to Shmuel’s opinion from the mishna. <b>Come</b> and <b>hear,</b> as <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> An animal crossbred from <b>diverse kinds, and a <i>tereifa</i>, and</b> an animal <b>born by caesarean section, and a <i>tumtum</i></b> animal, <b>and a hermaphrodite</b> animal are <b>not sacred</b> through consecration <b>and they do not sanctify</b> non-sacred animals by means of substitution. <b>And Shmuel says</b> in explanation: They <b>are not sacred with regard to substitution,</b> i.e., if one said that any of these animals should be a substitute for a sacrificial animal it does not become sanctified; <b>and</b> they <b>do not sanctify</b> non-sacred animals, i.e., if one of these types of animal was sanctified and one attempted <b>to render</b> a non-sacred animal <b>a substitute</b> for it, that non-sacred animal does not become sanctified.
<b>And it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Meir said: But since they do not become sanctified, how can they sanctify</b> another non-sacred animal? <b>Rather, you find</b> the ruling of Rabbi Elazar applies <b>only when</b> one <b>sanctifies an animal and afterward it becomes a <i>tereifa</i>.</b> The Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>This</b> indicates that if it <b>was a <i>tereifa</i> from the outset,</b> then <b>inherent sanctity does not rest upon it;</b> this ruling contradicts the opinion of Shmuel, who says that such an animal must be buried when it dies.

Daf 17b

The Gemara explains that <b>Shmuel</b> could have <b>said to you: Perhaps this</b> <i>tanna</i> <b>also holds</b> that <b>anywhere that</b> an animal <b>is not fit itself</b> for sacrificing, <b>inherent sanctity does not rest upon it.</b> Shmuel himself holds in accordance with those who disagree with this opinion.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>These are the sacrificial</b> animals for which the halakhic status of <b>their offspring and substitutes</b> is <b>like their own</b> halakhic status: <b>The offspring of peace offerings, and their substitute</b> animals, and even <b>the offspring</b> of their offspring or their substitute animals, <b>and</b> even <b>the offspring of their offspring, until the end of all time [<i>ad sof kol ha’olam</i>]. They are</b> all endowed with the sanctity and halakhic status of <b>peace offerings, and</b> therefore <b>they require placing hands</b> on the head of the animal, <b>and libations, and the waving of the breast and the thigh</b> in order to give them to the priest.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>Since</b> the <i>tanna</i> already <b>taught: The offspring</b> of the offspring or the substitute <b>and the offspring of their offspring, why do I</b> need him to state: <b>Until the end of all time?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>The <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna <b>heard that Rabbi Elazar said</b> in the next mishna that <b>the offspring of a peace offering is not sacrificed as a peace offering,</b> but rather it is sequestered and left to die, <b>and the <i>tanna</i> of our</b> mishna <b>said to him: It is not necessary</b> to state <b>with regard to their offspring that I do not concede</b> to you, as I maintain that it is sacrificed upon the altar as a peace offering, <b>but even</b> with regard to all of the offsprings <b>until the end of time I do not concede to you,</b> as I rule that they are all sacrificed as peace offerings.
§ With regard to the mishna’s statement that the halakhic status of the offspring of peace offerings and their substitutes are like that of the peace offering itself, the Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? The Gemara answers: <b>As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: “And if his offering be a sacrifice of peace offerings: If he offer of the herd, if male if female, he shall offer it without blemish before the Lord” (Leviticus 3:1). Since the verse already states: “If he offer of the herd,” the words “if male if female” are unnecessary. Rather, the word <b>“male”</b> serves <b>to include the offspring</b> of a peace offering as having the the same halakhic status as a peace offering.
The <i>baraita</i> objects: <b>But</b> could this <b>not</b> be derived through an <i>a fortiori</i> <b>inference: If a substitute</b> of a peace offering, <b>which</b> is lenient in that it <b>is not grown from consecrated</b> property, i.e., it is not the offspring of a sacrifical animal, <b>is sacrificed</b> as a peace offering, then with regard to <b>the offspring</b> of a peace offering, <b>which is</b> more stringent since it is <b>grown from consecrated</b> property, <b>is it not logical that it be sacrificed</b> as a peace offering?
The <i>baraita</i> responds that this <i>a fortiori</i> inference may be refuted: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about</b> the halakhic status of <b>a substitute?</b> It is unique in <b>that</b> the <i>halakha</i> of substitution <b>applies to all sacrificial</b> animals that belong to an individual, and therefore the substitute of a peace offering is offered as a peace offering. <b>Shall you say</b> the same <b>with regard to the offspring, which</b> is more lenient in that it <b>does not apply to all sacrificial</b> animals, as some are male and do not give birth, and therefore there is reason to say that it <b>is not sacrificed</b> as a peace offering, <b>since</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to the sanctity of the offspring <b>does not apply to all sacrificial</b> animals? Therefore, <b>the verse states</b> the word <b>“male” to include the offspring,</b> and it states the word <b>“female” to include the substitute</b> of a peace offering, indicating that both have the status of a peace offering.
<b>I have</b> derived <b>only</b> that <b>the substitute of an unblemished</b> peace offering <b>and the offspring of an unblemished</b> peace offering have the status of a peace offering. <b>From where</b> do I derive that <b>the offspring of blemished</b> animals <b>and the substitute of blemished</b> animals which are themselves unblemished have the status of peace offerings? <b>The verse states</b> in a more expanded form: <b>“If male,” to include the offspring of blemished</b> animals, and <b>“if female,” to include the substitute of blemished</b> animals.
The Gemara analyzes the <i>baraita</i>: <b>Rav Safra said to Abaye: But</b> why should <b>I</b> not <b>reverse</b> the interpretations and say that the words “if male” serve to include the substitute of blemished animals, and the words “if female” serve to include the offspring of blemished animals? Abaye responds: It <b>stands to reason</b> that <b>from the</b> same <b>place that the substitute of unblemished</b> animals <b>is included,</b> i.e., from the word “female,” <b>the substitute of blemished</b> animals is also <b>included,</b> i.e., from the expanded form “if female.” Likewise, since the word “male” teaches that the offspring of an unblemished peace offering is included, the offspring of a blemished peace offering is included from the phrase “if male.”
Rav Safra <b>said to</b> Abaye in explanation of his question: <b>Did I say to you to reverse</b> the interpretations of the phrases <b>“if male”</b> and <b>“if female”? I</b> actually <b>said</b> that the interpretation of <b>the entire verse</b> should be reversed as follows: <b>Say</b> that the word <b>“male”</b> serves <b>to include the substitute</b> of both a blemished and an unblemished offering, and the word <b>“female”</b> serves <b>to include the offspring</b> of both a blemished and an unblemished offering. Abaye <b>said to him</b> in response: It is logical to interpret the verse as the <i>baraita</i> does, as the word <b>offspring [<i>valad</i>] indicates a masculine form,</b> while the word <b>substitute [<i>temura</i>] indicates a feminine form.</b>
§ The <i>baraita</i> stated that the offspring of a blemished peace offering has the status of a peace offering. The Gemara asks: <b>For what</b> matter is that <b><i>halakha</i></b> relevant? <b>Shmuel said:</b> It is relevant with regard <b>to sacrificing</b> it on the altar as a peace offering, even though its mother is blemished and disqualified for the altar. <b>And</b> the <i>baraita</i> is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Elazar,</b> who rules that if one sets aside a female animal for a burnt offering, and that animal gave birth to a male, the offspring is offered as a burnt offering, even though its mother may not be offered as a burnt offering (see 18b).
The <i>baraita</i> therefore teaches that the same is true with regard to the offspring of a blemished peace offering. And although Rabbi Elazar already stated this principle, it was necessary for the <i>baraita</i> to repeat it in this case, <b>lest you say</b> that <b>when Rabbi Elazar says</b> his opinion, it is only with regard to <b>a burnt offering,</b> due to the fact <b>that</b> there the discrepancy between mother and offspring is their sex, and <b>there is burnt offering status</b> for females, i.e., the case of a bird that is the same sex <b>as its mother,</b> as one may sacrifice a female bird burnt offering. <b>But</b> perhaps <b>these offspring</b> of a blemished peace offering <b>are not sacrificed,</b> as there is no case of a blemished animal that may be offered as a peace offering. Therefore, it <b>teaches us</b> that even these offspring may be sacrificed.
<b>Bar Padda said:</b> The statement of the <i>baraita</i> that the offspring of a blemished peace offering has the status of a peace offering is relevant only with regard <b>to grazing</b> the animal until it develops a blemish, after which it may be redeemed and the money used to bring a burnt offering. But the animal itself may not be sacrificed on the altar. And the <i>baraita</i> is in accordance with all opinions. <b>As it was stated</b> that the following <i>amora’im</i> disagreed about the same point as did Shmuel and bar Padda: <b>Rava said</b> that the offspring of a blemished offering is consecrated with regard <b>to sacrificing</b> it upon the altar, <b>and</b> this is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar,</b> whereas <b>Rav Pappa said</b> that this <i>halakha</i> applies only with regard <b>to grazing, and</b> this is <b>in accordance with all</b> opinions.
§ In the <i>baraita</i>, the <i>halakha</i> governing the substitute and offspring of a peace offering is derived from the superfluous words “male” and “female.” The Gemara notes: <b>But this <i>tanna</i> cites</b> the source of this <i>halakha</i> <b>from here:</b> “Only the holy things you have, and your vows, you shall take and go to the place which the Lord shall choose; and you shall offer your burnt offerings, the flesh and the blood, upon the altar of the Lord your God; and the blood of your sacrifices shall be poured out against the altar of the Lord your God, and you shall eat the flesh” (Deuteronomy 12:26–27).
With regard to the words: <b>“Only your holy things,” these are the substitutes; “that you have,” these are the offspring.</b> And with regard to these, it states: <b>“You shall take and go</b> to the place which the Lord shall choose,” i.e., they must be brought to the Temple as offerings.
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one should bring them into the Temple but withhold water and food from them so that they die.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And you shall offer your burnt offerings, the flesh and the blood,</b> upon the altar of the Lord your God.” This indicates that <b>in the manner that you treat a burnt offering, you should treat the substitute</b> of a burnt offering, and <b>in the manner that you treat a peace offering, you should treat the offspring of a peace offering and the substitute</b> of a peace offering.
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>this</b> is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals, i.e., that their substitutes and offspring are sacrificed upon the altar. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Only</b> your holy things,” which, as the Gemara will explain shortly, excludes certain offspring from this principle. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yishmael. Rabbi Akiva says:</b> The word “only” <b>is unnecessary,</b> as <b>it states</b> with regard to a guilt offering: <b>“It is a guilt offering; he is certainly guilty before the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 5:19). The term “It is [<i>hu</i>],” indicates that only <b>it,</b> the guilt offering itself, <b>is sacrificed</b> upon the altar, <b>but its substitute is not sacrificed.</b>
§ <b>The Master said</b> above: Due to the phrase in the verse <b>“You shall take and go,”</b> one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>one should bring</b> the substitute and offspring <b>into the Temple</b> but withhold water and food from them so that they die. Therefore, the verse states: “And you shall offer your burnt offerings, the flesh and the blood, upon the altar of the Lord your God.”
The Gemara asks: <b>From where</b> would <b>this</b> be <b>derived,</b> that these animals should be brought into the Temple and then left to die? <b>Since</b> the <i>halakha</i> that <b>five sin offering</b> are left to die <b>is learned</b> through a tradition transmitted to Moses from Sinai, and the offspring of a sin offering and the substitute of a sin offering are included among those five, it would have been reasonable to conclude that only those sin offerings are left to die, but <b>these</b> animals, the offspring of a peace offering and the substitute of a peace offering, <b>are sacrificed</b> upon the altar.
The Gemara answers: The verse is necessary, <b>lest you say</b> that these <b>five sin offerings</b> may be left to <b>die in any location, but these,</b> the offspring and substitute of a peace offering, must be left to <b>die</b> specifically <b>in the Temple.</b> Therefore, the verse <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so, as the offspring and substitute of a peace offering may be offered upon the altar.
§ <b>The master said</b> above: One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>this</b> is the <i>halakha</i> <b>even</b> with regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals that their substitutes and offspring may be sacrificed upon the altar just as the sacrificial animals themselves are. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Only your holy things,”</b> indicating that this principle does not apply equally in all cases. The Gemara asks: <b>The offspring of which</b> offering is referred to here? <b>If</b> it is referring to the offspring <b>of a burnt offering,</b> a burnt offering <b>is male, and is</b> therefore <b>not capable of giving birth. And if</b> it is referring to the offspring or substitute <b>of a sin offering,</b> it <b>is learned</b> as a tradition <b>that</b> such an animal is <b>left to die.</b>

Daf 18a

And <b>if</b> it is referring <b>to</b> the substitute of <b>a guilt offering,</b> it <b>is learned</b> as a tradition <b>that</b> this offering <b>goes</b> out <b>to graze, as</b> in <b>any</b> case <b>where a sin offering</b> is left to <b>die, in</b> the parallel case involving <b>a guilt offering,</b> the animal is left to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish, after which it is redeemed and the money is used <b>to</b> purchase <b>a burnt offering.</b> Therefore, there is no need for a verse to exclude the substitute of a guilt offering. The Gemara explains: <b>Actually,</b> the <i>baraita</i> is referring <b>to</b> the offspring and substitute of <b>a sin offering, and</b> a verse is required to exclude them, despite the <i>halakha</i> that was learned as a tradition in their regard, for this <b><i>halakha</i> was learned</b> only <b>with regard to</b> letting the animal <b>die, and the verse</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> them <b>from being sacrificed</b> on the altar.
The Gemara objects: <b>But this,</b> the <i>halakha</i> of sacrificing the animal upon the altar, <b>depends on that,</b> the <i>halakha</i> of letting it die. <b>Since it goes to its death,</b> it is clearly <b>self-evident</b> that <b>it is not sacrificed.</b> The Gemara offers a different resolution: <b>Rather, the <i>halakha</i></b> transmitted to Moses from Sinai is referring <b>to a sin offering, and the verse</b> serves <b>to exclude</b> the case of <b>a substitute of a guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara asks: But <b>this <i>halakha</i></b> with regard to the substitute of a guilt offering <b>was also learned</b> by the Sages as a tradition transmitted to Moses from Sinai, <b>as they said</b> that in <b>any</b> case <b>where a sin offering</b> is left to <b>die, in</b> the parallel case involving <b>a guilt offering,</b> the animal is left to <b>graze. Rather, the verse is necessary</b> to teach an additional <i>halakha</i>, <b>that if one transgressed and sacrificed</b> the offspring of a sin offering or a guilt offering, or the substitute of a sin offering or a guilt offering, <b>he stands</b> in violation <b>of</b> a prohibition inferred from <b>a positive mitzva.</b> Since the verse teaches that only the offspring of a burnt offering or a peace offering may be offered, it may be inferred that the offspring of a guilt offering or a sin offering may not be offered, and the violation of a prohibition stemming from a command formulated as a positive mitzva is considered the violation of a positive mitzva.
§ The <i>baraita</i> stated that in contrast to Rabbi Yishmael, <b>Rabbi Akiva says</b> that the word “only” <b>is unnecessary</b> to exclude the offspring and substitutes of other sacrificial animals from being sacrificed upon the altar, as the verse that discusses guilt offerings: “It is a guilt offering” (Leviticus 5:19), teaches that only <b>it,</b> the guilt offering itself, <b>is sacrificed, but its substitute is not offered.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>a verse</b> to teach this? <b>Didn’t</b> the Sages <b>learn this <i>halakha</i>,</b> that the substitute of a guilt offering is left to graze, as a tradition transmitted to Moses from Sinai, since they said that in any case where a sin offering is left to die, in the parallel case involving a guilt offering, the animal is not sacrificed but rather left to graze?
The Gemara answers: <b>Yes,</b> it <b>is indeed so,</b> that the verse is unnecessary for that purpose. <b>Rather, why do I</b> need <b>the verse? It is necessary to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> <b>that Rav Huna</b> stated, <b>as Rav Huna said:</b> With regard to <b>a guilt offering that was consigned to grazing,</b> i.e., it had been ruled that the animal may not be sacrificed as a guilt offering, it must be left to graze until it develops a blemish, at which point it is sold and the proceeds used for voluntary burnt offerings. An example of this is where the owner had already fulfilled his obligation with the sacrifice of a different animal. If the owner nevertheless transgressed <b>and slaughtered it</b> before it developed a blemish as <b>an unspecified</b> offering, <b>it is valid</b> and sacrificed <b>for the sake of a burnt offering.</b>
The Gemara infers: If the animal was <b>consigned</b> to grazing, <b>yes,</b> it is valid as a burnt offering, but if it was <b>not consigned</b> to grazing, <b>no,</b> it is disqualified as a burnt offering. <b>What is the reason?</b> It is that the verse states with regard to a guilt offering: <b>“It is</b> a guilt offering,” meaning that <b>it shall remain in its present</b> state of a guilt offering.
§ The Gemara previously (17b) cited two <i>baraitot</i> that stated that the offspring of a peace offering has the status of a peace offering. According to one, this is derived from the phrase in the verse in Leviticus “if male if female,” while according to the other it is derived from the phrase in the verse in Deuteronomy “You shall take and go.” The Gemara objects: <b>And according to this <i>tanna</i></b> of the second <i>baraita</i>, <b>who derives</b> this <i>halakha</i> <b>from these verses:</b> “You shall take and go,” and: “And you shall offer your burnt offerings, the flesh and the blood,” <b>let him derive</b> it <b>from</b> the words <b>“male” and “female.”</b> The Gemara explains: <b>He requires that</b> verse <b>to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> of the <b>offspring of a blemished</b> animal and <b>to</b> teach the <i>halakha</i> of <b>the substitute of a blemished</b> animal.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> let <b>him derive all of</b> the <i>halakhot</i>, that of the offspring and the substitute of an unblemished peace offering as well as that of the offspring and the substitute of a blemished offering, <b>from this verse:</b> “If male if female,” in the same manner that the <i>tanna</i> of the first <i>baraita</i> derived them from that verse. The Gemara answers: <b>He does not learn</b> anything from the word <b>“if,”</b> which is a common term and is not considered superfluous.
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to this <i>tanna</i></b> of the first <i>baraita</i>, <b>who derives</b> all of the <i>halakhot</i> <b>from</b> the words “if <b>male if female,” what does he do with</b> the phrase in the verse <b>“You shall take and go”?</b> The Gemara answers: That verse is necessary to teach that one must bring his offerings to the Temple when he ascends to Jerusalem for the Festival <b>even</b> if he must take them <b>from their</b> place of <b>grazing,</b> and he should not delay bringing them until the following Festival. <b>Another version</b> of this answer is that the verse is necessary to teach that one must bring the animals he intends to consecrate even if he must take them <b>from their</b> place of <b>threshing,</b> i.e., even if they are currently being used to thresh, rather than delay bringing the animals to Jerusalem until a later stage.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> Although the previous mishna stated plainly that the offspring of a peace offering is itself sacrificed as a peace offering, its status is actually subject to a dispute between the <i>tanna’im</i>. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: The offspring of a peace offering is not sacrificed</b> on the altar as <b>a peace offering;</b> rather it is sequestered and left to die. <b>And the Rabbis say: It is sacrificed</b> as a peace offering.
<b>Rabbi Shimon said:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis <b>do not disagree</b> with regard to the status <b>of the offspring of the offspring of a peace offering or</b> with regard to the status <b>of the offspring of the offspring of the substitute</b> of a peace offering. In those cases, they all agree <b>that</b> the animal <b>is not sacrificed</b> on the altar as a peace offering. <b>With regard to what</b> case <b>do they disagree?</b> They disagree about the case <b>of the offspring</b> of a peace offering itself. <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: It is not sacrificed</b> as a peace offering, <b>whereas the Rabbis say: It is sacrificed.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua and Rabbi Pappeyas testified about the offspring of a peace offering that it is sacrificed</b> as <b>a peace offering. Rabbi Pappeyas said: I testify that we</b> ourselves <b>had a cow</b> that was <b>a peace offering, and we ate it on Passover, and we ate its offspring</b> as <b>a peace offering on</b> a different <b>Festival.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Ami said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: What is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> that the offspring of a peace offering does not have the status of a peace offering? It is that <b>the verse states</b> with regard to a peace offering: <b>“And if his offering be a sacrifice of peace offerings for the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 3:1). The term <b>“and if [<i>ve’im</i>],”</b> can be read as: And a mother [<i>ve’em</i>], which teaches that the mother may be offered as a peace offering, <b>but not the offspring.</b>
<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said to Rabbi Ami: If that is so,</b> then with regard to the verse: <b>“If [<i>im</i>] he offer it for a thanksgiving”</b> (Leviticus 7:12), <b>so too</b> one should interpret it to mean <b>that</b> one may sacrifice <b>the mother [<i>em</i>] but not the offspring</b> as a thanks offering. <b>And if you would say</b> that Rabbi Eliezer <b>indeed</b> holds that the offspring of a thanks offering is not sacrificed as a thanks offering, <b>isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> the following <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>the offspring</b> of a thanks offering <b>and its substitute and its replacement,</b> if the original animal was lost and then found again, rendering both animals suitable for an offering: <b>From where</b> is it derived that all these are sacrificed as thanks offerings? <b>The verse states: “If for a thanksgiving,”</b> which indicates that they may be offered as a thanks offering <b>in any case.</b>
<b>Rather, Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said: This is the reason of Rabbi Eliezer,</b> that the offspring of a peace offering is not sacrificed as a peace offering: It is a rabbinic <b>decree</b> that it may not be sacrificed, <b>lest</b> the owner delay sacrificing the peace offerings that he is obligated to bring so that he may <b>raise</b> entire <b>herds from them</b> in order to sell the animals to people who need peace offerings.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>Rabbi Shimon said:</b> Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis <b>do not disgree</b> with regard to the status of the offspring of the offspring of a peace offering, or with regard to the status of the offspring of the offspring of the substitute of a peace offering. In those cases, they all agree that the animal is not sacrificed on the altar as a peace offering. With regard to what case do they disagree? They disagree only about the offspring of a peace offering itself.
<b>A dilemma was raised before</b> the Sages: <b>How</b> precisely <b>is</b> the mishna <b>taught?</b> Is it saying that Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis <b>do not disagree</b> whether the offspring of the offspring are sacrificed or <b>they are not sacrificed,</b> but <b>rather</b> they all agree, even Rabbi Eliezer, that they are <b>sacrificed? Or perhaps</b> it is saying that <b>they do not disagree</b> whether the offspring of the offspring <b>are sacrificed</b> or they are not sacrificed, but <b>rather</b> they all agree, even the Sages, that they are <b>not sacrificed.</b>
<b>Rabba said:</b> It <b>stands to reason</b> that the mishna is saying that <b>they do not disagree whether</b> the offspring of the offspring <b>are not sacrificed,</b> but <b>rather</b> all agree that they are <b>sacrificed. What is the reason?</b> It is that <b>Rabbi Eliezer disagrees with the Rabbis only with regard to the offspring, but</b> with regard to the <b>offspring of the offspring,</b> Rabbi Eliezer does not render it prohibited to sacrifice them, as <b>it is merely a chance,</b> i.e., an uncommon occurrence, that one delays sacrificing an offering to the point that the offspring of its offspring have already been born.
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi says</b> that the correct reading of the mishna is: <b>They do not disagree whether</b> the offspring of the offspring <b>are sacrificed,</b> but <b>rather</b> all agree that they <b>are not sacrificed. What is the reason?</b> It is that <b>the Rabbis disagree with Rabbi Eliezer only with regard to the offspring, but</b> with regard to the <b>offspring of the offspring, from</b> the result of his <b>actions</b> with regard to <b>it,</b> i.e., that he delayed sacrificing the original offering for such a long period of time, <b>his intention is evident that he wants to raise</b> herds from <b>it.</b> Therefore, the <i>halakha</i> is that the offspring of the offspring may not be sacrificed, in order to deter him from the outset from raising herds.

Daf 18b

§ <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya teaches</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>to support</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi,</b> that all agree that the offspring of the offspring of a peace offering is not sacrificed: The verse states: <b>“If he sacrifices a lamb</b> for his offering, then shall he present it before the Lord” (Leviticus 3:7). Two limitations are derived from this verse: The word “lamb” indicates that <b>the first offspring,</b> i.e., the offspring of the peace offering, <b>is sacrificed,</b> but <b>the second offspring,</b> i.e., the offspring of the offspring, <b>is not sacrificed.</b>
In addition, the word <b>“he [<i>hu</i>]”</b> is interpreted as referring to the offspring of the peace offering rather than the owner, as it is obvious from the context that the owner is sacrificing the animal and the word would be unnecessary if written only for that purpose. Therefore, it teaches that the offspring of a peace offering <b>is sacrificed, but the offspring of all</b> other <b>sacrificial</b> animals <b>is not sacrificed.</b> This indicates that even those Sages who rule that the offspring of a peace offering is sacrificed agree that the offspring of the offspring is not sacrificed.
The Gemara clarifies the second limitation, that the offspring of other sacrificial animals is not sacrificed: <b>The offspring of which</b> type of offering is referred to here? <b>If</b> it is referring to that <b>of a burnt offering or guilt offering, they are male and</b> therefore <b>not capable of</b> giving birth to <b>offspring. If</b> it is referring to the offspring <b>of a sin offering,</b> the verse is unnecessary, as the Sages <b>learned this <i>halakha</i></b> as a tradition <b>that it is left to die</b> and may not be offered.
<b>Ravina said:</b> The verse serves <b>to include,</b> in the limitation that the offspring of other sacrificial animals are not sacrificed, <b>the offspring of</b> an animal that was consecrated as animal <b>tithe</b> and then gave birth. The Gemara asks: <b>Why do I</b> need <b>a verse</b> to include <b>the offspring of</b> the animal <b>tithe? It is derived through</b> a verbal analogy between the word <b>passing [<i>avara</i>]</b> mentioned with regard to animal tithe, and the word <b>passing [<i>avara</i>]</b> mentioned with regard to <b>a firstborn</b> animal, which is male and cannot give birth to offspring. With regard to animal tithe the verse states: “And all the tithe of the herd or the flock, whatsoever passes [<i>ya’avor</i>] under the rod, the tenth shall be holy unto the Lord” (Leviticus 27:32), and with regard to a firstborn, the verse states: “And you shall set apart [<i>veha’avarta</i>] for the Lord all that opens the womb; every firstborn that is a male, which you have that comes of an animal, shall be the Lord’s” (Exodus 13:12).
The Gemara answers: This verse is <b>necessary,</b> as it might <b>enter your mind to say</b> that <b>one does not derive the possible from the impossible,</b> as in this case, where one seeks to derive by way of analogy the <i>halakha</i> of the offspring of animal tithe from that of a firstborn, which is a male and therefore cannot give birth. Therefore, the verse <b>teaches us</b> that the offspring of animal tithe is not sacrificed on the altar as an offering.
§ The mishna teaches: <b>Rabbi Shimon and Rabbi Pappeyas testified</b> about the offspring of a peace offering that it is sacrificed as a peace offering. Rabbi Pappeyas added the testimony that he had a cow that was eaten as a peace offering on Passover, and its offspring was eaten as a peace offering on a different Festival [<i>beḥag</i>].
Since the term <i>ḥag</i> generally refers to <i>Sukkot</i>, the Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Rava, who said</b> with regard to <b>sacrificial</b> animals that <b>once one pilgrimage Festival has passed</b> from when they were consecrated, and the owner has not yet brought them to the Temple, <b>each day he transgresses with regard to them</b> the prohibition <b>of: You shall not delay,</b> which is derived from the verse that states: “When you shall vow a vow to the Lord your God, you shall not delay to pay it” (Deuteronomy 23:22), already <b>from <i>Shavuot</i> he must eat</b> the offspring of the peace offering that was sacrificed on Passover. Why then did they wait until <i>Sukkot</i>? <b>Rav Zevid said in the name of Rava:</b> It is referring to a case where the owner was unable to sacrifice the offspring of the peace offering on <i>Shavuot</i> due to circumstances beyond his control, <b>for example,</b> if the animal <b>was ill on <i>Shavuot</i></b> [<b><i>ba’Atzeret</i></b>]<b>.</b>
<b>Rav Ashi said</b> an alternative explanation: <b>What</b> is the meaning of the word <b><i>ḥag</i> that is taught</b> in the mishna in the testimony of Rabbi Pappeyas? It is referring to <b>the festival of <i>Shavuot</i>,</b> not <i>Sukkot</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>And the other</b> <i>amora</i>, Rav Zevid, why didn’t he explain the mishna in this manner? The Gemara answers: <b>Wherever</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaches</b> the word <b><i>Pesaḥ</i></b> in a mishna, and <b>he</b> wishes also to <b>teach</b> something about <i>Shavuot</i>, he uses the term <b><i>atzeret</i></b> rather than the word <i>ḥag</i>.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> that the offspring of the peace offering was not sacrificed on <i>Shavuot</i> due to illness, as suggested by Rava, or that it was indeed sacrificed on <i>Shavuot</i>, as proposed by Rav Ashi, <b>what</b> is the purpose of <b>the testimony</b> of Rabbi Pappeyas? He certainly cannot be excluding the opinion shared by Rava, as might have been indicated by a straightforward reading of the mishna. The Gemara answers: His testimony serves <b>to exclude</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Eliezer, who said</b> that <b>the offspring of a peace offering is not sacrificed as a peace offering.</b> Rabbi Pappeyas therefore <b>testifies that</b> the offspring of a peace offering <b>is sacrificed</b> as a peace offering, and that this was the actual practice in the Temple.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The offspring of a thanks offering and the substitute</b> of a thanks offering, and <b>the offspring</b> of the offspring and its substitute, <b>and the offspring of their offspring until the end of all time, they are</b> all <b>like thanks offerings,</b> with the <b>only</b> difference being <b>that they do not require</b> the accompanying <b>loaves,</b> unlike the thanks offering itself.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>From where is this matter</b> derived, that the offspring and substitute of a thanks offering do not require the accompanying loaves? The Gemara answers: <b>As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, with regard to the verse: “If he offers it for a thanksgiving, then he shall offer with the sacrifice of thanksgiving unleavened cakes mingled with oil, and unleavened wafers spread with oil” (Leviticus 7:12). <b>What</b> does the term <b>“offer it [<i>yakrivenu</i>]”</b> serve to <b>say?</b>
The <i>baraita</i> explains: The term is referring to one who <b>designates a thanks offering and it was lost, and he separated another in its place, and</b> then <b>the first</b> animal <b>was found, and</b> now <b>both of them stand before him</b> available for sacrifice. <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>that he may sacrifice whichever</b> one <b>he wants and</b> bring <b>its</b> accompanying <b>loaves with it? The verse states: “He offers a thanks offering,”</b> indicating that he may offer either one.
One <b>might</b> have thought that even <b>the second should require</b> the accompanying <b>loaves.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Offer it,”</b> to teach that only <b>one</b> of them is brought with the loaves, <b>and not two. From where</b> is it derived that <b>the offspring, substitutes, and replacement</b> offerings, in the case where the animal was lost, another was separated in its place, and then the original animal was found, are <b>included</b> and may be sacrificed as well? <b>The verse states: “And if…as a thanksgiving,”</b> teaching that they may all be sacrificed as thanks offerings. One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>all of them require loaves.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “With the sacrifice of thanksgiving,”</b> which indicates that only <b>the thanks offering</b> itself <b>requires loaves, but its offspring and its substitute and its replacement do not require loaves.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>the substitute of a burnt offering, the offspring of the substitute,</b> e.g., if one substituted a female animal for a burnt offering, and it gave birth to a male, and <b>the offspring of the offspring of its offspring until the end of all time, they are</b> all <b>like burnt offerings and</b> therefore <b>they require flaying and cutting</b> into pieces <b>and</b> must be burned <b>completely in the fire.</b> In the case of <b>one who designates a female</b> animal <b>as a burnt offering,</b> which may be brought only from males, <b>and</b> that female <b>gave birth to a male,</b> although it is a male, <b>it</b> is left to <b>graze until it becomes unfit [<i>sheyista’ev</i>] and</b> then <b>it is sold, and he brings a burnt offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> The male offspring <b>itself is sacrificed as a burnt offering.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The first clause of the mishna states that the offspring of a female substitute for a burnt offering is brought as a burnt offering, whereas in the latter clause the Rabbis and Rabbi Elazar disagree about the status of a female animal that was consecrated as a burnt offering. The Gemara therefore asks: <b>What is different</b> in <b>the first clause that they do not disagree, and what is different</b> in <b>the latter clause that they disagree?</b>
<b>Rabba bar bar Ḥana says:</b> Even the first clause is <b>taught as</b> a matter in <b>dispute</b> between the Rabbis and Rabbi Elazar, <b>and</b> the ruling there <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar. And Rava said: You</b> may <b>even say</b> that the first clause is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> but they <b>disagree with Rabbi Elazar only with regard to</b> one who <b>designates a female</b> animal <b>for a burnt offering,</b> as they maintain that the offspring may not be sacrificed <b>because its mother may not be sacrificed. But</b> with regard to the offspring of <b>a substitute, since its mother,</b> i.e., the original burnt offering that is the source of the mother’s sanctity as a substitute, <b>may also be sacrificed, even the Rabbis concede</b> that the offspring may be sacrificed.
The Gemara asks: <b>And did Rabbi Elazar say</b> that the <b>offspring</b> of a female designated as a burnt offering <b>itself is sacrificed as a burnt offering? But</b> one may <b>raise a contradiction</b> from the mishna (20b): <b>The substitute of a guilt offering, the offspring of that substitute,</b> and <b>their offspring</b> and <b>the offspring of their offspring, until the end of all time,</b> all <b>graze until they become unfit, and then</b> each animal <b>is sold, and</b> the <b>money</b> received for the sale <b>is allocated for</b> communal <b>gift offerings.</b>
<b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> These animals are not left to graze until they become unfit and then each animal is sold; rather <b>they</b> are left to <b>die. Rabbi Elazar says:</b> Communal gift offerings are not purchased with the money from the sale; rather, the owner should <b>bring</b> an individual <b>burnt offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. The Gemara infers from Rabbi Elazar’s statement that <b>with</b> the <b>money</b> received for its sale, <b>yes,</b> one brings an individual burnt offering, but the offspring of the substitute <b>itself, no,</b> one may not bring it as a burnt offering.
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: Rabbi Elazar was speaking to them in accordance with the statement of the Rabbis,</b> as follows: <b>According to me, I hold that even the offspring</b> itself <b>is also sacrificed</b> as <b>a burnt offering.</b> But <b>according to you, who say</b> that the animal is left to <b>graze</b> until it becomes blemished and then it is sold and the money used for the purchase of burnt offerings, <b>concede to me at least that the remainder</b> of the money <b>goes for</b> the purchase of <b>individual gift</b> offerings, rather than communal offerings. <b>And</b> the Rabbis <b>said to him</b> in response that <b>the remainder</b> of the money <b>goes for communal gift</b> offerings.
<b>Rava said</b> another explanation: <b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that the offspring <b>itself is offered</b> as <b>a burnt offering only</b> in a case <b>where he designated a female</b> animal <b>as a burnt offering</b> and it gave birth, <b>since there is burnt offering status</b> for a bird that is the same sex as <b>its mother.</b>

Daf 19a

<b>But with regard to the offspring of the substitute of a guilt offering, where there is no burnt offering status for its mother,</b> as the animal for which it was substituted was a guilt offering, <b>Rabbi Elazar concedes that</b> an animal purchased <b>with its money,</b> received from selling the offspring, <b>yes,</b> it is sacrificed as a burnt offering, but the offspring <b>itself is not sacrificed</b> as a burnt offering.
<b>Abaye raised an objection</b> to Rava: <b>And does Rabbi Elazar require that there be burnt offering status for its mother,</b> in order for the offspring to be sacrificed as a burnt offering? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who designates a female</b> animal <b>for his Paschal offering,</b> which must be a male, the animal is left to <b>graze until it becomes unfit, and it is</b> then <b>sold and he brings a Paschal offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. If the female animal <b>gave birth</b> to a male,the offspring may not be sacrificed as a Paschal offering despite the fact that it is a male. Rather, <b>it</b> is left to <b>graze until it becomes unfit, and</b> then <b>it is sold and he brings a Paschal offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received from <b>its</b> sale.
If the animal <b>remained</b> without a blemish until <b>after Passover, it</b> is left to <b>graze until it becomes unfit, and he brings a peace offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. If <b>it gave birth</b> to a male after Passover, the offspring too is left to <b>graze until it becomes unfit, and</b> then <b>it is sold, and he brings a peace offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> disagrees in the latter case and <b>says:</b> The offspring <b>itself is sacrificed as a peace offering.</b>
Abaye explains his objection: <b>But here,</b> it is a case <b>where there is no peace offering status for its mother,</b> as the mother was consecrated as a Paschal offering, <b>and</b> yet <b>Rabbi Elazar says</b> that the offspring <b>is sacrificed</b> as <b>a peace offering.</b> Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye in response: <b>Do you say</b> that this statement of <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> with regard to a Paschal offering <b>after Passover</b> contradicts my explanation? Not so; the status of a Paschal offering <b>after Passover is different, as a leftover Paschal offering itself is sacrificed as a peace offering.</b> Therefore, a female animal designated as a Paschal offering has the status of a peace offering after Passover.
Abaye asked Rava: <b>If so,</b> that the reason Rabbi Elazar permits the offspring to be sacrificed is that the mother also has the status of a peace offering, <b>let</b> Rabbi Elazar <b>also disagree</b> with the Rabbis <b>in the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, where the female animal designated as a Paschal offering gave birth before Passover. Rabbi Elazar should state that this offspring itself may be brought as a peace offering, as here too the mother has the status of a peace offering, since a Paschal offering slaughtered before Passover as a peace offering is valid. Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>Yes,</b> it <b>is indeed so, and they disagree</b> in this case as well.
<b>Abaye</b> suggested another explanation of the opinion of Rabbi Elazar and <b>said:</b> In a case where the female animal designated as a Paschal offering gave birth before Passover, there is <b>nobody</b> who <b>disagrees;</b> rather, they all agree that the offspring may not be sacrificed. <b>As</b> it <b>is learned</b> as a tradition that <b>to the place that the leftover</b> offering <b>goes, the offspring goes</b> as well. Therefore, <b>after Passover, when the leftover</b> Paschal offering <b>is sacrificed</b> as <b>a peace offering, the offspring is also sacrificed as a peace offering.</b>
<b>But before Passover,</b> when the Paschal offering is not yet considered leftover, the offspring is endowed with the same sanctity as the mother. In <b>what</b> way <b>is the mother consecrated?</b> It is consecrated <b>for the value of a Paschal offering,</b> that is, so that it should be sold and a Paschal offering should be purchased with the proceeds, as the female animal itself may not be sacrificed as a Paschal offering. If so, <b>the offspring as well</b> is consecrated only <b>for the value of a Paschal offering.</b>
<b>Rav Ukva bar Ḥama raises an objection</b> to this explanation of Abaye: <b>And do we say</b> that Rabbi Elazar maintains <b>that as its mother</b> is consecrated only <b>for the value of</b> a Paschal offering, <b>the offspring as well</b> is consecrated only <b>for the value of</b> a Paschal offering? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who designates a female</b> animal <b>as a Paschal offering, it and its offspring</b> are left to <b>graze until they become unfit, and</b> then <b>they are sold, and he brings a Paschal offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>their</b> sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar says:</b> The offspring <b>itself is sacrificed</b> as <b>a Paschal offering.</b>
<b>But here</b> it is a case <b>where its mother</b> was consecrated <b>for the value of</b> a Paschal offering, <b>and</b> nevertheless <b>Rabbi Elazar said</b> that the offspring <b>itself is sacrificed as a Paschal offering, and we do not establish</b> the status of the offspring based upon the sanctity <b>of the mother.</b>
<b>Ravina says:</b> One can answer that here <b>we are dealing with</b> a case of one <b>who designates a pregnant animal,</b> and <b>Rabbi Elazar holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said</b> with regard to one who consecrates a pregnant animal for a specific purpose, that <b>if he left it out,</b> i.e., designated the fetus as having a different sanctity, it is <b>left out</b> from the sanctity of the mother and consecrated in accordance with the designated sanctity. The reason is <b>that a fetus is not</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother,</b> but rather the mother and its offspring are considered two separate animals. Here too, it is only <b>its mother that is not sanctified with the inherent sanctity</b> of a Paschal offering, but only for the value of a Paschal offering, as it is female. <b>But</b> the offspring <b>is consecrated</b> as a Paschal offering.
<b>Mar Zutra, son of Rav Mari, said to Ravina: This, too, stands to reason, that we are dealing with</b> a case where he designated <b>a pregnant animal, from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: It and its offspring.</b> This indicates that both the mother and its offspring were in existence at the time of the consecration. The Gemara comments: <b>Conclude from here</b> that this explanation is correct.

Daf 19b

<b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, said: And Rabbi Elazar concedes</b> to the opinion of the Rabbis with regard to a case <b>where</b> one <b>designates a female</b> animal <b>for a guilt offering,</b> which is only brought from a male animal, <b>that its offspring is not sacrificed</b> as <b>a guilt offering.</b> The Gemara asks: Isn’t that <b>obvious?</b> As <b>Rabbi Elazar states</b> that the offspring may be sacrificed <b>only if</b> one <b>designates a female</b> animal <b>for a burnt offering</b> and it gives birth, due to the fact <b>that there is burnt offering status</b> for a bird that is the same sex as <b>its mother. But with regard to</b> one who <b>designates a female</b> animal <b>as a guilt offering, where there is no guilt offering status for its mother, even Rabbi Elazar concedes that</b> its offspring <b>is not sacrificed</b> as <b>a guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara answers that this statement is nevertheless necessary, for <b>if</b> Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, had <b>not informed us</b> of this <i>halakha</i>, <b>I would say</b> that <b>the reason</b> for the ruling <b>of Rabbi Elazar,</b> that one who designates a female animal for a burnt offering may sacrifice the offspring as a burnt offering, <b>is not due to</b> the fact <b>that</b> there is <b>burnt offering status</b> for a bird with the same sex <b>as its mother; rather</b> it is <b>because the offspring is fit as an offering,</b> as it is a male, <b>and this too,</b> the offspring of the female that was designated as a guilt offering, <b>is</b> likewise <b>fit as an offering,</b> as it is a male. Therefore, he <b>teaches us</b> that this offspring is not sacrificed even according to the opinion of Rabbi Elazar.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> that Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina’s ruling is necessary to exclude the possibility that Rabbi Elazar’s reason is that the offspring is suitable as an offering, then say the following: <b>Rather than teach us that the offspring</b> of a female designated as a guilt offering <b>is not sacrificed</b> as <b>a guilt offering, let him teach us</b> a more expansive ruling, <b>that its offspring is not sacrificed</b> as <b>a burnt offering,</b> despite the fact that the mother is left to graze until it becomes unfit, at which point it is sold, and the proceeds are used for the purchase of a burnt offering. <b>And</b> from that ruling one would know that <b>the same is true</b> that the offspring is not sacrificed <b>as a guilt offering.</b>
The Gemara explains: <b>If he would teach us</b> that according to Rabbi Elazar the offspring is not sacrificed as <b>a burnt offering, I would say</b> that it is only as <b>a burnt offering that</b> the offspring <b>is not sacrificed, because he did not consecrate the mother</b> with the same type of <b>sanctity</b> for the sake of which <b>the fetus</b> would be sacrificed. This is because the mother was consecrated as a guilt offering whereas the offspring would have been sacrificed as a burnt offering. <b>But</b> with regard to the option of sacrificing the offspring as <b>a guilt offering,</b> when the offspring has the same type of sanctity as that with which the mother was consecrated, I might <b>say</b> that <b>the offspring is sacrificed as a guilt offering.</b> Therefore, he <b>teaches us</b> that it is not sacrificed as a guilt offering, even according to the opinion of Rabbi Elazar.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who designates a female</b> animal <b>for a guilt offering,</b> which may be brought only from males, <b>it</b> is left to <b>graze until it becomes blemished and</b> then <b>it is sold, and he brings a guilt offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale. <b>And if</b> in the interim, he designated a male animal and <b>his guilt offering was</b> already <b>sacrificed,</b> so that a guilt offering is no longer needed, the <b>money</b> received for the sale of the blemished female <b>is allocated for</b> communal <b>gift offerings.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> Since a female is unfit to be sacrificed as a guilt offering, its halakhic status is like that of a blemished animal in the sense that it does not become inherently sacred; rather, its value alone becomes sacred. Therefore, <b>it may be sold without a blemish,</b> and a guilt offering is purchased with the money received for its sale.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara asks: <b>And why do I</b> need to wait until the female animal that was designated as a guilt offering <b>becomes blemished</b> before it can be sold? <b>Let it be sold</b> immediately even without a blemish: <b>Since it is</b> a female and therefore <b>unfit for the matter</b> for which it was designated, <b>this is</b> the same thing as <b>a blemish.</b>
<b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: This is the reason</b> that the female animal may not be sold before it becomes blemished, <b>for we say</b> that <b>as sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value has descended on it,</b> therefore <b>inherent sanctity has descended on it as well.</b> Although it may not be sacrificed in any event, its inherent sanctity still mandates that it may not be sold until it becomes blemished. <b>Rava says: That is to say</b> that even if one <b>consecrated a male</b> animal with the intention of selling it and bringing a burnt offering or guilt offering with <b>the money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale, as it becomes sanctified with sanctity that inheres in its value, it also becomes <b>sanctified</b> with <b>inherent sanctity.</b> And as the animal is fit to be brought as a burnt offering or as a guilt offering, it is sacrificed.
It <b>was stated</b> that this issue is subject to a dispute of <i>amora’im</i>: If one <b>consecrated a male</b> animal with the intention of selling it and bringing a burnt offering or guilt offering with the <b>money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale, <b>Rav Kahana says</b> that it is <b>sanctified</b> with <b>inherent sanctity,</b> whereas <b>Rava says</b> that it <b>is not sanctified</b> with <b>inherent sanctity. But Rava</b> later <b>retracted</b> his statement and agreed with the opinion <b>of Rav Kahana, due to</b> the aforementioned statement of <b>Rav Yehuda</b> citing that which <b>Rav said,</b> that as the animal becomes sanctified with sanctity that inheres in its value, inherent sanctity also takes effect.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Shimon</b> disagrees with the opinion of the Rabbis and <b>says</b> that a female animal that was designated as a guilt offering <b>may be sold without a blemish,</b> as it is unfit to be sacrificed as the offering for which it was designated, and this itself is considered a blemish. <b>Rav Ḥiyya bar Avin said to Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> Why doesn’t Rabbi Shimon say that <b>as sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value has descended on it, inherent sanctity should descend on it as well,</b> and therefore it may not be sold until it becomes blemished?
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Rav Ḥiyya bar Avin: <b>Rabbi Shimon</b> conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning, as he says</b> with regard to <b>anything that is not fit itself</b> to be sacrificed upon the altar, that <b>inherent sanctity does not descend upon it. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>a guilt offering</b> that should be sacrificed when it is <b>in its</b> first <b>year,</b> such as a guilt offering of a nazirite or of a leper, <b>but</b> the owner <b>brought it</b> when it was <b>in its second</b> year, or a guilt offering that should be sacrificed when it is <b>in its second</b> year, such as a guilt offering for robbery, for misuse of consecrated property, or for a designated maidservant, <b>and</b> the owner <b>brought it</b> when it was <b>in its</b> first <b>year,</b> the offering <b>is fit, but it does not satisfy the obligation of the owner</b> to bring a guilt offering, and he must bring another one.
<b>Rabbi Shimon says</b> that the offering is disqualified, as these offerings <b>themselves are not consecrated,</b> due to the fact that the proper time of their offering has either not yet arrived or has already passed. This indicates that Rabbi Shimon maintains that in such a case there is no inherent sanctity.
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> consider the case of one who consecrates an animal <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, e.g., an animal that is less than eight days old, <b>which is unfit</b> to be sacrificed, <b>and</b> yet <b>Rabbi Shimon said that it is sanctified</b> with regard to the prohibition against the slaughter of sacrificial animals outside the Temple courtyard. This proves that according to Rabbi Shimon, even an offering that is not fit to be sacrificed has inherent sanctity. The Gemara explains that an animal <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived <b>is different, as it is fit</b> to be offered <b>tomorrow,</b> i.e., automatically at a later stage, and is therefore considered to be fit for an offering.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so,</b> that any animal which will be fit to be sacrificed when its time arrives is sanctified with inherent sanctity, the same should also apply to <b>a guilt offering</b> that should be offered when it is <b>in its second</b> year <b>but</b> the owner <b>brought it</b> to be sacrificed when it was <b>in its</b> first <b>year,</b> as it will be <b>fit</b> for sacrifice <b>in</b> another <b>year.</b> Why, then, doesn’t it have inherent sanctity according to Rabbi Shimon? <b>Rather, this is the reason of Rabbi Shimon in</b> the case of an animal <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived, <b>as he derives it from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>a firstborn.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon:</b> An animal <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived <b>enters the pen to be tithed</b> together with the other animals. <b>And it is</b> considered in this regard to be <b>like a firstborn: Just as a firstborn is sanctified before the time</b> when <b>it</b> is fit to be sacrificed has arrived, i.e., immediately after birth, <b>and it is sacrificed after its time,</b> following the eighth day, <b>so too,</b> an animal <b>whose time has not yet</b> arrived <b>is sanctified before its time</b> has arrived <b>and is sacrificed after its time.</b>
§ The Gemara continues to discuss this dispute between Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis: <b>The Sages taught</b> that <b>one who consecrates a female</b> animal <b>for his burnt offering</b>

Daf 20a

<b>or for his Paschal offering or for his guilt offering,</b> although these offerings are brought only from male animals, it is still consecrated with inherent sanctity. Therefore, if one exchanges for it a non-sacred animal, he <b>renders</b> that animal <b>a substitute,</b> which is consecrated with the same sanctity as the original animal.
<b>Rabbi Shimon</b> disagrees with the Sages and <b>says:</b> The <i>halakha</i> is not the same in all these cases. Granted, if he consecrates a female animal <b>for his burnt offering, it renders</b> the non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute,</b> as there is burnt offering status for female birds. For this reason it is consecrated with inherent sanctity and can be sold only after it has become blemished. But if he consecrates a female animal <b>for his Paschal offering or for his guilt offering</b> it is not consecrated with inherent sanctity, and it may be sold even without a blemish. Therefore, <b>it does not render</b> the non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b>
This ruling is based upon the principle that <b>no</b> animal <b>renders</b> the non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute except</b> for an animal that has inherent sanctity, which means that even if it may not be sacrificed, it must still be left to <b>graze</b> in order <b>to become blemished,</b> after which it is sold, and the proceeds from the sale are used to purchase a fit offering.
<b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says,</b> with regard to this dispute between Rabbi Shimon and the first <i>tanna</i>: <b>I do not agree with the statement of Rabbi Shimon with</b> regard to the <b>Paschal offering,</b> that a non-sacred animal exchanged for a female which was designated for a Paschal offering does not become consecrated as a substitute. I disagree, <b>since a leftover Paschal</b> offering <b>is sacrificed</b> after Passover as <b>a peace offering,</b> and a peace offering is brought even from female animals. Therefore, a female animal that was designated for a Paschal offering should be consecrated with inherent sanctity, as there is peace offering status for female animals. It should not be sold unless it has a blemish, and it should render a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it a substitute.
The Gemara clarifies the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi: <b>And let him</b> also <b>say: I do not agree with the statement of Rabbi Shimon</b> with regard <b>to a guilt offering,</b> for the same reason that Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi disagrees with regard to the Paschal offering, <b>since a leftover guilt offering is sacrificed as a burnt offering,</b> and a bird burnt offering is brought even from female animals. Therefore, a female animal designated as a guilt offering should be consecrated with inherent sanctity, and it should render a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it a substitute. The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis, who say: Leftover</b> guilt offerings <b>are used for communal gift offerings,</b> i.e., they are sold and the money is used to purchase communal gift offerings, <b>and a substitute cannot</b> be designated <b>for a communal</b> offering.
§ The Gemara analyzes the opinion of Rabbi Shimon in the <i>baraita</i>: It might <b>enter your mind</b> to say that <b>this is the reason for</b> the ruling of <b>Rabbi Shimon with regard to</b> one who <b>designates a female</b> animal <b>for his burnt offering:</b> One <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute due to that</b> reason, <b>that it has burnt offering status</b> because of the case <b>of a bird burnt offering,</b> which may be brought as a female.
But <b>if that is so,</b> in the case <b>of a High Priest who designated a female cow</b> instead <b>of his</b> male <b>bull</b> that he is obligated to bring as a sin offering on Yom Kippur, <b>it should be sanctified</b> with regard to rendering a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it a substitute. Here too, the status of a sin offering <b>is</b> upon it, as <b>the</b> red <b>heifer of purification</b> is similar to a sin offering and is female. The Gemara answers: The red heifer is not consecrated with inherent sanctity, as it is not sacrificed upon the altar; rather, <b>it is consecrated</b> for <b>Temple maintenance, and</b> items <b>consecrated</b> for <b>Temple maintenance do not render</b> a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for them <b>a substitute.</b>
The Gemara objects: According to Rabbi Shimon, if <b>an individual, who</b> is obligated to bring a female animal as a sin offering, <b>designated</b> a male <b>goat</b> instead <b>of his female goat, it should be sanctified</b> with inherent sanctity with regard to rendering a substitute, <b>as it has</b> sin offering status, due to the case of the male <b>goat</b> brought as a sin offering by <b>the king. Alternatively,</b> if <b>a king, who</b> is obligated to bring a male animal as a sin offering, <b>designated a female goat</b> instead of <b>his</b> male <b>goat, it should be sanctified</b> with inherent sanctity with regard to rendering a substitute, since here too it has sin offering status, <b>as an individual designates a female goat</b> as a sin offering. Therefore, Rabbi Shimon should have stated that these animals render non-sacred animals exchanged for them consecrated as substitutes.
The Gemara explains: Neither a female goat brought as a sin offering by a king nor a male goat brought as a sin offering by an individual are considered to have sin offering status. The reason is that <b>these,</b> the king and an individual, <b>are two</b> distinct <b>bodies,</b> and the status of an offering can be conferred only when such an offering is brought by people of the same status.
The Gemara objects: If so, then in a case where an individual <b>sinned</b> and is obligated to bring a female goat as a sin offering <b>prior to being appointed</b> as king, and he did not designate an animal as his offering before his appointment, <b>if he designates</b> a male <b>goat</b> instead <b>of</b> his <b>female goat</b> following his appointment, <b>it should be sanctified</b> with inherent sanctity with regard to substitution. In this situation, it should have sin offering status, <b>as, if he sinned now, he is obligated to bring</b> a male <b>goat.</b> The Gemara explains: This is not correct, <b>as</b> he <b>did not sin</b> when he was a king, and therefore <b>was not</b> actually <b>obligated to</b> bring a male <b>goat,</b> but a female goat.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> that the individual’s appointment as king does not give sin offering status to the male goat designated as a sin offering for a sin committed prior to his appointment, <b>here too,</b> one should say that a female animal designated for a burnt offering does not have burnt offering status, as the person is not a poor leper, and therefore he <b>does not bring a bird burnt offering.</b> Why, then, does Rabbi Shimon maintain that such an animal renders a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it a substitute?
The Gemara explains: The <i>baraita</i> is not referring to an obligatory burnt offering but rather to a voluntary burnt offering, and <b>Rabbi Shimon holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya,</b> who says that anyone, even a wealthy man, can bring a bird if he vows to bring a burnt offering. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Menaḥot</i> 107a) that one who says: <b>It</b> is incumbent <b>upon me</b> to bring <b>a burnt offering,</b> without specifying which animal, <b>brings a lamb,</b> which is the smallest animal that a wealthy man can bring as a burnt offering. <b>Rabbi Elazar ben Azarya says:</b> He may even bring <b>a dove or pigeons</b> as a bird burnt offering.
§ The Gemara continues to discuss Rabbi Shimon’s opinion in the <i>baraita</i>: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna <b>elsewhere</b> (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:7): In the case of <b>one who consecrates</b> all <b>his possessions</b> without specifying for what purpose, <b>and among them there is an animal that is suitable</b> to be sacrificed <b>on the altar,</b> or multiple such animals that are <b>males and females,</b> what should be done with them?
<b>Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> Since he did not specify otherwise, everything is consecrated for Temple maintenance. Therefore, any <b>males</b> should <b>be sold for the needs of burnt offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as burnt offerings. <b>And</b> any <b>females,</b> as they cannot be brought as burnt offerings, should <b>be sold for the needs of peace offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such. <b>And their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale is <b>allocated with the rest of his property for Temple maintenance.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua says:</b> Although he did not specify for what purpose he consecrated his possessions, it may be assumed that he intended the animals to be consecrated as burnt offerings. Therefore, any <b>males</b> should <b>themselves be sacrificed as burnt offerings, and</b> any <b>females,</b> since they cannot be brought as burnt offerings, should <b>be sold for the needs of peace offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals who will sacrifice them as such, <b>and their monetary value</b> that is received from their sale should be used to purchase and <b>bring burnt offerings. And</b> according to both opinions, <b>the rest of the property,</b> which is not suitable for sacrificial use, <b>is allocated for Temple maintenance.</b>
