<b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said to Rabbi Yoḥanan: According to</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua, who said</b> that any <b>males</b> should <b>themselves be sacrificed as burnt offerings,</b> as that was the intention of the one who consecrated them, <b>how can</b> the buyers <b>sacrifice the females</b> as <b>peace offerings?</b> He explains the difficulty: <b>Their</b> status <b>stems from deferred sanctity,</b> as they were consecrated to be burnt offerings, and a female that was designated as a burnt offering is not sacrificed upon the altar, but instead is left to graze until it becomes blemished and is then sold.
The Gemara cites <b>another version of</b> this statement: <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said to Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> From the fact <b>that Rabbi Yehoshua said</b> that any <b>males</b> should <b>themselves be sacrificed as burnt offerings, is this to say that he consecrated them</b> with <b>inherent sanctity? If so, why are the females sold for the needs of peace offerings?</b> Since they were consecrated as burnt offerings, it is <b>required</b> that they be left to <b>graze</b> until they become blemished.
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba: <b>Rabbi Yehoshua holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> with regard to <b>anything that is not fit</b> to be sacrificed <b>itself</b> upon the altar that <b>inherent sanctity does not descend upon it. As we learned</b> in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> If one designated a female for a guilt offering, for which one must bring a male, <b>it may be sold without a blemish,</b> and a guilt offering is purchased with the money received for its sale. <b>And we say that the reason of Rabbi Shimon</b> is that <b>as a female</b> animal <b>is not fit</b> to be sacrificed <b>as a guilt offering, inherent sanctity does not descend upon it.</b>
Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba objected to Rabbi Yoḥanan: You can <b>say that Rabbi Shimon</b> expressed his opinion specifically <b>with regard to</b> one who consecrates <b>a female for a guilt offering,</b>

Daf 20b

<b>where there is no guilt offering status</b> for an animal that is the same sex <b>as its mother,</b> as a female guilt offering is never brought. <b>But with regard to</b> one who designates <b>a female</b> animal <b>as a burnt offering, where there is burnt offering status</b> for a bird that is the same sex <b>as its mother, even Rabbi Shimon concedes</b> that it has inherent sanctity, and therefore it should not be sold for the needs of a peace offering.
<b>In addition,</b> another objection can be raised against the explanation of Rabbi Yoḥanan, as <b>we have heard that Rabbi Shimon</b> maintains that one who designates a female animal <b>as a burnt offering renders</b> a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> This indicates that it does have inherent sanctity with regard to this issue. Therefore, the question remains: How can the buyers sacrifice the females as peace offerings, when their status stems from deferred sanctity?
Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>said to</b> Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in response: I meant that <b>Rabbi Yehoshua holds in accordance with</b> the interpretation of <b>the other <i>tanna</i> with regard</b> to the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Shimon ben Yehuda says in the name of Rabbi Shimon: Even</b> one who consecrates a female <b>as his burnt offering does not render</b> a non-sacred animal that is exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> Even though there is burnt offering status for female birds, a female animal consecrated as a burnt offering does not have inherent sanctity. Therefore, it is sold and sacrificed as a peace offering, and its status is not considered to stem from deferred sanctity.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>the substitute of a guilt offering, the offspring of</b> that <b>substitute, their offspring</b> and <b>the offspring of their offspring, until the end of all time,</b> they are all left to <b>graze until they become unfit, and</b> then <b>they are sold, and</b> the <b>money</b> received for the sale <b>is allocated for</b> communal <b>gift offerings. Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> These animals are not left to graze; rather <b>they</b> are left to <b>die. And Rabbi Elazar says:</b> Communal gift offerings are not purchased with the money from the sale; rather, the owner should <b>bring</b> an individual <b>burnt offering with the money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale.
These <i>tanna’im</i> similarly disagree about the following case: <b>A guilt offering whose owner died, and</b> a guilt offering <b>that</b> was lost and <b>its owner gained atonement</b> with another animal, <b>graze until they become unfit, and</b> then <b>they are sold, and</b> the <b>money</b> received for the sale <b>is allocated</b> for communal <b>gift offerings. Rabbi Eliezer says:</b> These animals are left to <b>die. Rabbi Elazar says:</b> The owner must <b>bring</b> an individual <b>burnt offering with the money</b> received for <b>its</b> sale.
The mishna objects: <b>But</b> even according to the Rabbis, <b>isn’t a gift offering</b> also <b>a burnt offering? And what</b> then <b>is</b> the difference <b>between the statement of Rabbi Elazar and the statement of the Rabbis? Rather,</b> the Rabbis are referring to a communal burnt offering and Rabbi Elazar is referring to an individual burnt offering, and there are several differences between these two offerings: <b>When</b> the animal <b>comes</b> as an individual <b>burnt offering,</b> the owner <b>places</b> his <b>hands upon it and brings</b> the accompanying meal offering and <b>libations, and its libations</b> come <b>from his</b> own property. <b>If</b> the owner of the animal <b>was a priest,</b> the right to perform <b>its Temple service and</b> the right to <b>its hide are his.</b>
<b>And when it is a</b> communal <b>gift offering,</b> the owner of the animal that was sold <b>does not place</b> his <b>hands upon it,</b> as there is no placing of hands for communal offerings, <b>and he does not bring its libations;</b> rather, <b>its libations</b> are brought <b>from</b> the property <b>of the community.</b> Furthermore, <b>although</b> the owner of the animal that was sold <b>is a priest,</b> the right to perform <b>its Temple service and</b> the right to <b>its hide</b> are divided <b>among the members of the priestly watch</b> serving in the Temple that week.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that Rabbi Eliezer and the Sages disagreed about two cases, the substitute of a guilt offering and the <i>halakha</i> of a guilt offering whose owner died. The Gemara explains that both disputes are <b>necessary.</b>
<b>As, had</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught us</b> the dispute only in the case of <b>the guilt offering</b> where the owner achieved atonement through another animal, I might have said that perhaps it is <b>in this</b> case alone that <b>Rabbi Eliezer says that</b> the animals are left to <b>die.</b> This would be <b>because he</b> holds that there is a rabbinic <b>decree concerning</b> what to do with a guilt offering <b>following</b> the owner having achieved <b>atonement, due to</b> the case of a guilt offering <b>before</b> the owner achieved <b>atonement.</b> If burnt offerings were brought with the money from the sale in a case where the owner already achieved atonement by means of another offering, people might mistakenly say that if a guilt offering was lost and another was designated in its place, there too the other animal is sold and burnt offerings are brought from the proceeds. In fact, in that case, as atonement has not yet been achieved, that money must actually be used for a guilt offering.
<b>But with regard to the substitute of a guilt offering</b> and <b>the offspring of its substitute,</b> which in any case are sent to graze, even if the owner did not achieve atonement with another animal, one might <b>say</b> that Rabbi Eliezer <b>concedes to the Rabbis,</b> as there is no need for such a decree.
<b>And had</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught us</b> the dispute only <b>there,</b> in the case of the substitute of a guilt offering and the offspring of the substitute, I might have said that perhaps it is only <b>in this</b> case that <b>the Rabbis say</b> the animals are sent to graze, as there is no reason for a decree. <b>But with regard to</b> the case of <b>a guilt offering</b> where the owners achieved atonement, one might say that <b>they concede to Rabbi Eliezer</b> that the animals are left to die, as a decree. Therefore, both cases <b>are necessary.</b>
§ <b>Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh says:</b> This <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis with regard to the offspring of a substitute applies only <b>after atonement</b> has been achieved, i.e., after the guilt offering has been sacrificed. <b>But before atonement</b> is achieved, and the consecrated animal and the substitute are both present, <b>everyone agrees</b> that even the offspring <b>itself is sacrificed</b> as <b>a guilt offering,</b> if the owner wishes.
<b>Rava said:</b> There are <b>two refutations of</b> this <b>statement: One</b> is <b>that a person cannot achieve atonement through an item that comes from a transgression,</b> and this offspring comes from a transgression, as its mother offspring was rendered a substitute, which is prohibited. <b>And in addition, didn’t Rav Ḥananya teach</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>in support of</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi, who said</b> that only <b>the first offspring</b> of a peace offering <b>is sacrificed,</b> but <b>the second offspring,</b> i.e., the offspring of the offspring, <b>is not sacrificed?</b> Here too, the offspring of a substitute is considered like the second offspring, as it is two stages removed from the original offering.
<b>Rather, if</b> it <b>was stated,</b> it <b>was stated like this: Rav Naḥman says</b> that <b>Rabba bar Avuh says:</b> The <b>dispute</b> between Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis with regard to the offspring of a substitute applies only <b>before atonement</b> has been achieved,as Rabbi Eliezer holds that it is left to die, due to a concern that it might be sacrificed as a guilt offering, whereas the Rabbis say that it is left to graze, as there is no concern that it will be brought as a guilt offering. <b>But after</b> the original guilt offering is sacrificed and <b>atonement</b> has been achieved, everyone agrees that even the offspring <b>itself is sacrificed</b> as <b>a burnt offering,</b> as there is no further concern that it might be sacrificed as a guilt offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>But didn’t Rav Ḥananya teach</b> a <i>baraita</i> <b>in support of Rabbi Yehoshua ben Levi</b> that the second offspring is not sacrificed? How, then, can the offspring of the substitute be sacrificed, as it too should be treated like the second offspring? The Gemara concedes: That <b>is difficult.</b>
§ <b>Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya inquired of Rabbi Avin bar Kahana:</b> If one <b>designated a female</b> animal <b>as a guilt offering</b> and it gave birth to a male, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to whether <b>its offspring is sacrificed as a burnt offering?</b> The Gemara questions the necessity of this inquiry: <b>And let him resolve</b> the inquiry <b>from</b> the statement <b>of Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Ḥanina, who said</b> that <b>Rabbi Elazar concedes</b> that if one designates a female animal as a guilt offering, the offspring is not sacrificed as a guilt offering, as there is no guilt offering status for animals that are the same sex as the mother, and likewise it is not sacrificed as a burnt offering, as the mother was designated as a guilt offering. The Gemara answers: Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya <b>never heard</b> that statement of Rabbi Yosei bar Ḥanina.
The Gemara returns to the inquiry of Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya: <b>What</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Rabbi Avin bar Kahana <b>said to</b> Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya: <b>Its offspring is sacrificed as a burnt offering.</b> Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya challenges his response: <b>What is this? Rabbi Elazar says</b> in the mishna (18b) that the offspring itself is offered as a burnt offering <b>only</b> when <b>he designates a female</b> animal <b>as a burnt offering</b> and then it gives birth to a male, due to the fact <b>that there is burnt offering status</b> for a bird that is the same sex as <b>its mother. But with regard to</b> a female animal that was designated as <b>a guilt offering</b> and subsequently gave birth to a male, <b>where there is no guilt offering status</b> for animals that are the same sex <b>as its mother, even Rabbi Eliezer concedes</b> that its offspring is not sacrificed at all.
Rabbi Avin bar Kahana <b>said to him</b> in response: <b>The reason</b> for the statement <b>of Rabbi Elazar,</b> that the offspring of a female animal designated as a burnt offering is sacrificed as a burnt offering, is <b>not because</b> there is <b>burnt offering status</b> for an animal that is the same sex <b>as its mother,</b> but <b>rather because it is fit to be sacrificed</b> upon the altar, <b>and this</b> offspring of the female animal designated as a guilt offering <b>is also fit to be sacrificed.</b>
Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya <b>raised an objection to</b> Rabbi Avin bar Kahana from the mishna: Rabbi Elazar says that in the case of <b>the offspring</b> of a female animal that was designated as a guilt offering or the offspring of the substitute of a guilt offering, <b>and the offspring of their offspring until the end of all time,</b> they graze until they become blemished, and then they are sold, and the owner <b>brings</b> an individual <b>burnt offering with</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>their</b> sale. The Gemara infers: <b>With</b> the <b>money</b> received for <b>their</b> sale, <b>yes,</b> he brings a burnt offering,

Daf 21a

but the offspring <b>itself</b> is <b>not</b> sacrificed as a burnt offering. Rabbi Avin bar Kahana responds: <b>What are we dealing with here?</b> It is a case <b>where</b> the female designated as a guilt offering or as the substitute of a guilt offering <b>gave birth to a female,</b> which cannot itself be offered as a guilt offering. Rabbi Avin bar Ḥiyya asks: This is problematic, as the mishna states that the same is true with regard to the offspring of its offspring until the end of time. <b>But</b> could the mishna be referring to a case where <b>until the end of time not</b> even <b>one male was born?</b> Rabbi Avin bar Kahana <b>said to him</b> in response: <b>I answer forced answers,</b> in the manner of the <b>Babylonians,</b> and say that the mishna is referring to a case <b>where it gave birth</b> to <b>females,</b> and those females also gave birth to females, <b>until the end of time.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> With regard to <b>the substitute of a firstborn</b> offering <b>and</b> the substitute of an animal <b>tithe</b> offering, and the <b>offspring</b> of those substitutes and <b>the offspring of their offspring until the end of time,</b> the halakhic status of <b>these</b> animals <b>is like</b> that of <b>a firstborn</b> offering <b>and like</b> that of an animal <b>tithe</b> offering in that they must be treated with sanctity: They graze until they become blemished, <b>and</b> at that point <b>they may be eaten in their blemished</b> state, the substitute of the firstborn by the priests and the substitute of the animal tithe <b>by</b> their <b>owners.</b> They are not sacrificed upon the altar like the original firstborn and animal tithe offerings.
<b>What is</b> the practical difference <b>between a firstborn</b> offering <b>and</b> an animal <b>tithe</b> offering <b>and all the</b> other <b>sacrificial animals?</b> The difference is <b>that all the</b> other <b>sacrificial animals</b> that were blemished and redeemed <b>are sold in the butchers’ market [<i>ba’itliz</i>]</b>, <b>and slaughtered in the butchers’ market, and weighed</b> and sold <b>by the <i>litra</i>,</b> in the manner that non-sacred meat is slaughtered and sold. This is the case with regard to all consecrated animals <b>except for the firstborn and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offerings, which are sold only from the home and not by the <i>litra</i>.
<b>And</b> in addition, all sacrificial animals that became blemished <b>are subject to redemption</b> through sale, at which point the money becomes sacred and the animal becomes non-sacred, <b>and their substitutes</b> are also subject <b>to redemption</b> through sale.This is true for all consecrated animals, <b>except for the firstborn and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offerings, which are not subject to redemption. <b>And</b> all sacrificial animals <b>come</b> to be sacrificed in the Temple even <b>from outside of Eretz</b> Yisrael, <b>except for the firstborn and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offerings, <b>which</b> should not be brought from outside Eretz Yisrael <i>ab initio</i>. But <b>if they came unblemished, they are sacrificed</b> in the Temple like a regular firstborn or animal tithe offering coming from Eretz Yisrael; <b>and if they are blemished</b> animals, <b>they may be eaten in their blemished</b> state, the firstborns by the priests and the animal tithes <b>by</b> their <b>owners.</b>
<b>Rabbi Shimon says: What</b> is the <b>reason</b> for this last difference between them? It is <b>that the firstborn and</b> animal <b>tithe</b> offerings <b>have a remedy in their place</b> outside Eretz Yisrael, as they can graze until they become blemished and then can be eaten there. It is not necessary to bring them to Eretz Yisrael in order to eat them. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>all other sacrificial animals, even if a blemish develops in them, these</b> animals remain <b>in their sanctity,</b> and one must redeem them and bring another offering with the money of their redemption. Therefore, when they are unblemished it is proper to bring these animals themselves to Eretz Yisrael.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> With regard to the status of the substitute of firstborn and animal tithe offerings that was discussed in the mishna, <b>Rava bar Rav Azza said</b> that <b>they inquired in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael: <b>What is</b> the <i>halakha</i> with regard to <b>one who inflicts a blemish on the substitute of a firstborn</b> offering <b>or</b> on the substitute of an animal <b>tithe</b> offering? <b>Do we say</b> that <b>since they are not sacrificed</b> on the altar like the actual firstborn or animal tithe offering, one who does this <b>is not liable,</b> as the prohibition against inflicting a blemish on a sacrificial animal applies only when one thereby disqualifies the animal from the altar, and that is not the case here; <b>or perhaps since they are sanctified, he is liable</b> for inflicting a blemish?
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava bar Rav Azza: <b>And let the dilemma be raised</b> in a case of <b>one who inflicts a blemish on</b> other sacrificial animals, such as <b>the ninth</b> animal counted while selecting the animal <b>tithe</b> offering, which was mistakenly declared to be the tenth. This animal is consecrated in that one is prohibited to work it or shear it until it becomes blemished, but it may not be sacrificed upon the altar.
<b>Rather, what is different</b> about the <b>ninth</b> animal, which one called the tenth, <b>that you did not raise the dilemma</b> about it? It is <b>because the Merciful One excluded it,</b> with the verse: <b>“The tenth</b> shall be holy to the Lord” (Leviticus 27:32), which serves <b>to exclude</b> from the altar <b>the ninth</b> that was mistakenly declared to be the tenth. One may derive from here that one who inflicts a blemish upon this ninth animal is not liable.
<b>Here, too,</b> with regard to the substitute of a firstborn, <b>the Merciful One excluded it</b> from the altar: “But the firstborn…<b>you shall not redeem; they are holy”</b> (Numbers 18:17). This indicates that <b>they,</b> the firstborns themselves, <b>are sacrificed, but their substitute is not sacrificed,</b> and it may be derived from here that one is also not liable for inflicting a blemish upon the substitute of a firstborn.
<b>Rav Naḥman bar Yitzḥak teaches</b> that statement in <b>this</b> way, in the opposite manner to the previous version: <b>Rav Aḥa, son of Rav Azza, said</b> that <b>they inquired in the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael: In the case of <b>one who inflicts a blemish upon the ninth</b> animal counted while selecting the animal <b>tithe</b> offering, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Abaye <b>said to him: And let the dilemma be raised</b> with regard to <b>one who inflicts a blemish upon the substitute of a firstborn</b> offering <b>or</b> the substitute of an animal <b>tithe</b> offering.
<b>Rather, what is different</b> about <b>the substitute of a firstborn or</b> the substitute of an animal <b>tithe that you did not raise the dilemma</b> about them? It is <b>because the Merciful One excluded them,</b> as it is stated: <b>“They are holy,”</b> which indicates that <b>they</b> themselves <b>are sacrificed, but their substitutes are not sacrificed.</b> With regard to <b>the ninth</b> animal counted while selecting the animal <b>tithe as well, the Merciful One excluded it,</b> as it is stated: <b>“The tenth,”</b> which serves <b>to exclude</b> from the altar <b>the ninth</b> that was mistakenly declared to be the tenth.
§ The mishna teaches that although the firstborn and animal tithe offerings should not be brought from outside Eretz Yisrael <i>ab initio</i>, <b>if they came unblemished,</b> they may be sacrificed. With regard to this issue, the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction:</b> There was an incident where <b>ben Antigonus brought up firstborn</b> animals <b>from Babylonia</b> in order to sacrifice them <b>and they did not accept them</b> in the Temple. <b>Rav Ḥisda said:</b> That is <b>not difficult: This</b> statement in the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yishmael,</b> while <b>this</b> case of ben Antigonus is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Akiva.</b>
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that Rabbi Akiva and Rabbi Yishmael disagreed over this matter: <b>Rabbi Yosei says three matters in the name of three elders:</b> Rabbi Yishmael, Rabbi Akiva, and Ben Azzai, each of whom issued a different statement with regard to a firstborn and second tithe. <b>Rabbi Yishmael says</b> that one <b>might</b> have thought that <b>a person may bring up second tithe in the present</b> day to Jerusalem <b>and eat it in Jerusalem.</b>
<b>But</b> the opposite conclusion should be derived through <b>logical inference</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of a firstborn: <b>A firstborn requires</b> that it be <b>brought</b> to a specific <b>place,</b> i.e., Jerusalem, <b>and</b> second <b>tithe requires</b> that it be <b>brought</b> to a specific <b>place. Just as a firstborn is eaten only in the presence of the Temple, so too,</b> second <b>tithe is eaten only in the presence of the Temple.</b> This comparison can be refuted: <b>No, if you said</b> that this is true <b>with regard to a firstborn,</b> that it may be eaten only in the presence of the Temple, <b>as it requires placing the blood and sacrificial portions</b> of the offering <b>upon the altar, shall you</b> also <b>say</b> that this is the case <b>with regard to</b> second <b>tithe,</b> for <b>which</b> this <b>is not</b> required?
Rather, perhaps you will <b>say</b> that the <i>halakha</i> of eating second tithe in Jerusalem in the present is derived from a comparison to first fruits: <b>First fruits require</b> that they be <b>brought</b> to a specific <b>place,</b> i.e., Jerusalem, <b>and</b> second <b>tithe requires</b> that they be <b>brought</b> to a specific <b>place. Just as first fruits may be eaten only in the presence of the Temple, so too,</b> second <b>tithe may be eaten only in the presence of the Temple.</b>
But this comparison is also flawed, as <b>what</b> is unique <b>about first fruits?</b> They are unique in <b>that they require placement</b> before the altar, as it is stated: “And the priest shall take the basket out of your hand, and set it down before the altar of the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 26:4). Perhaps it is for this reason that they must be eaten in the presence of the Temple. <b>Shall you say</b> that this is also the case with regard <b>to</b> second <b>tithe, which is not</b> obligated in placement before the altar?
<b>The verse states</b> with regard to second tithe: <b>“And you shall eat before the Lord your God</b> in the place that He shall choose to cause His name to dwell there, <b>the tithe of your grain, of your wine, and of your oil, and the firstborn of your cattle and sheep”</b> (Deuteronomy 14:23). This verse <b>compares</b> second <b>tithe to the firstborn: Just as a firstborn is eaten only in the presence of the Temple, so too,</b> second <b>tithe is eaten only in the presence of the Temple.</b> This concludes the statement of Rabbi Yishmael.
The Gemara analyzes the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael: <b>But let the inference return</b> and compare second tithe to both the firstborn and first fruits together, <b>and let</b> the <i>halakha</i> of second tithe <b>be derived by</b> analogy from <b>the common element [<i>bema hatzad</i>]</b> of the two sources: Just as the firstborn and first fruits both require that they be brought to a specific place and are not eaten in the present time, so too, second tithe, which also requires that it must be brought to a specific place, should not be eaten in the present time.
<b>Rav Ashi said:</b> The <i>halakha</i> of second tithe cannot be derived in this manner, <b>because it can be said: What</b> is notable <b>about the common element of these</b> cases? They are notable in <b>that they have an aspect</b> of their <i>halakha</i> that involves <b>the altar:</b> A firstborn must have its blood and sacrificial portions placed upon the altar, and first fruits must be placed before the altar, whereas no obligation with regard to the altar applies to the second tithe at all.
According to Rabbi Yishmael, a firstborn is not eaten in the present day, but with regard to second tithe he is uncertain about the <i>halakha</i>. The Gemara asks: <b>And what does he hold? If he holds</b> that the <b>initial consecration</b> both <b>sanctified</b> Jerusalem <b>for its time and sanctified</b> Jerusalem <b>forever,</b> including the period after the destruction of the Temple, then <b>a firstborn is no different and</b> second <b>tithe is no different,</b> as both <b>are capable of being brought</b> to Jerusalem: In the case of the firstborn, an altar may be built upon which it may be offered, while second tithe may be eaten in Jerusalem, and the presence of the Temple is not required for either one. <b>And if he holds</b> that the <b>initial consecration sanctified</b> Jerusalem <b>for its time but did not sanctify</b> Jerusalem <b>forever,</b> and he is still uncertain about the status of second tithe, then <b>raise the dilemma even</b> with regard to the <b>firstborn.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>Actually, he holds</b> that the <b>initial consecration sanctified</b> Jerusalem <b>for its time but did not sanctify it forever, and here we are dealing with</b> a case <b>where the blood of a firstborn</b> offering <b>was sprinkled</b> upon the altar <b>before the Temple</b> was destroyed, <b>and</b> then <b>the Temple was destroyed, but its flesh was still in existence.</b>
<b>Since if the blood is</b> still <b>there</b> and had not yet been sprinkled upon the altar, it is <b>not subject to sprinkling,</b> as the Temple has been destroyed, and consequently <b>the meat</b> of the firstborn offering may not be eaten. This <i>halakha</i> <b>comes</b> and is <b>derived from</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>the blood,</b> based upon the juxtaposition in the following verse: “You shall sprinkle their blood against the altar… and their flesh shall be yours” (Numbers 18:17–18). This teaches that the meat may be eaten only when the blood is fit to be sprinkled upon the altar. By contrast, there is no such juxtaposition with regard to second tithe, and therefore Rabbi Yishmael remains uncertain whether it may be eaten in the present time in Jerusalem.

Daf 21b

<b>And</b> then, as Rabbi Yishmael holds that a firstborn definitely may not be eaten in the present, he says that second <b>tithe comes and</b> its status <b>is derived from</b> the juxtaposition to <b>the firstborn.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>And can we derive</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>sacrificial</b> food <b>from one another? But doesn’t Rabbi Yoḥanan say:</b> In <b>the entire Torah, we may derive</b> that which is <b>derived from</b> a <i>halakha</i> that was itself <b>derived</b> from another source, <b>except for</b> the case of <b>sacrificial</b> animals, <b>where</b> we <b>do not derive</b> that which is <b>derived from</b> that which was already <b>derived</b> from another source.
The Gemara answers: Second <b>tithe is</b> considered <b>non-sacred.</b> Therefore, this derivation does not pertain to consecrated items. The Gemara asks: <b>This works out well according to the one who said</b> that <b>we follow</b> the <i>halakha</i> that is <b>derived.</b> If it is a consecrated item, it may not be derived from another <i>halakha</i> that was derived from another source, but if it is non-sacred, such as second tithe, it may be derived in this manner. <b>But according to the one who said</b> that <b>we follow</b> the <i>halakha</i> that <b>teaches, what is there to say?</b> In this case the <i>halakha</i> that teaches, i.e., the source of the derivation, is the meat of the firstborn, which is a consecrated item that is derived itself from the status of the blood of the firstborn. The Gemara answers: The status of <b>the meat and the blood</b> of a firstborn offering <b>are one matter.</b> Therefore, the meat is not considered as derived from another <i>halakha</i>.
§ The Gemara cites the second statement of Rabbi Yosei from the elders, in the continuation of the <i>baraita</i> above: <b>Rabbi Akiva says:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>a person may bring up a firstborn from outside Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>to Eretz</b> Yisrael <b>when the Temple is standing and sacrifice it.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And you shall eat before the Lord your God,</b> in the place which He shall choose to cause His name to dwell there, <b>the tithe of your grain, of your wine, and of your oil, and the firstborn of your herd and of your flock”</b> (Deuteronomy 14:23). Rabbi Akiva derives from here that only <b>from the place that you bring up the tithe of grain</b> to Jerusalem, i.e., from Eretz Yisrael, <b>may you bring up a firstborn</b> to the Temple as an offering.
<b>But from a place that you may not bring up the tithe of grain,</b> i.e., outside Eretz Yisrael, <b>you may not bring up a firstborn</b> from there. Therefore, the statement of the mishna that if one did bring up an unblemished firstborn from outside Eretz Yisrael it may be sacrificed, is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yishmael. By contrast, the statement that ben Antigonus brought firstborn offerings from Babylonia and they were not accepted as offerings is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Akiva.
§ The Gemara relates the third statement in the <i>baraita</i>, which discusses second tithe: <b>Ben Azzai says</b> that one <b>might</b> have thought that he may <b>bring up second tithe and eat it in any</b> place <b>that overlooks</b> Jerusalem.<b>But</b> could this matter not be derived through <b>logical inference: A firstborn</b> offering <b>requires</b> that it be <b>brought</b> to the <b>place,</b> i.e., Jerusalem, <b>and</b> a second <b>tithe requires</b> that it be <b>brought</b> to the <b>place. Just as a firstborn</b> animal <b>is eaten only within the walls</b> of Jerusalem, <b>so too,</b> second <b>tithe is eaten only within the walls</b> of Jerusalem.
This inference may be refuted: <b>What</b> is unique <b>about a firstborn?</b> It is unique in <b>that it requires the placement of the blood and sacrificial portions upon the altar. Shall you</b> also <b>say</b> that this is the case <b>with regard to</b> second <b>tithe, where</b> that <b>is not</b> required? Therefore, <b>the verse states: “And you shall eat before the Lord your God,</b> in the place which He shall choose to cause His name to dwell there, <b>the tithe of your grain, of your wine, and of your oil, and the firstborn of your herd and of your flock.”</b> The verse <b>juxtaposes</b> second <b>tithe</b> and the <b>firstborn,</b> to teach that <b>just as the firstborn is eaten only within the walls, so too,</b> second <b>tithe is eaten only within the walls.</b>
The Gemara comments: <b>What is difficult for</b> ben Azzai <b>that</b> prompted him to <b>say:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that it is permitted to eat second tithe in any place that overlooks Jerusalem? The Gemara explains: <b>You</b> can <b>say</b> that it is difficult for him <b>since we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Megilla</i> 9b): <b>The difference between</b> the Tabernacle in <b>Shiloh and</b> the Temple in <b>Jerusalem is only that in Shiloh one eats offerings of lesser sanctity,</b> e.g., individual peace offerings, thanks offerings, and the Paschal lamb, <b>and</b> also <b>the second tithe, in any place that overlooks</b> Shiloh,<b>but in Jerusalem</b> one eats those consecrated items only <b>within the walls.</b>
<b>And likewise, offerings of the most sacred order</b> are eaten only in the area <b>within the hangings,</b> which surrounded the courtyard in the Tabernacle in Shiloh, which was equivalent to the area within the surrounding wall in the Temple courtyard in Jerusalem. <b>Lest you say</b> that even <b>in Jerusalem, they may bring second tithe</b> and eat it <b>in any</b> location <b>that overlooks</b> the walls, since, unlike the firstborn animal, second tithe does not possess any unique stringency, the verse <b>teaches us</b> that this is not so, as taught in the mishna.
§ Rabbi Yosei cites one more statement in the <i>baraita</i> with regard to a firstborn: The mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 26b) states that a firstborn animal is eaten from one year to the next, i.e., within its first year, as it is stated: “You shall eat it before the Lord your God year by year” (Deuteronomy 15:20). Rabbi Yosei adds that <b>others say:</b> One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>a firstborn whose first year has passed should</b> have the same status <b>as disqualified consecrated</b> animals <b>and</b> therefore <b>be disqualified.</b>
Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “And you shall eat before the Lord your God…<b>the tithe of your grain, of your wine, and of your oil,</b> and the firstborn of your herd and of your flock.” The verse thereby <b>juxtaposes a firstborn</b> animal <b>to</b> second <b>tithe,</b> which teaches that <b>just as</b> second <b>tithe is not disqualified from</b> one <b>year to the next, so too, a firstborn</b> animal <b>is not disqualified from</b> one <b>year to the next.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to the Rabbis,</b> i.e., Rabbi Yishmael, Rabbi Akiva, and ben Azzai, <b>who expound</b> this verse <b>for another explanation, from where do they</b> derive that a firstborn animal is not disqualified <b>from</b> one <b>year to the next?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>They derive it from</b> the verse <b>“You shall eat it before the Lord your God year by year.”</b> The formulation “year by year” indicates two years, thereby <b>teaching with regard to a firstborn</b> animal <b>that it is not disqualified from</b> one <b>year to the next.</b>
The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>the others,</b> who derive this <i>halakha</i> from the juxtaposition of the firstborn and second tithe, with regard to <b>what do they interpret</b> the verse: <b>“You shall eat it before the Lord your God year by year”?</b> The Gemara answers: <b>It is necessary for that which is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to the time for the consumption of a firstborn animal: The phrase “year by year” teaches that there is a manner in which it may be eaten over two years: During <b>one day of this year, and</b> during <b>one day of the next year.</b> The verse therefore <b>teaches with regard to a firstborn</b> offering <b>that it may be eaten for two days and one night</b> in between.
The Gemara asks: <b>And the Rabbis,</b> Rabbi Yishmael, Rabbi Akiva, and ben Azzai, <b>from where do they</b> derive the <i>halakha</i> <b>that</b> a firstborn offering <b>may be eaten for two days and one night?</b> The Gemara answers that <b>the verse states</b> with regard to the firstborn: “And their flesh <b>shall be yours, like the breast of the waving</b> and the right thigh, it shall be yours” (Numbers 18:18). The repetition of the expression “It shall be yours” teaches that one may eat the firstborn for one more day than a standard thanks offering, which may be eaten only for one day and one night.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>The offspring of a sin offering and the substitute for a sin offering, and a sin offering whose owner has died shall</b> be sequestered and left to <b>die. And</b> with regard to a sin offering that is unfit for sacrifice because <b>its</b> first <b>year</b> from birth <b>has passed, and</b> a sin offering <b>that was lost and</b> when it was <b>found,</b> it was <b>blemished, if</b> it was <b>after the owner achieved atonement</b> through sacrifice of another animal as a sin offering, the blemished animal <b>shall die, and it does not render</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b>

Daf 22a

Furthermore, one <b>may not derive benefit</b> from any of these sin offerings <i>ab initio</i>, <b>but</b> if one derived benefit from them, after the fact, he is <b>not</b> liable to bring a sin offering for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated items. <b>And if</b> the lost animal was found and discovered to be unfit <b>before the owner achieved atonement</b> for his sin with a different animal, <b>it shall graze until it becomes blemished, and</b> then <b>it shall be sold. And he must bring another</b> sin offering <b>with the money</b> received from the sale. <b>And</b> this animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute, and</b> one who derives benefit from this animal is liable for <b>misusing it.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> Although there are five types of sin offerings that are sequestered and left to die, the mishna initially states: The offspring of a sin offering, and the substitute for a sin offering, and a sin offering whose owner has died shall be sequestered and left to die, and only then mentions the remaining two types, a sin offering that is more than one year old and a sin offering that was lost and then found after the owner achieved atonement through the sacrifice of another animal. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason</b> the mishna <b>does not teach them</b> all <b>together?</b> The Gemara responds: With regard to those sin offerings taught in <b>the first clause,</b> there is <b>an absolute</b> rule that applies equally in all circumstances. But with regard to the remaining types taught in <b>the latter clause, there is no absolute</b> rule, as these sin offerings are left to die only if the owner has already achieved atonement with another animal.
The Gemara asks: This mishna also appears in tractate <i>Me’ila</i> (10b). <b>Why do I</b> need the <i>tanna</i> <b>to teach</b> this mishna <b>in</b> tractate <b><i>Me’ila</i></b> and <b>why do I</b> need him <b>to teach it in</b> tractate <b><i>Temura</i>?</b> The Gemara responds: <b>Here,</b> in <i>Temura</i>, the mishna <b>taught</b> the <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to <b>substitution,</b> which is the central theme of tractate <i>Temura</i>. <b>And since it taught</b> the <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to <b>substitution, it also taught</b> the relevant <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to <b>misuse of consecrated property. And</b> similarly, <b>since it taught in</b> tractate <b><i>Me’ila</i></b> the <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to <b>misuse of consecrated property,</b> which is the subject of tractate <i>Me’ila</i>, <b>it also taught</b> the relevant <i>halakhot</i> pertaining to <b>substitution.</b>
§ <b>Reish Lakish says:</b> With regard to <b>a sin offering whose</b> first <b>year</b> from birth <b>has passed, one considers it as though it is standing in a cemetery,</b> where a priest cannot enter in order to retrieve it and sacrifice it, <b>and</b> it is left to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish, after which it is sold and the money received in its sale is used to purchase another animal for a sin offering. Since Reish Lakish makes no distinction between an owner who already achieved atonement with another animal and one who did not, it seems that according to Reish Lakish, this is the <i>halakha</i> in all cases of a sin offering whose first year from birth has passed.
The Gemara objects: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: With regard to a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year</b> from birth <b>has passed and</b> a sin offering that <b>was lost and</b> when it was <b>found</b> it was <b>blemished, if</b> it was <b>after the owner achieved atonement</b> through the sacrifice of another animal as a sin offering, the blemished animal is left to <b>die.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Reish Lakish.</b>
The Gemara explains that <b>Reish Lakish</b> could <b>say to you: When</b> the mishna <b>teaches</b> in <b>the first clause</b> that the animal is sequestered and left to <b>die,</b> it is referring only <b>to</b> the case where it was <b>lost and</b> when it was <b>found</b> it was <b>blemished.</b> In that situation, <b>if</b> it was <b>after the owner achieved atonement</b> through the sacrifice of another animal as a sin offering, the blemished animal <b>shall</b> be left to <b>die.</b> By contrast, a sin offering whose first year from birth has passed is indeed left to graze until it develops a blemish.
The Gemara objects: <b>If so, say the latter clause: If</b> the lost animal was found and discovered to be unfit <b>before the owner achieved atonement</b> for his sin with a different animal, <b>it shall graze until it becomes blemished.</b> Now, <b>if</b> the mishna is referring specifically to an animal that was lost and found when it was <b>blemished,</b> why is this statement necessary? <b>Doesn’t</b> it already <b>stand blemished?</b>
<b>Rabba said</b> in response that <b>this</b> is what <b>the <i>tanna</i> is saying:</b> In a case <b>where</b> it was <b>lost and</b> subsequently <b>found</b> when it <b>had a temporary blemish,</b> then <b>if</b> it was <b>after the owner achieved atonement</b> with another sin offering, the blemished animal <b>shall</b> be left to <b>die.</b> But <b>if</b> this was <b>before the owner achieved atonement</b> with another sin offering, <b>it shall graze until it becomes blemished with a permanent blemish, and</b> then <b>it shall be sold.</b>
<b>Rava says:</b> There are <b>two refutations of</b> Rabba’s <b>statement: One</b> refutation is <b>that if</b> it is <b>so,</b> that the mishna is referring to an animal that was lost and then found when it had a temporary blemish, then instead of stating: It shall graze until it becomes blemished, the mishna <b>should have taught: One observes</b> the animal to see whether the temporary blemish becomes permanent. <b>And furthermore,</b> according to this interpretation of the mishna, when it mentions the case of a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year</b> from birth <b>has passed, with regard to what <i>halakha</i> is</b> that case <b>taught?</b>
<b>Rather, Rava said</b> a different explanation of the mishna so that it does not contradict the statement of Reish Lakish: <b>This</b> is what the mishna <b>is teaching:</b> With regard to a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year</b> from birth <b>has passed and was lost, or</b> one that <b>was lost and</b> when it was <b>found</b> it was <b>blemished,</b> which means that in each case, there are two disqualifying factors, the <i>halakha</i> is as follows: If the first animal is found <b>after the owner achieved atonement</b> with another sin offering, the animal shall be left to <b>die.</b> If it is found <b>before the owner achieved atonement</b> with another sin offering, <b>it shall graze until it becomes blemished and</b> then <b>it shall be sold.</b> Reish Lakish, by contrast, is referring to a situation where the sin offering was not lost. In such a case there is only one disqualifying factor, and therefore the animal is left to graze regardless of whether or not the owner achieved atonement with another animal.
Rava continues: <b>And</b> it was <b>necessary</b> for the mishna <b>to teach: If it was lost, with regard to</b> a sin offering that was found to be <b>blemished, and</b> also to teach that clause <b>with regard to</b> a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year has passed.</b> In other words, it was necessary for the mishna to teach that in each of these cases losing the animal means that it must be sequestered and left to die if the owner already achieved atonement with another animal. <b>As if it</b> had <b>taught</b> this <i>halakha</i> only <b>with regard to</b> a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year has passed, I would say:</b> It is only <b>there that</b> the fact that it was <b>lost is effective</b> in requiring that the animal must be left to die, <b>because it is not fit for its</b> original <b>purpose,</b> as an animal more than one year old is inherently unfit for a sin offering. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a blemished</b> animal, <b>which would</b> have been <b>fit</b> for sacrifice <b>if not for its blemish,</b> I might <b>say</b> that the fact that it was <b>lost is not effective</b> in causing the animal to be left to die.
<b>And</b> similarly, <b>if</b> the mishna had <b>taught</b> the <i>halakha</i> only <b>with regard to a blemished</b> animal, I would say: It is only <b>there that</b> the fact that it was <b>lost is effective</b> in requiring that the animal must be left to die, <b>because</b> a blemished animal <b>is not fit for sacrifice</b> at all. <b>But</b> with regard to a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year has passed, which is fit for sacrifice</b> as a different offering, I might <b>say</b> that the fact that it was <b>lost is not effective</b> in causing the animal to be left to die. Consequently, it was <b>necessary</b> for the mishna to teach the <i>halakha</i> with regard to both cases.
The Gemara asks: <b>And did Rava</b> actually <b>say this,</b> that an animal whose first year has passed and was then lost is left to die? <b>But didn’t Rava say:</b> A sin offering <b>lost at night is not called lost?</b> In other words, if the offering is lost at a time that it is not fit for sacrifice, the animal is not considered lost. Consequently, provided that the owner finds the sin offering before morning, it is left to graze and not to die, even if the owner designated another animal in its stead. By the same logic, as a sin offering whose first year has passed is not fit for sacrifice as a sin offering, it should not be considered lost even if the owner cannot locate it. Therefore, it should not be left to die.
The Gemara responds: The case of a sin offering lost at night is <b>not comparable</b> to that of a sin offering lost after its first year has passed. When a sin offering is <b>lost at night, it is not fit</b> at all, <b>neither for itself,</b> i.e., to be sacrificed, <b>nor for</b> its <b>value,</b> to be sold and the proceeds used to purchase another offering. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>this</b> sin offering that was lost after its first year has passed, <b>granted that it is not fit for itself,</b> but <b>it is fit for</b> its <b>value.</b>
The Gemara presents another challenge to the opinion of Rava: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 62a): On Yom Kippur, a pair of goats are brought to the Temple, and lots are drawn to determine which goat is to be sent to Azazel as the scapegoat, and which goat is sacrificed as a sin offering. If after the lots were drawn for both goats, the scapegoat died, another pair of goats is brought and lots are drawn for the second pair. In such a case, there are two goats that were selected as the sin offering, i.e., the remaining goat from the first pair and the goat selected from the second. One of them is sacrificed and <b>the second</b> goat <b>shall graze until it becomes blemished,</b> at which point <b>it will be sold, and the money</b> received from <b>its</b> sale <b>will be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift offerings.</b> This is <b>because a communal sin offering</b> is <b>not</b> left to <b>die.</b> One may infer from the mishna that under similar circumstances, the sin offering <b>of an individual</b> is left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara continues: <b>And</b> as the mishna does not specify which of the two goats is sacrificed as the sin offering, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> The goat from the second pair is sacrificed, since <b>living animals are rejected.</b> That is, animals that were consecrated for a specific purpose but were not fit to be used for that purpose at the proper time are permanently disqualified as a sacrifice. Accordingly, as the first goat was unfit for sacrifice when the first scapegoat died, it is permanently disqualified from the altar. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>when</b> the High Priest <b>achieves atonement</b> on behalf of the Jewish people, he <b>achieves atonement with the second</b> goat <b>of the second pair [<i>zug</i>].</b>
The Gemara concludes: <b>And the other</b> goat, i.e., the <b>first</b> goat selected as the sin offering, <b>is like</b> a sin offering <b>whose</b> first <b>year has passed,</b> in that both are disqualified due to an external factor. <b>And</b> the Gemara infers that <b>the reason</b> the first goat is not left to die is <b>that</b> it is a <b>communal</b> sin offering; <b>but</b> if it was the sin offering <b>of an individual,</b> it would be left to <b>die.</b> Evidently, a sin offering whose first year has passed is left to die even if it was not lost.
The Gemara responds: Rava could <b>say to you:</b> The case of sin offerings that were <b>rejected is discrete and</b> the case of sin offerings that were <b>lost is discrete,</b> i.e., the two cases cannot be compared. <b>What is the reason</b> for this? In the case of <b>lost</b> sin offerings, the owner’s <b>mind is on them,</b> as he thinks that <b>perhaps they</b> will be <b>found</b> and will be fit for sacrifice. Therefore, they are not left to die simply because they are lost. But in the case of sin offerings that have been <b>rejected, they do not become fit</b> ever <b>again,</b> and therefore the owner’s mind is not on them.

Daf 22b

§ The Gemara discusses <b>the</b> matter <b>itself,</b> which was cited above: <b>Rava said:</b> A sin offering <b>lost at night,</b> when one cannot sacrifice an offering, <b>is not called lost.</b> Provided that the offering is found before morning, then even if the owner designated another sin offering in its stead, the first sin offering is not left to die. The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this statement? <b>If we say</b> that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that a sin offering is not left to die unless the owner achieved atonement with another offering, <b>why</b> does Rava <b>specifically</b> mention that it was <b>lost at night? Even if it was lost during the day</b> it is not left to die, <b>as the Rabbis say:</b> A sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was <b>designated</b> in its stead, but was found before this second animal was sacrificed, is left to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish.
<b>Rather,</b> you must say that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, who maintains that if a sin offering was lost at the time when another animal was designated in its stead, the first sin offering is left to die even if it was found before the second animal was sacrificed. Rava teaches that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>states</b> his opinion <b>only</b> with regard to an animal <b>lost during the day, but</b> if the animal was <b>lost</b> only <b>at night, even Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>concedes that it goes to graze</b> and is not left to die.
<b>If you wish, say: Actually,</b> Rava’s statement is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis. And</b> as for the question of why Rava specifies that it is lost only at night when the same <i>halakha</i> should apply even if it was lost during the day, one may respond: <b>Here we are dealing with</b> an animal that is still <b>lost at the time</b> when the owner achieves <b>atonement</b> with another animal. Rava teaches that when <b>the Rabbis say</b> that a sin offering that remained <b>lost at the time</b> when the owner achieved <b>atonement</b> with another animal is left to <b>die,</b> this applies <b>only where the initial loss</b> of the animal was <b>during the day,</b> when the animal could have been sacrificed. <b>But</b> in a case <b>where the initial loss</b> of the animal was <b>at night,</b> it is <b>not</b> left to die. According to this interpretation, Rava is not merely saying that a sin offering is not classified as lost provided that it is found before morning. Rather, he maintains if it was initially lost at night it is never considered lost at all.
§ With regard to this matter, <b>Abaye said: We have a tradition</b> that a sin offering that was <b>lost</b> is left to die, <b>but not</b> a sin offering that was <b>stolen;</b> a sin offering that was <b>lost</b> is left to die, <b>but not</b> a sin offering that was <b>robbed.</b> If a sin offering was stolen or robbed, and the animal was returned after the owner achieved atonement with another animal, the first sin offering is left to graze until it develops a blemish, after which it is sold, and the proceeds are used to purchase a gift offering.
The Gemara asks: <b>What are the circumstances</b> in which an animal is considered to be <b>lost? Rabbi Oshaya says: Even one</b> sin offering intermingled with other animals <b>in</b> the owner’s <b>flock,</b> to the extent that he cannot discern which animal is the sin offering, is considered lost. <b>And even one</b> sin offering intermingled <b>with one</b> non-sacred animal, so that the owner does not know which is the sin offering, is considered lost. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> An animal found <b>behind the door</b> is considered to have been lost.
<b>A dilemma was raised before the</b> Sages with regard to the statement of Rabbi Yoḥanan: With regard to <b>what</b> case <b>is he speaking?</b> Does Rabbi Yoḥanan mean that <b>it is</b> only when the animal is found <b>behind the door</b> that it is considered to have been lost, <b>as there is no one who sees it, but</b> if the animal was <b>outside</b> and was intermingled with other animals, <b>where there are</b> people <b>who see it, it is not</b> considered to be <b>lost? Or perhaps</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan means that even when the animal is found <b>behind the door, where if</b> he would <b>turn his face he</b> would <b>see it,</b> the animal <b>is</b> considered <b>lost, and all the more so</b> if the animal was <b>outside, where he does not see it,</b> it is considered lost. Since no resolution is offered, the Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
The Gemara further discusses a sin offering that was lost. <b>Rav Pappa said: We learn</b> through tradition that if a sin offering is <b>lost from</b> the owner <b>but</b> is <b>not</b> lost <b>from the shepherd</b> of the flock, i.e., the shepherd knows which animal is the sin offering, <b>it is not</b> considered <b>lost. And all the more so,</b> if the animal is <b>lost from the shepherd but not lost from</b> the owner, it is not considered lost. The Gemara asks: If the sin offering was <b>lost from</b> the owner <b>and the shepherd, but</b> there is <b>one</b> individual located far away, even <b>at the end of the world,</b> who <b>recognizes</b> the animal, <b>what</b> is the <i>halakha</i>? Again the Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
§ <b>Rav Pappa raises a dilemma:</b> In a case where a sin offering was <b>lost,</b> and the owner designated another sin offering in its stead, and the second sin offering was slaughtered, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i> if the original sin offering was found after the blood of the second offering was collected <b>in a cup</b> and stands ready to be sprinkled upon the altar? The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion does Rav Pappa raise this dilemma? <b>If we say</b> that he raises it <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, this cannot be correct, as <b>didn’t</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>say</b> that even a sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was merely <b>designated</b> in its stead is left to <b>die?</b>
<b>Rather, when</b> Rav Pappa <b>raises the dilemma,</b> he does so <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis,</b> and the dilemma is as follows: <b>Do we say</b> that <b>when the Rabbis said:</b> A sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was <b>designated</b> in its stead is left to <b>graze,</b> this applies only if the original animal was found <b>before one collects the blood</b> of the other sin offering <b>in a cup; but here,</b> where the other sin offering was already slaughtered and its blood was collected in a cup, <b>the</b> Rabbis <b>hold: Any</b> blood <b>that stands</b> ready <b>to be sprinkled</b> upon the altar <b>is considered like</b> it has already been <b>sprinkled,</b> and it is as though the owner has achieved atonement? If so, this sin offering that was found after the collection of the blood should be left to die.
<b>Or perhaps</b> the Rabbis maintain that <b>as long as the blood has not</b> been <b>sprinkled</b> on the altar, <b>it is</b> still <b>considered like</b> a case where the original offering was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was <b>designated</b> in its stead but was found by the time the second offering was brought to the altar. If so, as the first sin offering was found before the sprinkling of the blood, it should be left to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish.
<b>And there are</b> those <b>who say</b> an alternate explanation of the dilemma: <b>Actually,</b> Rav Pappa raises his dilemma <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And when</b> Rav Pappa <b>raises the dilemma,</b> it is not with regard to a case involving two sin offerings, as described above. Rather, it is with regard to a case <b>where</b> the original sin offering was slaughtered and the priest <b>collected its blood in two cups, and one of them was lost</b> at the time when the priest sprinkled the blood of the remaining cup.
The Gemara elaborates: <b>And you should not raise the dilemma according to</b> the opinion <b>of the one who said</b> that when blood is collected in two cups, one <b>cup renders</b> the blood of <b>the other</b> cup <b>rejected,</b> as in such a case, the entire offering is disqualified. Rather, <b>when should you raise the dilemma?</b> Raise it <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of the one who says</b> that one <b>cup renders the other</b> cup <b>a remainder,</b> i.e., the blood in the second cup is considered the remainder of the first cup and is poured out at the base of the altar.
The dilemma is as follows: Do we say that <b>this statement,</b> that the second cup is considered the remainder of the first cup, applies only in a case <b>where both</b> cups <b>are present, since</b> with <b>whichever</b> cup the priest <b>wants, he may sprinkle; but here,</b> since one of the cups <b>is not</b> present, the missing cup is therefore considered rejected and the offering is disqualified? <b>Or perhaps</b> it makes <b>no difference</b> whether both cups are present or not. The Gemara concludes: The dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> In the case of <b>one who designates a sin offering, and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he designated another in its stead</b> and sacrificed it, <b>and thereafter the first</b> animal <b>was found;</b> that is a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal, and <b>it shall</b> be left to <b>die.</b>
In the case of <b>one who designates money for</b> purchase of <b>his sin offering, and</b> the money <b>was lost,and he designated</b> an animal as <b>a sin offering in its stead,</b> and he sacrificed it, <b>and thereafter, the money was found,</b> it is prohibited to derive benefit from the money, as the money attains the halakhic status of the sin offering that was to be purchased with it, and that sin offering would be left to die because the owner achieved atonement with another animal. Therefore, <b>he must take</b> the money and cast it <b>into the Dead Sea,</b> from where it cannot be recovered.
In the case of <b>one who designates money for</b> purchase of his <b>sin offering, and</b> the money <b>was lost, and he designated other money in its stead, and he did not manage to purchase a sin offering with</b> that money <b>before the original</b> money <b>was found, he should bring a sin offering from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money designated in its stead, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
In the case of <b>one who designates money for</b> purchase of <b>his sin offering, and</b> the money <b>was lost, and he designated</b> an animal as <b>a sin offering in its stead,</b> and <b>he did not manage to sacrifice</b> the animal <b>before the money was found, and</b> the animal that he designated as <b>a sin offering is blemished,</b> the animal <b>shall be sold; and he brings a sin offering from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money received for the sale of the blemished animal, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
In the case of <b>one who designates a sin offering and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he designated money in its stead, and he did not manage to purchase a sin offering with</b> that money <b>before his sin offering was found, and</b> the animal <b>is blemished,</b> the animal <b>shall be sold; and he brings a sin offering from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> money that he designated <b>and that</b> money received for the sale of the blemished animal, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
In the case of <b>one who designates his sin offering and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he designated another</b> animal <b>in its stead,</b> and <b>he did not manage to sacrifice</b> the sin offering <b>before the first</b> sin offering <b>was found, and both of</b> the animals <b>are blemished,</b> the animals <b>shall be sold; and he brings a sin offering from</b> a third animal that he buys with a combination of the money from the sale of <b>this</b> animal <b>and</b> from the sale of <b>that</b> animal, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
In the case of <b>one who designates</b> his <b>sin offering and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he designated another</b> animal <b>in its stead,</b> and <b>he did not manage to sacrifice</b> the sin offering <b>before the first</b> sin offering <b>was found,</b> and <b>both of</b> the animals <b>are unblemished</b> and fit for sacrifice, <b>one of them shall be sacrificed as a sin offering and the other shall</b> be left to <b>die;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And the Rabbis say: A sin offering is not</b> left to <b>die unless it was found after</b> its <b>owner achieved atonement; and the money is not taken to the Dead Sea unless it was found after its owner achieved atonement.</b>
In the case of <b>one who designates a sin offering and</b> the animal <b>is blemished, he sells</b> the animal <b>and</b> must <b>bring another</b> sin offering <b>with the money</b> received in <b>its</b> sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says: If the second</b> animal <b>is sacrificed before</b> the first <b>is slaughtered</b> for non-sacred consumption, the first animal <b>shall</b> be left to <b>die.</b> Although it was sold and rendered non-sacred, its status is now that of a sin offering <b>whose owner already achieved atonement</b> with another animal.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The first clause of the mishna states that if one designated an animal as a sin offering and the animal was lost, and he sacrificed another animal in its stead, and subsequently the first animal was found, it is left to die. The Gemara infers: The <b>reason</b> the animal is left to die is <b>that he sacrificed another</b> animal <b>in its stead; but</b> if <b>he did not sacrifice another in its stead,</b> and instead merely designated it, then the original sin offering is left to <b>graze.</b> In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is this statement? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, who say</b> that if a sin offering was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was <b>designated</b> in its stead, the original sin offering is left to <b>graze.</b>
The Gemara continues: But now <b>say the latter clause</b> of the mishna: In the case of <b>one who designates money for</b> purchase of his <b>sin offering, and</b> the money <b>was lost, and he designated other</b> money <b>in its stead, he should bring</b> a sin offering <b>from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money designated in its stead, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. One may infer that the <b>reason</b> that the remainder is allocated for communal gift offerings is that he brings a sin offering <b>from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money designated in its stead; <b>but</b> if the owner <b>brought</b> a sin offering <b>from one of the</b> two sums of money, <b>he</b> must <b>take the other</b> sum of money and cast it <b>into the Dead Sea.</b> If so, <b>we arrive</b> at the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, <b>who said:</b> A sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when another animal was <b>designated</b> in its stead is left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara asks: Can it be that <b>the first clause</b> of the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis, while the latter clause</b> is in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? The Gemara notes: <b>This works out well according to Rav Huna, as Rav Huna said</b> that <b>Rav said:</b>

Daf 23a

<b>All concede,</b> even the Rabbis, <b>that if</b> the owner did not consult the court about which animal to sacrifice, but instead he <b>took one</b> of the animals <b>and sacrificed it,</b> he has demonstrated a lack of concern for the fate of the remaining animal, and <b>that</b> therefore <b>the second</b> animal is left to <b>die.</b> Consequently, <b>you find</b> that both clauses of the mishna are consistent with the opinion of the Rabbis, as one may interpret the latter clause as referring to a case <b>where</b> the owner <b>took one of the</b> two sums of money, used it to purchase an animal, <b>and sacrificed</b> the animal, <b>and</b> in such a case, <b>all agree</b> that the other sum of money is cast into the Dead Sea.
<b>But according to</b> that which <b>Rabbi Abba</b> says that <b>Rav says,</b> the mishna cannot be interpreted in this manner. <b>As</b> Rabbi Abba <b>said: All concede</b> that <b>in</b> a case where <b>one achieves atonement through the one that was not lost, the one that was lost</b> is left to <b>die. With regard to what</b> case do the Rabbis and Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>disagree?</b> They disagree <b>with regard to</b> the case of <b>one who achieves atonement with the one that was lost. As Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that if one <b>designates</b> a sin offering instead of one that was <b>lost, it is considered like the lost</b> sin offering. Accordingly, just as the lost animal is left to die if the second sin offering was sacrificed, the replacement animal is left to die if the original animal was sacrificed. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that if one designates a sin offering instead of one that was lost, <b>it is not considered like the lost</b> animal. Rather, if the original animal is sacrificed, the replacement animal is left to graze, as it was never lost.
Therefore, one must say that according to this explanation, the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught us the first clause</b> of the mishna in <b>an unattributed</b> manner <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> and he <b>taught us the latter clause</b> in <b>an unattributed</b> manner <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara asks: <b>And what</b> is the <i>tanna</i> <b>teaching us</b> by teaching its clauses in accordance with different opinions? Presumably, he is teaching us <b>that Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and the Rabbis disagree</b> about this matter. But the <i>tanna</i> already <b>teaches the dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and the Rabbis explicitly in the latter clause:</b> In the case of <b>one who designates</b> his <b>sin offering and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he designated another</b> animal <b>in its stead, and thereafter the first</b> sin offering <b>was found,</b> and <b>both of</b> the animals <b>stand</b> fit for sacrifice, <b>one of them shall be sacrificed and the other shall</b> be left to <b>die;</b> this is <b>the statement of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. <b>And the Rabbis say: A sin offering is not</b> left to <b>die unless it was found after</b> its <b>owner achieved atonement; and the money is not taken to the Dead Sea unless it was found after its owner achieved atonement.</b>
The Gemara answers: <b>This</b> is what the <i>tanna</i> <b>is teaching us</b> in the latter clause, that <b>this matter</b> that was presented in a contradictory manner between the first and second clauses of the mishna is actually <b>a dispute</b> between <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>and the Rabbis.</b>
§ The Gemara returns to <b>the</b> matter <b>itself,</b> which was cited above: <b>Rav Huna says</b> that <b>Rav says: All concede that if</b> the owner did not consult the court about which animal to sacrifice but instead <b>took one</b> of the animals <b>and sacrificed it, the second</b> animal is left to <b>die. They disagree only in</b> a case where the owner <b>comes to consult</b> the court. <b>As Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds:</b> The Sages <b>did not</b> care to <b>enact</b> a protective <b>ordinance with regard to consecrated</b> items, <b>and</b> it was of no concern to them that one of the animals would be left to die. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>we say</b> to the owner: <b>Go achieve atonement with</b> the sin offering <b>that was not lost, and the</b> one that was <b>lost</b> shall be left to <b>die. And the Rabbis hold:</b> The Sages <b>enacted</b> a protective <b>ordinance with regard to consecrated</b> items, <b>and we</b> therefore <b>say</b> to the owner: <b>Go achieve atonement with</b> the sin offering <b>that was lost, and the</b> one <b>that was not lost shall</b> be left to <b>graze.</b>
<b>Rav Mesharshiyya raises an objection: And</b> is there such an opinion that the Sages <b>did not</b> care to <b>enact</b> a protective <b>ordinance with regard to consecrated</b> items, to prevent them from being left to die? <b>But isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> with regard to a verse that deals with the remainder of a meal offering: “And that which is left thereof shall Aaron and his sons eat; it shall be eaten without leaven in a holy place; in the court of the Tent of Meeting they shall eat it” (Leviticus 6:9). <b>Why</b> must <b>the verse state</b> at the end the apparently redundant expression: <b>“They shall eat”?</b> The verse <b>teaches that if</b> the remainder constitutes <b>a small</b> amount for <b>consumption,</b> the priests <b>eat non-sacred</b> food <b>and <i>teruma</i> with it, so that</b> the remainder <b>will be eaten while satiated.</b>
Furthermore, <b>why</b> must <b>the verse state: “They shall eat it”?</b> The verse teaches <b>that if</b> the remainder constitutes <b>a large</b> amount for <b>consumption,</b> e.g., there are many remainders from the meal offerings, then <b>one does not eat non-sacred</b> food <b>or <i>teruma</i> with it, so that it will not be eaten</b> in <b>an excessive</b> manner. Otherwise, one might fail to consume the entire remainder, and some of it would be rendered leftover from an offering after the time allotted for its consumption [<i>notar</i>].
Rav Mesharshiyya concludes his objection: This <i>baraita</i> teaches that an ordinance was enacted in order to prevent the remainder of a meal offering from being rendered <i>notar</i>. The same reasoning should apply to other consecrated items, including a sin offering. <b>What, is it not</b> that this <i>baraita</i> is stated <b>even</b> according to the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi? The Gemara responds: <b>No,</b> this <i>baraita</i> is stated specifically according to the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Abba says</b> that <b>Rav</b> says: <b>All concede</b> that <b>in</b> a case where <b>one achieves atonement through the one that was not lost, the one that was lost</b> is left to <b>die. With regard to what</b> case do the Rabbis and Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>disagree?</b> They disagree with regard to a case <b>where one achieves atonement with the one that was lost. As Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>holds</b> that if one <b>designates</b> a sin offering instead of one that was <b>lost, it is considered like the lost</b> sin offering. Therefore, the second animal is left to die. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that if one designates a sin offering instead of one that was lost, <b>it is not considered like the lost</b> animal. Accordingly, the second animal is left to graze.
The Gemara objects to the explanation of Rabbi Abba in the name of Rav: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 62a): On Yom Kippur, a pair of goats is brought to the Temple, where lots are drawn to determine which goat is sent to Azazel as the scapegoat, and which goat is sacrificed as a sin offering. If the scapegoat died after the lots were drawn for both goats, another pair of goats is brought and lots are drawn for the second pair. This means that there are two goats that were selected as the sin offering, i.e., the remaining goat from the first pair and the goat selected from the second pair. One of these is sacrificed; and <b>the second</b> goat <b>shall graze until it becomes blemished, and it shall</b> then <b>be sold, and the money</b> received from <b>its</b> sale <b>will be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings. This is <b>because a communal sin offering</b> is <b>not</b> left to <b>die.</b> One may infer from the mishna that under similar circumstances, the sin offering <b>of an individual</b> is left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara continues its objection: <b>And Rav says</b> with regard to this mishna: <b>Living animals are not rejected.</b> In other words, the sin offering from the first pair is not disqualified on account of the death of the first scapegoat. And therefore, <b>when he achieves atonement,</b> he may even <b>achieve atonement with the second</b> goat <b>of the first pair. And the other</b> sin offering from the <b>latter</b> pair <b>is like</b> an animal <b>designated</b> instead of an offering that was <b>lost,</b> as the second pair was brought due to the death of the first scapegoat. <b>And</b> the mishna states that <b>the reason</b> the remaining sin offering is not left to die is <b>that</b> it is <b>a communal</b> sin offering, <b>but</b> if it were the sin offering <b>of an individual,</b> it would be left to <b>die.</b>

Daf 23b

The Gemara concludes: <b>What, is it not</b> that this mishna is stated <b>even</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis?</b> If so, the mishna is teaching that even according to the Rabbis, an offering designated in the stead of a lost animal is considered like the lost animal, which would contradict the statement of Rabbi Abba. The Gemara responds: <b>No, it is</b> stated only in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara poses a challenge to the explanations of both Rav Huna and Rabbi Abba from that which <b>we learned</b> in the mishna: In the case of <b>one who designates a sin offering, and</b> the animal <b>was lost, and he</b> designated <b>another in its stead and sacrificed</b> it, and the first animal was subsequently found, that is a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal, and <b>it shall</b> be left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara infers: The <b>reason</b> that the original animal is left to die is <b>that</b> the second animal was already <b>sacrificed, but</b> if the owner <b>did not sacrifice</b> the second animal before the original animal was found, then the original animal is left to <b>graze.</b> The mishna apparently indicates that there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>achieves atonement with the</b> sin offering that was <b>lost</b> and there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>achieves atonement with the</b> sin offering <b>that was not lost.</b> It can likewise be inferred from the mishna that there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>took</b> the animal and sacrificed it without consulting the court and there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>did not take</b> the animal and sacrifice it without consulting the court. In all cases, if the owner had not yet achieved atonement then the remaining animal is left to graze.
If so, this is a <b>conclusive refutation of</b> the opinions of <b>both of them,</b> since according to Rav Huna everyone agrees that an animal is left to die if the owner took one of the animals without consulting the court; and according to Rabbi Abba everyone agrees that if the owner achieved atonement with the second animal, the lost animal is left to die.
The Gemara responds: One should not infer in that manner from the mishna. Rather, the <i>tanna</i> of the mishna <b>taught a matter that was absolute,</b> i.e., a case where the second animal was sacrificed before the original animal was found, as the lost animal is always left to die. <b>He did not teach a matter that was not absolute,</b> i.e., a matter that depends on some other factor, e.g., whether the owner took the animal without consulting the court, or whether the owner achieved atonement with the lost animal.
The Gemara poses another difficulty from that which <b>we learned</b> in the mishna: In the case of <b>one who designates money for</b> purchase of his <b>sin offering, and</b> the money <b>was lost, and he designated other</b> money <b>in its stead, and thereafter the original</b> money <b>was found, he should bring a sin offering from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money designated in its stead, <b>and the remainder shall be allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
