The Gemara infers: The <b>reason</b> that the remainder is allocated for communal gift offerings is <b>that he atones</b> by bringing a sin offering <b>from</b> a combination of <b>this</b> original money <b>and that</b> money designated in its stead, <b>but</b> if the owner brought a sin offering <b>from one of</b> the two sums of money, <b>he</b> must <b>take</b> the other money and cast it <b>into the Dead Sea.</b>
The mishna apparently indicates that there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>achieves atonement with the</b> money that was <b>lost and</b> there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>achieves atonement with the</b> money <b>that was not lost, and</b> there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>took</b> the money and brought a sin offering without consulting the court <b>and</b> there <b>is no difference</b> whether he <b>did not take</b> the money without consulting the court. In all cases, the other money is cast into the Dead Sea. If so, this is <b>a conclusive refutation of</b> the opinions of <b>both</b> Rav Huna and Rabbi Abba, as the mishna does not conform to either explanation. The Gemara responds: <b>Here too,</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>taught a matter that was absolute,</b> i.e., a case where the owner achieved atonement with one of the two sums of money, <b>but he did not teach a matter that was not absolute.</b>
§ With regard to designating money for purchasing a sin offering, <b>Rabbi Ami says:</b> In the case of <b>one who designates two piles of money as a guarantee,</b> so that in the event that one pile is lost he will purchase his sin offering with the other pile, <b>he achieves atonement with one of them, and the second</b> pile is <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion is this statement? <b>If we say</b> that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, it is <b>obvious that</b> the second pile of money is <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, as <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> that the surplus money is cast into the Dead Sea <b>only</b> in a case <b>where</b> one <b>designated</b> the money instead of money that was <b>lost. But</b> if one separated the two sums of money at the same time <b>as a guarantee,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi <b>concedes</b> that the unused money is allocated for gift offerings.
<b>Rather,</b> you will say that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of the Rabbis.</b> But if so, isn’t it <b>obvious that</b> the unused money is <b>allocated for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings, due to the following <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>Now</b> that <b>the Rabbis say</b> with regard to <b>one who designates</b> money to replace money that was <b>lost</b> that it <b>is not considered like</b> the <b>lost</b> money and is not cast into the Dead Sea, <b>is it necessary</b> to teach that money designated <b>as a guarantee</b> is allocated for communal gift offerings and is not cast into the sea?
<b>Rather, it was necessary for</b> Rabbi Ami to teach this <i>halakha</i> <b>according to</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon,</b> who holds that whenever the owner achieved atonement with another sin offering the remaining animal is left to die, even if it was initially designated as a guarantee. <b>Lest you say</b> that just as <b>Rabbi Shimon does not hold</b> that an animal is left to graze if the owner achieved atonement with another animal, he also does not maintain that the unused money is allocated for communal <b>gift</b> offerings, Rabbi Ami <b>teaches us that</b> even Rabbi Shimon <b>holds</b> that if one separates additional money as a guarantee, the unused money is allocated for communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>But how can you say</b> that one might think that <b>Rabbi Shimon does not hold</b> that the unused money is allocated for communal <b>gift</b> offerings? <b>Isn’t it taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 18a): <b>There were thirteen</b> collection <b>horns in the Temple, and</b> the intended use of the funds <b>was written upon</b> each one, as follows: <b>New shekels,</b> for shekels given for the current year; <b>and old</b> shekels, for shekels given belatedly for the previous year; <b>pairs</b> of birds, whose funds were used to purchase obligatory bird offerings, e.g., for a woman who gave birth; <b>and fledglings</b> designated for voluntary <b>burnt offerings; wood</b> for the arrangement on the altar; <b>and frankincense</b> that accompanied meal offerings; <b>and gold</b> donated <b>for the Ark Cover. And</b> the remaining <b>six</b> horns were designated <b>for</b> the purchase of communal <b>gift</b> offerings.
<b>And it is taught with regard to</b> that mishna: The funds contained in the <b>six</b> horns designated <b>for</b> communal <b>gift</b> offerings were used <b>to</b> purchase <b>burnt offerings that come from the surplus</b> funds which had been allocated for the purchase of sin offerings or guilt offerings and were not needed for those purposes. Such burnt offerings were brought in the absence of other offerings, so that the altar would not remain idle. Unlike other burnt offerings, the <b>hides</b> of these offerings were <b>not</b> given <b>to the priests;</b> rather, they were sold, and the money received from their sale was allocated for additional communal gift offerings. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yehuda.</b>
<b>Rabbi Neḥemya said to him, and some say</b> it was <b>Rabbi Shimon</b> who said to Rabbi Yehuda: <b>If so, the teaching of Jehoiada the</b> High <b>Priest</b> has been <b>negated. As it is taught</b> in the aforementioned mishna: <b>This teaching was taught</b> by <b>Jehoiada the</b> High <b>Priest:</b> There is an apparent contradiction in a verse. On the one hand, the verse states: <b>“It is a guilt offering”</b> (Leviticus 5:19), indicating that the meat of this guilt offering is eaten by the priests, as is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to all guilt offerings. On the other hand, the same verse concludes: “He is certainly guilty before the Lord,” which indicates that the <b>entire</b> offering is for God, like a burnt offering. Rather, the verse comes <b>to include</b> the following <i>halakha</i>: With regard to <b>anything that comes from surplus</b> funds designated for <b>sin offerings or guilt offerings,</b> those <b>funds</b> should be used <b>to purchase burnt offerings. The meat</b> of these burnt offerings <b>is</b> entirely <b>for God, and the hides</b> are given <b>to the priests,</b> as is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to burnt offerings.
The Gemara concludes: <b>Apparently, Rabbi Shimon does hold</b> that surplus funds which were designated for sin offerings are allocated for communal <b>gift</b> offerings. The statement of Rabbi Ami is therefore unnecessary even according to the opinion of Rabbi Shimon. The Gemara responds: It <b>was necessary</b> for Rabbi Ami to teach this <i>halakha</i>, as it might <b>enter your mind to say: When Rabbi Shimon holds</b> that surplus money which was designated for a sin offering is allocated for communal <b>gift</b> offerings, that applies only <b>in</b> a case where the surplus is <b>from one set</b> of money, e.g., one allocated funds to purchase a goat and the price of goats depreciated, and therefore there is now a surplus from the money that was set aside.

Daf 24a

<b>But in</b> a case where one designates <b>two</b> distinct <b>sets</b> of money, such as an additional set of money as a guarantee, Rabbi Shimon does <b>not</b> state that the surplus money is allocated for communal gift offerings. Rabbi Ami therefore <b>teaches us</b> that even in this case, Rabbi Shimon agrees that the surplus money is allocated for communal gift offerings.
§ <b>Rabbi Hoshaya says:</b> In the case of <b>one who designates two</b> animals as <b>sin offerings as a guarantee,</b> so that if one animal is lost he will achieve atonement with the other animal, <b>he achieves atonement with one of them, and the other</b> is left to <b>graze</b> until it develops a blemish. It is then sold, and the money received from its sale is used to purchase communal gift offerings.
The Gemara asks: <b>In accordance with whose</b> opinion does Rabbi Hoshaya state this <i>halakha</i>? <b>If we say</b> that it is <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> then it is unnecessary. The Gemara explains: <b>Now</b> that in a case where <b>one designates</b> a sin offering instead of one that was <b>lost, the Rabbis say</b> that it is <b>not considered like</b> the <b>lost</b> animal, and it is therefore left to graze, <b>is it necessary</b> to state that in a case where one designates an additional sin offering as a <b>guarantee,</b> the second animal is left to graze?
<b>Rather,</b> you will say that Rabbi Hoshaya states this <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Shimon.</b> But <b>didn’t Rabbi Shimon say</b> that there are <b>five sin offerings</b> that are left to <b>die,</b> one of which is a sin offering whose owner has achieved atonement with another animal? If so, even in a case where one designated an additional animal as a guarantee, once the owner has achieved atonement the remaining animal is left to die.
<b>Rather,</b> Rabbi Hoshaya states this <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi. Rabbi Hoshaya is teaching that <b>when Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>said</b> that a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal is left to die, that applies only <b>in</b> a case where one designated the second animal instead of <b>a lost</b> sin offering. <b>But</b> in a case where one initially designated an additional sin offering as a <b>guarantee,</b> Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi does <b>not</b> hold that the remaining offering is left to die.
The Gemara poses a challenge to this explanation: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna (22b): In the case of <b>one who designates a sin offering and</b> the animal <b>is blemished, he sells</b> the animal <b>and brings another</b> sin offering <b>in its stead</b> with the money received in its sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says: If the second</b> animal <b>is sacrificed before</b> the first <b>is slaughtered</b> for non-sacred consumption, the first animal <b>shall</b> be left to <b>die, as</b> it is considered an animal <b>whose owner already achieved atonement</b> with another animal.
The Gemara continues: It <b>enters your mind</b> to say <b>that Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, who says that if one achieved atonement with one sin offering, the remaining animal is left to die. Likewise, even if the remaining animal is no longer sacred, it is left to die if the owner achieved atonement with the other animal. <b>And</b> if so, <b>even</b> if one initially designated an additional sin offering as <b>a guarantee,</b> the remaining animal should <b>also</b> be left to die. Consequently, the statement of Rabbi Hoshaya cannot be in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara responds: <b>No, perhaps Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon,</b> does not hold in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi. Rather, he <b>holds in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>his father,</b> Rabbi Shimon, <b>who said</b> that there are <b>five sin offerings</b> left to <b>die,</b> one of which is a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal. Since Rabbi Shimon does not qualify this statement, he evidently maintains that the remaining sin offering is always left to die, even when it is no longer sacred. If so, the statement of Rabbi Hoshaya can be in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi.
The Gemara poses a challenge to the statement of Rabbi Hoshaya: <b>We learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Yoma</i> 62a): With regard to the lottery involving the two goats brought on Yom Kippur, if after the lots were drawn for both goats, the scapegoat died, another pair of goats is brought and lots are drawn for the second pair. With regard to the two goats selected to be the sin offering, i.e., the remaining goat from the first pair and the goat selected from the second, one is sacrificed and the second goat shall graze until it becomes blemished, after which it is sold and the money received is allocated for communal gift offerings. This is <b>because a communal sin offering</b> is <b>not</b> left to <b>die.</b> One may infer from the mishna that under similar circumstances, the sin offering <b>of an individual</b> is left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara continues: And as the mishna does not specify which of the two goats is brought as the sin offering, <b>Rav says</b> that <b>living animals are not rejected.</b> In other words, the sin offering from the first pair is not disqualified on account of the death of the first scapegoat. And therefore, <b>when he achieves atonement,</b> he may <b>achieve atonement</b> even <b>with the second</b> goat <b>of the first pair.</b> The Gemara explains the difficulty: <b>And the other</b> goat that was selected as the sin offering from the second pair <b>is</b> like one that was initially consecrated <b>as a guarantee,</b> as it was not consecrated to replace a lost offering. <b>And</b> yet the mishna <b>teaches</b> that in a comparable case involving the sin offering <b>of an individual,</b> it is left to <b>die.</b> This apparently contradicts the ruling of Rabbi Hoshaya.
The Gemara responds: <b>Rav</b> conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning, as he said:</b> It is a <b>mitzva</b> to sacrifice <b>the first</b> offering that was designated. Consequently, when the second sin offering is consecrated, it is already known that it will not be sacrificed. It is therefore considered like an animal designated instead of one that was lost. But in a case where one initially designates an additional sin offering as a guarantee, he is permitted to achieve atonement with either animal <i>ab initio</i>. Therefore, the one that is not sacrificed is left to graze.
§ With regard to the dispute between Rabbi Yehuda HaNasi and the Rabbis, <b>Rav Shimi bar Ziri taught</b> the following <i>baraita</i> <b>before Rav Pappa:</b> In a case where a sin offering was <b>lost at the time</b> when one <b>designated</b> a sin offering in its stead, and one found the lost sin offering before sacrificing the replacement, there is a dispute between <i>tanna’im</i>. <b>According to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, the offering that is not sacrificed is left to <b>die,</b> and <b>according to the Rabbis it shall</b> be left to <b>graze.</b> If the first sin offering was <b>lost at the time</b> when one achieved atonement with another animal, then <b>according to the Rabbis</b> the first sin offering is left to <b>die,</b> and <b>according to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi it is left to <b>graze.</b>
Rav Pappa said to Rav Shimi bar Ziri: This cannot be correct, due to <b>an <i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>And what,</b> if in the case of a sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when its owner <b>designated</b> an animal in its stead, <b>where the Rabbis say</b> that the offering that is not sacrificed is left to <b>graze,</b> and yet <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says</b> it is left to <b>die,</b> then in the case of a sin offering that was <b>lost at the time</b> when its owner achieved atonement with another animal, <b>where according to the Rabbis</b> the remaining animal is left to <b>die,</b> is it <b>not all the more so</b> true that <b>according to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi it is left to die?
<b>Rather, teach</b> the <i>baraita</i> in <b>this</b> manner: If a sin offering was <b>lost at the time</b> when one <b>designated</b> an animal in its stead, and the owner found the lost sin offering before sacrificing the replacement, <b>according to Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi the offering that is not sacrificed is left to <b>die,</b> and <b>according to the Rabbis</b> it is left to <b>graze.</b> If the first sin offering was lost <b>at the time</b> when one achieved atonement with another animal, <b>all agree</b> that it is left to <b>die.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: In the case of one who designates a sin offering and the animal is blemished, he sells the animal and must bring another sin offering with the money received from its sale. <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says:</b> If the second animal is sacrificed before the first is slaughtered for non-sacred consumption, the first animal shall be left to die. Although it was sold and rendered non-sacred, its status is that of a sin offering whose owner already achieved atonement with another animal.
<b>The Sages taught: One may not flay</b> the skin of an animal <b>from its feet on a Festival.</b> Although it is permitted to slaughter and skin an animal on a Festival, one may not do so in such a manner to retain the hide intact to enable it to function as a vessel, e.g., a water skin. <b>Similarly, one may not flay a firstborn</b> kosher animal <b>from its feet</b> even on a weekday, and even if the animal is blemished, <b>nor</b> may one flay from the feet <b>disqualified consecrated</b> animals that have been redeemed, as flaying from the feet is considered degrading.
The Gemara asks: <b>Granted,</b> everyone agrees that one may not flay an animal from its feet <b>on a Festival, as one</b> who does so <b>exerts effort that is not fit for</b> the Festival. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>a firstborn</b> offering, <b>who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> that flaying from its feet is prohibited?
<b>Rav Ḥisda said: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai, who say</b> that <b>a firstborn</b> animal <b>retains its sanctity</b> even after it becomes blemished. <b>As we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 32b) that <b>Beit Shammai say: An Israelite may not be granted a portion</b> of a blemished <b>firstborn</b> animal along <b>with a priest.</b> Just as Beit Shammai prohibit an Israelite from partaking of even a blemished firstborn animal, they prohibit flaying the skin of a firstborn animal from its feet.
The Gemara asks: With regard to <b>disqualified consecrated</b> animals, <b>who</b> is the <i>tanna</i> who <b>taught</b> that one may not flay them from their feet? <b>Rav Ḥisda says: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon. As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>there were two sin offerings before him,</b> where one was designated as a guarantee for the other should it become lost or disqualified, and <b>one was unblemished and one</b> became <b>blemished</b> after it was designated, then the <b>unblemished</b> animal <b>shall be sacrificed</b> and the <b>blemished</b> animal <b>shall be redeemed.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: If the <b>blemished</b> animal <b>was slaughtered</b> after it was redeemed, and this occurred <b>before the blood of the unblemished</b> animal was <b>sprinkled</b> upon the altar, then the meat of the blemished animal is <b>permitted.</b> But if it was slaughtered <b>after the blood of the unblemished</b> animal <b>was sprinkled</b> on the altar, it is <b>prohibited</b> to derive benefit from the blemished animal, as it has the status of a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal.
<b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, says: Even</b> if the <b>meat of the blemished</b> animal is already cooking <b>in the pot, and the blood of the unblemished</b> sin offering <b>was</b> then <b>sprinkled</b> on the altar, it is prohibited to derive benefit from the meat of the blemished animal, and it <b>goes to the place</b> designated <b>for burning</b> disqualified offerings, as it is considered a sin offering whose owner achieved atonement with another animal. Just as Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, prohibits the meat of a disqualified sin offering that was redeemed and slaughtered, he likewise prohibits one from flaying it from its feet.
The Gemara objects: <b>And let Rav Ḥisda establish</b> both <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> and <b>that</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Shammai.</b> Why does Rav Ḥisda establish the prohibition against flaying disqualified consecrated animals from the feet specifically in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon? Presumably, just as Beit Shammai prohibit flaying a firstborn from its feet, they also prohibit one from doing so to disqualified consecrated animals.
The Gemara explains: <b>Perhaps when Beit Shammai said</b> that a firstborn animal retains its sanctity even after it is blemished, they said that only <b>with regard to a firstborn, whose sanctity</b> is attained <b>from the womb,</b> i.e., from birth. <b>But with regard to disqualified consecrated</b> animals, perhaps Beit Shammai do <b>not</b> hold that they retain their sanctity after they become blemished and are redeemed.
The Gemara objects from the other angle: <b>But let</b> Rav Ḥisda <b>establish</b> both <b>this</b> <i>halakha</i> and <b>that</b> <i>halakha</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon.</b> Presumably, just as Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, prohibits one from flaying disqualified consecrated animals from the feet, he also prohibits one from doing so to firstborn animals.
The Gemara explains: <b>Perhaps when Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, said</b> that the sanctity of a blemished consecrated animal remains even if it was already redeemed and is cooking in the pot, he said this only <b>with regard to disqualified consecrated</b> animals, <b>whose</b> sanctity is <b>strong</b> enough <b>to apply</b> to <b>the redemption</b> money paid for these animals. <b>But in</b> the case of a blemished <b>firstborn</b> animal, which cannot be redeemed, Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, does <b>not</b> hold that it retains its sanctity even after it is blemished.
The Gemara objects: <b>But does Rabbi Elazar, son of Rabbi Shimon, not hold</b> in accordance with <b>that which we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 31a): <b>All disqualified consecrated</b> animals that have been redeemed <b>may be slaughtered in the butchers’ market and sold in the butchers’ market,</b> where there are many buyers, <b>and</b> their meat is <b>weighed by the <i>litra</i>,</b> like non-sacred meat. <b>Apparently, once you permit</b> the sale of disqualified consecrated meat in the manner of non-sacred meat, one may <b>increase</b> the sale price <b>and sell</b> it at a greater profit. Since the Temple treasury stands to gain from such a sale, it is permitted to handle the disqualified consecrated meat in the manner of non-sacred meat. Here, too, as an animal that may be flayed from its feet would be sold for a higher price, the flaying from the feet of disqualified consecrated animals should be permitted, as the Temple treasury stands to gain from that sale.
<b>Rav Mari, son of Rav Kahana, says</b> in response: <b>That which improves the hide damages the meat.</b> If one is careful to remove the entire hide without cutting it, he will inevitably cut away some of the meat, thereby lowering the proceeds from the meat. Consequently, the ability to flay a disqualified consecrated animal from its feet does not increase its overall sale price.
<b>In the West,</b> Eretz Yisrael, <b>they said in the name of Rabbi Avin</b> another reason it is prohibited to flay disqualified consecrated animals from their feet: It is <b>because it appears as</b> though one is <b>performing labor with sacrificial</b> animals, which is prohibited.
<b>Rabbi Yosei bar Avin says:</b> The prohibition against flaying disqualified consecrated animals from the feet is a rabbinic <b>decree, lest one</b> delay slaughtering the animals until he finds someone to purchase the hide, and in doing so, he will <b>raise many flocks</b> of disqualified consecrated animals, which will likely lead to a transgression of the prohibitions of shearing or working disqualified consecrated animals.

Daf 24b

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>How</b> may <b>one employ artifice to</b> circumvent the obligation to give <b>the firstborn</b> to the priest and utilize the animal for a different offering that he is obligated to bring? The owner approaches <b>an animal</b> that is going to <b>give birth to its firstborn</b> while <b>that</b> animal <b>was</b> still <b>pregnant,</b> and <b>says:</b> That <b>which is in the womb of this</b> animal, <b>if</b> it is <b>male,</b> is designated as a <b>burnt offering.</b> In that case, if the animal <b>gave birth to a male, it will be sacrificed as a burnt offering. And</b> in a case where he says: <b>If it is female,</b> it is designated as a <b>peace offering,</b> if the animal <b>gave birth to a female, it will be sacrificed as a peace offering.</b>
In a case where the owner says: <b>If</b> it is <b>male</b> it is designated as a <b>burnt offering, and if it is female</b> it is designated as a <b>peace offering,</b> and the animal <b>gave birth to a male and a female, the male will be sacrificed as a burnt offering and the female will be sacrificed as a peace offering.</b> If the animal <b>gave birth to two males, one of them will be sacrificed as a burnt offering and the second will be sold to</b> those <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>a burnt offering,</b> who will sacrifice it as a burnt offering; and the <b>money</b> received from <b>its</b> sale is <b>non-sacred.</b>
If the animal <b>gave birth to two females, one of them will be sacrificed as a peace offering and the second will be sold to</b> those <b>obligated</b> to bring <b>a peace offering,</b> who will sacrifice it as a peace offering, and the <b>money</b> received from <b>its</b> sale is <b>non-sacred.</b> If the animal <b>gave birth to a <i>tumtum</i>,</b> whose gender is unknown, <b>or a hermaphrodite,</b> which has both male and female sexual organs, both of which are unfit for sacrifice, <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: They are not imbued with sanctity.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Yehuda says:</b> It is permitted <b>to inflict a blemish upon a firstborn before it left</b> the womb and entered <b>into the air of the world,</b> as it has not yet become sanctified as a firstborn. The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: <b>A person says</b> about an animal that is going to give birth to its firstborn: <b>That which is in the womb of this</b> animal, if it is male, is designated as a <b>burnt offering.</b> This indicates that as <b>a burnt offering, yes,</b> one may designate it in this manner, as the sanctity of a burnt offering is more stringent than that of a firstborn but as <b>a peace offering, no. And</b> yet <b>you say that one may</b> entirely <b>abrogate</b> the animal’s <b>sanctity</b> by inflicting a blemish upon it before it is born.
<b>Rav Yehuda</b> could have <b>said to you: That statement,</b> that one is permitted to consecrate a firstborn fetus only with a more stringent sanctity, applies <b>when the Temple is standing</b> and offerings are sacrificed upon the altar. By contrast, when <b>I say</b> that one is permitted to inflict a blemish upon a firstborn and abrogate the firstborn’s sanctity, I am referring to <b>today, when</b> offerings <b>are not able to be sacrificed,</b> and therefore the animal cannot be consumed until it becomes blemished.
The Gemara asks: <b>If</b> Rav Yehuda is referring only to <b>today, what</b> is the purpose <b>of stating</b> this <i>halakha</i>? Isn’t it obvious? The Gemara answers that Rav Yehuda’s statement is necessary, <b>lest you say: Let us issue a decree</b> against inflicting a blemish on a firstborn fetus, <b>lest most of the</b> fetus’s <b>head emerge</b> from the womb, which is when the sanctity of the firstborn takes effect, <b>and</b> then <b>one inflicts a blemish upon</b> the animal. That would constitute the unlawful infliction of a blemish upon a sacred animal. Rav Yehuda therefore teaches us that such a decree was not issued.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> one can <b>say</b> that <b>indeed,</b> a decree should be issued prohibiting inflicting a blemish on a fetus due to that concern. The Gemara explains: <b>Even so,</b> inflicting the blemish on the fetus <b>is preferable</b> to leaving it alone, despite that concern, <b>as</b> otherwise one might <b>come to</b> transgress the prohibitions against <b>shearing</b> the firstborn <b>or working with it.</b> It is prohibited to work or shear a firstborn, even if it is blemished. If the firstborn is born blemished, it may be slaughtered and eaten immediately. By contrast, if it is unblemished, then one must wait for it to become blemished, and there is a concern that one might work or shear the animal in the meantime.
§ The latter clause of the mishna teaches that in a case where the owner of an animal that is going to give birth to its firstborn says: <b>If it is female,</b> it is designated as <b>a peace offering,</b> then if the animal gave birth to a female, it will be sacrificed as a peace offering. The Gemara asks: <b>Does a female</b> animal become <b>consecrated</b> with <b>firstborn</b> status, such that the owner must designate the fetus as a peace offering in order to circumvent the obligation of the firstborn? The sanctity of the firstborn takes effect only upon male animals. The Gemara answers: <b>The latter clause</b> of the mishna <b>comes</b> to discuss the offspring of <b>an animal consecrated</b> as a sin offering. Since the offspring of a sin offering is put to death, the owner may wish to employ artifice to circumvent the sanctity of the mother by consecrating the fetus with a different sanctity.
§ The mishna teaches: If the animal <b>gave birth to two males,</b> one of them will be sacrificed as a burnt offering and the second will be sold to those obligated to bring a burnt offering, who will sacrifice it as a burnt offering; and the money received from its sale is non-sacred. The Sages <b>say: If the animal</b> that gave birth to two males was <b>consecrated</b> as a sin offering, then it is understandable <b>that</b> the newborn animal <b>which is consecrated</b> to be <b>a burnt offering</b> should <b>be a burnt offering.</b> But with regard to <b>the other, let it</b> retain <b>the sanctity of its mother.</b> Why does the mishna rule that the money received from its sale is non-sacred? The Gemara answers: In <b>the last clause</b> of the mishna, <b>we come to</b> the case of <b>a non-sacred animal</b> that is about to give birth to its firstborn.
§ The mishna teaches: If the animal <b>gave birth to a <i>tumtum</i> or a hermaphrodite,</b> Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel says: They are not imbued with sanctity. This indicates that they are not imbued with sanctity in any case, even if they are the offspring of a consecrated animal.

Daf 25a

The Gemara asks: <b>Why aren’t they imbued with the sanctity</b> of the mother? The Gemara answers: <b>Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel holds</b> that <b>the offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are imbued with sanctity</b> only <b>as they come into being,</b> i.e., from the moment they are born. <b>As, if it enters your mind</b> that <b>they are sanctified</b> already <b>in the womb of their mother, why are they not imbued with the sanctity</b> of the mother? After all, <b>the sanctity of their mother has taken hold of them. Rather, learn from it</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are sanctified</b> only <b>as they come into being.</b>
<b>And this <i>tanna</i></b> disagrees with Rabban Shimon ben Gamliel and <b>holds</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are sanctified in the womb of their mother. As the Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It is written with regard to a firstborn: “But the firstborn among animals, which is born as a firstling to the Lord, man shall not consecrate it” (Leviticus 27:26). <b>If</b> that verse were <b>not</b> complete but merely <b>stated: The firstborn shall not consecrate, I would have said</b> that the verse is teaching that <b>a firstborn</b> man <b>shall not consecrate</b> non-sacred <b>items.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Man</b> shall not consecrate <b>it,”</b> i.e., <b>you may not consecrate it,</b> a firstborn animal, <b>but a firstborn man may consecrate</b> non-sacred <b>items.</b>
<b>But still, I</b> would <b>say</b> that specifically a firstborn man <b>may not consecrate</b> a firstborn animal with a different sanctity, <b>but others</b> who are not firstborns <b>may consecrate</b> a firstborn animal. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Among animals,”</b> indicating that <b>I am dealing with</b> firstborn <b>animals,</b> not firstborn men, i.e., the verse is teaching that all people are prohibited to consecrate a firstborn animal with a different sanctity that that of a firstborn.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that one also <b>cannot consecrate</b> the fetus of an animal pregnant with its firstborn while the fetus is still <b>in the womb</b> of its mother. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “Which is born as a firstling to the Lord,”</b> indicating that <b>from</b> the time the animal <b>is born and consecrated to the Lord as a firstborn you cannot consecrate</b> it with a different sanctity, <b>but you may consecrate</b> it with a different sanctity while it is still <b>in the womb.</b>
One <b>might</b> have thought that <b>even</b> with regard to <b>the offspring of all</b> other <b>consecrated</b> animals it is <b>so,</b> that one may consecrate them with a different sanctity when they are still in the womb. Therefore, <b>the verse states: “But [<i>akh</i>],”</b> which <b>distinguished</b> between a firstborn animal, which one may consecrate with a different sanctity while it is still in the womb, and the offspring of other consecrated animals, which one may not consecrate with a different sanctity even when they are in the womb. <b>Evidently,</b> this <i>tanna</i> <b>holds</b> that the <b>offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>are sanctified in the womb of their mother.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that one may employ artifice to circumvent the obligation to give the firstborn to the priest by consecrating the fetus as a burnt offering before the time of birth, which is the point at which the animal becomes sanctified as a firstborn. <b>Rav Amram said to Rav Sheshet:</b> If an owner <b>said with regard to</b> the fetus of an animal pregnant with its <b>firstborn:</b> This fetus will be consecrated as <b>a burnt offering</b> at the same time that the sanctity of the firstborn takes effect, which is <b>when the majority</b> of the animal <b>leaves</b> the womb, what is the status of the offspring? <b>Is</b> the offspring <b>a burnt offering or is it a firstborn?</b>
The Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Is it a burnt offering, as</b> the sanctity of a burnt offering is more stringent than the sanctity of a firstborn and therefore <b>each and every bit that exits</b> the womb becomes a burnt offering, which is <b>entirely consumed</b> upon the altar? <b>Or</b> perhaps <b>it is a firstborn,</b> because normally the sanctity of the firstborn takes effect at the moment of birth, and therefore <b>with regard to each and every bit</b> of the animal <b>that exits</b> the womb, <b>its status is</b> that of a firstborn.
The Gemara cites <b>another version</b> of the explanation of the dilemma: Is it a burnt offering <b>because it is imbued with</b> the more stringent <b>sanctity</b> of a burnt offering? <b>Or perhaps it is a firstborn because</b> God declared <b>its sanctity</b> as a firstborn upon exiting <b>from the womb.</b>
Rav Sheshet <b>said to</b> Rav Amram: <b>What is your dilemma?</b> Your dilemma <b>is</b> the same as that dilemma <b>which Ilfa raised:</b> With regard to the <b>gleanings</b> left for the poor, which the wealthy may not take, if the owner of a field at the harvest time <b>said:</b> This grain that is now on the stalk <b>will become ownerless</b> property, meaning that even the wealthy can acquire it, at precisely the same time as the <i>halakha</i> of gleanings left for the poor takes effect, which is <b>when the majority</b> of the grain <b>falls</b> off the stalk, what is the status of the grain after it falls off the stalk? <b>Is it gleanings</b> left for the poor <b>or is it ownerless</b> property that even the wealthy may take?
The Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Is it gleanings, as its sanctity</b> is dictated <b>by the hand of Heaven</b> at the moment that the grain falls off the stalk? <b>Or perhaps it is ownerless</b> property, because that status is more inclusive, <b>as</b> both the <b>poor and</b> the <b>wealthy may acquire</b> the grain.
<b>And Abaye said</b> in response to Ilfa’s dilemma: <b>What is your dilemma?</b> When there is a conflict between <b>the statement of the Master,</b> i.e., God, who commanded that the grain become gleanings for the poor, <b>and the statement of the student,</b> the one who declared the grain to be ownerless property that may be acquired even by the wealthy, <b>to whose statement should one listen? So too,</b> with regard to Rav Amram’s dilemma, when there is a conflict between the statement of God and the statement of the student, <b>to whose statement should one listen?</b> Accordingly, the fetus is consecrated with the sanctity of a firstborn, not that of a burnt offering.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>One who says: The offspring of this</b> non-sacred animal <b>is a burnt offering and</b> the animal <b>itself is a peace offering, his statement stands,</b> i.e., is effective. If he says: The animal <b>itself is a peace offering and its offspring is a burnt offering,</b> then since consecration of the mother preceded consecration of the offspring, it is <b>the offspring of a peace offering,</b> whose halakhic status is that of a peace offering; this is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: If that was his intent from the outset,</b> to designate the offspring as a burnt offering when he designated the mother as a peace offering, then <b>since it is impossible to call</b> it by <b>two designations simultaneously, his statement stands,</b> and the mother is a peace offering and the offspring a burnt offering. <b>And if</b> it was only <b>after he said: This</b> animal <b>is hereby a peace offering,</b> that <b>he reconsidered and said: Its offspring is a burnt offering, that offspring is a peace offering,</b> as before he reconsidered, the offspring had already assumed the status of the offspring of a peace offering.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> In the case of <b>one who separated a pregnant</b> animal as <b>a sin offering and</b> that animal later <b>gave birth</b> to a female, both animals are consecrated as sin offerings. If <b>he wishes he may achieve atonement</b> by sacrificing the mother <b>itself,</b> and if <b>he wishes he may achieve atonement</b> by sacrificing <b>the offspring.</b> Since he consecrated the animal when it was already pregnant, his act of consecration took effect with regard to both the mother and the offspring.
<b>What is the reason</b> for this <i>halakha</i>? <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan holds</b> that in the case of one who consecrated a pregnant animal, <b>if he reserved</b> the fetus and designated it as non-sacred, e.g., he said: The fetus is non-sacred and the animal itself is designated as a sin offering, then the offspring is considered <b>reserved</b> and is non-sacred, despite the fact that the mother is consecrated.
The reason is that Rabbi Yoḥanan holds that <b>a fetus is not</b> considered <b>the thigh,</b> i.e., a part, <b>of its mother.</b> Therefore, just as one can reserve the fetus from being consecrated with its mother, so too, one can consecrate the fetus with a separate sanctity than that of the mother. Consequently, <b>this</b> case <b>is like</b> that of one who <b>separates two sin offerings as a guarantee;</b> if <b>he wishes he may gain atonement with one</b> of them, and if <b>he wishes he may gain atonement with the other.</b>
<b>Rabbi Elazar raises an objection</b> from the mishna: If one says: The animal <b>itself is a peace offering and its offspring is a burnt offering,</b> then <b>it is the offspring of a peace offering. And if it enters your mind</b> that if <b>one reserved</b> the fetus from the consecration of the mother it is considered <b>reserved,</b> and its sanctity is independent from that of the mother, why does the mishna state with regard to the offspring that <b>it is the offspring of a peace offering,</b> which indicates that it receives its sanctity from that of the mother? The mishna <b>should teach</b> that the offspring <b>is a peace offering</b> with an independent sanctity.
<b>Rav Tavla said:</b> The discussion of this topic should be held <b>apart from this</b> version of the mishna, as it is incorrect. <b>Didn’t Rav say to the <i>tanna</i></b> who was reciting <i>mishnayot</i> that he should not recite the mishna with the phrase: It is the offspring of a peace offering, but rather, he should <b>teach: It is a peace offering.</b>
The Gemara <b>raises</b> another <b>objection</b> to Rabbi Yoḥanan’s statement from a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who says to his</b> Canaanite <b>maidservant: You are hereby</b> still <b>a maidservant but your</b> unborn <b>child is a freeman,</b> and he writes a bill of manumission for the child and places it in her hand, <b>if she was pregnant</b> at that time, then <b>she acquired</b> freedom <b>for</b> the unborn child.
The Gemara explains the objection: <b>Granted, if you say</b> that <b>if one reserved</b> a certain consecration it is <b>not</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> and therefore the sanctity of the mother takes effect on her offspring, and likewise that <b>a fetus is</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother,</b> it is <b>due to that</b> reason that <b>she acquired</b> freedom <b>for</b> the unborn child. <b>And</b> the explanation is that <b>this</b> case <b>is like</b> that of <b>one who emancipates half of his slave,</b> as that half is thereby emancipated. <b>And whose</b> opinion is this? <b>It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Meir, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>:

Daf 25b

With regard to a master <b>who emancipates half of his slave,</b> half of the slave is <b>emancipated,</b> and he is a half-slave half-freeman. Although a slave does not have the ability to acquire items, in this case he does acquire his freedom because <b>his bill</b> of manumission <b>and his ability</b> to acquire himself <b>come at the same time.</b>
<b>But if you say</b> that if <b>one reserved</b> a consecration <b>it is</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> and <b>a fetus is not</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother,</b> but the mother and the fetus are separate entities, <b>why</b> does the mother <b>acquire</b> freedom <b>for</b> the fetus in a case where the master freed the fetus but not the mother? The Gemara continues: <b>And isn’t it taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: It <b>appears</b> to be the case <b>that a slave can acquire a bill of manumission for another</b> slave <b>from the hand of his</b> fellow’s <b>master who is not</b> also <b>his</b> own master, <b>but not from the hand of his own master,</b> i.e., not if both of them are enslaved to the same person. Here too, how can the mother receive the bill of manumission for the fetus from the hand of their shared master? <b>Rather, conclude from it</b> that <b>if he reserved</b> a consecration <b>it is not</b> considered <b>reserved, and the refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> is indeed <b>a conclusive refutation.</b>
The Gemara suggests: <b>Shall we say</b> that the claim that <b>if one reserved</b> a consecration <b>it is reserved is</b> subject to a dispute between <b><i>tanna’im</i>? As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>one who says to his</b> pregnant Canaanite <b>maidservant: You are hereby a free woman but your offspring</b> shall remain <b>a slave, the offspring is</b> emancipated <b>like her.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Yosei HaGelili. And the Rabbis say: His statement is upheld.</b> This is <b>because it is stated: “The woman and her children shall be her master’s”</b> (Exodus 21:4).
The Gemara expresses surprise at this ruling: <b>What is the derivation</b> of the <b>verse</b> here that indicates support <b>for</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who rule in this case that the child of a freed maidservant remains a slave? <b>Rava said:</b> The verse does not support the opinion of the Rabbis; rather, <b>the verse</b> that the <i>baraita</i> <b>states</b> is cited as support <b>for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei HaGelili, as it teaches,</b> i.e., the <i>baraita</i> should be understood as follows: <b>The offspring is</b> emancipated <b>like her, as it is stated: “The woman and her children shall be her master’s,”</b> which indicates that <b>when the woman</b> belongs <b>to the master her offspring</b> likewise belongs <b>to the master,</b> but if the woman is freed the offspring is also freed.
<b>What, is it not</b> correct to say <b>that</b> the Sages <b>disagree over this</b> point, <b>that Rabbi Yosei</b> HaGelili <b>holds</b> that <b>if he reserved it, it is not</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> and therefore the mother and the fetus are treated as one entity, <b>and the Rabbis maintain</b> that <b>it is</b> considered <b>reserved.</b> If so, this would mean that Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion is the subject of a dispute between <i>tanna’im</i>.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could have <b>said to you that everyone</b> holds that if <b>he reserved it, it is</b> considered <b>reserved, and here this is the reason</b> that Rabbi Yosei HaGelili rules that the offspring is freed with the mother, as Rabbi Yosei HaGelili himself explains: It is <b>that the verse states: “The woman and her children shall be her master’s.”</b> This verse teaches that the status of the offspring follows the status of the mother with regard to slavery, but not because the fetus is considered a part of its mother.
<b>Rather, certainly we shall say</b> that the opinion that if he reserved it, it is reserved, <b>is</b> subject to a dispute between these <b><i>tanna’im</i>, as it is taught</b> that there are two <i>baraitot</i> that discuss the case of <b>one who slaughters a sin offering,</b> which is assumed to have been consecrated when it was pregnant, <b>and he found a four-month-old</b> fetus <b>alive</b> inside. <b>It is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i>: The slaughter of the mother is also considered a slaughter with regard to the fetus, and therefore the fetus is considered a sin offering. This means that its meat is <b>eaten only by males of the priesthood, and it is eaten only for one day, and it is eaten only inside the curtains.</b> In the Tabernacle in the wilderness this area was surrounded by curtains. The corresponding area in the Temple is the courtyard.
<b>And it is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: The slaughter of the mother is also considered a slaughter for the fetus to the extent that it permits it to be eaten, but the fetus is non-sacred, and therefore <b>it may be eaten by any person, and may be eaten in any place, and may be eaten forever,</b> with no time limit.
<b>What, is it not</b> correct to say that these <b><i>tanna’im</i></b> disagree over this point, <b>that the first <i>tanna</i> holds:</b> If <b>he reserved it, it is not</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> and therefore the mother and the fetus are considered one entity and the status of the mother applies to the fetus; <b>and the Sage</b> in the second <i>baraita</i> <b>holds: If he reserved it, it is</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> which means that the fetus is considered an independent entity and is non-sacred. If so, Rabbi Yoḥanan’s opinion is the subject of a dispute between <i>tanna’im</i>.
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could <b>say to you that everyone</b> holds that <b>if he reserved it, it is</b> considered <b>reserved,</b> and therefore a person can consecrate an animal while reserving the fetus as non-sacred. <b>And these <i>tanna’im</i> disagree over this</b> following point: In the case of an animal that was consecrated as a sin offering and only then became pregnant, one <b>Sage holds: The offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>become consecrated</b> when they are born and <b>come into being,</b> and as that fetus which was found alive inside the mother was never born, it never became consecrated. <b>And</b> one <b>Sage holds: The offspring of sacrificial</b> animals <b>become consecrated in the womb of their mother,</b> which means that the fetus that was found alive inside the mother was already consecrated.
<b>If you wish, say</b> instead an alternative explanation of the dispute between these two <i>baraitot</i>: It is <b>not difficult</b> for Rabbi Yoḥanan. Everyone holds that if he reserved it, it is considered reserved, and everyone also maintains that offspring of sacrificial animals become consecrated when they are born. Rather, these two <i>baraitot</i> are discussing two different cases. <b>Here,</b> the second <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where</b> the owner <b>consecrated</b> the animal before it became pregnant <b>and</b> only <b>afterward it became pregnant.</b> Consequently, the offspring is non-sacred, as it was not in the womb when the mother was consecrated, and as it was never born, it was not consecrated at the moment of birth either.
By contrast, <b>there,</b> the first <i>baraita</i> is referring to a case <b>where</b> the animal first <b>became pregnant and afterward</b> the owner <b>consecrated it.</b> Since the owner did not reserve the fetus from the consecration of the mother, the fetus was consecrated as a sin offering together with the mother.
<b>Rava objects to this</b> claim: <b>From where</b> is it known <b>that the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> is that <b>if he reserved it, it is</b> considered <b>reserved? Perhaps</b> Rabbi Yoḥanan maintains that if he reserved it, it is not considered reserved, and <b>this is the reason for</b> the ruling of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> It is <b>that</b> one can achieve atonement with the offspring of a pregnant animal he set aside as a sin offering, as he maintains that <b>a person can achieve atonement with</b> the <b>enhancement of consecrated</b> property, and the fetus is considered an enhancement of consecrated property.
<b>Rav Hamnuna said</b> to Rava: <b>Rabbi Elazar</b> was <b>the pupil of Rabbi Yoḥanan, and he sat before Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> and Rabbi Elazar assumed that the reason for the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan was that he held that if he reserved it, it is considered reserved, and he questioned Rabbi Yoḥanan himself about his statement based on that assumption. <b>And Rabbi Yoḥanan did not answer him</b> with <b>this answer,</b> i.e., that the real reason for his opinion is that a person can achieve atonement with an enhancement of consecrated property. <b>And</b> if so, how can <b>you say</b> that <b>the reason for</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> is <b>that a person can achieve atonement with</b> the <b>enhancement of consecrated</b> property? Therefore, it must be that the reason for the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan is that he held that if he reserved it, it is considered reserved.
§ The mishna teaches: And <b>if</b> it was only <b>after he said: This</b> animal <b>is</b> hereby <b>a peace offering,</b> that <b>he reconsidered</b> and said: Its offspring is a burnt offering, that offspring is a peace offering, as before he reconsidered the offspring had already assumed the status of the offspring of a peace offering. The Gemara asks: If he reconsidered after saying: This animal is a peace offering, it is <b>obvious</b> that he cannot remove the status he already applied to the animal, and that <b>the offspring</b> remains <b>a peace offering. Can one change his mind anytime that he wants?</b>
<b>Rav Pappa said:</b> This <i>halakha</i> <b>is necessary only</b> in a case <b>where</b> he reconsidered and <b>said:</b> Its offspring is a burnt offering, <b>within the time required for speaking a short phrase. Lest you say:</b> The halakhic status of a pause that lasts <b>less than the time required for speaking a short phrase is like</b> that of continuous <b>speech,</b> and therefore the offspring is a burnt offering, <b>and that</b> time <b>he was</b> merely <b>prolonging his thought</b> but not changing his mind; consequently, the mishna <b>teaches us</b> that he changed his mind and it is not considered continuous speech.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one had two animals standing before him, one a burnt offering and the other a peace offering, and he said with regard to a third, non-sacred animal: <b>This</b> animal <b>is hereby the substitute of the burnt offering, the substitute of the peace offering, that</b> animal <b>is the substitute of the burnt offering.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b> Once he designated it as the substitute of the burnt offering, his initial statement takes effect and the animal assumes the sanctity of the burnt offering.
<b>Rabbi Yosei said: If that was his intent from the outset,</b> when he said that the animal is the substitute of the burnt offering, to state that the animal is also the substitute of the peace offering, then <b>since it is impossible to call two designations simultaneously,</b> i.e., one must first say one designation and then the other, <b>his statement stands,</b> and the animal is half a burnt offering and half a peace offering. <b>And if</b> it was only <b>after he said:</b> This animal is hereby <b>the substitute of the burnt offering,</b> that <b>he reconsidered and said: The substitute of the peace offering, that</b> entire animal <b>is the substitute of the burnt offering.</b>

Daf 26a

<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rabbi Yitzḥak, son of Rabbi Yosei, says</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says,</b> with regard to the disagreement between Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei: <b>Everyone concedes in</b> a case <b>where one says:</b> The consecration of this animal as a substitute for a burnt offering <b>will take effect and afterward</b> the consecration of this animal as a substitute for a peace offering <b>will take effect.</b> In this case, <b>everyone agrees</b> that one <b>attends</b> only to <b>the first statement,</b> i.e., the sanctity of the burnt offering takes effect but the sanctity of the peace offering does not take effect.
Likewise, everyone agrees that in a case where one says: Consecration of this animal as a substitute for a burnt offering <b>shall not take effect unless</b> the consecration of this animal as a substitute for a peace offering <b>shall</b> also <b>take effect,</b> the animal is <b>consecrated</b> as <b>both</b> a burnt offering and a peace offering.
Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei <b>disagree only with regard to</b> a case <b>such as</b> the one in <b>our mishna, where</b> one <b>said:</b> This animal is <b>the substitute of the burnt offering, the substitute of the peace offering. As Rabbi Meir holds: Since</b> if he wanted both sanctities to take effect, <b>he should have said: The substitute of the burnt offering and the peace offering, but</b> instead <b>he said: The substitute of the burnt offering, the substitute of the peace offering, he is</b> considered <b>like one who says:</b> The consecration of this animal as a substitute for a burnt offering <b>will take effect and afterward</b> the consecration of this animal as a substitute for a peace offering <b>will take effect.</b> Therefore, according to Rabbi Meir only the first sanctity takes effect; the second sanctity cannot take effect because the animal is already consecrated.
<b>And Rabbi Yosei holds</b> that from the outset he intended for both sanctities to take effect, and he did not say: <b>The substitute of the burnt offering and the peace offering,</b> because he reasoned that <b>if he said</b> that, one <b>would say</b> that he intends for the animal to be <b>consecrated</b> with two sanctities, half as a substitution of the burnt offering and half as a substitution of the peace offering. <b>And</b> if so, the animal <b>cannot be sacrificed</b> in the Temple, because it is impossible to sacrifice half the animal as one type of offering and half as another type of offering. Therefore, Rabbi Yosei <b>teaches us</b> that when he says: The substitute of the burnt offering the substitute of the peace offering, he intends for the animal to be both entirely a burnt offering and entirely a peace offering, as he mistakenly thinks that such an animal can be sacrificed in the Temple. But since he intended for both types of sanctity to take effect, the animal is half a burnt offering and half a peace offering.
§ <b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of one who had two animals standing before him, one a burnt offering and one a peace offering, and <b>he said</b> with regard to one of his non-sacred animals: <b>This animal, half of it is the substitute of the burnt offering, and half of it is the substitute of the peace offering,</b> that animal is the substitute of the burnt offering and is therefore <b>entirely sacrificed as a burnt offering.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir. And the Rabbis say:</b> It is left to <b>graze until it becomes blemished, and</b> then <b>it is sold, and</b> the owner <b>brings a substitute burnt offering with the payment for half</b> the animal <b>and a substitute peace offering with the payment for half</b> the animal.
The <i>baraita</i> concludes: <b>Rabbi Yosei said: If that was his intent from the outset,</b> then <b>since it is impossible to call two designations simultaneously, his statement stands,</b> and the animal is half a burnt offering and half a peace offering. The Gemara objects: The opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei is identical to</b> the opinion of <b>the Rabbis.</b> The Gemara explains: <b>Rabbi Yosei taught the entire</b> <i>baraita</i>, and the opinion of the Rabbis in the <i>baraita</i> is Rabbi Yosei’s opinion.
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: In a case where one says: <b>This animal, half of it</b> is designated as <b>a burnt offering and half of it</b> is designated as <b>a sin offering,</b> Rabbi Meir holds that we attend only to the first statement that he uttered, and therefore half of the animal becomes consecrated as a burnt offering. Since the animal cannot live if half of its body were removed, the sanctity spreads to the entire animal and <b>it is sacrificed entirely as a burnt offering.</b> This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Meir.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yosei says:</b> Both sanctities take effect, and as an animal cannot be sacrificed as two offerings, <b>it must</b> be left to <b>die.</b> The <i>baraita</i> adds: <b>And</b> Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei <b>agree</b> in a case where <b>one says: Half</b> of this animal is designated as <b>a sin offering and half of it</b> is designated as <b>a burnt offering, that</b> the animal <b>must</b> be left to <b>die.</b>
The Gemara clarifies: That case with regard to which the <i>baraita</i> states that Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei <b>agree, who is</b> conceding to whom? Clearly <b>Rabbi Meir</b> concedes to Rabbi Yosei that in that case the <i>halakha</i> is that the animal must be left to die. The Gemara asks: Isn’t it <b>obvious</b> that Rabbi Meir concedes in that case? Just as in the first case of the <i>baraita</i> where one mentioned the burnt offering first and the sin offering second, Rabbi Meir holds that only the first expression takes effect, so too if one mentioned the sin offering first and the burnt offering second, only the first expression takes effect; this means that the animal is a sin offering, and therefore it must be left to die, as he is not obligated to bring a sin offering.
The Gemara explains that the ruling of the <i>baraita</i> is necessary, <b>lest you say: If it hadn’t taught us</b> the opinion of Rabbi Meir in the latter case, <b>I would say</b> that <b>the reason for</b> the ruling <b>of Rabbi Meir</b> in the first clause of the mishna <b>is not that</b> we <b>attend</b> only to <b>the first statement. Rather,</b> I would say that <b>this is</b> Rabbi Meir’s <b>reason:</b> In a case of the sanctity of <b>a sin offering</b> that is <b>mixed</b> with another sanctity in the same animal, the animal <b>is sacrificed,</b> and that is the reason Rabbi Meir rules that the animal is sacrificed as a burnt offering despite the fact that the sanctity of a sin offering is also mixed with it.
<b>And</b> if that is Rabbi Meir’s reasoning, then <b>even if one said: Half</b> of this animal is designated as <b>a sin offering, and then said: Half of it</b> is designated as <b>a burnt offering,</b> the animal <b>is sacrificed.</b> Therefore, the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us that</b> this is <b>not</b> the reason for Rabbi Meir’s opinion. Rather, his ruling is due to the principle that we attend only to the first statement, and therefore Rabbi Meir and Rabbi Yosei agree in the latter clause of the <i>baraita</i> that the animal must be left to die.
<b>It is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who said: This animal, half of it</b> is designated as <b>a burnt offering and half of it</b> is designated as <b>a peace offering,</b> the animal <b>is consecrated but is not sacrificed.</b> The animal grazes until it becomes blemished, at which point it is sold, and the owner brings a burnt offering with the money of half the animal and a peace offering with the money of half the animal. This animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal that it is exchanged for consecrated as <b>a substitute, and its substitute</b> is treated <b>like it,</b> i.e., it is not sacrificed; rather, it grazes until it becomes blemished, and it is then sold, and the owner brings a burnt offering with the money of half the animal and a peace offering with the money of half the animal.
The Gemara asks: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion is the <i>baraita</i> taught? <b>It is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei,</b> who holds one accountable for both expressions that he uttered. The Gemara asks: Isn’t it <b>obvious</b> that Rabbi Yosei holds <b>that</b> in that case the animal is <b>consecrated but is not sacrificed?</b> Why is it necessary to teach this?
The Gemara answers: <b>It was necessary</b> for the <i>baraita</i> to teach that <b>its substitute</b> is also not sacrificed. <b>Lest you say: Granted that</b> the animal <b>itself is not sacrificed,</b> but <b>its substitute is sacrificed,</b> therefore the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches us</b> that the substitute is also not sacrificed. The Gemara explains the ruling of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>What is different</b> about the animal <b>itself, that it is not sacrificed?</b> It is not sacrificed <b>because its sanctity is deferred</b> from the altar, since it is neither entirely a burnt offering nor fully a peace offering. <b>So too its substitute,</b> whose sanctity <b>comes from the force of deferred sanctity,</b> as it was the substitution of an animal whose sanctity was deferred, may not be sacrificed either.
§ <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says:</b> With regard to <b>an animal</b> that belongs <b>to two partners,</b> if one of the partners <b>consecrated his half</b> of the animal, <b>and then he acquired the other half</b> of the animal from his partner <b>and consecrated it,</b> the animal <b>is consecrated but is not sacrificed.</b> When he originally consecrated his half of the animal, the animal was not fit for sacrifice, as only half of it was consecrated. Although the animal is now fully consecrated, it can never again become fit for sacrifice, since it was once disqualified. And this animal <b>renders</b> a non-sacred animal that it is exchanged for it consecrated as <b>a substitute, and its substitute</b>

Daf 26b

is treated <b>like it,</b> as it too is consecrated but not sacrificed. <b>Conclude three</b> <i>halakhot</i> <b>from this</b> statement of Rabbi Yoḥanan: <b>Conclude from it</b> that an animal that was consecrated with a <b>sanctity</b> that inheres in its <b>value is deferred.</b> One who consecrates only half an animal has consecrated that animal with a sanctity that inheres in its value but not with an inherent sanctity, as the animal cannot be sacrificed.
<b>And</b> second, <b>conclude from it</b> that not only can an offering that has already been slaughtered become permanently deferred from the altar, but also <b>living animals</b> that cannot be sacrificed for whatever reason <b>are</b> permanently <b>deferred. And</b> finally, <b>conclude from it that deferral at the outset,</b> when the animal is first consecrated, <b>is</b> considered a permanent <b>disqualification.</b> Not only is an animal that was fit to be sacrificed when initially consecrated and only later disqualified permanently deferred, but the same applies to an animal that was disqualified from the outset, when it was initially consecrated, e.g., if only half of it was initially consecrated.
§ With regard to the consecration of an animal with two sanctities, <b>Abaye said: Everyone concedes</b> that in a case <b>where one said: Half</b> the animal is designated as <b>a burnt offering and half of it</b> is designated as an animal <b>tithe, everyone agrees</b> that this animal is consecrated and <b>sacrificed</b> entirely as <b>a burnt offering,</b> not as a tithed animal. The animal tithe is consecrated when it is the tenth of a group of animals that are passed underneath a rod, which is not the case here.
But in a case <b>where one said</b> with regard to his non-sacred animal: <b>Half of</b> this animal is designated as <b>a substitute and half of it</b> is designated as an animal <b>tithe,</b> which consecration takes effect? Both consecrations are invalid: Consecration as a substitute is valid when a consecrated animal is standing before the substitute, which is not the case here, and consecration as an animal tithe is valid through the process of passing a group of animals underneath a rod. <b>What</b> is the status of the animal in this case?
The Gemara explains the dilemma: Is the animal <b>sacrificed as a substitute, as</b> the sanctity of substitution <b>applies to all sacrificial</b> animals? <b>Or perhaps</b> the animal <b>is sacrificed as</b> an animal <b>tithe, as</b> the consecration of the animal tithe is more comprehensive, since if one mistakenly counted the ninth animal to pass underneath the rod as the tenth or the eleventh as the tenth, <b>he consecrates</b> those animals that passed <b>before and after</b> the tenth. The Gemara states that the dilemma <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> This mishna discusses the language that serves to effect substitution. If one said: <b>This</b> non-sacred animal <b>is hereby in place of that</b> consecrated animal, or if he said: It is <b>the substitute of that</b> consecrated animal, or if one said: It is <b>the exchange for that</b> consecrated animal, <b>that</b> non-sacred animal <b>is a substitute.</b> If he said: <b>This</b> consecrated animal <b>is desacralized,</b> with its sanctity transferred <b>to that</b> non-sacred animal, <b>that</b> non-sacred animal <b>is not a substitute.</b>
<b>And if the consecrated</b> animal <b>was blemished,</b> and he said: This consecrated animal is desacralized, with its sanctity transferred to that non-sacred animal, the consecrated animal is desacralized <b>and assumes non-sacred</b> status, by Torah law. By rabbinic law, the owner <b>is required to conduct</b> an appraisal to ascertain the relative <b>value</b> of the two animals. If the consecrated animal was worth more than the non-sacred animal, he must pay the difference to the Temple treasury.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The mishna teaches that if one said: This non-sacred animal is hereby in place of that consecrated animal, that non-sacred animal is a substitute. The Gemara asks: Is this <b>to say that</b> the phrase: <b>In place of [<i>taḥat</i>], is a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another, i.e., the transference of substitution? <b>And</b> the Gemara <b>raises a contradiction</b> from a <i>baraita</i>: In a case where one’s non-sacred animal was standing before <b>a consecrated animal</b> belonging to <b>Temple maintenance,</b> and <b>he said:</b> This non-sacred animal is hereby <b>the exchange for that</b> consecrated animal, or if he said: It is the <b>substitute of that</b> consecrated animal, <b>he has not said anything,</b> as these terms indicate the transfer of sanctity via substitution, and substitution does not apply to consecrated animals belonging to the Temple maintenance.
But if he said: This non-sacred animal is hereby <b>in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] that</b> consecrated animal, or if he said: This consecrated animal is <b>desacralized,</b> with its sanctity transferred <b>to that</b> non-sacred animal, <b>his statement takes effect,</b> as these terms indicate desacralization, and a consecrated animal belonging to the Temple maintenance can be desacralized, with its sanctity transferred to a non-sacred animal.
The Gemara explains the apparent contradiction: <b>And if it enters your mind</b> that the term <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another, and therefore it effects substitution, as taught in the mishna, <b>what is different</b> in <b>the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, which states that the terms exchange and substitute are terms that do not effect desacralization, <b>and what is different</b> in <b>the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, which teaches that the term <i>taḥat</i> effects desacralization despite the fact that it indicates substitution?
<b>Abaye said,</b> in response: <b>You find</b> that the term: <b>In place of [<i>taḥat</i>], is</b> sometimes <b>a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another, <b>and</b> it <b>is</b> sometimes <b>a term that</b> indicates <b>desacralization.</b> It is <b>a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another, <b>as it is written:</b>

Daf 27a

<b>“But if the white leprous spot stays in its place [<i>taḥteha</i>]”</b> (Leviticus 13:23). In this verse, the word “<i>taḥteha</i>” indicates that the white leprous spot remains in its place. This usage of the word is fitting for substitution, since when sanctity is transferred by substitution from a consecrated animal to a non-sacred animal, the sanctity of the consecrated animal remains in place, despite the fact that the non-sacred animal is also consecrated.
Yet <i>taḥat</i> is also a term <b>that</b> indicates <b>desacralization, as it is written: “In place of [<i>taḥat</i>] brass I will bring gold,</b> and in place of iron I will bring silver, and in place of wood brass, and in place of stones iron” (Isaiah 60:17). In this verse, <i>taḥat</i> means replacement, which is what occurs in desacralization, where one item is replaced by another. <b>And therefore,</b> the term should be understood in accordance with the context: <b>With regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated</b> for sacrifice upon the <b>altar, which render</b> a non-sacred animal for which they are exchanged <b>a substitute,</b> <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another. <b>With regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, which do not render</b> a non-sacred animal for which they are exchanged <b>a substitute,</b> <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates <b>desacralization.</b>
<b>Rava said: Even</b> with regard to animals <b>consecrated</b> for sacrifice upon the <b>altar,</b> which render a non-sacred animal for which they are exchanged a substitute, <b>you</b> can <b>find</b> that <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates <b>desacralization. For example,</b> in a case <b>where a consecrated</b> animal <b>was blemished</b> and can be desacralized because it is unfit to be sacrificed, and one placed that consecrated animal next to a non-sacred animal and said: This animal is in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] that animal. Although the blemished sacred animal can render a non-sacred animal for which it is exchanged consecrated as a substitute, in this context the term <i>taḥat</i> means desacralization.
<b>Rabbi Ashi said: Even in</b> the case of <b>a blemished</b> animal, mentioned by Rava, sometimes <b>you find</b> that <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates <b>desacralization and</b> sometimes <b>you find</b> that <i>taḥat</i> <b>is a term that</b> indicates the <b>associating</b> of one sanctity with another. If the owner’s <b>hand</b> is resting <b>on the consecrated</b> animal and he says: This animal is in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] that animal, it <b>is</b> a term of <b>desacralization,</b> as the placement of his hand indicates that his intention is to desacralize the consecrated animal, with its sanctity transferred to that non-sacred animal. If the owner’s <b>hand</b> is resting <b>on the non-sacred</b> animal when he says: This animal is in place of that animal, then <i>taḥat</i> <b>is</b> a term of substitution, as the placement of his hand indicates that his intention is for the non-sacred animal to be <b>consecrated</b> with the same sanctity as the consecrated animal.
<b>Abaye raises a dilemma:</b> If <b>there were</b> standing <b>before him two sacrificial animals</b> that <b>were blemished and two non-sacred animals</b> that <b>were unblemished,and,</b> without placing his hands on any animal, <b>he says: These</b> animals <b>are hereby in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] those</b> animals, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>?
The Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Did he</b> intend to <b>say</b> <i>taḥat</i> <b>as association,</b> i.e., to consecrate the two non-sacred animals as substitutes, <b>and</b> therefore <b>he is flogged</b> two sets of lashes for the two violations of the prohibition against consecrating an animal as a substitute? <b>Or perhaps,</b> does one say that since <b>anywhere that there exists</b> both <b>a permitted</b> and a prohibited manner to perform an action, <b>a person does not cast aside the permitted</b> manner <b>and perform the prohibition?</b> If so, here too his intention was to desacralize the two blemished animals, and therefore he is not flogged.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>anywhere that there exists</b> both <b>a permitted</b> and a prohibited manner to perform an action, <b>a person does not cast aside</b> the permitted manner <b>and perform the prohibition,</b> in a case where <b>there were</b> standing <b>before him two sacrificial animals, one of which was blemished</b> and one of which was unblemished, <b>and two non-sacred animals, one of which was blemished</b> and one of which was unblemished, <b>and he said: These</b> animals <b>are hereby in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] those</b> animals, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>? Although he certainly intended to transfer the sanctity of the unblemished sacrificial animal to a non-sacred animal by substitution, it is unclear which of the two non-sacred animals he intended to consecrate as a substitute.
Once again, the Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Did he</b> intend to <b>say</b> that <b>the unblemished</b> non-sacred animal should be consecrated as a substitute <b>in place of the unblemished</b> consecrated animal, and with regard to this pairing he intended <b>to associate</b> the sanctity by substitution, while <b>the blemished</b> non-sacred animal should be consecrated <b>in place of the blemished</b> consecrated animal, and for this pairing he intended <b>to desacralize</b> the consecrated animal. If so, he is flogged only for the first pairing and not for the second pairing.
<b>Or perhaps,</b> he intended that <b>the unblemished non-sacred</b> animal should be consecrated as a substitute <b>in place of the blemished consecrated</b> animal, and <b>the blemished non-sacred</b> animal should be consecrated as a substitute <b>in place of the unblemished consecrated</b> animal, <b>and</b> since he intended to perform substitution in both cases he is <b>flogged for both</b> pairings.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>anywhere that there exists</b> both <b>a permitted</b> and a prohibited manner to perform an action, one <b>does not perform the prohibition,</b> this would resolve the previous dilemma, as his intention must have been <b>to desacralize</b> the blemished consecrated animal, <b>and</b> therefore he is <b>not flogged</b> two sets of lashes, one can raise the following dilemma: In a case where <b>there were three sacrificial animals before him, one of which was blemished</b> while the other two were unblemished, <b>and three non-sacred animals,</b> all of which <b>were unblemished, and he said: These</b> animals <b>are hereby in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] those</b> animals, what is the <i>halakha</i>?
The Gemara explains the dilemma: <b>Do we say</b> that <b>since</b> he intended for <b>the unblemished</b> non-sacred animals to be consecrated as substitutes <b>in place of the unblemished</b> consecrated animals, i.e., <b>to associate</b> their sanctity, <b>so too</b> he intended to consecrate <b>the unblemished</b> non-sacred animal as a substitute <b>in place of the blemished</b> consecrated animal, i.e., <b>to associate</b> its sanctity, and he therefore is flogged three sets of lashes? <b>Or perhaps, here too</b> one applies the principle that <b>anywhere that there exists</b> both <b>a permitted</b> and a prohibited manner to perform an action, a person <b>does not perform the prohibition.</b> If so, his intention with regard to <b>this latter</b> blemished consecrated animal was <b>to desacralize</b> it, and therefore he is not flogged for transferring its sanctity to a substitute.
<b>And if you say</b> that <b>here too, since</b> this <b>man</b> has <b>not</b> yet been <b>established as</b> one who regularly transgresses <b>a prohibition,</b> until he has done so three times, therefore it is assumed that he <b>does not cast aside the permitted</b> manner <b>and perform the prohibition, Rav Ashi</b> therefore <b>raises</b> the following <b>dilemma:</b> If <b>there were four sacrificial animals</b> standing <b>before him, one of which was blemished</b> and the other three were unblemished, <b>and four non-sacred animals,</b> all of which were unblemished, <b>and he said: These</b> animals <b>are hereby in place of [<i>taḥat</i>] those</b> animals, <b>what is</b> the <i>halakha</i>?
Again, the Gemara clarifies the dilemma: <b>Here, since</b> it is <b>certain</b> that he intended for three unblemished non-sacred animals to be consecrated as substitutes in place of the three unblemished consecrated animals, this <b>man</b> has been <b>established as</b> one who regularly transgresses <b>a prohibition.</b> Therefore, it is assumed that he also intended for one of the unblemished non-sacred animals to be consecrated as a substitute in place of the blemished consecrated animal, which means he is liable to be <b>flogged four</b> sets of <b>lashes</b> for the four substitutions.
<b>Or perhaps, even though</b> in this case he has been <b>established as</b> one who transgresses <b>the prohibition,</b> nevertheless even such <b>a person does not cast aside the permitted</b> manner <b>and perform the prohibition. And</b> if so, his intention with regard to <b>this latter</b> blemished animal was <b>to desacralize</b> it. The Gemara concludes that all of these dilemmas <b>shall stand</b> unresolved.
