Rav Ashi <b>said to</b> Rava from Parzakya: There was <b>nothing preventing the verse from writing:</b> You shall not bring <b>the payment of a prostitute or a dog</b> into the House of the Lord your God. Since the verse states: “You shall not bring the payment of a prostitute or the price of a dog,” this indicates that these are two different cases, and there is no prostitution with regard to an animal.
The Gemara adds that <b>this is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>From where</b> is it derived <b>with regard to the payment</b> of a lamb to another for engaging in intercourse with <b>a dog, or</b> as <b>the price of a prostitute</b> to purchase her as his maidservant, <b>that</b> their sacrifice <b>is permitted? As it is stated:</b> “For even both of them are an abomination to the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 23:19), from which it is inferred: <b>Two</b> are prohibited, payment to a prostitute and the price of a dog, <b>and not four,</b> i.e., payment for intercourse with a dog and the price of a prostitute, which are permitted. Furthermore, sacrifice of <b>their offspring is permitted, as it is stated: “Even both of them,”</b> which indicates that it is <b>“them”</b> that are forbidden, <b>and not their offspring.</b>
The Gemara discusses other cases of offspring of animals with regard to sacrifice on the altar. <b>Rava says: The offspring</b> of a female <b>animal that was the object of bestiality</b> when it was pregnant is <b>prohibited</b> to be sacrificed on the altar, like its mother. The reason is that it is considered as though <b>it,</b> the mother, <b>and its offspring</b> both <b>were the object of bestiality.</b> Likewise, if a pregnant cow <b>gores</b> and kills a Jew, its offspring is <b>prohibited</b> to be sacrificed, like its mother, as it is as though <b>it,</b> the mother, <b>and its offspring</b> both <b>gored.</b>
But <b>the offspring of a set-aside</b> animal <b>and</b> an animal <b>worshipped</b> as an idol are <b>permitted</b> to be sacrificed on the altar. <b>What is the reason?</b> It is that only <b>the mother</b> was <b>set aside,</b> and not the offspring. Likewise, <b>they worshipped</b> only <b>its mother,</b> not the offspring. <b>There are</b> those <b>who say</b> a different version of Rava’s statement: <b>The offspring</b> of <b>a set-aside</b> animal <b>and</b> the offspring of an animal <b>worshipped</b> as an idol are <b>prohibited. What is the reason?</b> It is <b>that</b> it is <b>beneficial to</b> the owner if <b>its girth</b> is large, as a pregnant animal is more impressive than an ordinary one. Consequently, its offspring is also considered set-aside and worshipped, and it is therefore prohibited.
<b>Rav Aḥadevoi bar Ami says</b> that <b>Rav says:</b> With regard to <b>one who betroths</b> a woman <b>with the excrement of an ox that is</b> sentenced to be <b>stoned,</b> if the excrement is worth at least one <i>peruta</i>, the woman <b>is betrothed.</b> Although it is not permitted to derive benefit from the ox itself, one may derive benefit from its excrement. But if one betroths her <b>with the excrement of calves</b> that were used for <b>idol worship, she is not betrothed,</b> as even their excrement is prohibited. The Gemara explains: <b>What is the reason</b> for the difference between the excrement of an ox that is stoned and the excrement of calves used for idolatry? <b>If you wish, say</b> that this is learned from <b>a verse; if you wish, say</b> instead that it is derived by means of <b>a logical argument.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: <b>If you wish, say</b> it is derived by means of <b>a logical argument: With regard to</b> calves used for <b>idol worship,</b> a calf’s additional <b>girth</b> due to the excrement stored in its body <b>is beneficial to</b> the owner, as fatter animals are more impressive. Therefore, the excrement is considered part of the offering and is prohibited. By contrast, <b>with regard to an ox that is stoned, its</b> additional <b>girth is not beneficial to him,</b> as he gains nothing from it, and consequently it is not considered part of the animal.
<b>If you wish, say</b> that this is learned from <b>a verse: It is written with regard to</b> an animal used for <b>idol worship:</b> “And you shall not bring an abomination into your house, <b>and be [<i>vehayita</i>] accursed like it”</b> (Deuteronomy 7:26). This teaches that <b>anything that you create [<i>mehayye</i>] from</b> the idol <b>is</b> prohibited <b>like</b> the idol <b>itself.</b> But <b>with regard to an ox that is stoned, it is written:</b> “The ox shall surely be stoned, and <b>its flesh shall not be eaten”</b> (Exodus 21:28). This teaches that only <b>its flesh is forbidden,</b> but <b>its excrement is permitted.</b>
<strong>MISHNA:</strong> If one <b>gave money</b> to a prostitute as her payment, it is <b>permitted</b> to purchase an offering with <b>that</b> money, as the money itself is not sacrificed. If he paid her with <b>wine, or oil, or flour, or any</b> other <b>item the like of which is sacrificed on the altar,</b> sacrifice of those items <b>is prohibited.</b> If <b>he gave her consecrated</b> items for her services, <b>their</b> sacrifice <b>is permitted.</b> Since they were already consecrated, they do not belong to him, and one cannot prohibit an item that is not his.
If he paid her with non-sacred <b>birds, their</b> sacrifice <b>is prohibited.</b> The mishna elaborates: <b>As, by right, it should be</b> inferred <i>a fortiori</i>: <b>If</b> in the case of <b>consecrated</b> items, <b>which a blemish disqualifies,</b> the prohibition of <b>payment</b> to a prostitute <b>and</b> the <b>price</b> of a dog <b>do not take effect with regard to them;</b> with regard to <b>a bird, which a blemish does not disqualify, is it not right that</b> the prohibition of <b>payment</b> to a prostitute <b>and</b> the <b>price</b> of a dog <b>should not take effect with regard to them?</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “You shall not bring the payment of a prostitute, or the price of a dog, into the House of the Lord your God <b>for any vow”</b> (Deuteronomy 23:19). This serves <b>to include the bird</b> in the prohibition.
The mishna adds a principle: With regard to <b>all</b> animals <b>whose</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar is prohibited,</b> sacrifice of <b>their offspring is permitted. Rabbi Eliezer says: The offspring of an animal with a wound that will cause it to die within twelve months [<i>tereifa</i>]shall not be sacrificed on the altar. Rabbi Ḥanina ben Antigonus says: A kosher</b> animal <b>that suckled from a <i>tereifa</i> is disqualified from</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar.</b> With regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals <b>that became <i>tereifot</i>, one may not redeem them</b> and render them non-sacred, <b>as</b> their consumption is forbidden and <b>one does not redeem sacrificial</b> animals <b>to feed them to dogs,</b> as this is considered a degradation of sacrificial animals.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>The Sages taught: If one gave wheat to</b> a prostitute <b>and</b> she <b>made</b> the wheat into <b>flour,</b> or if he gave her <b>olives and she made them</b> into <b>oil,</b> or if he gave her <b>grapes and she made them</b> into <b>wine,</b> it <b>is taught</b> in <b>one</b> <i>baraita</i> that these products <b>are prohibited</b> to be used as an offering in the Temple, <b>and</b> it <b>is taught</b> in <b>another</b> <i>baraita</i> that they <b>are permitted,</b> as the physical change transforms them into new objects. <b>Rav Yosef said</b> that <b>Guryon of Aspork teaches</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>Beit Shammai prohibit</b> these products <b>and Beit Hillel permit</b> them. If so, the two <i>baraitot</i> cited above reflect a dispute between Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel.
The Gemara explains that Beit Shammai and Beit Hillel both derive their ruling from the verse: “You shall not bring the hire of a harlot, or the price of a dog, into the House of the Lord your God for any vow; for even both of them are an abomination to the Lord your God” (Deuteronomy 23:19). <b>Beit Hillel,</b> who permit these items after they have undergone a physical change, <b>maintain</b> that the word <b>“them”</b> teaches that this prohibition applies only to the original items, <b>but not</b> to <b>their offspring.</b> Likewise, it applies only to <b>them,</b> i.e., the items in their original form, <b>but not</b> to <b>their changed</b> status.
<b>Beit Shammai maintain</b> that one indeed derives: <b>“Them,” but not their offspring.</b> But they maintain that the word <b>“even”</b> serves <b>to include</b> in the prohibition items in <b>their changed</b> status. The Gemara asks: <b>And according to</b> the opinion of <b>Beit Hillel, isn’t it written “even”?</b> How do they interpret that word? The Gemara responds: The word <b>“even”</b> is in fact <b>difficult according to Beit Hillel.</b>
<b>The Sages taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, with regard to the verse: “You shall not bring the hire of a harlot, or the price of a dog, into <b>the House of the Lord your God,”</b> that this <b>excludes a</b> red <b>heifer, which is not brought to the House</b> of the Lord, i.e., the Temple, but is slaughtered outside Jerusalem on the Mount of Olives. This is <b>the statement of Rabbi Elazar. And the Rabbis say:</b> This verse serves <b>to include</b> in the prohibition beaten <b>plates</b> of gold in the Temple, if the gold was used as payment to a prostitute or as the price of a dog.
The Gemara asks: <b>Who are the Rabbis</b> cited here? <b>Rav Ḥisda says: It is</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yosei, son of Rabbi Yehuda, as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: If <b>one gave</b> a prostitute <b>gold</b> as her payment, <b>Rabbi Yosei bar Yehuda says: One may not fashion</b> hanging beaten <b>plates</b> of gold for the Temple from it, <b>even</b> for the area <b>behind the Hall of the Ark Cover,</b> i.e., the eleven cubits of space behind the Holy of Holies up to the wall of the Temple courtyard, which was at the western end of the Sanctuary. Athough this area is of lesser sanctity than the rest of the Temple, one may not place there any gold given as payment to a prostitute.
§ The mishna teaches: If <b>he gave her consecrated</b> items for her services, <b>their</b> sacrifice <b>is permitted,</b> whereas if he paid her with non-sacred birds, their sacrifice is prohibited. The prohibited status of birds is derived from the verse: “You shall not bring the hire of a harlot, or the price of a dog, into the House of the Lord your God for any vow,” despite an <i>a fortiori</i> inference from consecrated animals that indicates they should be permitted.
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> if these birds are disqualified as sacrifices, <b>let</b> the prohibitions of <b>payment</b> to a prostitute <b>and</b> the <b>price</b> of a dog <b>apply to consecrated</b> animals, <b>by</b> the following <b><i>a fortiori</i></b> inference: <b>If</b> in the case of <b>birds, which a blemish does not disqualify,</b> the status of a prostitute’s <b>payment and</b> a dog’s <b>price applies to them,</b> then with regard to <b>consecrated</b> items, <b>which a blemish does disqualify, is it not logical that</b> the status of a prostitute’s <b>payment and</b> a dog’s <b>price should apply to them?</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states: “For any vow.”</b> This serves <b>to exclude</b> those animals that have <b>already been vowed</b> to be sacrificed.
The Gemara objects: The only <b>reason</b> consecrated animals are disqualified <b>is that the verse excludes them,</b> through the word “vow.” <b>But had the verse not excluded them, I would say</b> that <b>if one gives</b> a prostitute <b>sacrificial</b> animals, or gives these animals in exchange for a dog, the prohibition of <b>payment</b> to a prostitute <b>or</b> a dog’s <b>price applies to them. But</b> this conclusion is problematic, as a consecrated animal <b>is not his property,</b> and there is an established principle that one cannot render an item prohibited if it does not belong to him. How then could one render it prohibited by giving it to a prostitute?
<b>Rav Hoshaya says:</b> This is referring <b>to one who registers</b> the prostitute <b>for his Paschal offering</b> (see <i>Pesaḥim</i> 61a), giving her a portion in it as payment for her services, <b>and it is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi, who maintains that a Paschal offering is considered one’s personal property for the purposes of allowing additional people to register for it. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: The verse states, with regard to the Paschal offering: <b>“And if the household be too little for a lamb,</b> then he and his neighbor next to his house shall take one” (Exodus 12:4). The phrase “if the household be too little” is interpreted to mean that the household cannot afford the basic necessities of the Festival.
Based on this interpretation, the phrase “for a lamb [<i>mihyot miseh</i>]” is interpreted to mean: <b>Sustain him from the lamb [<i>haḥyeihu miseh</i>],</b> i.e., he may use the Paschal offering as a means of supporting himself. He takes money from his neighbor in return for registering that neighbor for a portion of his Paschal offering and uses the money to purchase his needs. This <i>halakha</i> applies only if one lacks <b>sufficient means</b> to purchase food to <b>eat, but not</b> if he lacks merely <b>sufficient means</b> to <b>purchase</b> other items.
<b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> This lenient <i>halakha</i> applies <b>even</b> if one lacks <b>sufficient means</b> to <b>purchase</b> other necessary items, <b>as, if he does not have</b> sufficient funds <b>he may register others with him for his Paschal offering and for his Festival peace offering. And his money</b> that he receives for registering that person <b>is non-sacred, as it is on this condition</b> of registering others in exchange for the money which he receives from them that <b>the Jewish people consecrate their Paschal offerings.</b>
§ The mishna teaches: With regard to <b>all</b> animals <b>whose</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar is prohibited,</b> sacrifice of their offspring is permitted. <b>Rav says:</b> This ruling that with regard to <b>all</b> animals <b>whose</b> sacrifice <b>on the altar is prohibited,</b> sacrifice of <b>their offspring is permitted,</b> is not unanimous but is the opinion of the Rabbis. <b>And</b> indeed <b>it is taught with regard to</b> this case: <b>And Rabbi Eliezer prohibits</b> their offspring to be sacrificed on the altar.
<b>Rav Huna bar Ḥinnana says</b> that <b>Rav Naḥman says:</b> This <b>dispute</b> applies in a case <b>where</b> the animals <b>became pregnant and then were the objects of bestiality,</b> thereby becoming prohibited to sacrifice on the altar. <b>As Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that <b>a fetus is</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother</b> and regarded as part of the animal, and is consequently prohibited like its mother. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that a fetus <b>is not</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother. But</b> if the animals <b>were the objects of bestiality and then became pregnant, everyone agrees</b> that <b>they are permitted.</b>
<b>Rava says:</b> This <b>dispute</b> applies in a case <b>where</b> the animals <b>were the objects of bestiality and then became pregnant, as Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that when both <b>this</b> permitted factor <b>and that</b> prohibited factor <b>cause</b> a certain situation, the resulting item <b>is prohibited.</b> Since the mother of this offspring is prohibited, the offspring is likewise prohibited, despite the fact that its father is permitted. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that when <b>this</b> permitted factor <b>and that</b> prohibited factor <b>cause</b> a result, it, i.e., the offspring, is <b>permitted</b> like its father.
<b>But</b> in a case where the animals <b>became pregnant and then were the object of bestiality, everyone agrees</b> that <b>they are prohibited. And</b> the Gemara adds that <b>Rava</b> conforms <b>to his</b> standard line of <b>reasoning</b> in this regard, <b>as Rava said: The offspring</b> of a female animal that <b>was the object of bestiality</b> when it was pregnant is <b>prohibited</b> to be sacrificed on the altar, as it is considered as though <b>it,</b> the mother, <b>and its offspring were</b> both <b>the object of bestiality.</b> Likewise, <b>the offspring</b> of a pregnant cow that <b>gores</b> and kills a person <b>is prohibited,</b> because it is as though <b>it,</b> the mother, <b>and its offspring</b> both <b>gored.</b>
<b>Some state another version</b> of the previous discussion: <b>Rav Huna bar Ḥinnana says</b> that <b>Rav Naḥman says:</b> This <b>dispute</b> applies in a case <b>where</b> the animals <b>were the object of bestiality when they</b> already had the status of <b>sacrificial</b> animals. <b>As Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that the offspring are also prohibited to be sacrificed on the altar, as their sacrifice is considered a degradation of consecrated items, and <b>degradation</b> of consecrated items is <b>significant. And the Rabbis hold</b> that the offspring may be sacrificed, as they maintain that this is <b>not</b> considered a degradation of consecrated items. <b>But</b> in a case where the animals <b>were the object of bestiality when they were non-sacred,</b> and were subsequently consecrated, <b>since</b> their status <b>was changed</b> by their consecration, <b>everyone agrees</b> that <b>they are permitted.</b>
In contrast, <b>Rava says</b> that <b>Rav Naḥman</b> says: This <b>dispute</b> applies in a case <b>where</b> the animals <b>were the object of bestiality when they were non-sacred, as Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that even the sacrifice of this offspring is considered degrading to sacrificial animals, and <b>degradation</b> of sacrificial animals <b>is significant,</b> and therefore they are prohibited. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>since</b> their status <b>was changed</b> by their consecration, <b>they are permitted. But</b> in a case where the animals <b>were the object of bestiality when they</b> already had the status of <b>sacrificial</b> animals, <b>everyone agrees</b> that <b>they are prohibited.</b>

Daf 31a

§ The mishna teaches that with regard to <b>the offspring of a <i>tereifa</i>,</b> Rabbi Eliezer prohibits it to be sacrificed on the altar, whereas the Rabbis permit it. The Gemara explains: There is a dispute as to whether a <i>tereifa</i> is capable of giving birth. <b>According to the one who says</b> that <b>a <i>tereifa</i> can give birth, you find</b> this dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis in a case <b>where</b> the animal first <b>became a <i>tereifa</i> and then became pregnant.</b>
The Gemara elaborates: <b>And this is</b> the matter over which <b>they disagree, as Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that when <b>both this</b> permitted factor <b>and that</b> prohibited factor <b>cause</b> a certain situation, the resulting item <b>is prohibited.</b> Here too, as the mother of this offspring is prohibited, the offspring is likewise prohibited, despite the fact that its father is permitted. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that when <b>both this</b> permitted factor <b>and that</b> prohibited factor <b>cause</b> a situation, the resulting item is <b>permitted.</b> Therefore, the offspring in this case is permitted like its father.
By contrast, <b>according to the one who says</b> that <b>a <i>tereifa</i> cannot survive,</b> i.e., cannot give birth, <b>you find</b> this dispute in a case <b>where</b> the animal first <b>became pregnant and then became a <i>tereifa</i>. And this is</b> the point over which <b>they disagree, as Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that <b>a fetus is</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother,</b> and therefore just as its mother is prohibited to be sacrificed on the altar as a <i>tereifa</i>, the same applies to its offspring. <b>And the Rabbis hold</b> that <b>a fetus is not</b> considered <b>the thigh of its mother,</b> and consequently there is no reason to prohibit it to be sacrificed on the altar.
§ The Gemara continues to discuss the dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and the Rabbis. <b>Rav Huna says: The Rabbis concede to Rabbi Eliezer in</b> the case of <b>a chick</b> that emerges from <b>the egg of a <i>tereifa</i></b> dove <b>that it is prohibited</b> to be sacrificed on the altar. <b>What is the reason</b> that the Rabbis rule stringently here? The Rabbis <b>disagree with Rabbi Eliezer</b> and claim that the offspring of a <i>tereifa</i> is permitted <b>only with regard to the offspring of an animal, which grows from</b> its own <b>space,</b> i.e., even when it is in its mother’s womb it develops as an independent entity. <b>But</b> with regard to <b>an egg of a <i>tereifa</i></b> hen, which <b>grows from the body of the hen, even the Rabbis concede</b> that the chick which emerges from this egg may not be sacrificed on the altar.
<b>Rava said to Rav Huna: That</b> which <b>is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>supports your</b> opinion: With regard to <b>a full ladle [<i>tarvad</i>] of worms that come from a living person</b> who subsequently died, <b>Rabbi Eliezer</b> holds that it <b>transmits ritual impurity</b> in the same manner as a corpse, as a full ladle is the minimum amount of dust from the dead that imparts impurity; <b>and the Rabbis deem it pure.</b> Rava analyzes this <i>baraita</i>: <b>The Rabbis disagree with</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>only with regard to worms, which are merely a secretion. But</b> with regard to <b>an egg, which is</b> part <b>of the hen’s body, even the Rabbis concede</b> that in the case of a <i>tereifa</i>, the chick that emerges from the egg is prohibited to the altar.
<b>Abaye said to</b> Rava: No proof can be brought from here for the opinion of Rav Huna, as <b>on the contrary, the opposite is reasonable: Rabbi Eliezer disagrees with the Rabbis only with regard to worms,</b> as he maintains that worms are considered part of a person even if they emerged from him while he was still alive, <b>as a person is called a worm while</b> he is still <b>alive, as it is written: And the hope of man is a worm, and the son of man, that is a maggot</b> (see Job 25:6).
<b>But with regard to</b> a chick that emerges from <b>an egg,</b> one can say: <b>When</b> does the chick <b>grow</b> out of and emerge from the egg? It does so <b>when</b> the egg <b>rots, and when</b> it <b>rots it is</b> considered <b>merely dust</b> and is no longer attributed to the hen that laid it. Therefore, despite the fact that the hen is a <i>tereifa</i>, <b>even Rabbi Eliezer</b> should <b>concede</b> that the chick that emerges from its egg is permitted to be sacrificed. <b>And furthermore, it is explicitly taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> that <b>Rabbi Eliezer concedes to the Rabbis with regard to the chick</b> that emerged from the <b>egg of a <i>tereifa</i></b> animal <b>that it is permitted</b> to be sacrificed on the altar, which is not in accordance with the opinion of Rav Huna. Rava <b>said to</b> Abaye: <b>If this</b> <i>baraita</i> <b>is taught, it is taught,</b> and I cannot take issue with it.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>Rabbi Ḥanina ben Antigonus says: A kosher</b> animal that suckled from a <i>tereifa</i> is disqualified from being sacrificed on the altar. The Gemara asks: <b>What is the reason? If we say that</b> it is because the kosher animal <b>was fattened from</b> the <i>tereifa</i> animal, <b>if that is so,</b> then in a case where its owner <b>fed it vetches of idolatry, so too,</b> should one say <b>that it is prohibited?</b> Feeding an animal vetches of idolatry renders it prohibited only if the animal has been set aside for idolatry; a regular animal is not prohibited by this action.
<b>Rather, Rabbi Ḥanina Terita taught</b> the meaning of the mishna <b>before Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> This is referring to a case <b>where one gave</b> the kosher animal <b>to suckle</b> from the <i>tereifa</i> <b>warm,</b> i.e., fresh, <b>milk every morning. Since</b> the kosher animal <b>can maintain</b> its existence <b>based upon</b> this suckling <b>for</b> a twenty-four-hour <b>period,</b> until the next suckling, it survives only due to the <i>tereifa</i> animal. Consequently, the kosher animal is prohibited to be sacrificed on the altar.
§ The mishna further teaches: With regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals <b>that became <i>tereifa</i>, one may not redeem</b> them and render them non-sacred, as their consumption is prohibited, and one does not redeem sacrificial animals to feed them to dogs. The Gemara asks: <b>From where are these matters</b> derived? <b>As the Sages taught</b> with regard to the verse: “Notwithstanding you may slaughter and eat flesh within all your gates” (Deuteronomy 12:15). This verse, which is referring to disqualified consecrated animals that have been redeemed, is expounded as follows: <b>“You may slaughter,” but</b> you may <b>not shear</b> their wool; <b>“and eat,” but</b> you may <b>not</b> give <b>to your dogs</b> to eat; “and eat <b>flesh,” but not</b> their <b>milk. From here</b> it is derived <b>that one does not redeem sacrificial</b> animals <b>to feed them to dogs.</b>
<b>There are</b> those <b>who state</b> a different version: <b>“You may slaughter and eat,”</b> this teaches that <b>you have permission</b> to derive benefit <b>from them only from the time of slaughter and onward,</b> i.e., those benefits that come after slaughter, such as the consumption of its flesh, are permitted. But one may not derive benefit from their shearing or their milk, as these occur even when the animal is alive. It can be inferred from here that the <i>tanna</i> of this <i>baraita</i> <b>maintains</b> that <b>one may redeem sacrificial</b> animals <b>to feed them to dogs,</b> as this occurs after the slaughter.

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> <b>There are</b> elements that apply <b>to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar that do not</b> apply <b>to</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, and there are</b> elements that apply <b>to</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that do not</b> apply <b>to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar.</b>
One element exclusive to animals consecrated for the altar is <b>that</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar render</b> an animal exchanged for them <b>a substitute, and</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance do not render</b> an animal exchanged for them <b>a substitute.</b> In addition, if one slaughters an animal <b>consecrated for the altar</b> with the intention to eat it beyond its designated time, or if he ate the offering after its designated time, or if he ate the offering while ritually impure, <b>he is liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i> <b>for eating it due to</b> violation of the prohibitions of <b><i>piggul</i>, <i>notar</i>, and</b> eating while <b>ritually impure,</b> respectively.

Daf 31b

If animals consecrated for the altar became pregnant and then became blemished and gave birth after redemption, <b>their offspring and their milk are forbidden after their redemption. And one who slaughters them outside</b> the Temple courtyard is <b>liable</b> to receive <i>karet</i>. And the Temple treasurer <b>does not give compensation to craftsmen from</b> money designated for purchasing animals consecrated for the altar. And in all these instances, <b>that is not so with regard to</b> money <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance.</b>
<b>There are</b> elements that apply <b>to</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> that do not apply to animals consecrated for the altar, in <b>that unspecified consecrations are</b> designated <b>for Temple maintenance; consecration for Temple maintenance takes effect on all</b> items; <b>and one</b> is liable to bring a guilt offering and pay an additional payment of one-fifth for <b>misuse</b> of consecrated property, not only for the items themselves, but <b>for their by-products,</b> e.g., milk of a consecrated animal or eggs of a consecrated chicken; <b>and there is no benefit for the owner</b> from items consecrated for Temple maintenance, in contrast to some animals consecrated for the altar, e.g., a peace offering, from which there is benefit for the owner.
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> The Gemara questions the statement of the mishna that an item consecrated for the altar renders an animal exchanged for it a substitute: <b>And is</b> it an established <b>principle that anything consecrated for the altar renders</b> an animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute? But</b> there are offerings brought from <b>birds,</b> i.e., doves and pigeons, <b>which are consecrated for the altar, and</b> yet they do not render an animal exchanged for them a substitute, as <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Temura</i> 13a): <b>Meal offerings and bird</b> offerings <b>do not render</b> an animal exchanged for them <b>a substitute.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>When</b> this <i>halakha</i> of the mishna <b>was taught,</b> it was taught only <b>with regard to animal</b> offerings.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> there is <b>the offspring</b> of an animal consecrated for the altar, which <b>is consecrated for the altar, and</b> yet <b>we learned</b> in a mishna (<i>Temura</i> 12a): <b>The offspring</b> of an animal consecrated for the altar <b>does not render</b> an animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> The Gemara responds: In accordance with <b>whose</b> opinion <b>is this</b> mishna here (31a)? It <b>is</b> in accordance with the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehuda, who says: The offspring</b> of an animal consecrated for the altar <b>renders</b> an animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> there is the case of <b>a substitute itself, which</b> is <b>consecrated for the altar, and</b> yet <b>we learned</b> in that mishna (12a): <b>A substitute does not render</b> an animal exchanged for it <b>a substitute.</b> The Gemara responds: <b>When</b> this principle, that all animals consecrated for the altar render an animal exchanged for them a substitute, <b>was taught</b> in the mishna, it was taught only <b>with regard to the principal offering,</b> i.e., an animal initially consecrated as an offering, not an animal consecrated by extension, e.g., a substitute.
The Gemara comments: <b>Now that you have arrived at this</b> qualification of the established principle, one does not need to resolve the difficulty from the case of offspring of consecrated animals by saying that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda. Rather, <b>you</b> may <b>even say</b> that the mishna is in accordance with the opinion of <b>the Rabbis,</b> who maintain that the offspring of an animal does not render an animal exchanged for it a substitute, since <b>when</b> this principle <b>was taught</b> in the mishna it was taught only with regard to <b>the principal offering.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that the Temple treasurer <b>does not give craftsmen compensation from</b> money designated for purchasing animals consecrated for the altar. The Gemara asks: It may be inferred from the mishna <b>that consecrations for Temple maintenance are given</b> to craftsmen. From where is this <i>halakha</i> derived? <b>Rabbi Abbahu said:</b> It is derived from a verse, <b>as the verse states,</b> with regard to the mitzva to build the Tabernacle: <b>“And let them make Me</b> a sanctuary, that I may dwell among them” (Exodus 25:8). The verse indicates that the Tabernacle can be constructed <b>from</b> that which is <b>Mine,</b> i.e., consecrated property. Consequently, consecrated property may be used to pay the wages of a craftsman for work performed in the service of the Tabernacle.
§ The mishna teaches that <b>there are</b> elements that apply <b>to</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> that do not apply to animals consecrated for the altar, as unspecified consecrations are designated for Temple maintenance rather than for the altar. Additionally, consecration for Temple maintenance takes effect on all items, but not all items may be consecrated for the altar. The Gemara discusses these <i>halakhot</i>: <b>The Master said</b> in the mishna that <b>unspecified consecrations</b> are designated <b>for Temple maintenance, and</b> consecration for Temple maintenance <b>takes effect</b> on <b>all</b> items. <b>Who is the <i>tanna</i></b> whose ruling is stated in this mishna? <b>Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said</b> that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan said:</b> The mishna is <b>not in accordance with</b> the opinion of <b>Rabbi Yehoshua.</b>
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a mishna (<i>Shekalim</i> 4:7): In the case of <b>one who consecrates his possessions</b> without specification, <b>and among</b> the consecrated property <b>there was an animal suitable</b> for sacrifice <b>upon the altar, male or female,</b> what should be done with it? <b>Rabbi Eliezer says: Males</b> that are suitable to be brought as a burnt offerings should <b>be sold for the needs of burnt offerings,</b> i.e., to individuals obligated to bring a burnt offering. <b>And females</b> should <b>be sold for the purpose of</b> being used for <b>peace offerings. And</b> the <b>money</b> received from <b>their</b> sale is <b>allocated with the rest of</b> his <b>property for Temple maintenance.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yehoshua says:</b> The <b>males</b> are not sold; rather, they <b>themselves</b> should <b>be sacrificed as burnt offerings, and</b> the <b>females</b> should <b>be sold for the purpose of</b> being used for <b>peace offerings</b> and they will be offered as such, <b>and</b> one should purchase and <b>bring burnt offerings with the money</b> received from <b>their</b> sale. <b>And the rest of the property,</b> which is unsuitable for sacrificial use, is allocated <b>for Temple maintenance.</b> Evidently, Rabbi Yehoshua maintains that unspecified consecrations are not necessarily designated for Temple maintenance.
The Gemara notes: <b>And</b> this explanation of the dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua, stated by Rabbi Ḥiyya bar Abba in the name of Rabbi Yoḥanan, <b>disagrees with</b> the explanation of <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava. As Rav Adda bar Ahava says</b> that <b>Rav says: With regard to a herd that is entirely male, even Rabbi Eliezer concedes</b> that suitable animals are brought as burnt offerings, <b>as a person does not set</b> aside his ability to consecrate to the altar an animal fit to be <b>consecrated for the altar and</b> instead <b>consecrate</b> it <b>for Temple maintenance.</b> Even if the owner consecrated these animals without specification, it is presumed that he consecrated them for sacrifice upon the altar.
Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua <b>disagree only with regard to</b> a case where one consecrates <b>a herd that has one-half males and one-half females. And</b> their dispute is as follows: <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that one does <b>not divide his vow,</b> i.e., state a single vow that must be fulfilled in different ways, <b>and</b> therefore, <b>from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <b>females are not</b> consecrated as <b>burnt offerings,</b> as burnt offerings must be male, it must be that <b>the males are also not</b> consecrated as <b>burnt offerings.</b>
<b>And Rabbi Yehoshua holds</b> that a person <b>does divide his vow.</b> Accordingly, the males, which are fit to be brought as burnt offerings, are consecrated for the altar, whereas the females are sold to those who must bring peace offerings, and the money from their sale is used to purchase burnt offerings. According to this interpretation, Rabbi Eliezer concedes that one does not consecrate for Temple maintenance an animal fit for the altar. Consequently, the mishna is not in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Eliezer either.
The Gemara adds: <b>Some say another version</b> of the explanation of the dispute between Rabbi Eliezer and Rabbi Yehoshua. <b>Rav Adda bar Ahava says</b> that <b>Rav says: In</b> a case <b>where</b> one <b>consecrated</b> without specification <b>only</b> the type of <b>animal</b> that is suitable as an offering, <b>even Rabbi Eliezer concedes</b> that all the animals are consecrated for the altar. Accordingly, the males are brought as burnt offerings, and the females are sold to those who require peace offerings, and the money from their sale is used to purchase burnt offerings. The reason is <b>that a person does not set</b> aside his ability to consecrate for the altar an animal fit to be <b>consecrated for the altar and</b> instead <b>consecrate</b> it <b>for Temple maintenance.</b>
<b>They disagree only</b> with regard to a case <b>where there is other property</b> that was consecrated <b>with</b> the animals. <b>And</b> their dispute is as follows: <b>Rabbi Eliezer holds</b> that <b>a person does not divide his vow.</b> Consequently, <b>from</b> the fact <b>that</b> the <b>other property is not consecrated for the altar,</b> as it is not fit for sacrifice, it must be that the <b>animals are also not consecrated for the altar. And Rabbi Yehoshua holds</b> that <b>a person divides his vow.</b> Accordingly, the animals are consecrated for the altar, while the other property is consecrated for Temple maintenance. According to this explanation as well, the mishna is not in accordance with the opinions of either Rabbi Yehoshua or Rabbi Eliezer.
The Gemara discusses the two versions of the explanation of Rav Adda bar Ahava, in light of the statement of Rabbi Eliezer in the mishna cited above in <i>Shekalim</i> (4:7): <b>And</b> the <b>money</b> from <b>their</b> sale is <b>allocated with the rest of his property for Temple maintenance.</b> The Gemara asks: <b>Granted, according to the latter version,</b> that Rabbi Eliezer maintains that when one consecrates animals and other property he does not divide his vow, and as some of his property is unfit for the altar, all of his property is consecrated for Temple maintenance, <b>this</b> explanation is consistent with <b>that which</b> Rabbi Eliezer <b>teaches:</b> The money from their sale is allocated <b>with the rest of his property for Temple maintenance,</b> i.e., the money from their sale is consecrated for Temple maintenance due to the consecration of his other property.
<b>But according to the first version,</b> that Rabbi Eliezer maintains that one does not divide his vow even when he consecrates only animals, i.e., he does not consecrate some as burnt offerings and others as peace offerings, <b>let</b> the mishna <b>teach:</b> The money from their sale is <b>allocated for Temple maintenance,</b> without mentioning the rest of his property. The Gemara responds: Indeed, the mishna should be taught in such a manner, and <b>this is also taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> the <b>money</b> from <b>their</b> sale is <b>allocated for Temple maintenance.</b>
§ The mishna teaches that <b>consecration for Temple maintenance takes effect on all</b> items. This emphasis indicates that it takes effect even on items that one might otherwise have thought are not consecrated. The Gemara asks: <b>What</b> does the mishna mean <b>to add</b> by this emphasis? <b>Ravina said:</b> It means <b>to add shavings and fallen leaves</b> of a tree that was consecrated for Temple maintenance.
The mishna further teaches that <b>one</b> is liable for <b>misuse</b> of items consecrated for Temple maintenance and for <b>their by-products,</b> but one is not liable for misuse of by-products of animals consecrated for the altar. The Gemara elaborates: When the mishna states that one is not liable for misuse of by-products of animals consecrated for the altar, this serves <b>to add what? Rav Pappa said:</b> It serves <b>to add</b> that one is not liable for misuse <b>of</b> by-products of animals <b>consecrated for the altar,</b> such as <b>milk</b> from <b>sacrificial</b> animals <b>and eggs of pigeons.</b>
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>milk of sacrificial</b> animals <b>and eggs of pigeons,</b> one <b>may not derive benefit</b> from them <i>ab initio</i>, <b>but</b> if one derived benefit from them, he is <b>not</b> liable for <b>misuse. In what</b> case <b>is this statement said?</b> It is said <b>with regard to</b> milk and eggs of animals <b>consecrated for the altar. But with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> e.g., if <b>one consecrated a chicken</b> for Temple maintenance, one is liable for <b>misusing its eggs,</b> or if one consecrated <b>a female donkey</b> for Temple maintenance, one is liable <b>for misusing its milk.</b>
<b>And even according to the one who said</b> that one is liable for <b>misusing by-products of</b> animals consecrated for the <b>altar, this statement</b> applies only to <b>by-products that are fit for the altar, but</b> with regard to <b>by-products that are not fit for the altar,</b> e.g., eggs and milk, one is <b>not</b> liable for <b>misusing them.</b>

Daf 32a

<strong>MISHNA:</strong> While the previous mishna enumerated differences between consecration for the altar and consecration for Temple maintenance, this mishna enumerates <i>halakhot</i> that apply to both. With regard to <b>both</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar and</b> items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, one may not alter their</b> designation <b>from</b> one form of <b>sanctity to</b> another form of <b>sanctity. But one may consecrate</b> animals already <b>consecrated</b> for the altar by <b>a consecration of</b> their <b>value,</b> and that value is donated to the Temple treasury for maintenance. <b>And one may dedicate them</b> for the purpose of giving their value to the priests. <b>And if</b> animals consecrated either for the altar or for Temple maintenance <b>died, they must be buried. Rabbi Shimon says:</b> Although that is the <i>halakha</i> with regard to animals consecrated for the altar, <b>if</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance died, they can be redeemed.</b>
<strong>GEMARA:</strong> <b>Rav Huna says:</b> With regard to animals <b>consecrated for the altar, if one associated</b> such objects of his vow <b>with dedications</b> for the <b>priests,</b> i.e., he vowed to give their value as a dedication to the priests, <b>he has done nothing. What is the reason? The verse states:</b> “Notwithstanding, no devoted item that a man may devote unto the Lord of all that he has, whether of man or animal, or of his ancestral field, shall be sold or redeemed; <b>every dedicated item is most holy unto the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 27:28). The verse is interpreted to mean that <b>any dedicated</b> item <b>that is most holy,</b> i.e., that was consecrated for the altar and then dedicated to the priests, <b>that</b> item <b>should be for the Lord,</b> and the priests are not entitled to it.
The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to the statement of Rav Huna from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to items <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that one associated</b> with another sanctity, <b>whether with a consecration for the altar,</b> whose sanctity is more stringent than consecration for Temple maintenance, <b>or with a dedication to the priests, he has done nothing.</b> The reason is that one cannot remove the sanctity of an item consecrated for Temple maintenance, and he has no ownership of value in it. As for the benefit of discretion, i.e., the right to choose the priest that will receive the offering, this is not relevant to items consecrated for Temple maintenance, and therefore it cannot be suggested that his act of consecration was with regard to the benefit of discretion.
Similarly, in the case of <b>a dedication to priests that one associated</b> with another sanctity, <b>whether with a consecration for the altar or with a consecration for Temple maintenance, he has done nothing.</b> One does not have any rights to an item dedicated to the priests, not even the benefit of discretion, as the dedicated item must be given to the members of the priestly watch serving in the Temple at the time of its dedication.
The Gemara explains the objection: Since the <i>baraita</i> teaches only that one may not associate with another sanctity items consecrated for Temple maintenance or dedications to priests, it may be inferred with regard to animals <b>consecrated for the altar that one associated with dedications to priests,</b> that <b>what he did is done,</b> i.e., it takes effect. If so, this <i>baraita</i> is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the statement <b>of Rav Huna.</b>
The Gemara responds: <b>Rav Huna</b> could have <b>said to you</b> that one cannot draw such an inference from the <i>baraita</i>, as it is possible that the <i>tanna</i> chose to discuss only those two instances while omitting the case of an animal consecrated for the altar. And as for the fact <b>that</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>omitted</b> this latter case, <b>he omitted</b> it due <b>to this</b> reason, <b>that</b> the <i>halakha</i> differs depending on the case: <b>With regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar that one associated with</b> a consecration <b>for Temple maintenance, what he did is done,</b> and he must pay the Temple treasury a consecration of value, <b>but</b> if he associated it <b>with a dedication to priests, he has done nothing.</b>
The Gemara objects: <b>But</b> if so, <b>let</b> the <i>tanna</i> <b>teach</b> explicitly that one who associates an animal consecrated for the altar with a dedication to the priests has done nothing, <b>alongside these</b> other cases of items consecrated for Temple maintenance and dedications to priests. The Gemara explains: The <b><i>tanna</i> teaches</b> instances <b>where there are two</b> types of consecration with which the item cannot be associated, e.g., an item consecrated for Temple maintenance, which cannot be associated with either a consecration for the altar or a dedication to the priests. By contrast, the <i>tanna</i> <b>does not teach</b> a case <b>where there are not two</b> instances with which the item cannot be associated, i.e., an animal consecrated for the altar. Although an animal consecrated for the altar cannot be associated with a dedication to the priests, it can be associated with a consecration for Temple maintenance.
The Gemara raises another objection to the statement of Rav Huna from that which <b>we learned</b> in the mishna: <b>One may consecrate</b> animals already consecrated for the altar by <b>a consecration of</b> their <b>value,</b> and one can dedicate them. The Gemara asks: <b>What, is it not</b> correct to say that when the mishna is referring to <b>a consecration of</b> their <b>value,</b> this is a consecration <b>for Temple maintenance, and</b> when it states that <b>one dedicates</b> them, this is referring <b>to dedications to a priest?</b> Evidently, one can associate an animal consecrated for the altar with a dedication to priests.
The Gemara responds: <b>No,</b> this is not the meaning of the mishna. Rather, both <b>this and that</b> are referring to <b>consecrations for Temple maintenance. And</b> the mishna is teaching that there <b>is no difference</b> whether <b>he expresses a term of consecration,</b> i.e., if he states: This animal is consecrated for Temple maintenance, in which case the animal is consecrated <b>for Temple maintenance, and</b> there <b>is no difference</b> if <b>he expresses a term of dedication,</b> i.e., he states: This animal is dedicated for Temple maintenance, as in this case too, the animal is consecrated <b>for Temple maintenance.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But this is not</b> how it is <b>taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>. <b>As it is taught in a <i>baraita</i>:</b> One can consecrate animals consecrated for the altar by <b>a consecration of</b> its <b>value,</b> that is, <b>a consecration for Temple maintenance,</b> and <b>one can dedicate them</b> by <b>a dedication to priests. And furthermore, isn’t it taught</b> in another <i>baraita</i> with regard to animals <b>consecrated for the altar that</b> one subsequently <b>consecrated</b> their value <b>as dedications to the priests,</b> that <b>what he did is done?</b> The Gemara concludes: <b>The refutation of</b> the opinion of <b>Rav Huna</b> is <b>a conclusive refutation.</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But Rav Huna says a verse</b> as the source for his opinion. Since his opinion is refuted, how should the verse be interpreted? <b>Ulla said:</b> That verse is not interpreted in the manner of Rav Huna. The <b>verse states:</b> “Notwithstanding, no dedicated item that a man may devote unto the Lord of all that he has, whether of man or animal, or of his ancestral field, shall be sold or redeemed; every <b>dedicated</b> item is most holy unto the Lord” (Leviticus 27:28). The emphasis that <b>“every dedicated item”</b> is most holy indicates that a dedication to the priests takes effect on every item, even with regard to animals consecrated for the altar.
Since Ulla holds that animals consecrated for the altar may be associated with a dedication to priests, evidently he also holds that they may be associated with a consecration for Temple maintenance, as even Rav Huna agrees they may be associated with such a consecration. The Gemara therefore asks: <b>And did Ulla</b> actually <b>say this? But didn’t Ulla say</b> that <b>one who associates a burnt offering</b> with a consecration <b>for Temple maintenance has only the delay</b> of

Daf 32b

the <b>treasurers alone</b> preventing the offering from being sacrificed. In other words, the owner of an offering must stand over the animal when it is sacrificed, and in the case of a burnt offering that was associated with a consecration for Temple maintenance, the Temple treasurer is the owner. Evidently, Ulla maintains that the offering does not require an appraisal for a consecration of its value before it may be sacrificed, despite the fact that it was associated with a consecration for Temple maintenance.
The Gemara responds: <b>Actually,</b> this <i>halakha</i>, that one who associates an animal consecrated for the altar with a consecration for Temple maintenance must give the value of the animal for Temple maintenance, was instituted by <b>the Sages;</b> by Torah law, there is only the delay attributed to the treasurers. This is also the opinion of Ulla. <b>And</b> as for the <b>verse</b> that Ulla cites as a source for the <i>halakha</i> that an animal consecrated for the altar can be associated with dedications to priests (Leviticus 27:28), it is mere support for this rabbinic law. The verse actually <b>comes</b> to teach <b>that</b> one is subject to the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse</b> in the case of dedications to priests.
The Gemara asks: If the term in the verse “every dedicated item” serves to teach only that one is subject to the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse</b> in the case of dedications to priests, <b>why does one</b> need this <b>verse</b> at all? After all, an expression of <b>most holy is written in</b> the verse, as it states: “Is most holy unto the Lord,” and the <i>halakhot</i> of misuse apply to all sanctified items.
The Gemara rejects this claim: <b>And according to your reasoning,</b> that liability for misuse in the case of dedications to priests is derived from the term “is most holy,” then consider <b>that which Rabbi Yannai said:</b> The <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse are</b> written <b>explicitly in the Torah only</b> in the case <b>of</b> one who misuses <b>a burnt offering, as it is stated</b> with regard to the guilt offering brought for misuse: <b>“If anyone commit a trespass, and sin through error, in the holy items of the Lord”</b> (Leviticus 5:15). The verse indicates only that one is liable for misuse of burnt offerings, which are <b>exclusively for the Lord. But</b> the fact that the <i>halakhot</i> of misuse apply to <b>a sin offering or a guilt offering,</b> from which the priests or owners partake, <b>is derived only from that</b> which <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi taught.
This is <b>as it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>, that <b>Rabbi</b> Yehuda HaNasi <b>says:</b> The verse states at the conclusion of the passage discussing the sacrifice of a peace offering: “And the priest shall make them smoke upon the altar…<b>all the fat is the Lord’s”</b> (Leviticus 3:16). The word “all” serves <b>to include the portions of offerings of lesser sanctity in</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse;</b> all the more so is it the case that offerings of the most sacred order, e.g., a sin offering or guilt offering, are included.
The Gemara concludes its reasoning: <b>Why,</b> then, <b>do I</b> need <b>a verse</b> to include these offerings? After all, an expression of <b>“most holy,” is written with regard to</b> a sin offering and guilt offering. <b>Rather,</b> it must be that <b>even though “most holy” is written with regard to them,</b> nevertheless, <b>a verse</b> is <b>required to include them in</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse.</b> With regard to <b>dedications as well, even though “most holy” is written with regard to them, a verse is required to include them in</b> the <i>halakhot</i> of <b>misuse.</b>
§ The Gemara earlier cited a statement of Ulla with regard to one who associates a burnt offering with a consecration for Temple maintenance. The Gemara discusses <b>the</b> matter <b>itself: One who consecrates a burnt offering for Temple maintenance has only the delay</b> attributed to <b>the treasurers alone</b> preventing the offering from being sacrificed. The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to this opinion from a <i>baraita</i>: In the case of <b>one who consecrates a burnt offering for Temple maintenance,</b> it is <b>prohibited to slaughter it until it is redeemed.</b>
The Gemara explains that this <i>baraita</i> is referring to the <i>halakha</i> <b>by rabbinic law;</b> by Torah law, a burnt offering cannot be consecrated by means of a consecration of value, and only the presence of the treasurers is required for it to be sacrificed. The Gemara adds: <b>This too stands to reason, from</b> the fact <b>that the latter clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i> <b>teaches: If he transgressed and slaughtered it</b> before redeeming it <b>what he did is done</b> and he is not required to redeem it. But if the burnt offering must be redeemed by Torah law, how can the <i>baraita</i> state that what he did is done? Evidently, the burnt offering is redeemed by rabbinic law.
The Gemara asks: <b>Rather, what</b> will you say? Will you say that the <i>baraita</i> is referring to the <i>halakha</i> <b>by rabbinic law? If so, say the last clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>: <b>And</b> anyone who derives benefit from this burnt offering <b>misuses</b> consecrated property on <b>two</b> counts of <b>misuse,</b> one for deriving benefit from an animal consecrated for the altar and one for deriving benefit from an item consecrated for Temple maintenance. <b>But if</b> the burnt offering is consecrated with a consecration of value <b>by rabbinic law, why</b> does one who derives benefit from this burnt offering <b>misuse</b> consecrated property on <b>two</b> counts of <b>misuse?</b> The Gemara explains that <b>this</b> is what the <i>baraita</i> <b>is saying: And</b> if the consecration of value of this burnt offering was by Torah law, <b>it is fit to</b> be liable for <b>misuse on two</b> counts of <b>misuse.</b>
§ The mishna cites a dispute between Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis with regard to animals consecrated for the altar and animals consecrated for Temple maintenance. The Rabbis hold that <b>if they died</b> and were not redeemed, they cannot be redeemed and <b>they must be buried,</b> whereas Rabbi Shimon maintains that this is the <i>halakha</i> only with regard to animals consecrated for the altar; animals consecrated for Temple maintenance can be redeemed.
With regard to this dispute, <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan says: According to the Rabbis, both</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar and</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were included in the</b> <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> as it states in the Torah with regard to the redemption of consecrated animals: “And if it be any unclean animal, of which they may not bring an offering unto the Lord, then he shall set the animal before the priest. And the priest shall value it, whether it be good or bad; as you the priest values it, so shall it be” (Leviticus 27:11–12). Any consecrated animal that cannot be stood before the priest, e.g., one that died, is not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and must be buried.
<b>And Reish Lakish says: According to the Rabbis,</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> as that verse is discussing animals consecrated for Temple maintenance. Accordingly, if an animal consecrated for Temple maintenance died and was not redeemed, it must be buried without redemption. But animals <b>consecrated for the altar were not included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> as the verse is not referring to such animals. Accordingly, if they died they are redeemed.
The Gemara adds: <b>And</b> both <b>this</b> one <b>and that</b> one, Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish, <b>agree</b> that <b>according to Rabbi Shimon,</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were not included</b> in the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> and <b>therefore</b> if they died they are redeemed; <b>and</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar were included</b> in the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> and if they died they are not redeemed. <b>And</b> according to both Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish, <b>everyone agrees,</b> both the Rabbis and Rabbi Shimon, that if one consecrated for the altar an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset,</b> the animal <b>was not included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation</b> and it may be redeemed if it died.
The Gemara raises an objection to the explanation of Reish Lakish: <b>We learned</b> in the mishna: <b>Rabbi Shimon says</b> that animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that died can be redeemed.</b> The Gemara analyzes this statement of Rabbi Shimon: <b>Granted, according to</b> the explanation of <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan, who said</b> that <b>according to the Rabbis, both this and that,</b> i.e., animals consecrated for the altar and for Temple maintenance, <b>were included</b> in the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation</b> and if they died they are not redeemed; <b>this is</b> why it <b>was necessary</b> for <b>Rabbi Shimon to explain</b> that specifically in the case of animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance that died, they can be redeemed.</b> This serves to emphasize that Rabbi Shimon disagrees with the Rabbis only with regard to animals consecrated for Temple maintenance, not those consecrated for the altar.
<b>But according to</b> the explanation of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> that the Rabbis hold that only animals consecrated for Temple maintenance may not be redeemed if they died, <b>why must</b> Rabbi Shimon <b>explain</b> that he is referring to animals consecrated for Temple maintenance? <b>Let him say</b> simply: <b>If they died they can be redeemed,</b> and it would be obvious that he is speaking of animals consecrated for Temple maintenance, for if he were referring to animals consecrated for the altar, even the Rabbis agree that they may be redeemed.
The Gemara answers: <b>Reish Lakish</b> could have <b>said to you</b> that <b>Rabbi Shimon did not know what the first <i>tanna</i> said,</b> i.e., he was unsure whether the statement of the first <i>tanna</i>, the Rabbis, that the animal must be buried without redemption, is referring to animals consecrated for Temple maintenance or to animals consecrated for the altar. <b>And</b> therefore, <b>this</b> is what Rabbi Shimon <b>is saying to</b> the first <i>tanna</i>: <b>If</b> your statement is referring <b>to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar,</b> that they are included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and must be buried if they died without redemption, then <b>I concede to you.</b> By contrast, if your statement is referring <b>to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> then I disagree with you and rule that <b>if they died they can be redeemed.</b>
The Gemara notes that <b>it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i> <b>in accordance with</b> the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan:</b> The verse states: “And <b>if</b> it be any non-kosher <b>animal, of which they</b> may not <b>bring an offering</b> unto the Lord, then he shall set the animal before the priest. And the priest shall value it” (Leviticus 27:11–12). The Sages teach that <b>the verse is speaking of blemished</b> animals, <b>that they can be redeemed.</b> The verse indicates that one redeems a blemished animal by means of standing and valuation.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Do you say</b> that the verse is <b>speaking of blemished animals; or</b> perhaps <b>it is</b> referring <b>only to a non-kosher animal,</b> and it is teaching that a non-kosher animal that was consecrated for Temple maintenance is redeemed through standing and valuation. The <i>baraita</i> answers: <b>When it says</b> later in that passage: “And <b>if it be of a non-kosher animal, then he shall redeem it according to your valuation”</b> (Leviticus 27:27), <b>a non-kosher animal</b> is thereby <b>stated</b> as subject to redemption.

Daf 33a

But if the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation is already mentioned with regard to a non-kosher animal, <b>how do I realize</b> the meaning of the verse: <b>“And if it be any</b> non-kosher <b>animal, of which they may not bring an offering unto the Lord,</b> then he shall set the animal before the priest. And the priest shall value it.” The verse is referring <b>to blemished</b> animals, which are unfit for sacrifice just like non-kosher animals, and it teaches <b>that they can be redeemed.</b>
The <i>baraita</i> continues: One <b>might</b> have thought that any blemished animal <b>can be redeemed,</b> even <b>for a temporary blemish.</b> Therefore, <b>the verse states:</b> “Non-kosher animal, <b>of which they may not bring an offering,”</b> which indicates that only a blemished animal <b>that may not be sacrificed at all,</b> i.e., one with a permanent blemish, is redeemed. <b>This</b> animal with a temporary blemish <b>is</b> thereby <b>excluded, as it may not be sacrificed today, but</b> it may be sacrificed <b>tomorrow.</b>
The Gemara concludes its reasoning: <b>And the Merciful One states</b> that one should <b>perform standing and valuation for</b> a blemished animal, in accordance with the verse “Then he shall set the animal before the priest. And the priest shall value it.” Since the verse requires that the animal have a permanent blemish to be redeemed, it must be referring to an animal consecrated for the altar, as all animals consecrated for Temple maintenance may be redeemed, even unblemished ones. Furthermore, as this is an unattributed <i>baraita</i> that appears in the <i>Sifra</i>, it is in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Yehuda, the disputant of Rabbi Shimon, and therefore it follows the opinion of the Rabbis of the mishna. It may therefore be concluded from the <i>baraita</i> that the Rabbis hold that one redeems an animal consecrated for the altar with standing and valuation, as maintained by Rabbi Yoḥanan.
§ The Gemara analyzes the opinion of Reish Lakish. <b>Rav Giddel said</b> that <b>Rav said: What is the reasoning of Reish Lakish, who said</b> that according <b>to the Rabbis,</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were included</b> in the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> whereas animals <b>consecrated for the altar were not included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation?</b> The reasoning is <b>that the verse states:</b> “Then he shall set the animal before the priest. <b>And the priest shall value it, whether it be good or bad”</b> (Leviticus 27:12).
Rav explained: <b>What is an item</b> for <b>which</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>is not divided between good and bad,</b> i.e., where there is no difference between unblemished and blemished animals? <b>You must say</b> that <b>this</b> is an item <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> which has the same sanctity whether blemished or unblemished. <b>And the verse states:</b> “And the priest shall value <b>it.”</b> The restrictive term “it” serves <b>to exclude</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation.
The Gemara asks: <b>And</b> according to <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan,</b> who says that according to the Rabbis, animals consecrated for the altar are also included in the obligation of standing and valuation, the term <b>“it”</b> serves <b>to exclude what?</b> The Gemara responds: It serves <b>to exclude</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset</b> and was consecrated for the altar.
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> this is problematic <b>according to the <i>tanna</i></b> of the <b>school of Levi, who said that even</b> an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset</b> is <b>included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation. As Levi taught: Everything was included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation, even</b> an animal <b>blemished from the outset. And Levi taught likewise in his <i>baraita</i>: Even an undomesticated animal, and even birds</b> that are not brought as offerings, e.g., geese, are included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation. But <b>isn’t it written “it,”</b> which must serve to exclude some animal from the obligation of standing and valuation? If so, how can Levi teach that everything is included? The Gemara concludes: Indeed, this poses <b>a difficulty.</b>
The Gemara discusses the opinion of Rabbi Shimon in the mishna. <b>Rav Yehuda said</b> that <b>Rav said: What is the reasoning of Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> that animals <b>consecrated for the altar were included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> and therefore if they died without redemption they are buried, but animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were not</b> included in this <i>halakha</i>? <b>As the verse states:</b> “Then he shall set the animal before the priest. <b>And the priest shall value it, whether it be good or bad”</b> (Leviticus 27:11–12). <b>What is an item</b> for <b>which</b> the <i>halakha</i> <b>is divided between good and bad,</b> i.e., between an unblemished and blemished animal? <b>You must say</b> that <b>this</b> is an animal <b>consecrated for the altar, and the verse states:</b> “And the priest shall value <b>it,”</b> which serves <b>to exclude</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance</b> from the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation.
The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> that the verse is referring to a consecration for which there is a distinction between good and bad, then it <b>should have</b> stated: And the priest shall value it <b>between good and bad,</b> to indicate that this distinction is made. The term “whether it be good or bad” indicates that the two types have the same <i>halakha</i>, as claimed by the Rabbis. The Gemara concludes: This poses <b>a difficulty</b> for the opinion of Rabbi Shimon.
§ The Gemara <b>raises an objection</b> to the opinions of Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish from a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to consecrated animals that <b>died,</b> if they were <b>unblemished</b> they are not redeemed and <b>shall be buried, and</b> if they were <b>blemished they can be redeemed. In what</b> case <b>is this statement said?</b> It is said <b>with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar. But with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, both unblemished and blemished</b> animals <b>shall be buried.</b> This is the statement of the Rabbis.
<b>Rabbi Shimon says: Both</b> in a case where the animals that died were <b>consecrated for the altar and</b> in a case where they were <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance,</b> if they were <b>unblemished they can be buried,</b> and if they were <b>blemished they can be redeemed.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the statement <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, from the first clause</b> of the <i>baraita</i>, as Rabbi Yoḥanan holds that according to the Rabbis, both animals consecrated for the altar and those consecrated for Temple maintenance are included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and are buried if they died.
The Gemara answers that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could <b>say to you: What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing <b>with</b> an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset.</b> In such a case, the Rabbis rule that the animal was not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and is therefore redeemed if it dies. But a consecrated animal that subsequently developed a blemish and died must be buried.
The Gemara adds: <b>So too, it is reasonable</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is discussing animals blemished from the outset, <b>as, if you say that</b> it is referring to animals <b>whose consecrations preceded their blemishes,</b> then <b>Rabbi Shimon should disagree with</b> the Rabbis on this point and maintain that such blemished animals consecrated for the altar should be buried, not redeemed. After all, Rabbi Shimon holds that animals consecrated for the altar are included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation. <b>Rather,</b> must one <b>not conclude from</b> this that the <i>baraita</i> is dealing <b>with</b> an animal that is <b>blemished from the outset?</b>
The Gemara raises a difficulty: <b>But</b> if the <i>baraita</i> is referring to animals blemished from the outset, and for this reason the Rabbis hold that the animal is redeemed, then it may be inferred that the Rabbis maintain that if the animal developed a blemish after its consecration and died, it must be buried. If so, <b>let us say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> <b>is a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion of <b>Reish Lakish,</b> who maintains that according to the Rabbis, animals consecrated for the altar were not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and are redeemed if they died.
The Gemara responds: <b>Reish Lakish interprets</b> the <i>baraita</i> as referring <b>to</b> animals <b>whose consecrations preceded their blemishes.</b> Consequently, the statement of the Rabbis is in accordance with his opinion that animals consecrated for the altar were not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and are therefore redeemed if they died. The Gemara asks: <b>If so,</b> then the earlier question recurs, as <b>Rabbi Shimon should disagree with</b> the statement of the Rabbis and say that such blemished animals consecrated for the altar are buried.
The Gemara answers: <b>Reish Lakish reverses</b> the text of that <i>baraita</i>, i.e., he maintains that in that <i>baraita</i>, Rabbi Shimon explicitly states that animals consecrated for the altar are buried, whether they are unblemished or blemished. <b>And</b> he <b>raises</b> his <b>objection</b> to the opinion of Rabbi Yoḥanan <b>from another <i>baraita</i>, like this:</b> With regard to consecrated animals that <b>died, whether</b> they were <b>unblemished or blemished, they shall be buried. With regard to what</b> case is this ruling <b>stated?</b> It is stated <b>with regard to</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar, but in</b> the case of animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance, they can be redeemed.</b> This is the statement of the Rabbis.
The <i>baraita</i> continues: <b>Rabbi Shimon says:</b> With regard to animals consecrated for Temple maintenance that died, if they were <b>unblemished they shall be buried, and</b> if they were <b>blemished they can be redeemed.</b> This is apparently <b>a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Rabbi Yoḥanan, from the latter clause</b> of the statement of the Rabbis in this <i>baraita</i>. According to the <i>baraita</i>, the Rabbis rule that animals consecrated for the altar are redeemed, whereas according to Rabbi Yoḥanan, the Rabbis maintain that animals consecrated for the altar are buried.
The Gemara answers that <b>Rabbi Yoḥanan</b> could <b>say to you: What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing <b>with</b> an animal that was <b>blemished from the outset.</b> The Gemara notes: <b>So, too, it is reasonable</b> that the <i>baraita</i> is referring to animals blemished from the outset, <b>as, if you say</b> that it is dealing with animals <b>whose consecrations preceded their blemishes,</b> and the Rabbis hold that animals consecrated for Temple maintenance are buried whereas those consecrated for the altar are redeemed, then <b>Rabbi Shimon should disagree with</b> the statement of the Rabbis on both accounts, rather than disagreeing merely in the case of animals consecrated for Temple maintenance.
The Gemara asks: <b>But</b> if so, <b>let us say</b> that the <i>baraita</i> <b>is a conclusive refutation</b> of the opinion <b>of Reish Lakish,</b> as Rabbi Shimon does not disagree with the Rabbis in the case of animals consecrated for the altar. The Gemara answers that <b>Reish Lakish</b> could <b>say to you: What are we dealing with here?</b> We are dealing <b>with</b> animals <b>whose consecrations preceded their blemishes.</b> The Gemara reiterates: <b>But</b> in that case, <b>Rabbi Shimon should disagree with</b> the Rabbis. The Gemara responds: <b>Reish Lakish</b> could <b>say to you: Indeed, Rabbi Shimon disagrees</b> with the Rabbis even with regard to animals consecrated for the altar. The <i>baraita</i> mentions Rabbi Shimon’s opinion only with regard to consecrations for Temple maintenance, but he also disagrees with regard to consecrations for the altar.
§ The Gemara further discusses the dispute between Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish. <b>Rabbi Yirmeya said to Rabbi Zeira: According to Reish Lakish, who says</b> that <b>according to the Rabbis,</b> animals <b>consecrated for the altar were not included</b> in the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation, and it is taught</b> with regard to animals <b>consecrated for the altar</b>

Daf 33b

that <b>blemished</b> animals <b>can be redeemed; and we established</b> that according to Reish Lakish, the <i>baraita</i> is dealing <b>with</b> animals <b>whose consecrations preceded their blemishes,</b> which means that although the animal has inherent sanctity because it was consecrated when unblemished, nevertheless it can be redeemed; let us <b>conclude from</b> the <i>baraita</i> that <b>one may redeem sacrificial</b> animals in order <b>to feed them to dogs.</b>
The Gemara rejects this suggestion: One cannot arrive at this conclusion from the <i>baraita</i>, since when the <i>baraita</i> states that the animals died it is not referring to deaths due to natural causes. Rather, <b>what are we dealing with here? With</b> a case <b>where</b> one <b>transgressed</b> the prohibition against slaughtering the blemished animals <b>and slaughtered them</b> before they were redeemed, an act that nevertheless renders them permitted for human consumption after redemption.
<b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: With regard to <b>all sacrificial</b> animals <b>that developed a blemish and</b> one <b>slaughtered them, Rabbi Meir says</b> that <b>they shall be buried,</b> as he holds in accordance with the opinion of Rabbi Shimon that animals consecrated for the altar were included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation and must be buried if they died. <b>And the Rabbis say:</b> Since an animal consecrated for the altar is not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation, even if one slaughtered it, <b>it can be redeemed.</b>
<b>Rabbi Yirmeya</b> further <b>said to Rabbi Zeira: According to Rabbi Shimon, who said</b> that animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were not included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation</b> and are redeemed if they died, <b>why</b> does he state, in the last <i>baraita</i> cited earlier, that <b>unblemished</b> animals consecrated for Temple maintenance <b>shall be buried</b> and not redeemed? Rabbi Zeira responds: It is <b>because they are fit for sacrifice</b> upon the altar.
The Gemara explains the significance of the fact that these animals, which were consecrated for Temple maintenance, are fit for the altar. <b>As it is taught</b> in a <i>baraita</i>: <b>One who associates unblemished</b> animals, i.e., consecrates them by means of associating their sanctity with sanctified items, <b>for Temple maintenance,</b> when they are redeemed by the treasurer <b>they are redeemed only for the altar,</b> i.e., they are sold to those who must bring an offering, and the animals are sacrificed upon the altar. They may be redeemed only for the altar, not for any non-sacred use, <b>as any</b> consecrated item <b>that is fit</b> to be sacrificed <b>on the altar may never leave</b> the custody of the <b>altar.</b>
§ The Gemara continues to analyze the opinions of Rabbi Yoḥanan and Reish Lakish. <b>Rav Pappa said to Abaye, and some say</b> that <b>Rava</b> said to Abaye: <b>According to Rabbi Yoḥanan, who interprets</b> the above <i>baraita</i> as referring <b>to</b> an animal <b>blemished from the outset, and</b> who further claims that <b>everyone,</b> i.e., Rabbi Shimon and the Rabbis, <b>holds that</b> an animal <b>blemished from the outset was not included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> one may ask: <b>And</b> do they both agree that an animal blemished from the outset was <b>not</b> included in that <i>halakha</i>?
<b>But didn’t we learn</b> in a mishna (<i>Bekhorot</i> 14a): <b>All the sacrificial</b> animals in <b>which a permanent blemish preceded their consecration</b> are not sanctified as offerings; rather, they are sold and the money received is used to purchase valid offerings. <b>And</b> once <b>they are redeemed,</b> they <b>are obligated in,</b> i.e., subject to accounting their offspring, <b>a firstborn; and in the</b> priestly <b>gifts</b> of the foreleg, jaw, and maw; <b>and they emerge</b> from their sacred status and assume complete <b>non-sacred</b> status in order <b>to be shorn and to be utilized for labor.</b>
And <b>their offspring and their milk are permitted after their redemption. And one who slaughters them outside</b> the Temple courtyard is <b>exempt</b> from <i>karet</i>, even if the animal was not yet redeemed, <b>and</b> those animals <b>do not render</b> an animal exchanged for them <b>a substitute. And if</b> these animals <b>died</b> before they were redeemed, <b>they may be redeemed.</b>
<b>And Rav Yehuda says</b> that <b>Rav says,</b> with regard to this mishna: <b>This</b> statement, that one who consecrates a blemished animal for the altar may redeem it if it dies, is in accordance with <b>the statement of Rabbi Shimon, who says</b> that animals <b>consecrated for the altar were included in</b> the <i>halakha</i> of <b>standing and valuation,</b> but animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance were not</b> included. <b>As we learned</b> in the mishna that <b>Rabbi Shimon says: If</b> animals <b>consecrated for Temple maintenance died, they can be redeemed,</b> since they were not included in the <i>halakha</i> of standing and valuation. But animals consecrated for the altar that died are buried, as they were included in this <i>halakha</i>.
